Refutation: Existence and the Status of Non-Existents
Refutation exposing inconsistency in denial of non-existents as things

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation critiques the deniers' inconsistency: they deny that non-existents are things yet hold that whatever can be known is a fixed thing.

Core Idea
The deniers' own principle that 'every known thing is a fixed thing' undermines their denial of non-existents as things.

After presenting the objection, the refutation now turns to expose a contradiction in the deniers' position. The deniers claim that the implication of existence, space, and accidents is valid, but they excuse it by saying these are negated and not fixed things. The refutation argues that this contradicts their own principle: whatever can be reported about and known is a fixed thing. This lesson follows the logical turn of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation's opening point
The refutation states that the implication of existence, occupying space, and receiving accidents is valid, and the deniers' excuse (that these are negated and not fixed) refutes their own doctrine.
The deniers try to avoid the consequences of their position by denying that these are 'things'.
2
Understand the principle invoked
The refutation quotes the deniers' own principle: every known thing that can be reported about and to which knowledge relates is a fixed thing. Then it asks: if existence cannot be known or reported, what do they know?
This exposes an inconsistency: the deniers must admit either that existence can be known (contradicting their denial) or that they know nothing.
3
Examine the comparison with impossibles
The refutation points out that impossibles (like the non-existence of divinity) are known and reported, so they should also be 'things' according to the deniers' own criterion.
This leads to absurd consequences: the non-existence of divinity would become a thing, and the world's substances and accidents would be fixed in pre-eternity.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The refutation concludes that the deniers' doctrine leads to infinite things and is therefore absurd. It ends by criticizing their misunderstanding of essential attributes.
The final line notes that the deniers repeated something they heard without understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت النفاة : العلم الأزلي يتعلق بالمعلومات كلها كما هي، فيتعلق بوجود العالم حتى يتحقق له الوجود، ويتعلق باستحالة وجوده أزلا وجواز وجوده قبل وجوده، لكن المتعلق الحقيقي هو الوجود وساير المعلومات من ضرورة ذلك التعلق، ولا يستدعي ذلك التعلق في حق الباري سبحانه تقديراً وهميا وترديدا خياليا، هذا كمن عرف وحدانية الباري سبحانه وتعالى في الإلهية عرف إنما سواه ليس باله، ولا يستدعي تعدد علوم بكل مخلوق أنه ليس باله، ومن عرف أن زيدا في الدار عرف أنه ليس بموضع آخر سواها، ولا يستدعي ذلك أن يتعدد علمه بأنه ليس في دار عمر وبكر وخالد، بل يقع ذلك معلومًا لزوما ضرورة، ومثل هذه المعلومات لا تتناهى ويستحيل أن يقال : هذه المعلومات أشياء ثابتة حتى يكون عدم زيد في مكان كذا، أو عدم كل جوهر في حيز كذا أو عدم كل عرض في محل كذا إلى ما لا يتناهى أشياء ثابتة في العدم، فإن ذلك خروج عن قضايا العقل وتيه في مفازة الجهل. وأما إلزام الوجود من حيث هو وجود، والتحيز وقبول العرض وجميع الصفات التابعة للحدوث فمتوجه والاعتذار بأنه منفي وليس بثابت ولا شيء نقض صريح لمذهبهم، فإن كل معلوم أمكن الإخبار عنه وتعلق العلم به فهو شيء ثابت عندهم ويا ليت شعري إذا لم يمكن الإخبار عن الوجود، ولم يمكن تعلق العلم به فعن أي شيء يخبر وعن أي شيء يعلم، وليس ذلك كالمحال الذي تمثلوا به على أن المحالات مما يعلم ويخبر عنها، فهلا كانت أشياء حتى يلزم أن يكون عدم الإلهية أشياء ما والعالم بما فيه من الجواهر والأعراض أشياء ثابتة في الأزل، وكما لا تتناهى المعلومات لا تتناهى الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها وأصنافها، والعياذ بالله من مذهب هذا مآله، وأما كلامكم في الصفات الذاتية أنها لا تحصل بفعل الفاعل، وإنما الوجود من حيث هو وجود متعلق القدرة فشيء ما سمعوه ولم يفهموه، وما أحسنوا إيراده؛ لأنهم لم يتحققوا إصداره.
The deniers said: Eternal knowledge relates to all known things as they are, so it relates to the existence of the world until its existence is realized, and it relates to the impossibility of its existence eternally and the possibility of its existence before its existence, but the real object of relation is existence, and the rest of known things are by necessity of that relation. And that relation does not require, in the case of the Exalted Creator, an imaginary supposition or hypothetical oscillation. This is like one who knows the Oneness of the Exalted Creator in divinity knows that what is other than Him is not a god, and that does not require multiple sciences of every creature that it is not a god. And one who knows that Zayd is in the house knows that he is not in another place besides it, and that does not require that his knowledge multiplies that he is not in the house of 'Amr, Bakr, and Khalid, rather that occurs as known necessarily. And such known things are infinite, and it is impossible to say: These known things are fixed things, such that the non-existence of Zayd in a certain place, or the non-existence of every substance in a certain location, or the non-existence of every accident in a certain substrate, ad infinitum, are fixed things in non-existence. For that is a departure from the rulings of reason and a wandering in the desert of ignorance. As for the implication of existence as existence, and occupying space, and receiving accidents, and all attributes that follow upon temporality, it is valid, and the excuse that it is negated and not fixed and not a thing is a clear refutation of their doctrine. For every known thing that can be reported and to which knowledge relates is a fixed thing according to them. Would that I knew: If reporting about existence is impossible, and knowledge cannot relate to it, then about what thing do they report and about what thing do they know? And that is not like the impossible which they gave as an example, because impossibles are known and reported about. So why are they not things, such that it would follow that the non-existence of divinity is some things, and the world with its substances and accidents are fixed things in pre-eternity? And just as known things are infinite, so things are infinite in their genera, species, and kinds. And we seek refuge with Allah from a doctrine whose outcome is this. As for your statement about essential attributes that they are not produced by the act of the agent, and that existence qua existence is the object of power, it is something they heard but did not understand, and they did not present it well, because they did not master its output.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the deniers (al-nufat) and a detailed refutation. The objection argues that God's eternal knowledge relates to all things as they are, so it relates to the existence of the world and also to the impossibility of its existence in pre-eternity and the possibility of its existence before its existence. The real object is existence; other known things are implied. This does not require imaginary suppositions in God, just as knowing God's oneness implies knowing that others are not gods without multiple separate sciences. Likewise, knowing Zayd is in the house implies he is not elsewhere without multiple knowledges. Such implied knowledge is infinite, but it is absurd to say that the non-existence of Zayd in a certain place is a fixed thing in non-existence. The deniers argue against the Ash'ari view that non-existents are 'things'. The refutation responds: The implication of existence as existence, occupying space, and receiving accidents is valid; the deniers' excuse that it is negated and not a thing contradicts their own principle that whatever can be reported about and known is a fixed thing. If existence cannot be reported about or known, then what do they know? Impossibles are known and reported; why are they not things? Otherwise, the non-existence of divinity and the world's substances and accidents would be fixed things in pre-eternity. The refutation concludes that the deniers' doctrine leads to infinite things and is therefore absurd. The final line criticizes the deniers for misunderstanding the statement about essential attributes not being from the agent and existence being the object of power.

Correction: The refutation shows that the deniers themselves hold that whatever can be known is a fixed thing, so their denial of non-existents as things contradicts their own principle.

?

?

Negators' Identity Claim and the Author's Challenge
Negators assert identity of existence and essence; author challenges with temporal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' claim that existence and essence are identical, and the author's critique that their view is inconsistent with attributes of temporality.

Core Idea
The negators reduce all distinctions between attributes and essence to mere words, but face inconsistency with temporal attributes.

This passage presents the debate between the Ash'ari author and the negators (likely Mu'tazila) over the relationship between existence and essence. The negators argue that existence, essence, and all attributes are identical, reducing distinctions to verbal ones. The author then challenges them: if attributes following temporality are not effects of power, why not say all essential attributes follow temporality?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing too deeply. Then read the translation. Notice the shift from the author's critique to the negators' response and then back to the author's challenge.
2
Identify the negators' core claim
Find where the negators state that existence, essence, substantiality, etc. are all the same referent. Understand that they see the distinction as merely verbal.
The phrase 'لا يثول إلى معنى ومعنى، بل إلى لفظ ولفظ' is key.
3
Understand the author's challenge
The author imposes on the negators the attributes that follow temporality, like occupying space, and asks why they do not apply the same logic to all essential attributes.
This reveals an inconsistency in the negators' position.
4
Consider the reversed relation
The passage mentions that the negators might alternatively argue that occupying space is by power and existence follows it. Reflect on how this would change the debate.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Think about what this argument says about divine attributes and causality in Islamic theology. How does the Ash'ari position differ from the negators'?
This connects to larger discussions on God's power and the nature of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كلامكم في الصفات الذاتية أنها لا تحصل بفعل الفاعل، وإنما الوجود من حيث هو وجود متعلق القدرة فشيء ما سمعوه ولم يفهموه، وما أحسنوا إيراده؛ لأنهم لم يتحققوا إصداره. وأجابت النفاة بأن قالوا: وجود الشيء وعينه وذاته وجوهريته وعرضيته عندنا عبارات عن معبر واحد، وما أوجده الموجد فهو ذات الشيء، والقدرة تعلقت بذاته كما تعلقت بوجوده، وأثرت في جوهريته كما أثرت في حصوله وحدوثه، والتميز بين الوجود وبين الشيئية مما لا يثول إلى معنى ومعنى، بل إلى لفظ ولفظ، وهم على اعتقاد أن الأجناس والأنواع والعموم والخصوص فيها راجع إلى الألفاظ المجردة أو الوجوه العقلية والتقديرات الوهمية، وألزموا عليهم الصفات التابعة للحدوث كالتحيز وقبول العرض وقيام العرض بالمحل فإنها ليست من آثار القدرة ثم لم يثبتوها قبل الحدوث، فهلا قالوا الصفات الذاتية كلها تتبع الحدوث أيضا، وربما عكسوا عليهم الأمر في التابع والمتبوع وألزموهم القول بأن التحيز يقع بالقدرة والوجود يتبعه.
As for your speech about the essential attributes that they are not produced by an act of the agent, and that existence qua existence is the object of power— that is something they have heard but not understood, and they have not presented it well because they have not verified its derivation. And the negators answered by saying: existence of a thing, its essence, its quiddity, its substantiality, and its accidentally are in our view expressions of a single referent. What the bringer-into-existence brings into existence is the essence of the thing, and power attaches to its essence just as it attaches to its existence, and it has effect on its substantiality just as it has effect on its occurrence and temporality. The distinction between existence and thingness does not amount to meaning and meaning, but rather to word and word. And they hold that genera, species, universality, and particularity in them refer back to bare words or to mental aspects and estimative considerations. And they imposed upon themselves the attributes that follow from temporality, such as occupying space, receptivity to accident, and the subsistence of an accident in a substrate, for these are not effects of power; then they did not establish them before temporality. So why did they not say that all essential attributes also follow temporality? And perhaps they reversed the matter regarding the follower and the followed, and imposed upon them the statement that occupying space occurs by power and existence follows it.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage contains both the author's critique of the negators' view on essential attributes and the negators' response. The author first criticizes that the negators claim essential attributes are not produced by an act of the agent, and that only existence as such is an object of power, but they have not properly understood or presented this. Then the negators respond: they assert that existence, essence, quiddity, substantiality, and accidentally are all expressions for the same single reality. What God brings into existence is the very essence of the thing. Power attaches to the essence just as it attaches to existence, and it affects substantiality just as it affects coming-into-being and temporality. The distinction between existence and thingness is merely verbal, not real. Furthermore, they hold that genera, species, universals, and particulars are reducible to mere words or mental concepts. Then the author notes a problem for the negators: they accept that attributes following temporality (like occupying space, receiving accidents) are not effects of power and are not established before temporality. So why do they not say that all essential attributes also follow temporality? Or they might reverse the relation, claiming that occupying space is by power and existence follows it. The passage ends with an implicit challenge to the consistency of the negators' position.

Correction: The negators actually admit a verbal distinction (لفظ ولفظ) but deny a real distinction in meaning.

Checkpoint

A) As real and meaningful.
B) As a distinction in meaning but not in words.
C) As merely verbal, not amounting to different meanings.
D) As a distinction of essence and accident.

Key Takeaway
The negators' reduction of essential attributes to verbal distinctions conflicts with the independent reality of temporal attributes, creating an inconsistency in their theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the essence of a thing is identical to its existence, as the negators claim, then what does it mean for God to 'bring something into existence'? Does that not imply a distinction between His act and the thing's essence? Pause and consider: Do you truly believe that God's creative act adds nothing real to a thing? How does that affect your understanding of creation ex nihilo?
?
The negators admit that temporal attributes like occupying space are not effects of God's power. Yet they insist essential attributes are. Are you willing to follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it forces you to admit that all of God's attributes are temporal or that His power does not truly produce anything? Which path would you take, and what does that reveal about your commitment to theological consistency?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God that you have never examined for internal consistency. Are you ready to subject it to the same scrutiny the author applies to the negators? Write down one such belief and ask yourself: Does this belief lead to a contradiction when taken to its logical conclusion? What would it cost you to revise it?

Existence and Essence Are Identical (The Negators' View)
The negators assert that existence and essence are identical expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' claim that existence and essence are identical and that any distinction between them is merely verbal.

Core Idea
Existence and essence are not two things; they are one reality expressed by different words.

In the debate over divine attributes, one central question is whether existence is an additional attribute to essence. The negators (al-nufāt) respond by asserting that existence and essence are fundamentally identical. This passage presents their core argument, explaining that all terms like existence, identity, substantiality, and accidentality refer to a single meaning, and any distinction is merely verbal or mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the key terms: existence, essence, substantiality, accidentality.
2
Identify the identity claim
The negators explicitly state that existence, identity, essence, substantiality, and accidentality are 'expressions for a single meaning'. Note that they deny any real distinction.
This is the core of their position.
3
Understand the implications for power and genera
Notice that they say power attaches to essence as it attaches to existence, and that genera and species are merely abstract words or mental considerations. Reflect on why this is important for their overall theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجابت النفاة بأن قالوا: وجود الشيء وعينه وذاته وجوهريته وعرضيته عندنا عبارات عن معبر واحد، وما أوجده الموجد فهو ذات الشيء، والقدرة تعلقت بذاته كما تعلقت بوجوده، وأثرت في جوهريته كما أثرت في حصوله وحدوثه، والتميز بين الوجود وبين الشيئية مما لا يثول إلى معنى ومعنى، بل إلى لفظ ولفظ، وهم على اعتقاد أن الأجناس والأنواع والعموم والخصوص فيها راجع إلى الألفاظ المجردة أو الوجوه العقلية والتقديرات الوهمية، وألزموا عليهم الصفات التابعة للحدوث كالتحيز وقبول العرض وقيام العرض بالمحل فإنها ليست من آثار القدرة ثم لم يثبتوها قبل الحدوث، فهلا قالوا الصفات الذاتية كلها تتبع الحدوث أيضا، وربما عكسوا عليهم الأمر في التابع والمتبوع وألزموهم القول بأن التحيز يقع بالقدرة والوجود يتبعه.
And the negators answered by saying: Existence of a thing, its identity, its essence, its substantiality, and its accidentality are for us expressions for a single meaning; and what the bringer into existence brings into existence is the essence of the thing; power attaches to its essence as it attaches to its existence, and it effects its substantiality as it effects its attainment and its origination; and the distinction between existence and thingness does not return to meaning and meaning, but to word and word; and they hold the belief that genera, species, universality, and particularity therein refer to abstract words or rational aspects and estimative estimations. And they imposed upon them the attributes that follow contingency, such as being in a place, accepting accidents, and subsistence of accidents in a substratum, for these are not effects of power, then they did not affirm them before contingency. So why did they not say that all essential attributes follow contingency as well? And perhaps they reversed the matter regarding the follower and the followed and obliged them to say that being in a place occurs by power and existence follows it.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the reply of the negators (al-nufāt) who deny that existence is an additional attribute to essence. They argue that existence, identity, essence, substantiality, and accidentality are all expressions for a single meaning. When the agent brings something into existence, it is the essence that is brought; power (qudra) attaches to the essence just as it attaches to existence, and it affects substantiality just as it affects obtaining and origination. Any distinction between existence and thingness (al-shay'iyya) is merely verbal, not real. Genera, species, universality, and particularity are merely abstract words or mental considerations. Then the author notes an objection they raise against their opponents: attributes that follow contingency (al-ḥudūth) — such as being located in a place (al-taḥayyuz), accepting accidents (qubūl al-ʿaraḍ), and accidents subsisting in a subject — are not effects of power, yet they are not affirmed before contingency. So why not say that all essential attributes follow contingency as well? The negators also reverse the relationship between follower and followed, insisting that being in a place occurs by power and existence follows it.

Key Terms
وجود
ذات
جوهر
عرض
شيئية
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the negators claim existence and essence are distinct but related, or that existence is an attribute of essence.
Correction: They explicitly say they are identical in meaning; any distinction is merely verbal. Existence is not an additional attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the negators, what is the relationship between existence and essence?
A) Existence is an additional attribute to essence.
B) Existence and essence are identical in meaning.
C) Essence is prior to existence.
D) Existence is an accident of essence.

Key Takeaway
The negators hold that existence and essence are not two distinct realities but are one and the same, expressed by different terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence and your existence are inseparable, then your entire being is present before God at every moment. Are you living as if every part of you—your thoughts, your actions, your heart—is exposed to Him? What would you change if you truly believed there is no hidden layer of yourself that escapes His sight?
?
The negators argue that distinctions between essence and existence are merely words. How many of your religious practices have become mere words—labels you apply to yourself without real transformation? Will you seek a faith that penetrates beyond verbal affirmations into the very core of your existence?
?
If genera and species are mental constructs, then your identity as 'Muslim' or 'believer' is not a fixed category but a reality you live out. Does your daily life reflect that your faith is not a label but the very substance of who you are? What would it mean to let go of comforting categories and simply be a servant before God?

5 min
The Negators' Objection: Attributes Follow Contingency
The negators argue that contingent attributes imply all essential attributes are contingent and reverse the causal order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the negators' argument that if some attributes are contingent, then all essential attributes should be contingent, and their reversal of the causal relationship between power and existence.

Core Idea
If some attributes follow contingency, then all essential attributes should do the same.

After asserting the identity of existence and essence, the negators launch a counter-objection against their opponents. They argue that attributes like being in a place and accepting accidents are not directly caused by power but follow contingency. If the opponents affirm these only after contingency, then they should also affirm that all essential attributes follow contingency. The negators further reverse the causal order, claiming that power causes location, and existence simply follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the attributes listed: being in a place (al-taḥayyuz), accepting accidents (qubūl al-ʿaraḍ), and subsistence of accidents in a substratum (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bil-maḥall).
2
Understand the logical challenge
The negators point out that these attributes are not effects of power but are concomitant with contingency. Their opponents do not affirm them before contingency. Then they ask: why not say all essential attributes follow contingency?
This is a consistency argument.
3
Examine the reversal
In the last part, the negators reverse the follower/followed relationship: they propose that being in a place is caused by power, and existence follows it. This is a key move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجابت النفاة بأن قالوا: وجود الشيء وعينه وذاته وجوهريته وعرضيته عندنا عبارات عن معبر واحد، وما أوجده الموجد فهو ذات الشيء، والقدرة تعلقت بذاته كما تعلقت بوجوده، وأثرت في جوهريته كما أثرت في حصوله وحدوثه، والتميز بين الوجود وبين الشيئية مما لا يثول إلى معنى ومعنى، بل إلى لفظ ولفظ، وهم على اعتقاد أن الأجناس والأنواع والعموم والخصوص فيها راجع إلى الألفاظ المجردة أو الوجوه العقلية والتقديرات الوهمية، وألزموا عليهم الصفات التابعة للحدوث كالتحيز وقبول العرض وقيام العرض بالمحل فإنها ليست من آثار القدرة ثم لم يثبتوها قبل الحدوث، فهلا قالوا الصفات الذاتية كلها تتبع الحدوث أيضا، وربما عكسوا عليهم الأمر في التابع والمتبوع وألزموهم القول بأن التحيز يقع بالقدرة والوجود يتبعه.
And the negators answered by saying: Existence of a thing, its identity, its essence, its substantiality, and its accidentality are for us expressions for a single meaning; and what the bringer into existence brings into existence is the essence of the thing; power attaches to its essence as it attaches to its existence, and it effects its substantiality as it effects its attainment and its origination; and the distinction between existence and thingness does not return to meaning and meaning, but to word and word; and they hold the belief that genera, species, universality, and particularity therein refer to abstract words or rational aspects and estimative estimations. And they imposed upon them the attributes that follow contingency, such as being in a place, accepting accidents, and subsistence of accidents in a substratum, for these are not effects of power, then they did not affirm them before contingency. So why did they not say that all essential attributes follow contingency as well? And perhaps they reversed the matter regarding the follower and the followed and obliged them to say that being in a place occurs by power and existence follows it.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the reply of the negators (al-nufāt) who deny that existence is an additional attribute to essence. They argue that existence, identity, essence, substantiality, and accidentality are all expressions for a single meaning. When the agent brings something into existence, it is the essence that is brought; power (qudra) attaches to the essence just as it attaches to existence, and it affects substantiality just as it affects obtaining and origination. Any distinction between existence and thingness (al-shay'iyya) is merely verbal, not real. Genera, species, universality, and particularity are merely abstract words or mental considerations. Then the author notes an objection they raise against their opponents: attributes that follow contingency (al-ḥudūth) — such as being located in a place (al-taḥayyuz), accepting accidents (qubūl al-ʿaraḍ), and accidents subsisting in a subject — are not effects of power, yet they are not affirmed before contingency. So why not say that all essential attributes follow contingency as well? The negators also reverse the relationship between follower and followed, insisting that being in a place occurs by power and existence follows it.

Key Terms
حدوث
تحيز
قبول العرض
قيام العرض بالمحل
قدرة
وجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the negators deny that power causes existence at all, or that they attribute existence purely to contingency.
Correction: They do not deny power's role; they simply reverse the order, making power directly cause location, and existence a consequence.

Checkpoint
According to the negators, which attributes are not effects of power?
A) Existence and essence.
B) Being in a place, accepting accidents, and subsistence of accidents.
C) Power and will.
D) All essential attributes.

Key Takeaway
The negators challenge their opponents to be consistent: if some attributes are contingent, then all essential attributes should be contingent, and they reverse the causal chain, making power directly cause location with existence as a consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there beliefs or practices where you accept inconsistency to avoid an uncomfortable conclusion? How would the negators' challenge to their opponents apply to you—are you willing to follow your principles to their logical end, even if it means changing something fundamental?
?
If you were to reverse the causal order of your priorities—putting God's power as the primary agent in every outcome and seeing your own existence as a mere consequence—how would that change the way you approach daily decisions and trials?
?
The negators force their opponents to confront the implications of their own premises. Where have you avoided thinking through the full implications of a theological conviction because it might demand a change in how you live or worship?

Divine Attributes

4 min
Dependence of Essence and Attributes on the Creator
Shahrastani argues that essence and attributes depend on the Creator as much as existence does.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that essential attributes such as essence and identity are not independent of the Creator but require Him just as existence does.

Core Idea
Essential attributes are not independent of the Creator.

Some theologians claimed that only existence is attributed to the Creator, while essential attributes are not. In this passage, Shahrastani refutes this claim by showing that the essence, identity, and attributes of a possible being all depend on the Creator in the same way existence does. The passage presents a logical argument that ties the possibility of existence to the possibility of being an essence or attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument. Note that the passage has two parts: a main refutation and a brief second argument that is cut off.
Focus on the first argument as it is complete.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the statement 'They mentioned that the essential attributes are not attributed to the agent, rather what is attributed is existence.' This is the claim Shahrastani argues against.
3
Follow the refutation logic
Shahrastani distinguishes between attributes of an unspecified thing and attributes of a specified thing, concluding that essence, identity, and essentiality are expressions of a single meaning. Just as existence needs a Creator, so do these.
Notice how he ties possibility of existence (jawāz al-wujūd) to possibility of establishment (jawāz al-thubūt).
4
Reflect on the implication
If only existence depended on the Creator, then all things would be independent of Him except existence—a logical absurdity. This confirms that all aspects of a possible being depend on the Creator.
This argument is foundational for understanding divine omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكروه أن الصفات الذاتية لا تنسب إلى الفاعل، بل الذي ينسب إلى الفاعل هو الوجود، قيل : ما ثبت للشيء الغير المعين من الصفات التي هو بها قد تعين غير، وما ثبت للشيء المعين من الصفات التي هو بها وقد تفنن وتنوع صفات ذاتية إلا بمعنى أنها عبارات عن ذاته المعينة، فيكون غير، والأول لا يسمى وجوده وجوهريته وعينه وذاته عبارات عن معبر واحد، وكما يحتاج في وجوده إلى الموجد يحتاج في ذاته وعينه وجوهريته، وجواز الوجود هو بعينه جواز الثبوت، وهو بعينه في أن يكون عينًا وبجوهريته في أن يكون جوهراً لا يستغني عن الموجد، وإلا فيلزم أن تستغني الأشياء كلها عن الموجد من جميع وجوهها وصفاتها إلا الوجود فحسب على أنه حال لا يوصف بالوجود.

وأيضا فإن الوجود ليس يفتقر إلى الموجد، وإلا فيلزم وجود القديم بل وجود مخصص هو بصفة الإمكان يفتقر إلى الموجد، ثم الموجد الممكن من حيث
And what they mentioned that the essential attributes are not attributed to the agent, rather what is attributed to the agent is existence, it was said: What is established for an unspecified thing of the attributes by which it is unspecified, becomes other; and what is established for a specified thing of the attributes by which it has varied and diversified are essential attributes only in the sense that they are expressions of its specific essence, so it becomes other. And the former is not called its existence, and its essentiality, and its identity, and its essence are expressions of a single meaning. And just as it needs in its existence the Creator, it needs in its essence and identity and essentiality. And the possibility of existence is identical with the possibility of establishment, and it is identical in that it is an identity and in its essentiality in that it is a substance; it is not independent of the Creator. Otherwise, it would imply that all things are independent of the Creator from all their aspects and attributes except existence alone, as being a state not described with existence.

And also, existence does not need a Creator, otherwise the existence of the eternal would be necessary. Rather, a specified existence which has the attribute of possibility needs a Creator, then the possible creator from where
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage contains two arguments. First, the author refutes the claim that only existence is attributed to the Creator, while essential attributes are not. He argues that the attributes of an unspecified thing make it 'other' (i.e., different from the Creator), and the attributes of a specified thing are expressions of its essence. The essence, identity, and essentiality are all expressions of a single meaning, and just as existence needs a Creator, so do essence and attributes. The possibility of existence is identical to the possibility of being an essence, so everything about a possible being depends on the Creator. If not, all things would be independent of the Creator except existence, which is absurd. Second, the author adds that existence itself does not need a Creator (otherwise the eternal would be necessary), but rather a specific existence with the attribute of possibility needs a Creator. This second argument is cut off at 'then the possible creator from where'.

Key Terms

الموجد
جواز الوجود
الإمكان
جواز الثبوت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only existence depends on the Creator; essential attributes do not.
Correction: Essential attributes (e.g., essence, identity, essentiality) are expressions of the same reality as existence, so they also depend on the Creator.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is identical to the possibility of existence?
A) The essence
B) Necessity
C) The possibility of establishment
D) The attribute of existence

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes are not independent of the Creator; they require the Creator just as existence does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your very essence and identity are independent of Allah, as if only your existence needs Him? In which areas of your life do you act as though your personality, talents, or character are self-sustaining?
?
If every aspect of who you are—your essence, your attributes—depends moment by moment on the Creator, what changes in your worship or reliance? How would your daily supplications and trust shift if you truly believed that?
?
The argument shows that even the possibility of being an essence requires the Creator. Does your heart truly feel that you have no independent ground of existence? What would it mean to internalize that and let it transform your relationship with Allah starting today?

Divine Attributes
advanced
5 min
Essential Attributes Depend on the Agent as Much as Existence
Refutation of the claim that essential attributes are not attributed to the agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that only existence is attributed to the agent is false, and that a thing's essence and attributes are equally dependent on the cause.

Core Idea
Your existence and your essence: both need the Creator equally.

Some theologians argued that only existence is a gift from God; essential attributes like substantiality are not. This passage refutes that by showing that a thing's essence, identity, and attributes are inseparable from its existence. Just as a thing needs a cause for its existence, it needs a cause for what it is.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Read the opening line which states the claim: 'essential attributes are not attributed to the agent, but rather what is attributed is existence'.
The claim separates existence from essence, attributing only existence to God.
2
Understand the two cases of attributes
The response distinguishes between attributes of a non-particular thing and attributes of a particular thing. For a non-particular thing, attributes specify it as 'other'. For a particular thing, essential attributes are merely expressions of its particular essence.
This shows that attributes are not independent of the essence.
3
Grasp the parallel need for the cause
The passage argues that just as a thing needs the cause for its existence, it needs Him for its essence, identity, and substantiality. The possibility of existence is identical to the possibility of establishment.
There is no distinction in dependence between existence and essence.
4
Consider the consequence if the claim were true
If the claim were true, all things would be independent of the cause in all aspects except existence, which is absurd because existence is not a state that exists without being described by existence.
This reductio ad absurdum shows the fallacy of the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكروه أن الصفات الذاتية لا تنسب إلى الفاعل، بل الذي ينسب إلى الفاعل هو الوجود، قيل: ما ثبت للشيء غير المعين من الصفات التي هو بها قد تعين غير، وما ثبت للشيء المعين من الصفات التي هو بها وقد تفنن وتنوع صفات ذاتية إلا بمعنى أنها عبارات عن ذاته المعينة، فيكون غير، والأول لا يسمى وجوده وجوهريته وعينه وذاته عبارات عن معبر واحد، وكما يحتاج في وجوده إلى الموجد يحتاج في ذاته وعينه وجوهريته، وجواز الوجود هو بعينه جواز الثبوت، وهو بعينه في أن يكون عينًا وبجوهريته في أن يكون جوهراً لا يستغني عن الموجد، وإلا فيلزم أن تستغني الأشياء كلها عن الموجد من جميع وجوهها وصفاتها إلا الوجود فحسب على أنه حال لا يوصف بالوجود.
And what they mentioned that essential attributes are not attributed to the agent, but rather what is attributed to the agent is existence, it was said: What is established for the non-particular thing of attributes by which it has become specified as other, and what is established for the particular thing of attributes by which it has varied and diversified as essential attributes only in the sense that they are expressions of its particular essence, then it becomes other, and the first is not called – its existence, its substantiality, its identity, and its essence are expressions of one signified; and just as it needs the cause (al-mūjid) for its existence, it needs Him for its essence, its identity, and its substantiality; and the possibility of existence is exactly the possibility of establishment, and it is exactly that it be an entity and by its substantiality that it be a substance does not dispense with the cause; otherwise it would follow that all things dispense with the cause in all their aspects and attributes except existence alone, as if it were a state not described by existence.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that essential attributes (such as substantiality) are not attributed to the agent (al-fā‘il), and that only existence is. The response argues that attributes of a non-particular thing (like absolute substantiality) specify it as 'other' than particular things. For a particular thing, its essential attributes are merely expressions of its particular essence, which is distinct. The existence, substantiality, identity, and essence of a thing are all expressions of one reality. Just as a thing needs the cause for its existence, it needs Him for its essence, identity, and substantiality. The possibility of existence is identical to the possibility of establishment (thubūt). A thing, in being an entity and in being a substance, does not dispense with the cause. Otherwise, everything would be independent of the cause in all aspects except existence, which is absurd because existence is not a state that exists without being described by existence.

Key Terms
صفات ذاتية (essential attributes)
فاعل (agent)
وجود (existence)
موجد (cause/creator)
جوهرية (substantiality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only existence is attributed to the agent; essential attributes like substantiality are not.
Correction: The passage shows that essence, identity, and substantiality are as dependent on the agent as existence, because they are all expressions of the same reality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between a thing's need for the cause regarding its existence and regarding its essence?
A) It needs the cause for existence but not for essence.
B) It needs the cause for essence but not for existence.
C) It needs the cause for both equally.
D) It needs the cause for neither.

Key Takeaway
A thing does not need God only for its existence, but for its very essence and all its attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you treat your personality, talents, and character as if they are self-made, independent of God's creation? How does this passage challenge you to acknowledge that your very essence is a gift from Him, not just your existence?
?
You might believe God gave you life, but have you truly surrendered the core of who you are—your identity, your 'substantiality'—to Him? What would it look like to live today knowing that even your deepest attributes are sustained by Him every moment?
?
If you were to internalize that your essence depends on God just as much as your existence, what would change in how you view your worth, your failures, and your purpose?

The Contradiction in the Theory of States
Critique of the Ash'ari theory of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari theory of states (ahwal) leads to a logical contradiction regarding the knowledge of types.

Core Idea
Types cannot be both known in non-existence and unknowable in existence.

The author begins with astonishment at those who affirm states, claiming that types like substantiality are known in non-existence but become unknowable states in existence. This passage exposes the inherent contradiction in such a view.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'والعجب كل العجب' to 'غير معلوم في الوجود' and its translation. Notice the author's amazement at the affirmers of states.
The author uses rhetorical questions to highlight the absurdity.
2
Identify the two claims
The affirmers claim types are 'things established in non-existence' (because known) and also 'states in existence not known in themselves'. Identify both claims in the passage.
3
Notice the contradiction
The author points out: a type is known in non-existence but not known in existence. Ask why this is contradictory.
This challenges the coherence of the theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب كل العجب من مثبتي الأحوال أنهم جعلوا الأنواع مثل الجوهرية والجسمية والعرضية واللونية أشياء ثابتة في العدم؛ لأن العلم قد تعلق بها، والمعلوم يجب أن يكون شيئاً حتى يتوكأ عليه العلم، ثم هي بأعيانها - أعني: الجوهرية والعرضية واللونية والسوادية أحوال في الوجود ليست معلومة على حيالها، ولا موجودة بانفرادها، فيا له من معلوم في العدم يتوكأ عليه العلم وغير معلوم في الوجود ولو أنهم اهتدوا إلى مناهج العقول في تصورها الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها لعلموا أن تصورات العقول ماهيات الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها لا تستدعي كونها موجودة محققة، أو كونها أشياء ثابتة خارجة عن العقول، أو ما لها بحسب ذواتها وأجناسها وأنواعها في الذهن من المقومات الذاتية التي تتحقق ذواتها بها لا تتوقف على فعل الفاعل حتى يمكن أن تعرف هي والوجود لا يخطر بالبال، فإن أسباب الوجود غير وأسباب الماهية غير، ولعلموا أن إدراكات الحواس ذوات الأشياء بأعيانها وأعلامها تستدعي كونها موجودة محققة، وأشياء ثابتة خارجة عن الحواس، وما لها بحسب ذواتها من كونها أعياناً وأعلاما في الحس من المخصصات العرضية التي تحقق ذواتها المعينة بها هي التي تتوقف على فعل الفاعل حتى لا يمكن أن توجد هي عرية من تلك المخصصات فإن أسباب الماهية غير وأسباب الوجود غير. ولما سمعت المعتزلة من الفلاسفة فرقا بين القسمين ظنوا أن المتصورات في الأذهان هي أشياء ثابتة في الأعيان، فقضوا بأن المعدوم شيء، وظنوا بأن وجود الأجناس والأنواع في الأذهان هي أحوال ثابتة في الأعيان، فقضوا بأن المعدوم شيء وأن الحال ثابت ساء سمعا وساء إجابة ولولا أني التزمت على نفسي في هذا الكتاب أن أبين مصادر المذاهب ومواردها واشتراك منتهى إقدام العقول في مسائل الأصول، وإلا لما أهمني كشف الأسرار وهتك الأستار.
And the utmost wonder is from the affirmers of states (ahwal) that they made the types (anwa'), such as substantiality (jawhariyya), corporeality (jismiyya), accidentalness (aradiyya), and color-ness (lawniyya), as things established in non-existence ('adam) because knowledge has attached to them, and the known (ma'lum) must be something (shay') so that knowledge can lean upon it. Then these very same things—I mean substantiality, accidentalness, color-ness, and blackness—are states (ahwal) in existence (wujud), not known in themselves (ala hiyalaha), nor existing in isolation (bi-infiradiha). So what a known thing it is in non-existence upon which knowledge leans, and yet not known in existence! If they had been guided to the methods of intellects ('uqul) in conceiving things (ashya') by their genera (ajnas) and species (anwa'), they would know that the conceptions of intellects—the quiddities (mahiyyat) of things by their genera and species—do not require that they be actually existing (mujaqqiqa), nor that they be things established outside the intellects, nor that what they have in terms of their essences (dhawat), genera, and species in the mind (dhihn) of essential constituents (muqawwimat dhatiyya) by which their essences are realized do not depend on the action of an agent (fi'l al-fa'il) such that they can be known without existence being considered; for the causes of existence (asbab al-wujud) are other than the causes of quiddity (asbab al-mahiyya). And they would know that the perceptions of the senses (idrakat al-hawass) of the essences of things in their very selves (a'yaniha) and their marks (a'lamiha) require that they be actually existing, things established outside the senses, and what they have in terms of their essences of being entities (a'yan) and marks (a'lam) in sensation (hiss) of accidental particularizers (mukhasisat 'aradiyya) that realize their particular essences are those which depend on the action of an agent such that they cannot exist stripped of those particularizers. For the causes of quiddity are other than the causes of existence. And when the Mu'tazila heard from the philosophers a distinction between the two divisions, they thought that the mental conceptions (mutasawwirat fi al-adhhan) are things established in external entities (a'yan), so they judged that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing (shay'), and they thought that the existence of genera and species in minds are states (ahwal) established in external entities, so they judged that the non-existent is a thing and that the state (hal) is established. Evil hearing and evil response! And were it not that I have bound myself in this book to clarify the sources (masadir) of doctrines and their origins (mawarid) and the common limit (mushtarak muntaha) of the endeavors of intellects in matters of principles (usul), otherwise I would not have concerned myself with unveiling secrets and tearing away veils.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of the Ash'ari theory of states (ahwal) and its extension by the Mu'tazila. The author begins by expressing astonishment at the affirmers of states, who claim that types like substantiality, corporeality, color-ness, etc., are things established in non-existence because knowledge attaches to them. They argue that the known must be something for knowledge to lean on. But then, in existence, these same types are states that are not known independently or existing in isolation. The author points out the absurdity: something that is known in non-existence but not known in existence. He then suggests that if they had followed the intellect's method of conceiving things by genera and species, they would realize that intellectual conceptions of quiddities do not require actual external existence, nor do they need to be things outside the mind. The essential constituents in the mind do not depend on an agent's action such that they can be known without considering existence; the causes of existence and causes of quiddity are distinct. Furthermore, sensory perceptions of things' essences as individuals require actual existence and accidental particularizers that depend on an agent. Thus, quiddity and existence have different causes. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila for misunderstanding the philosophers' distinction between these two divisions (mental and external). The Mu'tazila mistakenly thought that mental conceptions are things established in external entities, leading them to claim that the non-existent is a thing. They also thought that the existence of genera and species in the mind are states in external entities, again concluding that the non-existent is a thing and that the state is established. The author dismisses this with 'evil hearing and evil response.' Finally, he explains his methodological commitment in this book: to clarify the sources and origins of doctrines and the common limit of intellectual endeavors in matters of principles, otherwise he would not have bothered to unveil these secrets.

Key Terms
الأحوال
المعدوم
المعلوم
الوجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Types (like substantiality) exist as real 'things' in non-existence and also as knowable states in existence.
Correction: The author shows this leads to a contradiction: a type cannot be a known entity in non-existence and an unknowable state in existence simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction the author points out in the affirmers of states' position?
A) They claim types are known in existence but not in non-existence.
B) They claim types are known in non-existence but not known in existence.
C) They deny that types are known at all.
D) They say types exist only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari theory of states is self-contradictory because it claims types are knowable in non-existence but unknowable in existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author expresses astonishment at those who accept a contradiction in their understanding of Allah's attributes. Are there areas in your own faith where you have kept a belief that, when logically pressed, seems contradictory? Will you seek a coherent understanding or remain comfortable with inconsistency?
?
The Ash'aris wanted to safeguard divine simplicity but ended up with a logical flaw. When you think about Allah's attributes, do you ever settle for vague explanations just to avoid mental discomfort? What would it cost you to pursue a more rigorous understanding?
?
If types are 'known' in non-existence but 'unknown' in existence, then your knowledge of real categories is unstable. Do you ever treat concepts like justice or mercy as real only when it suits you, and then downplay them when they demand action? Commit today to treat Allah's attributes as real and consistent in all circumstances.

Quiddity and Existence: Two Distinct Orders
Explains the difference between quiddity and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that quiddities (essences) in the mind do not require external existence, and why the Mu'tazila were mistaken in equating mental existence with external things.

Core Idea
Causes of quiddity are different from causes of existence.

The author contrasts intellectual conceptions (which do not need actual existence) with sensory perceptions (which do). He then explains that the Mu'tazila misunderstood the philosophers' distinction, leading them to claim that the non-existent is a thing. This passage clarifies the correct relationship between quiddity and existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'ولو أنهم اهتدوا' to 'ساء سمعا وساء إجابة'
Read both the Arabic and translation, focusing on the distinction between intellectual and sensory knowledge.
The author uses two parallel statements starting with 'لعلموا'.
2
Identify the two divisions
The author distinguishes between intellectual conceptions (based on genera/species) and sensory perceptions (based on individuals). Note that only the latter requires actual existence.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila error
The Mu'tazila thought mental conceptions are things in external reality, leading them to claim 'the non-existent is a thing'. See why this is mistaken.
4
Reflect on the causes
The passage ends with 'causes of quiddity are other than causes of existence'. Think about what this means.
This is a key philosophical principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب كل العجب من مثبتي الأحوال أنهم جعلوا الأنواع مثل الجوهرية والجسمية والعرضية واللونية أشياء ثابتة في العدم؛ لأن العلم قد تعلق بها، والمعلوم يجب أن يكون شيئاً حتى يتوكأ عليه العلم، ثم هي بأعيانها - أعني: الجوهرية والعرضية واللونية والسوادية أحوال في الوجود ليست معلومة على حيالها، ولا موجودة بانفرادها، فيا له من معلوم في العدم يتوكأ عليه العلم وغير معلوم في الوجود ولو أنهم اهتدوا إلى مناهج العقول في تصورها الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها لعلموا أن تصورات العقول ماهيات الأشياء بأجناسها وأنواعها لا تستدعي كونها موجودة محققة، أو كونها أشياء ثابتة خارجة عن العقول، أو ما لها بحسب ذواتها وأجناسها وأنواعها في الذهن من المقومات الذاتية التي تتحقق ذواتها بها لا تتوقف على فعل الفاعل حتى يمكن أن تعرف هي والوجود لا يخطر بالبال، فإن أسباب الوجود غير وأسباب الماهية غير، ولعلموا أن إدراكات الحواس ذوات الأشياء بأعيانها وأعلامها تستدعي كونها موجودة محققة، وأشياء ثابتة خارجة عن الحواس، وما لها بحسب ذواتها من كونها أعياناً وأعلاما في الحس من المخصصات العرضية التي تحقق ذواتها المعينة بها هي التي تتوقف على فعل الفاعل حتى لا يمكن أن توجد هي عرية من تلك المخصصات فإن أسباب الماهية غير وأسباب الوجود غير. ولما سمعت المعتزلة من الفلاسفة فرقا بين القسمين ظنوا أن المتصورات في الأذهان هي أشياء ثابتة في الأعيان، فقضوا بأن المعدوم شيء، وظنوا بأن وجود الأجناس والأنواع في الأذهان هي أحوال ثابتة في الأعيان، فقضوا بأن المعدوم شيء وأن الحال ثابت ساء سمعا وساء إجابة ولولا أني التزمت على نفسي في هذا الكتاب أن أبين مصادر المذاهب ومواردها واشتراك منتهى إقدام العقول في مسائل الأصول، وإلا لما أهمني كشف الأسرار وهتك الأستار.
And the utmost wonder is from the affirmers of states (ahwal) that they made the types (anwa'), such as substantiality (jawhariyya), corporeality (jismiyya), accidentalness (aradiyya), and color-ness (lawniyya), as things established in non-existence ('adam) because knowledge has attached to them, and the known (ma'lum) must be something (shay') so that knowledge can lean upon it. Then these very same things—I mean substantiality, accidentalness, color-ness, and blackness—are states (ahwal) in existence (wujud), not known in themselves (ala hiyalaha), nor existing in isolation (bi-infiradiha). So what a known thing it is in non-existence upon which knowledge leans, and yet not known in existence! If they had been guided to the methods of intellects ('uqul) in conceiving things (ashya') by their genera (ajnas) and species (anwa'), they would know that the conceptions of intellects—the quiddities (mahiyyat) of things by their genera and species—do not require that they be actually existing (mujaqqiqa), nor that they be things established outside the intellects, nor that what they have in terms of their essences (dhawat), genera, and species in the mind (dhihn) of essential constituents (muqawwimat dhatiyya) by which their essences are realized do not depend on the action of an agent (fi'l al-fa'il) such that they can be known without existence being considered; for the causes of existence (asbab al-wujud) are other than the causes of quiddity (asbab al-mahiyya). And they would know that the perceptions of the senses (idrakat al-hawass) of the essences of things in their very selves (a'yaniha) and their marks (a'lamiha) require that they be actually existing, things established outside the senses, and what they have in terms of their essences of being entities (a'yan) and marks (a'lam) in sensation (hiss) of accidental particularizers (mukhasisat 'aradiyya) that realize their particular essences are those which depend on the action of an agent such that they cannot exist stripped of those particularizers. For the causes of quiddity are other than the causes of existence. And when the Mu'tazila heard from the philosophers a distinction between the two divisions, they thought that the mental conceptions (mutasawwirat fi al-adhhan) are things established in external entities (a'yan), so they judged that the non-existent (ma'dum) is a thing (shay'), and they thought that the existence of genera and species in minds are states (ahwal) established in external entities, so they judged that the non-existent is a thing and that the state (hal) is established. Evil hearing and evil response! And were it not that I have bound myself in this book to clarify the sources (masadir) of doctrines and their origins (mawarid) and the common limit (mushtarak muntaha) of the endeavors of intellects in matters of principles (usul), otherwise I would not have concerned myself with unveiling secrets and tearing away veils.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of the Ash'ari theory of states (ahwal) and its extension by the Mu'tazila. The author begins by expressing astonishment at the affirmers of states, who claim that types like substantiality, corporeality, color-ness, etc., are things established in non-existence because knowledge attaches to them. They argue that the known must be something for knowledge to lean on. But then, in existence, these same types are states that are not known independently or existing in isolation. The author points out the absurdity: something that is known in non-existence but not known in existence. He then suggests that if they had followed the intellect's method of conceiving things by genera and species, they would realize that intellectual conceptions of quiddities do not require actual external existence, nor do they need to be things outside the mind. The essential constituents in the mind do not depend on an agent's action such that they can be known without considering existence; the causes of existence and causes of quiddity are distinct. Furthermore, sensory perceptions of things' essences as individuals require actual existence and accidental particularizers that depend on an agent. Thus, quiddity and existence have different causes. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila for misunderstanding the philosophers' distinction between these two divisions (mental and external). The Mu'tazila mistakenly thought that mental conceptions are things established in external entities, leading them to claim that the non-existent is a thing. They also thought that the existence of genera and species in the mind are states in external entities, again concluding that the non-existent is a thing and that the state is established. The author dismisses this with 'evil hearing and evil response.' Finally, he explains his methodological commitment in this book: to clarify the sources and origins of doctrines and the common limit of intellectual endeavors in matters of principles, otherwise he would not have bothered to unveil these secrets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Proving God's Attributes via Sibr
Shahrastani presents the rational argument for establishing God's power, knowledge, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational examination (sibr) of observable acts leads to establishing that God is powerful, knowing, and willing.

Core Idea
God’s attributes are deduced by examining what makes actions possible, perfect, and specified.

This passage presents the rational method of sibr, which means exhaustive analysis. By examining the conditions for action in living beings, we can infer the necessary attributes of the Creator. Shahrastani walks through three examples: power, knowledge, and will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the method of sibr
Sibr is an inductive examination where we list possible attributes of a living being to identify the one that enables action. This method is then applied analogically to God.
The argument assumes that the cause's ruling is constant between witnessed and absent.
2
Follow the proof for God's power
The passage argues that contingent acts need a preponderator, and that preponderator must be powerful. Then sibr shows that power is the only attribute that makes action possible.
3
Follow the proofs for knowledge and will
Similarly, perfect actions require knowledge, and specification of possibilities requires will. Each uses sibr to eliminate other attributes.
Note how each proof mirrors the structure.
4
Reflect on the analogical argument
The argument moves from observed (witnessed) to unobserved (absent) based on the uniformity of causal relations.
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب النظر في إثبات كونه قادرًا أولاً: إن الدليل قد قام على أن الصنع الجائز ثبوته والجائز عدمه إذا وجد احتاج إلى صانع يرجح جانب الوجود، فيجب أن يكون الصانع قادرًا؛ لأن من الأحياء من يتعذر عليه الفعل، ومنهم من يتيسر فسبرنا جملة صفات الحي روما للعثور على المعنى الذي لأجله ارتفع التعذر وتحقق التأتي والتيسر، فلم نجد صفة إلا القدرة أو كونه قادرًا، فكان الذي صحح الفعل من الحي كونه قادرًا هو علة لصحة الفعل والعلة لا تختلف حكمها شاهدا وغايبًا، وكذلك صادفنا أحكامًا وإتقانًا في الأفعال وسبرنا ما لأجله يصح الأحكام والإتقان من الفاعل، فلم نجد إلا كونه عالمًا. وكذلك رأينا الاختصاص ببعض الجائزات دون البعض مع تساوي الكل في الجواز وسبرنا ما لأجله يصح الاختصاص فلم نجد إلا كونه مريدا.
The followers of reasoning concerning establishing that He is powerful first said: The proof has been established that the act whose existence is possible and whose non-existence is possible, when it exists, requires a maker who gives preponderance to the side of existence. So the maker must be powerful, because among living beings some are incapable of action and some are capable. So we examined the totality of attributes of the living being seeking to find the meaning on account of which incapability is removed and capability is actualized, and we found no attribute except power or being powerful. So that which validates action from the living being is being powerful, and that is the cause for the validity of action, and the cause's ruling does not differ in the witnessed and the absent. Likewise, we encountered good design and perfection in actions, and we examined that on account of which good design and perfection are valid from the agent, and we found nothing except being knowing. And likewise, we saw specification of some possibilities over others despite equality in possibility, and we examined that on account of which specification is valid, and we found nothing except being a willer.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the detailed rational argument for establishing three essential divine attributes: power, knowledge, and will. The argument proceeds inductively using a method called sibr (examination). First, for power: any contingent act requires a maker who preponderates its existence over non-existence. This maker must be powerful because, when we examine living beings, we find that the attribute enabling action is power. By eliminating other attributes, we conclude that power is the cause of action's validity, and this cause applies analogously to God (the absent) as it does to observed beings (the witnessed). Second, for knowledge: when we observe perfect and well-designed acts, we ask what attribute makes such acts possible. Examination reveals that it is knowledge ('ilm). Third, for will: we observe that some possibilities are specified for actualization over others, even though all are equally possible. The attribute that accounts for such specification is will (irada). Thus, through inductive reasoning, we establish that God is powerful, knowing, and willing.

Key Terms
قادر
عالم
مريد
سبر
علة
صنع
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that sibr only works for observable beings and cannot be applied to God.
Correction: The argument explicitly states that the cause's ruling does not differ between the witnessed (shahid) and the absent (gha'ib), so the conclusion holds for God.

Checkpoint
How is God's knowledge established in this argument?
A) By examining the need for a maker
B) By examining the perfection and good design in actions
C) By examining specification of possibilities
D) By intuitive necessity

Key Takeaway
Through sibr, we deduce that God must be powerful, knowing, and willing from the nature of contingent acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is all-powerful, all-knowing, and all-willing, and you know this to be rational truth, how does that shift your trust in Him when you face uncertainty? What is one specific fear you will hand over to His power today?
?
The sibr argument shows that only a willer can specify among equally possible outcomes. Think of a decision you made today—was it truly yours, or did God's will determine it? How would your humility increase if you lived every moment aware that your choices are only possible because He wills?
?
If you truly believed that every perfect design in your life comes from God's knowledge, would you still take credit for your successes? What is one achievement you need to attribute solely to Him right now, and how will you do that today?

Proving God's Attributes of Power, Knowledge, Will, and Life
Al-Shahrastani proves four divine attributes through inductive elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars use inductive reasoning (sabr) from creation to establish four essential divine attributes: power, knowledge, will, and life.

Core Idea
God's attributes are known by induction from the order and specification of creation.

In this passage, al-Shahrastani presents a classic kalam argument that moves from the observable world to the invisible divine attributes. Rather than relying solely on scriptural proof, he uses a rational process of elimination (sabr) to identify the specific attributes necessary for a perfect Creator. This lesson unpacks each step of that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument. Notice how each attribute is introduced by a distinct observation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the method of sabr
Recognize that the author uses an inductive method called sabr: he surveys all possible attributes of a living agent to isolate the one that explains a particular effect. This is the core logical tool.
Sabr is compared to a process of elimination.
3
Trace each attribute argument
For each attribute (power, knowledge, will, life), follow the three-step pattern: (1) observation in the seen world, (2) induction by sabr to find the cause, (3) application to the unseen Creator.
The fourth attribute (life) is derived from the necessity of the other three.
4
Reflect on the argument from opposites
Note the concluding point: denying these attributes would force us to ascribe their opposites (incapacity, ignorance, death), which are imperfections incompatible with God's perfection. This strengthens the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أصحاب النظر في إثبات كونه قادرًا أولاً : إن الدليل قد قام على أن الصنع الجائز ثبوته والجائز عدمه إذا وجد احتاج إلى صانع يرجح جانب الوجود، فيجب أن يكون الصانع قادرًا؛ لأن من الأحياء من يتعذر عليه الفعل، ومنهم من يتيسر فسبرنا جملة صفات الحي روما للعثور على المعنى الذي لأجله ارتفع التعذر وتحقق التأتي والتيسر، فلم نجد صفة إلا القدرة أو كونه قادرًا، فكان الذي صحح الفعل من الحي كونه قادرًا هو علة لصحة الفعل والعلة لا تختلف حكمها شاهدا وغايبًا، وكذلك صادفنا أحكامًا وإتقانًا في الأفعال وسبرنا ما لأجله يصح الأحكام والإتقان من الفاعل، فلم نجد إلا كونه عالما. وكذلك رأينا الاختصاص ببعض الجايزات دون البعض مع تساوي الكل في الجواز وسبرنا ما لأجله يصح الاختصاص فلم نجد إلا كونه مريدا، ثم لم يتصور وجود هذه الصفات إلا وأن يكون الموصوف بها حيًّا؛ لأن الجماد لا يتصور منه أن يكون قادرًا أو عالما، فقلنا : القادر حي، وأيضا فإنا لو لم نصفه بهذه الصفات لزمنا وصفه بأضدادها من العجز والجهل والموت، وتلك نقائص مانعة الفعل المحكم، ويتعالى الصانع عن كل نقص.
The adherents of reflection said regarding proving His being capable first: Indeed the evidence has established that the artifact whose existence is possible and whose non-existence is possible, when it exists, needs a maker who preponderates the side of existence. So the maker must be capable; because among living beings there are those to whom action is impossible and those to whom it is easy. So we examined (sabarna) the totality of the attributes of the living being, seeking to find the meaning by which impossibility is removed and attainment and ease are realized, and we found no attribute except capability (al-qudra) or His being capable (kawnuhu qādiran). So that which made action correct from the living being—His being capable—is the cause for the correctness of action, and the cause does not differ in its ruling in the seen and the unseen. Similarly, we encountered order and perfection in actions, and we examined that by which order and perfection become correct from the agent, and we found nothing except His being knowing. And similarly we saw specification of some possibilities over others despite the equality of all in possibility, and we examined that by which specification becomes correct, and we found nothing except His being willing. Then it is not conceivable that these attributes exist unless the one described by them is living; because an inanimate object cannot be conceived to be capable or knowing. So we said: The capable is living. And also, if we did not describe Him with these attributes, we would be obliged to describe Him with their opposites—incapacity, ignorance, and death—and those are imperfections that prevent perfect action, and the Maker is exalted above every imperfection.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of four divine attributes: power (qudra), knowledge (ʿilm), will (irāda), and life (ḥayāt). The argument proceeds by induction (sabr wa-taqsīm) from observed realities in the created world. First, for power: contingent beings require a preponderator (murjīḥ) for their existence; that preponderator must be capable, because in living beings, ease of action is due to capability. We examine all attributes of living beings and find only capability as the cause of ease. This cause applies uniformly in the seen and unseen worlds. Second, for knowledge: the order and perfection observed in actions indicate the agent has knowledge; we induce that knowledge is the cause of such perfection. Third, for will: the specification of some possibilities over others, when all are equally possible, indicates a will that chooses. Finally, life: capable, knowing, and willing entail that the subject is living, since inanimate objects cannot possess these attributes. Additionally, the argument from opposites: denying these attributes would imply their opposites (incapacity, ignorance, death), which are imperfections incompatible with perfect agency. The author uses a standard kalam method of sabr (survey) to isolate the necessary attribute.

Key Terms
قادر
عالم
مريد
حي
سبر
علة
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attributes are proven independently and without reference to the seen world.
Correction: Each attribute is derived by induction (sabr) from observable phenomena in created beings, like ease of action, order, and specification.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do we establish that God is knowing (ʿālim)?
A) By direct revelation.
B) By induction from the order and perfection in actions.
C) By analogy with human knowledge.
D) By the necessity of will.

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes of power, knowledge, will, and life are rationally derived from the nature of contingent beings and the order in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order in your own life—your successes, your failures, the 'coincidences' that shaped you—do you honestly acknowledge that the One who specified those possibilities beyond your control is All-Knowing and All-Willing? Or do you secretly attribute them to luck or your own planning? This lesson demands that you submit every detail of your existence to His will. Where is the area of your life where you have not yet surrendered to His specification?
?
If God's power is proven by the fact that you exist at all, then why do you live as if your existence is self-made? Meditate on the fact that you are a contingent being—every breath you take is an act of His power. Does your daily schedule reflect that dependence, or do you operate as if God's power is on hold until you act?
?
The lesson ends by warning that denying these attributes implies their opposites—defects. Reflect: Is there any attribute of God that you have effectively denied in your heart by acting as if it does not apply to a particular area of your life? For example, have you ever doubted His knowledge of your secret thoughts? His will over your difficult circumstances? Repent now and affirm each attribute.
