The Parity Argument on Divine Acts and Attribute Reduction
Opponent's parity argument and author's reduction response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's parity argument that the act-essence relation parallels the act-will relation, and the author's response by reducing all attributes to the essence's necessary existence.

Core Idea
Reducing all attributes to the essence defuses the parity argument.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from rationalist opponents against the Ash'arī view of divine attributes. They claim that the same problem of specification that arises for will and power also arises for the essence. The author responds by showing how all attributes ultimately reduce to the essence's necessary existence, thereby sidestepping the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's parity argument
Read the first part of the passage where the opponent states that the relation of the act to the essence is like its relation to will and power in the Ash'arī view.
Notice the opponent's claim that will and power are single attributes that relate equally to all possibilities.
2
Identify the problem of specification
The opponent argues that if will and power do not specify one state of existence over another before creation, then the same applies to the essence. Ask yourself why this is a challenge.
This is a problem of why God created the world at a particular time rather than another.
3
Examine the reduction strategies
The author mentions that some theologians (al-Ka'bī, Abū al-Ḥusayn) reduce attributes further. Then the author states his own reduction of all attributes to the essence being necessary of existence.
The reduction is a response to the opponent's parity argument.
4
Reflect on the author's solution
Consider how reducing attributes to the essence avoids the specification problem. The essence itself is the source of all perfections, so no additional specification is needed.
This connects to the broader theme of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نازعوه في تماثل الأشكال وتشابه الأمثال، وذلك عناد وجدال. لكن الخصم يقول: نسبة الصنع عندنا إلى الذات كنسبته إلى الإرادة والقدرة عندكم، فإن الإرادة عندكم واحدة وهي في ذاتها صفة صالحة يتأتى بها التخصيص مطلقًا، ونسبتها إلى هذا المثل كنسبتها إلى مثل آخر، وكذلك القدرة، ولأنهما لا يتعلقان بالمرادات والمقدورات قبل الإيجاد، فما الموجب لتخصيص حال الوجود بالإيقاع ونسبة الإرادة والقدرة إلى هذه الحال كنسبتها إلى حال أخرى؟ فجوابكم عن تعلق الصفات بالمتعلقات إيجادًا هو جوابنا عن نسبة الذات إلى الموجبات إيجابًا، والسؤال مشكل، وقد أشرنا إلى حل الإشكال في حدث العالم. وربما يقول الخصم: ألستم رددتم معنى كونه حكيمًا إلى كونه مريدًا أو فاعلًا على مقتضى العلم؟ ورد الكعبي كونه مريدًا إلى كونه قادرًا عالمًا، ورد أبو الحسين البصري جملة الصفات إلى كونه قادرًا عالمًا على رأي، وإلى كونه قادرًا على رأي، وإلى كونه عالمًا على رأي، فنحن رددنا جملة الصفات إلى كونه ذاتًا واجبة الوجود على جلال وكمال تصدر منه.
And they [the author's school] have been disputed regarding the similarity of forms and the likeness of instances, and that is obstinacy and argumentation. But the opponent says: The relation of the act (ṣunʿ) to the essence, in our view, is like its relation to will and power in your view. For will, in your view, is one and in itself an attribute capable of specification absolutely, and its relation to this instance is like its relation to another instance, and similarly for power. And because they [will and power] do not relate to the objects of will and the objects of power before bringing into existence, what necessitates the specification of the state of existence by bringing into existence? The relation of will and power to this state is like their relation to another state. So your answer concerning the attachment of attributes to their objects in terms of bringing into existence is our answer concerning the relation of the essence to the necessitating things in terms of necessitation. And the question is problematic, and we have indicated the solution of the problem in [the discussion of] the temporality of the world. And perhaps the opponent says: Did you not reduce the meaning of His being wise (ḥakīm) to His being willing (murīd) or acting (fāʿil) in accordance with knowledge? And al-Kaʿbī reduced His being willing to His being powerful and knowing. And Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī reduced the totality of attributes to His being powerful and knowing in one view, and to His being powerful in another view, and to His being knowing in another view. So we have reduced the totality of attributes to His being an essence (dhāt) necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd), in majesty and perfection, from which [acts] proceed.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponent (presumably a philosopher or a rationalist) against the Ashʿarī position on divine attributes. The opponent argues that the relation of the act (ṣunʿ) to the essence (dhāt) is parallel to the relation of the act to will (irāda) and power (qudra) in your view. Since will and power are single attributes that relate equally to all possibilities before creation, there is no reason for them to specify one particular state of existence over another. The same problem arises for the essence. Thus, the opponent claims parity: the Ashʿarī's answer about how attributes relate to their objects is exactly the same as the opponent's answer about how the essence relates to its necessitations. The opponent then mentions that some theologians (al-Kaʿbī, Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī) reduce attributes further—al-Kaʿbī reduces will to power and knowledge; Abū al-Ḥusayn reduces all attributes to power and knowledge in one view, or to power alone, or to knowledge alone. The passage concludes with the author's own position: that he reduces all attributes to the essence being necessary of existence, in majesty and perfection, from which acts proceed. This response counters the reductionist tendencies by affirming that the essence itself is the source of all attributes without multiplicity. The passage is a meta-theological discussion about the structure of divine predication and the problem of specification.

Correction: The author does not deny attributes; he reduces them all to the essence, meaning the essence itself is the source of all attribute meanings. He states that the essence is 'necessary of existence, in majesty and perfection, from which acts proceed.'

Proof of Divine Attributes from Contingency
The author proves the necessity of attributes (will, power, knowledge, life) from the need for a preponderator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the contingency of the world proves that Allah must possess the attributes of will, power, knowledge, and life.

Core Idea
Contingency demands a preponderator with will, power, knowledge, and life.

This passage presents a rational proof for the necessity of divine attributes. It begins with the observation that the world is contingent, meaning it could have been otherwise. Such contingency requires a factor that tilts it from possibility to actuality. The author then systematically shows that this factor cannot be the divine essence alone but must involve attributes, specifically will, power, knowledge, and life.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for a preponderator
Recognize that contingent things need an external factor to specify their existence over non-existence. The world is contingent, so it needs a preponderator.
2
Eliminate the essence alone
Consider why the essence alone cannot be the preponderator. The argument shows that if the essence necessitated, it would necessitate all possibilities equally, which contradicts observed specificity.
This is the core of the proof against pure essentialism.
3
Identify the attribute of will
Understand that the specifying attribute is called 'will' in revelation. However, will only specifies, not creates, so another attribute is needed for actual creation.
Will is the attribute of specification, not execution.
4
Recognize knowledge and life
The perfection of creation implies knowledge; will requires knowledge in the willer. These attributes logically depend on life. Thus the maker must be living, knowing, powerful, and willing.
Life is the root attribute that enables the others.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows that the attributes are rationally necessary, not arbitrary. Contingency leads to a complete set of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم واحتياجه من حيث إمكانه إلى مرجح لأحد طرفي الإمكان والمرجح، إما أن يرجح بذاته ولذاته، أو بذاته على صفة، أو بصفة وراء الذات، واستحال الترجيح والتخصيص بالوجود بالذات من حيث هو ذات بأن الموجب بالذات لا يخصص عن مثل، فإن نسبة الذات إلى هذا المثل كنسبته إلى المثل الثاني، فيجب أن يكون بصفة وراء الذات أو بذات على صفة، وقد ورد التنزيل والشرع بتسمية تلك الصفة التي يتأتى بها التخصيص إرادة، ثم الإرادة تخصص ولا توقع، فالصفة التي يتأتى بها الإيقاع والإيجاد هي القدرة، والفعل إذا اشتمل على إتقان وإحكام وجب بالضرورة كون الصانع عالما، والإرادة أيضا لا تتعلق بشيء إلا بعد أن يكون المريد عالما، وهذه الصفات تستدعي في ثبوتها الحياة، فوجب أن يكون الصانع حيا، وقد نازعوه في تماثل الأشكال وتشابه الأمثال، وذلك عناد وجدال.
The world, by virtue of its contingency, needs a preponderator for one of its two possible states. The preponderator must either preponderate by itself and for itself, or by itself upon an attribute, or by an attribute beyond the essence. It is impossible for the preponderance and specification to be by existence through the essence as such, because that which necessitates by essence does not specify one of two similar things. For the relation of the essence to this similar is like its relation to the other similar. Therefore it must be by an attribute beyond the essence, or by itself upon an attribute. Revelation and law have named that attribute by which specification is attained 'will'. Then will specifies but does not bring into existence. The attribute by which bringing into existence and creation occur is power. When an action includes perfection and firmness, it is necessarily necessary that the maker be knowing. Also, will does not relate to anything unless the willer is knowing. These attributes require, in their existence, life. Therefore the maker must be living. They have disputed about the similarity of forms and resemblance of likenesses, and that is obstinacy and argument.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a proof for divine attributes from the contingency of the world. It begins by stating that the contingent world needs a preponderator (مرجح) to tilt it from non-existence to existence. This preponderator must act either by its essence alone, by its essence in conjunction with an attribute, or by an attribute distinct from the essence. The first option is impossible because if the essence necessitated existence, it would have to necessitate all similar possibilities equally, leading to all things existing simultaneously. Therefore, the preponderator must have an attribute beyond the essence. Revelation identifies this attribute as will (إرادة). However, will only specifies, not creates; so another attribute, power (قدرة), is needed for actual creation. Additionally, the perfection and order in the world indicate that the maker is knowing (عالم), and will cannot act without knowledge. Finally, these attributes require life (حياة), so the maker must be living. The author notes that opponents dispute this reasoning out of obstinacy.

إرادة
قدرة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divine essence alone is sufficient to create the world without any attributes.
Correction: The argument shows that if the essence alone were the cause, it would necessitate all possibilities equally, leading to all things existing simultaneously, which is not the case. Therefore, specification requires an attribute like will.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the preponderator be the mere essence?
A) Because the essence is simple and cannot have effects.
B) Because the essence would necessitate all possibilities equally, without specification.
C) Because the essence is unknowable.
D) Because the essence is not a cause.

Key Takeaway
Contingency of the world rationally necessitates that the Creator possesses will, power, knowledge, and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If will without knowledge is impossible for the Creator, how careful are you to seek knowledge before making decisions? In what area of your life are you acting on will without seeking God’s knowledge through His guidance?

Refuting Objections on Divine Attributes
The author rebuts objections about divine attributes and reductionism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author rebuts objections that divine attributes are redundant or lead to infinite regress, and how he grounds all attributes in the essence of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Attributes are not separate entities but perfections of the Essence itself.

The opponent raises two challenges: first, that attributing action to essence is no different from attributing it to will and power, leading to a regress; second, that various theological schools reduce attributes to a few, but the author's own reduction risks emptying them of meaning. The author responds by referring to earlier arguments on the world's temporality and by affirming that all attributes are rooted in the single, perfect essence of the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection on attributes
Read the first part where the opponent argues that the relation of will and power to their objects is parallel to the essence's relation to its effects.
Notice the opponent uses the author's own logic against him.
2
Identify author's response
The author points to his earlier solution in the discussion on the world's temporal origination. Think about how that might resolve the issue.
3
Analyze the reductionism objection
The opponent cites other theologians who reduce attributes. The author summarizes his own view: all attributes are reduced to the essence of the Necessary Existent, which is perfect and from which all things proceed in best order.
Consider how this is different from simply denying attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكن الخصم يقول: نسبة الصنع عندنا إلى الذات كنسبته إلى الإرادة والقدرة عندكم، فإن الإرادة عندكم واحدة وهي في ذاتها صفة صالحة يتأتى بها التخصيص مطلقا، ونسبتها إلى هذا المثل كنسبتها إلى مثل آخر، وكذلك القدرة، ولأنهما لا يتعلقان بالمرادات والمقدورات قبل الإيجاد، فما الموجب لتخصيص حال الوجود بالإيقاع ونسبة الإرادة والقدرة إلى هذه الحال كنسبتها إلى حال أخرى؟ فجوابكم عن تعلق الصفات بالمتعلقات إيجادا هو جوابنا عن نسبة الذات إلى الموجبات إيجابا والسؤال مشكل، وقد أشرنا إلى حل الإشكال في حدث العالم، وربما يقول الخصم: ألستم رددتم معنى كونه حكيما إلى كونه مريدا أو فاعلا على مقتضى العلم؟ ورد الكعبي كونه مريدا إلى كونه قادرا عالما، ورد أبو الحسين البصري جملة الصفات إلى كونه قادرا عالما على رأي، وإلى كونه قادرا على رأي، وإلى كونه عالما على رأي، فنحن رددنا جملة الصفات إلى كونه ذاتا واجبة الوجود على جلال وكمال تصدر منه الموجودات على أحسن نظام وأتقن إحكام.
But the opponent says: The attribution of making to the essence in our view is like its attribution to will and power in your view. For will in your view is one and in itself an attribute capable of specification absolutely, and its relation to this similar is like its relation to another similar, and similarly power. And because they do not relate to the willed and the empowered before creation, what necessitates the specification of the state of existence with bringing about? The relation of will and power to this state is like their relation to another state. So your answer about the relation of attributes to their objects in creating is our answer about the relation of the essence to that which necessitates by necessitation. The question is problematic. We have indicated the solution of the problem in (the discussion of) the temporal origination of the world. And perhaps the opponent says: Did you not reduce the meaning of His being Wise to His being Willer or doer according to knowledge? Al-Ka'bi reduced His being Willer to His being Powerful and Knowing. Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reduced all attributes to His being Powerful and Knowing according to one view, and to His being Powerful according to another view, and to His being Knowing according to another view. Then we reduced all attributes to His being an essence necessarily existent in majesty and perfection from which existences proceed in the best order and most perfect firmness.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response section. The opponent argues that if the essence needs attributes to specify, then those attributes themselves face the same problem: they are one and relate equally to all possibilities. The opponent claims that the author's answer to how attributes relate to their objects would apply equally to the essence's relation to effects. The author replies that the solution was already provided in the discussion on the temporal origination of the world (حدث العالم). The opponent further raises the issue of reductionism: other theologians (al-Ka'bi, Abu al-Husayn) reduced attributes to a few, but the author reduces all attributes to the essence of the Necessary Existent, which is perfect and from which all things proceed in best order, without multiplicity.

Correction: The author does not deny attributes; he sees them as perfections inherent in the essence, which is the ultimate source of all order and specification.

Divine Names: Relational and Negative
Classification of divine names into relational and negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two categories of divine names in Islamic theology: relational names derived from God's actions and negative names derived from His transcendence.

Core Idea
God's names are either relational (from His effects) or negative (from His transcendence).

The author begins by establishing how divine names are derived. He presents three possible derivations: from effects, from essence, or from transcendence. The first yields relational names (like 'Creator'), the second yields essential names (like 'Allah'), and the third yields negative names (like 'One'). However, he focuses on the first two categories for discussion, which form the basis of how we properly speak about God without compromising His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the three ways names are derived and which two categories the author highlights for discussion.
The author uses the terms 'أسماء إضافية' and 'أسماء سلبية'.
2
Identify examples of each category
Find the examples given: relational names include 'cause', 'principle', 'maker'; negative names include 'one', 'intellect', 'intelligent', 'necessary'.
Negative names do not mean God lacks positive attributes; they affirm transcendence.
3
Reflect on the purpose of this classification
Ask yourself: Why does the author categorize divine names? How does this help avoid misconceptions about God?
This classification prevents attributing human-like qualities to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يشتق له اسم من نحو آثاره لذاته، أو اسم لذاته، أو اسم من حيث تقدسه عن سمات مخلوقاته. الأول ويسمى أسماء إضافية؛ كالعلة والمبدأ والصانع، ويسمى الثاني أسماء سلبية؛ كالواحد والعقل والعاقل والواجب، وأشتهي أن تجري في هذه الأسامي مباحثة عقلية للإنصاف والانتصاف. فأقول: العلة والمبدأ عندكم يقال على كل ما استمر له وجود في ذاته ثم حصل منه وجود شيء آخر ويقوم به، وقد يكون الشيء علة للشيء بالذات وقد يكون بالعرض، وقد تكون علة قريبة، وقد تكون علة بعيدة، وواجب الوجود لذاته تام الوجود في ذاته وحصل منه العقل الأول أهو علة له بالذات أم بالعرض؟ فإن كان علة له بالذات لا بالعرض فهو مبدأ له بالقصد الأول لا بالقصد الثاني، ولم يكن واجب الوجود غنيا مطلقا، بل فقيرا محتاجا إلى كسب، ولم يكن كاملا مطلقا بذاته لولا معلوله بل كاملا بغيره ناقصا باعتبار ذاته، كيف وهم يأبون أن يكون المعلول الأول وسائر الموجودات إلا من لوازم تعقله بذاته، فلا يكون توجيه واجب الوجود الأزلي إلا إلى ذاته على سبيل الاستغناء المطلق عن الكل، ووجوه سائر الموجودات إليه على سبيل احتياج الكل إليه، وإن قالوا: هو علة بالعرض فباطل لأنه لا علة بالعرض إلا عن علة بالذات، وهو باطل.
Then there is derived for Him a name from the aspect of His effects for His essence, or a name for His essence, or a name from the aspect of His transcendence above the characteristics of His creatures. The first is called relational names, like cause, principle, maker; the second is called negative names, like one, intellect, intelligent, necessary. I desire that a rational discussion be conducted regarding these names for fairness and vindication. I say: Cause and principle in your view are said of everything for which existence in its essence continues and then from it the existence of another thing occurs and subsists by it. A thing may be a cause of another thing per se or by accident, and may be a proximate cause or a remote cause. The Necessary Existent in itself, perfect existence in itself, from which the first intellect occurred—is it a cause for it per se or by accident? If it is a cause for it per se not by accident, then it is a principle for it by primary intention not secondary intention, and the Necessary Existence would not be absolutely self-sufficient, but rather poor and needful of acquisition, and it would not be absolutely perfect in itself without its effect, but rather perfect through another, deficient with respect to its essence. How could they, while they refuse that the first effect and all existences are only from the necessary concomitants of its self-intellection? So the direction of the eternal Necessary Existence is only towards itself in the way of absolute independence from all, and the faces of other existences towards it in the way of the need of all towards it. And if they say: it is a cause by accident, then it is false because there is no cause by accident except from a cause per se, and it is false.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a philosophical refutation. First, the author divides divine names into two types: relational (أسماء إضافية) derived from effects (e.g., cause, maker) and negative (أسماء سلبية) derived from transcendence (e.g., one, necessary). He then enters into a discussion about causation. He poses a dilemma regarding the first intellect: is the Necessary Existent its cause per se or by accident? If per se, then the Necessary Existent would depend on the intellect for its perfection, contradicting absolute self-sufficiency. If by accident, then there must be a per se cause behind that accident, leading to an infinite regress, which is false. Thus the philosophical claim that the first intellect necessarily emanates from God is refuted. The author concludes that the Necessary Existent is directed only toward itself in absolute independence, and all other things are directed toward Him in need.

Key Terms
اسم إضافي
اسم سلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two categories of divine names explicitly named in the passage?
A) Essential names and negative names
B) Relational names and negative names
C) Relational names and essential names
D) Affirmative names and negative names

Key Takeaway
Divine names are either relational (derived from God's actions) or negative (derived from His transcendence), protecting both His activity and His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage distinguishes between names from God's actions and names from His transcendence. In your prayer life, do you more often approach God through what He does for you or through recognizing His absolute otherness? Which category do you need to grow in?

8 min
Refuting the First Intellect as Necessary Effect
Refutation of the first intellect as a necessary effect using a causal dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the philosophical claim that the first intellect is a necessary effect of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
If the first intellect were God’s effect per se, God would depend on it; if by accident, infinite regress follows.

After classifying divine names, the author engages in a rational discussion about causation. He targets the philosophical notion that the first intellect necessarily emanates from the Necessary Existent. Using a dilemma, he shows that this claim leads to either God's dependence or logical absurdity. This refutation upholds God's absolute self-sufficiency and independence from all creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
The author presents two options: either God is the cause per se of the first intellect, or He is the cause by accident. Consider each option and its implications.
Cause per se means direct, necessary causation; cause by accident means incidental.
2
Analyze the first horn: per se cause
If God is the cause per se, then He would not be absolutely self-sufficient or perfect without the intellect. He would need the intellect to be perfect, which contradicts His nature.
The text says: 'not absolutely self-sufficient, but poor and needful of acquisition.'
3
Analyze the second horn: cause by accident
If God is the cause by accident, then there must be a real per se cause behind that accident, leading to an infinite regress or abandoning the philosophical system.
The author dismisses this as false because every accidental cause presupposes a per se cause.
4
Conclude the refutation
Thus the claim that the first intellect is a necessary effect of God is logically untenable. God is absolutely independent, and all things depend on Him, not vice versa.
The author ends by asserting God's direction only to Himself and all things' need towards Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يشتق له اسم من نحو آثاره لذاته، أو اسم لذاته، أو اسم من حيث تقدسه عن سمات مخلوقاته. الأول ويسمى أسماء إضافية؛ كالعلة والمبدأ والصانع، ويسمى الثاني أسماء سلبية؛ كالواحد والعقل والعاقل والواجب، وأشتهي أن تجري في هذه الأسامي مباحثة عقلية للإنصاف والانتصاف. فأقول: العلة والمبدأ عندكم يقال على كل ما استمر له وجود في ذاته ثم حصل منه وجود شيء آخر ويقوم به، وقد يكون الشيء علة للشيء بالذات وقد يكون بالعرض، وقد تكون علة قريبة، وقد تكون علة بعيدة، وواجب الوجود لذاته تام الوجود في ذاته وحصل منه العقل الأول أهو علة له بالذات أم بالعرض؟ فإن كان علة له بالذات لا بالعرض فهو مبدأ له بالقصد الأول لا بالقصد الثاني، ولم يكن واجب الوجود غنيا مطلقا، بل فقيرا محتاجا إلى كسب، ولم يكن كاملا مطلقا بذاته لولا معلوله بل كاملا بغيره ناقصا باعتبار ذاته، كيف وهم يأبون أن يكون المعلول الأول وسائر الموجودات إلا من لوازم تعقله بذاته، فلا يكون توجيه واجب الوجود الأزلي إلا إلى ذاته على سبيل الاستغناء المطلق عن الكل، ووجوه سائر الموجودات إليه على سبيل احتياج الكل إليه، وإن قالوا: هو علة بالعرض فباطل لأنه لا علة بالعرض إلا عن علة بالذات، وهو باطل.
Then there is derived for Him a name from the aspect of His effects for His essence, or a name for His essence, or a name from the aspect of His transcendence above the characteristics of His creatures. The first is called relational names, like cause, principle, maker; the second is called negative names, like one, intellect, intelligent, necessary. I desire that a rational discussion be conducted regarding these names for fairness and vindication. I say: Cause and principle in your view are said of everything for which existence in its essence continues and then from it the existence of another thing occurs and subsists by it. A thing may be a cause of another thing per se or by accident, and may be a proximate cause or a remote cause. The Necessary Existent in itself, perfect existence in itself, from which the first intellect occurred—is it a cause for it per se or by accident? If it is a cause for it per se not by accident, then it is a principle for it by primary intention not secondary intention, and the Necessary Existence would not be absolutely self-sufficient, but rather poor and needful of acquisition, and it would not be absolutely perfect in itself without its effect, but rather perfect through another, deficient with respect to its essence. How could they, while they refuse that the first effect and all existences are only from the necessary concomitants of its self-intellection? So the direction of the eternal Necessary Existence is only towards itself in the way of absolute independence from all, and the faces of other existences towards it in the way of the need of all towards it. And if they say: it is a cause by accident, then it is false because there is no cause by accident except from a cause per se, and it is false.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a philosophical refutation. First, the author divides divine names into two types: relational (أسماء إضافية) derived from effects (e.g., cause, maker) and negative (أسماء سلبية) derived from transcendence (e.g., one, necessary). He then enters into a discussion about causation. He poses a dilemma regarding the first intellect: is the Necessary Existent its cause per se or by accident? If per se, then the Necessary Existent would depend on the intellect for its perfection, contradicting absolute self-sufficiency. If by accident, then there must be a per se cause behind that accident, leading to an infinite regress, which is false. Thus the philosophical claim that the first intellect necessarily emanates from God is refuted. The author concludes that the Necessary Existent is directed only toward itself in absolute independence, and all other things are directed toward Him in need.

Key Terms
علة بالذات
علة بالعرض
العقل الأول
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first intellect necessarily emanates from God as a necessary effect, making God dependent on it.
Correction: If that were true, God would not be absolutely self-sufficient or perfect; the only viable position is that God is independent and all effects are by His will, not necessary emanation.

Checkpoint
What happens if the Necessary Existent is a cause per se for the first intellect?
A) He remains absolutely self-sufficient.
B) He would not be absolutely perfect without the intellect.
C) The intellect would be a cause by accident.
D) The intellect would not exist.

Key Takeaway
The claim that the first intellect is a necessary effect of God is logically impossible because it either makes God dependent on creation or leads to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's creation of the universe was a necessary act, would you still feel that your worship is a free choice? How does this passage force you to see God's absolute independence as essential to your relationship with Him?
?

?
The author says that if God caused the first intellect per se, He would be 'poor and needful of acquisition.' Do you ever act as if God needs your deeds? How can you realign your understanding so that your worship flows from neediness toward Him, not from a sense of His need?

Classification of Divine Names: Relative and Negative
The author classifies divine names into relative and negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine names are classified into relative (positive) and negative categories, and the theological significance of this distinction.

Core Idea
Divine names either express a relation to creation or negate imperfection.

This passage introduces a fundamental distinction in Islamic theology: some names of God reflect His relationship with creation (like 'Creator'), while others affirm His transcendence by negating any imperfection (like 'One'). Understanding this classification helps us avoid confusing God's essence with His attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall meaning before analysing.
Notice the terms 'أسماء إضافية' and 'أسماء سلبية'.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two types of divine names mentioned. What are their characteristics and examples?
Relative names include 'cause', 'principle', 'maker'; negative names include 'One', 'Intellect', 'Intelligent', 'Necessary'.
3
Ask why this matters
Consider how this classification helps avoid theological errors. Why is it important to distinguish names that imply a relation from those that negate imperfections?
This connects to the broader principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجودات على أحسن نظام وأتقن إحكام، ثم يشتق له اسم من نحو آثاره لذاته، أو اسم لذاته، أو اسم من حيث تقدسه عن سمات مخلوقاته. الأول ويسمى أسماء إضافية كالعلة والمبدأ والصانع، ويسمى الثاني أسماء سلبية كالواحد والعقل والعاقل والواجب، وأشتهي أن تجري في هذه الأسامي مباحثة عقلية للإنصاف والانتصاف.
The existents are upon the best system and most perfect order. Then a name is derived for Him from the likes of His effects due to His essence, or a name for His essence, or a name from the perspective of His transcendence above the attributes of His creatures. The first is called relative names, like the cause, the principle, and the maker; and the second is called negative names, like the One, the Intellect, the Intelligent, and the Necessary. And I desire that there be a rational investigation of these names for fairness and victory.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine names. The author states that names for God are derived either from His effects (relative names) or from His essence (negative names). The first type includes names like 'cause' and 'maker' which imply a relation to creation. The second type includes names like 'One' and 'Necessary' which negate imperfection. The author expresses a wish to discuss these names rationally, indicating the importance of conceptual clarity in theology.

Correction: Divine names are classified into two categories: relative (positive) names that describe God's relation to creation, and negative names that affirm His transcendence by negating imperfection.

Conceptual Distinction Between Essence and Cause
Author argues that cause and essence have distinct concepts, refuting reduction to one reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concepts of 'cause' and 'necessary by essence' are distinct and how this refutes the claim that all divine attributes reduce to a single essence.

Core Idea
Cause is relational; necessary by essence is negative — they cannot be one.

In this passage, the author tackles a key debate in divine attributes: whether all descriptions of God ultimately refer back to a single essence or are conceptually distinct. He uses the specific example of 'cause' and 'necessary by essence' to show that their concepts differ in kind (relational vs. negative), thus undermining the reductionist view. This lesson walks through the logical steps of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very compact style with multiple contrasts.
2
Identify the two conceptual distinctions
Find where the author distinguishes 'cause' as relational and 'necessary by essence' as negative. Understand why these two categories are fundamentally different.
Relational (إضافي) depends on another, negative (سلبي) denies something.
3
Understand the refutation of opponents
The author attacks those who reduce all attributes to one essence, showing that even within their own schools (Ash'ari, Mu'tazili) they admit distinctions. His argument forces them to accept that relational and negative aspects cannot be collapsed into one.
The opponent's analogy of nearness and distance is dismissed as insufficient.
4
Grasp the final implication about causality and negation
The author concludes that being a cause is a negative state, not a positive attribute, and that the effect does not follow from mere negation of multiplicity. This solidifies the conceptual separation.
Consider: if cause were identical to essence, then the effect would be necessary from the essence alone, which is not the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول أيضًا كونه مبدأ وعلة لم يدخل في مفهوم كونه واجبًا بذاته، فإن مفهوم أنه علة أمر إضافي ومفهوم أنه واجب بذاته أمر سلبي، وقد تمايز الأمران والمفهومان ويدل كل واحد منهما على غير ما دل عليه الثاني، فمن أين يصح لكم رد المعاني إلى أمر واحد وهو الذات، فتمايز المفهومات والاعتبارات عندكم، وتمايز الأحوال عند أبي هاشم، وتمايز الصفات عند أبي الحسن على وتيرة واحدة، وكلكم يشير إلى مدلولات مختلفة الخواص والحقائق، واعتذاركم أن كثرة اللوازم والسلوب والإضافات لا تقتضي كثرة في صفات الذات، واستشهادكم بالقرب والبعد لا يغني عن هذا الإلزام، فإنا نلزمكم تمايز الوجوه والاعتبارات بين الإضافي والسلبي؛ إذ من المعلوم كونه علة ومبدأ للمعلول الأول أمر أو حال سلبي، وليس يوجب المعلول من حيث إنه انتفى عنه الكثرة، وليس كونه مسلوب الكثرة عنه موجبًا للمعلول، ولا يلزم وجوده يسلب عنه شيء من حيث يلزم وجوده شيء.
We also say: His being a principle and a cause does not enter into the concept of His being necessary by essence. For the concept of His being a cause is a relational matter, and the concept of His being necessary by essence is a negative matter. The two matters and concepts are distinct, each indicating something other than what the second indicates. So from where is it valid for you to reduce the meanings to a single thing, namely the essence? Thus the distinction of concepts and considerations with you, and the distinction of states with Abu Hashim, and the distinction of attributes with Abu al-Hasan, are on one pattern. All of you point to referents different in properties and realities. And your excuse that the multiplicity of consequences, negations, and relations does not require multiplicity in the attributes of the essence, and your citation of nearness and distance, does not suffice against this necessary implication. For we impose on you the distinction of aspects and considerations between the relational and the negative. Since it is known that His being cause and principle of the first effect is a negative matter or state, and that the effect is not necessitated from the aspect that multiplicity is negated of Him, and His being negated of multiplicity does not necessitate the effect, and His existence does not necessitate that something is negated of Him from the aspect that His existence necessitates something.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a proof that cause and essence are conceptually distinct, refuting the opponent's claim that they reduce to the same reality. The author first distinguishes the concepts: 'cause' is relational (إضافي), 'necessary by essence' is negative (سلبي). Since they indicate different things, they cannot be reduced to one essence. Then the author attacks the opponent's own schools: the Ash'aris accept distinction of concepts and attributes, the Mu'tazilites accept states, yet they all point to different realities. Their excuse that multiplicity of consequences etc. does not imply multiplicity of attributes is insufficient, especially their analogy of nearness and distance. The author then explains that being cause is a negative state (not a positive attribute), and the effect does not follow from mere negation of multiplicity. The argument culminates in showing that the opponent's position leads to contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceptual distinction implies multiple essences or composition in God.
Correction: The author only claims that the concepts are distinct, not that the essence is multiple. He is arguing against reducing all attributes to one concept, not that God has multiple essences.

Checkpoint
How does the author characterize the concept of 'cause' compared to 'necessary by essence'?
A) Both are positive.
B) Both are negative.
C) Cause is relational, necessary by essence is negative.
D) Cause is negative, necessary by essence is relational.

Key Takeaway
The concepts of cause and necessary by essence are distinct (relational vs. negative), so they cannot be reduced to one essence, refuting those who deny real attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says 'necessary by essence' is a negative concept—it removes need. Do you truly internalize that Allah needs nothing from you, or do you sometimes act as if He needs your worship?
?
If 'cause' and 'necessary by essence' are distinct, then your existence as an effect does not diminish His necessity. How does this truth challenge any hidden belief that you are somehow a burden to Allah?

The Ninth Foundation: Mu'tazilite Position on Attributes
Opening of the Mu'tazilite position on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic Mu'tazilite view that God is knowing, living, and powerful by His essence without separate attributes.

Core Idea
God’s attributes are His essence, not distinct additions.

This lesson opens the Ninth Foundation, which deals with proving the eternal attributes. We begin with the Mu'tazilite school, which held a strong position of divine simplicity: God’s attributes are not separate from His essence. This sets the stage for the broader debate within Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic from 'القاعدة التاسعة' to 'كما سنوردها مسائل أفراد إن شاء الله تعالى' and its translation.
This introduces the Mu'tazilite consensus on essential attributes.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Notice that they say God is حي عالم قادر لذاته (living, knowing, powerful by His essence) and explicitly deny separate attributes of knowledge, power, life.
3
Note the exception
The Mu'tazila differed regarding attributes like hearing, seeing, willing, speaking – these will be discussed later as individual issues.
This shows internal diversity within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة التاسعة في إثبات العلم بالصفات الأزلية. صارت المعتزلة إلى أن الباري تعالى حي عالم قادر لذاته لا يعلم وقدرة وحياة، واختلفوا في كونه سميعاً بصيراً مريداً متكلماً على طرق مختلفة، كما سنوردها مسائل أفراد إن شاء الله تعالى، وأبو الهذيل العلاف انتهج مناهج الفلاسفة، فقال: الباري تعالى عالم بعلم هو نفسه، ولكن لا يقال نفسه علم كما قالت الفلاسفة: عاقل وعقل ومعقول. ثم اختلفت المعتزلة في أن أحكام الذات هل هي أحوال الذات أم وجوه واعتبارات؟ فقال أكثرهم: هي أسماء وأحكام للذات وليست أحوال وصفات، كما في الشاهد من الصفات الذاتية للجوهر والصفات التابعة للحدوث. وقال أبو هاشم هي أحوال ثابتة للذات وأثبت حالة أخرى توجب هذه الأحوال. وقالت الصفاتية من الأشعرية والسلف: إن الباري تعالى عالم بعلم، قادر بقدرة، حي بحياة، سميع بسمع، بصير بيصر، مريد بإرادة متكلم بكلام باق ببقاء، وهذه الصفات زائدة على ذاته سبحانه، وهي صفات موجودة أزلية، ومعان قائمة بذاته.
The Ninth Foundation: On Proving the Knowledge of the Eternal Attributes. The Mu'tazila held that the Exalted Creator is living, knowing, powerful by His essence without knowledge, power, and life, and they differed concerning His being hearing, seeing, willing, speaking in various ways, as we will mention them as individual issues if God wills. And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf followed the paths of the philosophers, and said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by a knowledge that is His essence, but it is not said that His essence is knowledge, as the philosophers said: intellect, intellecting, and intelligible. Then the Mu'tazila differed as to whether the rulings of the essence are states of the essence or aspects and considerations. The majority of them said: They are names and rulings for the essence, not states and attributes, as in the witnessed case of the essential attributes of the substance and the attributes consequent upon temporality. And Abu Hashim said: They are states established for the essence, and he affirmed another state that necessitates these states. And the attribute-sect (al-Sifatiyya) from the Ash'aris and the Salaf said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, hearing by hearing, seeing by sight, willing by will, speaking by speech, enduring by endurance, and these attributes are additional to His essence, exalted be He, and they are existent eternal attributes and meanings subsisting in His essence.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a classification and comparative presentation of theological positions on God's attributes. The author introduces the Ninth Foundation on proving the eternal attributes. First, he states the Mu'tazilite position: God is knowing, living, powerful by His essence, without separate attributes. They differed on the attributes of hearing, seeing, etc. Then he mentions Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who followed the philosophers, holding that God's knowledge is His essence, but not that the essence is called 'knowledge', analogous to the philosophical triad of intellect, intellecting, and intelligible. Next, the author notes an internal Mu'tazilite disagreement: whether the attributes (ahkam) of the essence are 'states' (ahwal) or merely aspects and considerations. The majority said they are names and rulings, not states or attributes, comparing it to the essential attributes of a substance in the witnessed world. Abu Hashim affirmed that they are states established in the essence and posited an additional state that necessitates these states. Finally, the author presents the view of the 'Sifatiyya' (the attribute-affirming sect) from the Ash'aris and Salaf: God possesses real, eternal attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) that are additional to His essence, existing eternally as meanings subsisting in His essence.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الصفات الأزلية
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as described, how is God knowing?
A) By a separate attribute of knowledge
B) By His essence
C) By a state (haal)
D) By a meaning (ma'na)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite school insists that God's essential attributes are identical to His essence, not separate entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the Mu'tazila said God is knowing without knowledge and living without life, does your mind resist this simplicity? In what way do you need to purify your understanding of God from any trace of comparing Him to created beings?
?
The Mu'tazila differed on whether God is hearing, seeing, willing, and speaking. How do you handle theological uncertainty about God's attributes? Does it drive you to seek deeper understanding, or do you avoid it because it feels uncomfortable? Commit to reading one more source on this topic this week.

Abu al-Hudhayl and Mu'tazilite Dispute on States
Covers Abu al-Hudhayl's view and the ahwal dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hudhayl's philosophical position and the internal Mu'tazilite debate over whether attributes are states or mere aspects.

Core Idea
Are God's attributes states or just names? The Mu'tazila disagreed.

After stating the general Mu'tazilite position, the author turns to a key figure, Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who blended Mu'tazilism with philosophical concepts. Then the passage reports a crucial internal disagreement among the Mu'tazila: whether the 'rulings of the essence' (ahkam al-dhat) are real states (ahwal) or just aspects and considerations. This debate shaped later theology.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu al-Hudhayl's view
Read from 'وأبو الهذيل العلاف' to 'ولكن لا يقال نفسه علم' and its translation.
He says God knows by a knowledge that is His essence, but not that the essence is called 'knowledge'.
2
Understand his philosophical background
He followed the philosophers' triad: عاقل وعقل ومعقول (intellect, intellection, intelligible). This is an analogy.
3
Read the Mu'tazilite dispute on ahwal
Read from 'ثم اختلفت المعتزلة' to 'وأثبت حالة أخرى توجب هذه الأحوال'.
Two positions: majority said attributes are names/rulings, not states; Abu Hashim said they are states.
4
Reflect on the difference
Consider what is at stake: if attributes are states, they have some reality; if mere names, they are purely relational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة التاسعة في إثبات العلم بالصفات الأزلية. صارت المعتزلة إلى أن الباري تعالى حي عالم قادر لذاته لا يعلم وقدرة وحياة، واختلفوا في كونه سميعاً بصيراً مريداً متكلماً على طرق مختلفة، كما سنوردها مسائل أفراد إن شاء الله تعالى، وأبو الهذيل العلاف انتهج مناهج الفلاسفة، فقال: الباري تعالى عالم بعلم هو نفسه، ولكن لا يقال نفسه علم كما قالت الفلاسفة: عاقل وعقل ومعقول. ثم اختلفت المعتزلة في أن أحكام الذات هل هي أحوال الذات أم وجوه واعتبارات؟ فقال أكثرهم: هي أسماء وأحكام للذات وليست أحوال وصفات، كما في الشاهد من الصفات الذاتية للجوهر والصفات التابعة للحدوث. وقال أبو هاشم هي أحوال ثابتة للذات وأثبت حالة أخرى توجب هذه الأحوال. وقالت الصفاتية من الأشعرية والسلف: إن الباري تعالى عالم بعلم، قادر بقدرة، حي بحياة، سميع بسمع، بصير بيصر، مريد بإرادة متكلم بكلام باق ببقاء، وهذه الصفات زائدة على ذاته سبحانه، وهي صفات موجودة أزلية، ومعان قائمة بذاته.
The Ninth Foundation: On Proving the Knowledge of the Eternal Attributes. The Mu'tazila held that the Exalted Creator is living, knowing, powerful by His essence without knowledge, power, and life, and they differed concerning His being hearing, seeing, willing, speaking in various ways, as we will mention them as individual issues if God wills. And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf followed the paths of the philosophers, and said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by a knowledge that is His essence, but it is not said that His essence is knowledge, as the philosophers said: intellect, intellecting, and intelligible. Then the Mu'tazila differed as to whether the rulings of the essence are states of the essence or aspects and considerations. The majority of them said: They are names and rulings for the essence, not states and attributes, as in the witnessed case of the essential attributes of the substance and the attributes consequent upon temporality. And Abu Hashim said: They are states established for the essence, and he affirmed another state that necessitates these states. And the attribute-sect (al-Sifatiyya) from the Ash'aris and the Salaf said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, hearing by hearing, seeing by sight, willing by will, speaking by speech, enduring by endurance, and these attributes are additional to His essence, exalted be He, and they are existent eternal attributes and meanings subsisting in His essence.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a classification and comparative presentation of theological positions on God's attributes. The author introduces the Ninth Foundation on proving the eternal attributes. First, he states the Mu'tazilite position: God is knowing, living, powerful by His essence, without separate attributes. They differed on the attributes of hearing, seeing, etc. Then he mentions Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who followed the philosophers, holding that God's knowledge is His essence, but not that the essence is called 'knowledge', analogous to the philosophical triad of intellect, intellecting, and intelligible. Next, the author notes an internal Mu'tazilite disagreement: whether the attributes (ahkam) of the essence are 'states' (ahwal) or merely aspects and considerations. The majority said they are names and rulings, not states or attributes, comparing it to the essential attributes of a substance in the witnessed world. Abu Hashim affirmed that they are states established in the essence and posited an additional state that necessitates these states. Finally, the author presents the view of the 'Sifatiyya' (the attribute-affirming sect) from the Ash'aris and Salaf: God possesses real, eternal attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) that are additional to His essence, existing eternally as meanings subsisting in His essence.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل العلاف
أبو هاشم
أحوال
وجوه واعتبارات
الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazilites agree that attributes are only names with no reality.
Correction: Abu Hashim and his followers held that attributes are real states (ahwal), showing diversity within the school.

Checkpoint
What did Abu Hashim affirm regarding the rulings of the essence?
A) They are mere names and rulings.
B) They are aspects and considerations.
C) They are states established for the essence.
D) They are identical to the essence.

Key Takeaway
Even within Mu'tazilism, there was a realist minority that treated attributes as real states, not just names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is Knowing,' do you realize your words may not capture His reality as He truly is? How does the Mu'tazilite debate challenge you to be more humble in speaking about God? Will you pause today before describing Him, acknowledging the limits of human language?
?
The majority Mu'tazila said attributes are mere names. Do you ever treat God's attributes as empty titles without letting them transform your heart? Which attribute of God do you most need to internalize right now, and how will you act on it today?
?
Abu Hashim insisted attributes are real states. If you truly believe God's mercy is real, not just a name, how should that change your confidence in His mercy when you sin? Commit to one concrete act of seeking that mercy today.

The Ash'ari and Salaf Position: Real Eternal Attributes
Presents the Ash'ari/Salaf affirmation of real attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ash'aris and Salaf affirm that God has real, eternal attributes that are additional to His essence.

Core Idea
God’s attributes are real, eternal, and distinct from His essence.

Having surveyed the Mu'tazilite positions, the author now presents the view of the 'Sifatiyya'—the attribute-affirming school—which includes both Ash'aris and Salaf. This is the mainstream Sunni position: God possesses real, eternal attributes like knowledge, power, life, hearing, sight, will, speech, and endurance, each separate from the essence but not separate from God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sifatiyya position
Read from 'وقالت الصفاتية' to the end of the passage and its translation.
Notice the list of attributes and the claim that they are زائدة على ذاته (additional to His essence).
2
Count the attributes mentioned
The passage lists: علم, قدرة, حياة, سمع, بصر, إرادة, كلام, بقاء. Each is paired with its active participle.
This is a standard list of eight eternal attributes in Ash'ari theology.
3
Understand the key phrase
'صفات موجودة أزلية، ومعان قائمة بذاته' – they are existent, eternal, and meanings subsisting in the essence.
This establishes the reality and eternality of the attributes.

Mu'tazila Position on Divine Attributes
The Mu'tazila general position on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila stance that God is living, knowing, and powerful by His essence, and their disagreement on attributes like hearing, seeing, willing, and speaking.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila affirmed God's attributes as essence, not separate entities.

The Mu'tazila are known for their rational theology. One of their key positions is that God's essential attributes are not distinct from His essence. This passage presents their view that God is living, knowing, and powerful by Himself, not by separate attributes, and notes their internal differences on other attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key phrase 'عالم لذاته' (knowing by essence).
2
Identify the attributes
List the attributes mentioned: living (حي), knowing (عالم), powerful (قادر) are by essence; hearing, seeing, willing, speaking are disputed.
The Mu'tazila generally deny separate attributes for the first three but differ on the others.
3
Contrast with the Ash'ari view
Contrast this with the Ash'ari belief in eternal attributes distinct from essence (though not separate in number). This helps understand the Mu'tazila motivation of tawhid.
4
Reflect: Why by essence?
Ask yourself: Why would the Mu'tazila insist on this? Their focus on God's unity (tawhid) leads them to avoid multiplicity in the divine essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صارت المعتزلة إلى أن الباري تعالى حي عالم قادر لذاته لا يعلم وقدرة وحياة، واختلفوا في كونه سميعاً بصيراً مريداً متكلماً على طرق مختلفة، كما سنوردها مسائل أفراد إن شاء الله تعالى. وأبو الهذيل العلاف انتهج مناهج الفلاسفة، فقال : الباري تعالى عالم بعلم هو نفسه، ولكن لا يقال نفسه علم كما قالت الفلاسفة : عاقل وعقل ومعقول.
The Mu'tazila held that the Exalted Creator is living, knowing, powerful by His essence, not by knowledge, power, or life. They differed regarding His being hearing, seeing, willing, speaking according to various ways, as we will present them as individual issues, God willing. Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf followed the ways of the philosophers, and said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by a knowledge that is Himself, but it is not said that He is Himself knowledge, just as the philosophers said: intellect, intellector, and intelligible.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila stance on divine attributes. It states that the Mu'tazila believe God is living, knowing, powerful by His essence, not by separate attributes (knowledge, power, life). They differed on whether He is hearing, seeing, willing, and speaking. Then it mentions Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who adopted the philosophers' view: God knows by a knowledge that is identical to His essence, but one cannot say 'He is knowledge', paralleling the philosophical triad of intellect, intellector, and intelligible.

Key Terms

عالم لذاته
حي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny all attributes of God.
Correction: They deny separate attributes but affirm that God is living, knowing, and powerful by His essence.

Checkpoint

A) By a separate attribute of knowledge.
B) By His essence without a separate attribute.
C) Through a knowledge that is identical to His essence.
D) He is not knowing.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila affirm that God is living, knowing, and powerful by His essence, not by separate attributes, while differing on other attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes, do you naturally imagine them as separate 'things' added to His being, or as inherently part of who He is? How does the Mu'tazila emphasis on 'by His essence' challenge your default assumption, and will you reconsider how you speak about God's attributes from now on?
?
The Mu'tazila affirmed some attributes (life, knowledge, power) as essential but differed on others (hearing, seeing, etc.). What does this reveal about the limits of human reason in understanding God? Are you comfortable with the idea that some aspects of God's attributes may never be fully grasped by the mind, or do you feel pressured to have a complete explanation?
?
If you truly believe that God is 'living, knowing, and powerful by His essence,' how should that change the way you relate to Him in your daily life? Do you live as if He is intimately present and aware of every detail, or do you sometimes act as if He is distant and disconnected? What specific step will you take this week to align your actions with this truth?

Abu al-Hudhayl's Philosophers-Inspired View
Abu al-Hudhayl's view on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf's view that God's knowledge is identical to His essence, and his analogy to the philosophical triad of intellect, intellector, and intelligible.

Core Idea
Abu al-Hudhayl equated God's knowledge with His essence, mirroring the philosopher's triad.

Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf was a prominent Mu'tazila theologian who adopted concepts from Greek philosophy. This passage presents his nuanced position: God knows by a knowledge that is the same as His essence, yet we do not say 'He is knowledge', drawing an analogy to the philosophical terms 'intellect, intellector, intelligible'.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the sentences about Abu al-Hudhayl. Read the Arabic and translation.
2
Understand the core claim
He says God is 'عالم بعلم هو نفسه' (knowing by a knowledge that is Himself). That means knowledge is identical to the essence.
3
Grasp the nuance
He adds 'لكن لا يقال نفسه علم' (but it is not said that He is Himself knowledge). Why the distinction? To avoid pantheism or essentialism.
4
Analyze the philosophical analogy
The philosophers speak of 'عاقل وعقل ومعقول' (intellect, intellector, intelligible). In God, these three are one: He is the intellect, the knower, and the known. But we don't say He is intellect (knowledge) per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صارت المعتزلة إلى أن الباري تعالى حي عالم قادر لذاته لا يعلم وقدرة وحياة، واختلفوا في كونه سميعاً بصيراً مريداً متكلماً على طرق مختلفة، كما سنوردها مسائل أفراد إن شاء الله تعالى. وأبو الهذيل العلاف انتهج مناهج الفلاسفة، فقال : الباري تعالى عالم بعلم هو نفسه، ولكن لا يقال نفسه علم كما قالت الفلاسفة : عاقل وعقل ومعقول.

They differed regarding His being hearing, seeing, willing, speaking according to various ways, as we will present them as individual issues, God willing. Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf followed the ways of the philosophers, and said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by a knowledge that is Himself, but it is not said that He is Himself knowledge, just as the philosophers said: intellect, intellector, and intelligible.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila stance on divine attributes. It states that the Mu'tazila believe God is living, knowing, powerful by His essence, not by separate attributes (knowledge, power, life). They differed on whether He is hearing, seeing, willing, and speaking. Then it mentions Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who adopted the philosophers' view: God knows by a knowledge that is identical to His essence, but one cannot say 'He is knowledge', paralleling the philosophical triad of intellect, intellector, and intelligible.

Abu al-Hudhayl holds that God's knowledge is identical to His essence, but one should not say that God is knowledge, similar to the philosophical triad of intellect, intellector, intelligible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge, do you ever unconsciously treat it as a separate 'thing' that He possesses, rather than being His very essence? How might that subtle separation affect your trust that He knows every detail of your life—including the struggles you hide? This week, pick one moment where you feel unseen, and intentionally say, 'Ya Allah, Your knowledge is You—You are fully present in this.'
?
Abu al-Hudhayl warned against saying 'God is knowledge' to avoid reducing Him to an attribute. In your own worship, do you ever reduce God to one of His attributes—like focusing only on His mercy while ignoring His justice? How could a balanced view of all His attributes, as inseparable from His essence, change your repentance today?

Mu'tazila on States vs. Aspects
Mu'tazila disagreement on the nature of essential judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal Mu'tazila debate over whether God's essential judgments are real states or mere aspects.

Core Idea
God's attributes: real states or just names? The Mu'tazila split.

This passage reveals a key internal debate among the Mu'tazila. While they all denied eternal attributes distinct from the essence, they disagreed on the ontological status of the judgments we make about God. Are these judgments real 'states' (ahwal) attached to the essence, or merely 'aspects' (wujuh) we consider? The answer shapes their entire theory of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the two positions and the analogy given by the majority.
Pay attention to the phrase 'كما في الشاهد' - it signals an analogy from the observable world.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the majority view and Abu Hashim's view. Note how the majority compares divine attributes to the essential attributes of a substance.
The majority says judgments are 'names and judgments, not states and attributes.'
3
Reflect on the technical terms
Understand the difference between 'ahwal' (states) and 'wujuh wa i'tibarat' (aspects and considerations). Ask: why does this distinction matter for theology?
This debate influences later Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت المعتزلة في أن أحكام الذات هل هي أحوال الذات أم وجوه واعتبارات؟ فقال أكثرهم : هي أسماء وأحكام للذات وليست أحوال وصفات، كما في الشاهد من الصفات الذاتية للجوهر والصفات التابعة للحدوث. وقال أبو هاشم هي أحوال ثابتة للذات وأثبت حالة أخرى توجب هذه الأحوال.
Then the Mu'tazila differed over whether the judgments of the essence are states of the essence or aspects and considerations. Most of them said: They are names and judgments of the essence, not states and attributes, as in the observable world regarding the essential attributes of the substance and the attributes dependent on temporality. Abu Hashim said: They are established states of the essence, and he posited another state that necessitates these states.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage explains the internal Mu'tazila disagreement on the nature of the judgments (ahkam) of God's essence. The question is whether these judgments are 'states' (ahwal) or 'aspects and considerations' (wujuh wa i'tibarat). The majority held that they are merely names and judgments of the essence, not real states or attributes, analogous to observable attributes of a substance that are inherent to it. In contrast, Abu Hashim (al-Jubba'i) argued that they are 'states' (ahwal) that are established for the essence, and he further posited an additional state that brings about these states.

Key Terms
أحكام الذات
أحوال
وجوه واعتبارات
أبو هاشم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila were unanimous on the nature of divine attributes.
Correction: This passage shows a significant internal split: the majority viewed judgments as mere names, while Abu Hashim considered them real states.

Checkpoint
According to most Mu'tazila, the judgments of God's essence are:
A) Real states (ahwal) subsisting in the essence.
B) Names and judgments only, not states.
C) Attributes additional to the essence.
D) The same as the essence.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila split on the ontological status of divine judgments: majority saw them as mere names, Abu Hashim as real states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Merciful', do you feel you are stating a real quality in Him, or just using a name? How would your relationship with Him change if you firmly believed His mercy is a real state?
?
This lesson shows a theological debate over whether attributes are mere labels or real. Have you ever treated God's names as empty titles? What would it look like this week to live as if each attribute is a living reality?
?
The Mu'tazila majority saw attributes as judgments without extra reality. Do you ever find yourself treating God's attributes as theoretical concepts rather than truths that demand your response? Commit to one specific attribute you will actively rely on today.

Ash'ari Affirmation of Eternal Additional Attributes
Affirmation of seven eternal attributes as additional to essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'aris and Salaf affirm that God's attributes are real, eternal, and additional to His essence, subsisting in Him as meanings.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real and additional to His essence, not identical to it.

The Ash'ari school and the early Salaf agree that God possesses attributes such as knowledge, power, and life that are not merely names but real meanings (ma'ani) subsisting in His essence. This passage states their fundamental position clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the attributes are listed in pairs.
2
Identify the list of attributes
Find each attribute mentioned (knowledge, power, life, hearing, sight, will, speech, eternity). Note that each is paired with its corresponding noun.
Count them – there are eight attributes listed.
3
Note the three key phrases
The passage describes these attributes as 'additional to His essence', 'existent, eternal', and 'meanings subsisting in His essence'. These phrases are crucial for the orthodox position.
These phrases distinguish the Ash'ari view from other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الصفاتية من الأشعرية والسلف: إن الباري تعالى عالم بعلم، قادر بقدرة، حي بحياة، سميع بسمع، بصير بيصر، مريد بإرادة متكلم بكلام باق ببقاء، وهذه الصفات زائدة على ذاته سبحانه، وهي صفات موجودة أزلية، ومعان قائمة بذاته. وحقيقة الإلهية هي أن تكون ذات أزلية موصوفة بتلك الصفات. وزاد بعض السلف : قديم بقدم كريم بكرم، جواد بجود، إلى أن عد عبد الله بن سعيد الكلابي خمس عشرة صفة على غير فرق بين صفات الذات وصفات الأفعال.
And the attributes-positing group among the Ash'aris and the Salaf said: The Exalted Creator is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power, living by life, hearing by hearing, seeing by sight, willing by will, speaking by speech, eternal by eternity. These attributes are additional to His essence, Exalted is He, and they are existent, eternal attributes, and meanings subsisting in His essence. The reality of divinity is that the essence be eternal, described by these attributes. And some of the Salaf added: ancient by ancientness, generous by generosity, bountiful by bounty, until 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi counted fifteen attributes without distinguishing between attributes of essence and attributes of acts.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the orthodox Sunni position from the Ash'ari school and the Salaf. They affirm that God is knowing by a real knowledge, powerful by a real power, etc., and that these attributes are additional (za'ida) to His essence, meaning they are not identical to it. They are eternal, existent, and subsist in the essence (qaima bi-dhatihi). The definition of divinity (haqiqat al-ilahiyya) is an eternal essence described by these attributes. Some early Salaf increased the list, including 'ancient by ancientness' etc., and al-Kullabi enumerated fifteen attributes without separating attributes of essence from attributes of acts.

Key Terms
الصفاتية
الأشعرية
السلف
صفات زائدة
معان قائمة بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's attributes are merely negative or relational, not real.
Correction: The Ash'aris affirm that attributes are real meanings (ma'ani) subsisting in the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari and Salaf, God's attributes are:
A) Identical to His essence.
B) Additional to His essence and eternal.
C) Merely names for His essence.
D) Created in time.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari and Salaf affirm seven primary eternal attributes that are real and additional to God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah hears all,' do you truly believe His hearing is a real, eternal attribute that is always active, or do you dismiss it as a metaphor? How would your awareness of His constant hearing change the way you speak today?
?
If God's attributes are additional to His essence, then His knowledge and power are not just names but distinct realities. Have you ever treated His attributes as mere concepts? What would it mean for you to live with the conviction that His power is real and ever-present?
?
This passage affirms that God's attributes are 'meanings subsisting in His essence.' How does this deepen your understanding of tawheed? Can you identify any area in your life where you have unknowingly separated God's attributes from His oneness, and how will you correct it starting today?

Definition of Divinity and Expanded Attributes Lists
Definition of divinity and expanded attribute lists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris define the reality of divinity and that some early scholars extended the list of attributes to include others, with al-Kullabi enumerating fifteen.

Core Idea
Divinity is an eternal essence described by these attributes; lists expanded to include more.

After affirming the core attributes, the text gives a precise definition of what divinity is: an eternal essence described by those attributes. It also notes that some of the Salaf added further attributes like generosity, and al-Kullabi compiled a list of fifteen, without distinguishing between attributes of essence and acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of divinity
Read the sentence 'وحقيقة الإلهية هي أن تكون ذات أزلية موصوفة بتلك الصفات'. Understand that this defines what it means to be divine.
This definition anchors the attributes discussion.
2
Note the expansion by some Salaf
Some early Salaf added attributes like 'ancient by ancientness', 'generous by generosity', etc. This shows the list was not fixed.
These additions are not contradictory to the core list.
3
Understand al-Kullabi's enumeration
Al-Kullabi counted fifteen attributes without distinguishing between attributes of essence and attributes of acts. This indicates an early stage of theological development.
Later scholars would make that distinction.
