The Philosophers' Claim of Absolute Oneness
The philosophers deny any real multiplicity in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers assert that God, as the necessary existent, must be absolutely one with no real attributes, states, or aspects.

Core Idea
God's oneness excludes any real distinction within His essence.

The philosophers begin from the premise that the necessary existent cannot be composite or multiple in any way. Since God is necessary by essence, He cannot have attributes that add something to His essence. This lesson presents their rigorous denial of any real multiplicity in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on absolute oneness
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the strong emphasis on 'one from every aspect'.
2
Identify what is denied
Look for the list of things that are not applicable to God: attribute (sifa), state (hal), consideration (i'tibar), direction (hayth), aspect (wajh). Understand that these are all denied because they imply distinction.
The philosophers are negating any form of multiplicity in God's essence.
3
Understand the argument from necessity
The passage argues that nothing shares in necessary existence and eternity with God, so He cannot be divided or multiplied in any way. Ask yourself why this follows from being necessary.
Necessity implies simplicity and uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود بذاته لن يتصور إلا واحدا من كل وجه فلا صفة، ولا حال ولا اعتبار ولا حيث ولا وجه لذات واجب الوجود بحيث يكون أحد الوجهين والاعتبارين غير الآخر، أو يدل لفظ على شيء هو غير الآخر بذاته، ولا يجوز أن يكون نوع واجب الوجود لغير ذاته ؛ لأن وجود نوعه له لعينه ، ولا يشاركه شيء ما صفة كانت أو موصوفًا في وجوب الوجود والأزلية، ولا ينقسم هو، ولا يتكثر لا بالكم ولا بالمبادئ المقومة، ولا بأجزاء الحقيقة والحد تعم له صفات سلبية مثل : تقدسه عن الكثرة من كل وجه فيسمى لذلك واحدا حقا أحدًا صمدا ومثل : تنزهه عن المادة وتجرده عن طبيعة الإمكان والعدم، ويسمى لذلك عقلا وواجبا، وله صفات إضافية مثل : كونه صانعا مبدعًا حكيمًا قديرًا جوادًا كريما، وصفات مركبة من سلب وإضافة مثل كونه مريدا ؛ أي: هو مع عقليته ووجوبه بذاته مبدأ لنظام الخير كله، من غير كراهية لما يصدر عنه، وجوادًا ؛ أي : هو بهذه الصفة، وزيادة سلب؛ أي لا ينحو إعراضا لذاته، وأولا؛ أي هو مسلوب عنه الحدوث، مع إضافة وجود الكل إليه وصفاته عندهم إما سلبية
The philosophers said: The necessary existent by essence can only be conceived as one from every aspect. So there is no attribute, no state, no consideration, no direction, no aspect for the essence of the necessary existent such that one of the two aspects and considerations is other than the other, nor does any word indicate something other than the other by essence. It is not permissible that the type of the necessary existent be for other than its essence, because the existence of its type is for its very self, and nothing shares with it, whether attribute or described, in being necessary of existence and eternity. It does not divide, nor does it become multiple, neither by quantity nor by constitutive principles, nor by parts of reality and definition. It has negative attributes such as: His holiness from multiplicity from every aspect, thus He is called One, True, Unique, Eternal (Samad). And like His transcendence from matter and His abstraction from the nature of possibility and non-existence, thus He is called intellect and necessary. And He has relational attributes such as: His being a maker, originator, wise, powerful, generous, bountiful. And composite attributes from negation and addition such as: His being willing, which means: He, along with His intellectuality and necessity by essence, is the source of the entire order of good, without aversion to what emanates from Him. And 'generous' means He is with this attribute, and an addition of negation, i.e., He does not incline to aversion for His essence. And 'first' means: temporality is negated from Him, along with the addition of the existence of all things to Him. And His attributes according to them are either negative...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage outlines the philosophers' (falasifa) concept of God. Since God is the necessary existent by essence, He must be absolutely one from every aspect, hence no real multiplicity of attributes exists. There is no attribute (sifa), state (hal), consideration (i'tibar), direction (hayth), or aspect (wajh) that is distinct from another. Nothing shares in necessary existence and eternity. He is indivisible and not composed of parts or principles. Instead, the attributes we ascribe are of three types: (1) negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) like being one (wahid, ahad, samad), being free from matter (mujarrad), etc. (2) relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) like being creator, wise, etc. (3) composite attributes (sifat murakkaba) which combine negation and relation, e.g., willing (murid) meaning He is the source of all good without aversion; generous meaning with negation of aversion; first meaning negation of temporality with relation of causing existence. The passage ends by stating that attributes are either negative...

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
صفة
حال
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers completely deny any description of God.
Correction: They do not deny description; they reinterpret attributes as negative or relational, not as real additions to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can there be no attribute (sifa) for God?
A) Because attributes are impossible for any being.
B) Because God is absolutely one and any attribute would imply multiplicity.
C) Because language cannot describe God.
D) Because attributes are only for created things.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' absolute oneness of God means no real attributes or distinctions exist in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever subtly elevated something in your life—a person, a goal, a comfort—to a level of ultimacy that belongs only to the One who is necessary by essence? This lesson demands that you examine whether any part of your heart shares in the devotion that is due solely to God, whose oneness admits no partnership.
?
When you recite 'Allah' or call upon His names like 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful', do you truly believe that these are not additional beings added to His essence? Or does a trace of the philosophers' error lurk in your mind—thinking of His attributes as separate 'things'? Let this passage strip away any such illusion and purify your understanding of tawhid.
?
If God's oneness is so absolute that no real distinction exists within Him, then how can you worship Him with your whole being without dividing your heart? Your own thoughts about God must become unified, just as He is one. Ask yourself: is your concept of God fragmented by worldly distractions and competing loyalties? This is the moment to repent and realign your heart to the One who is one from every aspect.

Negative Attributes in the Philosophers' View
The philosophers list negative attributes of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers describe God through negative attributes, such as oneness and transcendence from matter and potentiality.

Core Idea
Negative attributes affirm God's perfection by denying imperfections.

Having established that God has no real positive attributes, the philosophers explain how we can still speak of His attributes by negating imperfections. This lesson covers two key negative attributes: oneness (which negates multiplicity) and abstraction from matter and potentiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the negative attributes passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the formula 'negative attributes such as...'. Identify the two examples given.
2
Analyze the first negative attribute: Oneness
The text says God is called 'One, True, Unique, Eternal' because He is holy from multiplicity. Understand that these names all negate any form of plurality.
Oneness here is a negation of multiplicity, not a positive quality.
3
Analyze the second negative attribute: Abstraction
God is described as free from matter and abstracted from possibility and non-existence. This leads to the names 'intellect' and 'necessary'. Reflect on why 'intellect' is used here.
Abstraction from matter implies intellect, as matter is seen as a hindrance to intellect.

Relational and Composite Attributes in the Philosophers' View
The philosophers list relational and composite attributes of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' concepts of relational attributes (like Creator) and composite attributes (like Will) as ways to describe God's relation to creation without implying multiplicity in His essence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are relations to creation or combinations of negation and relation.

After negative attributes, the philosophers introduce relational attributes that describe God's actions towards the world, and composite attributes that combine a negation and a relation. This lesson examines examples like 'Willing' and 'First', explaining how they avoid attributing any real addition to God's essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the relational and composite attributes passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the lists: first relational attributes (maker, originator, etc.), then composite attributes (willing, generous, first).
2
Analyze relational attributes
Attributes like 'maker' (sani'), 'originator' (mubdi'), 'wise' (hakim), etc., are purely relational. They describe God's relation to creation, not something in His essence.
These attributes are relative to us and the world.
3
Analyze composite attributes
Attributes like 'willing' (murid) combine a negation (no aversion) and a relation (source of good). Similarly, 'generous' and 'first' are analyzed as combinations. Understand the structure.
Composite attributes are a hybrid of negation and relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود بذاته لن يتصور إلا واحدا من كل وجه فلا صفة، ولا حال ولا اعتبار ولا حيث ولا وجه لذات واجب الوجود بحيث يكون أحد الوجهين والاعتبارين غير الآخر، أو يدل لفظ على شيء هو غير الآخر بذاته، ولا يجوز أن يكون نوع واجب الوجود لغير ذاته ؛ لأن وجود نوعه له لعينه ، ولا يشاركه شيء ما صفة كانت أو موصوفًا في وجوب الوجود والأزلية، ولا ينقسم هو، ولا يتكثر لا بالكم ولا بالمبادئ المقومة، ولا بأجزاء الحقيقة والحد تعم له صفات سلبية مثل : تقدسه عن الكثرة من كل وجه فيسمى لذلك واحدا حقا أحدًا صمدا ومثل : تنزهه عن المادة وتجرده عن طبيعة الإمكان والعدم، ويسمى لذلك عقلا وواجبا، وله صفات إضافية مثل : كونه صانعا مبدعًا حكيمًا قديرًا جوادًا كريما، وصفات مركبة من سلب وإضافة مثل كونه مريدا ؛ أي: هو مع عقليته ووجوبه بذاته مبدأ لنظام الخير كله، من غير كراهية لما يصدر عنه، وجوادًا ؛ أي : هو بهذه الصفة، وزيادة سلب؛ أي لا ينحو إعراضا لذاته، وأولا؛ أي هو مسلوب عنه الحدوث، مع إضافة وجود الكل إليه وصفاته عندهم إما سلبية
The philosophers said: The necessary existent by essence can only be conceived as one from every aspect. So there is no attribute, no state, no consideration, no direction, no aspect for the essence of the necessary existent such that one of the two aspects and considerations is other than the other, nor does any word indicate something other than the other by essence. It is not permissible that the type of the necessary existent be for other than its essence, because the existence of its type is for its very self, and nothing shares with it, whether attribute or described, in being necessary of existence and eternity. It does not divide, nor does it become multiple, neither by quantity nor by constitutive principles, nor by parts of reality and definition. It has negative attributes such as: His holiness from multiplicity from every aspect, thus He is called One, True, Unique, Eternal (Samad). And like His transcendence from matter and His abstraction from the nature of possibility and non-existence, thus He is called intellect and necessary. And He has relational attributes such as: His being a maker, originator, wise, powerful, generous, bountiful. And composite attributes from negation and addition such as: His being willing, which means: He, along with His intellectuality and necessity by essence, is the source of the entire order of good, without aversion to what emanates from Him. And 'generous' means He is with this attribute, and an addition of negation, i.e., He does not incline to aversion for His essence. And 'first' means: temporality is negated from Him, along with the addition of the existence of all things to Him. And His attributes according to them are either negative...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage outlines the philosophers' (falasifa) concept of God. Since God is the necessary existent by essence, He must be absolutely one from every aspect, hence no real multiplicity of attributes exists. There is no attribute (sifa), state (hal), consideration (i'tibar), direction (hayth), or aspect (wajh) that is distinct from another. Nothing shares in necessary existence and eternity. He is indivisible and not composed of parts or principles. Instead, the attributes we ascribe are of three types: (1) negative attributes (sifat salbiyya) like being one (wahid, ahad, samad), being free from matter (mujarrad), etc. (2) relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) like being creator, wise, etc. (3) composite attributes (sifat murakkaba) which combine negation and relation, e.g., willing (murid) meaning He is the source of all good without aversion; generous meaning with negation of aversion; first meaning negation of temporality with relation of causing existence. The passage ends by stating that attributes are either negative...

Definition of Divinity and the Salaf's Enumeration
Defines divinity and the Salaf's enumeration of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early Muslim scholars defined divinity and enumerated divine attributes.

Core Idea
Divinity is an eternal essence with attributes; the Salaf counted fifteen without distinction.

This lesson introduces the classical definition of divinity (haqiqa al-ilahiyya) as an eternal essence described with attributes. We then see how some early scholars, like Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi, enumerated specific attributes without distinguishing between essential and active ones. This sets the stage for the philosophical perspective in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation. Notice the definition of divinity and the mention of the Salaf.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the terms.
2
Identify key terms
Look for the Arabic term 'حقيقة الإلهية' and the names like 'عبد الله بن سعيد الكلابي'.
These are important for understanding the theological context.
3
Understand the definition
The passage defines divinity as an eternal essence qualified by attributes. Reflect on what this means for early Muslim theology.
4
Note the enumeration
Al-Kullabi counted fifteen attributes without distinguishing between attributes of essence and acts. Why might this distinction matter later?
This sets up the philosophical discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة الإلهية هي أن تكون ذات أزلية موصوفة بتلك الصفات، بعض السلف : قديم بقدم كريم بكرم، جواد بجود، إلى أن عد عبد الله بن سعيد الكلابي خمس عشرة صفة على غير فرق بين صفات الذات وصفات الأفعال. قالت الفلاسفة : واجب الوجود بذاته لن يتصور إلا واحدا من كل وجه فلا صفة، ولا حال ولا اعتبار ولا حيث ولا وجه لذات واجب الوجود بحيث يكون أحد الوجهين والاعتبارين غير الآخر، أو يدل لفظ على شيء هو غير الآخر بذاته، ولا يجوز أن يكون نوع واجب الوجود لغير ذاته ؛ لأن وجود نوعه له لعينه ، ولا يشاركه شيء ما صفة كانت أو موصوفًا في وجوب الوجود والأزلية، ولا ينقسم هو، ولا يتكثر لا بالكم ولا بالمبادئ المقومة، ولا بأجزاء الحقيقة والحد تعم له صفات سلبية مثل : تقدسه عن الكثرة من كل وجه فيسمى لذلك واحدا حقا أحدًا صمدا ومثل : تنزهه عن المادة وتجرده عن طبيعة الإمكان والعدم، ويسمى لذلك عقلا وواجبا، وله صفات إضافية مثل : كونه صانعا مبدعًا حكيمًا قديرًا جوادًا كريما، وصفات مركبة من سلب وإضافة مثل كونه مريدا ؛ أي: هو مع عقليته ووجوبه بذاته مبدأ لنظام الخير كله، من غير كراهية لما يصدر عنه، وجوادًا ؛ أي : هو بهذه الصفة، وزيادة سلب ؛ أي لا ينحو إعراضا لذاته، وأولا؛ أي هو مسلوب عنه الحدوث، مع إضافة وجود الكل إليه وصفاته عندهم إما سلبية محضة، وإما إضافية محضة، وإما مؤلفة من سلب وإضافة، والسلوب والإضافات لا توجب كثرة في الذات.
And the reality of divinity is that it be an eternal essence described by those attributes. Some of the Salaf: eternal by eternity, generous by generosity, bountiful by bounty, until Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Kullabi counted fifteen attributes without distinguishing between attributes of the essence and attributes of acts. The philosophers said: The Necessary of Existence in itself cannot be conceived except as one from every aspect, so no attribute, no state, no consideration, no where, no aspect for the essence of the Necessary of Existence such that one of the two aspects or considerations is other than the other, nor does a word indicate a thing that is other than the other in essence, nor is it permissible for the Necessary of Existence to have a kind besides its essence, because the existence of its kind is for its very self, and nothing shares with it, whether attribute or described, in the necessity of existence and eternality. It does not divide, nor does it multiply, neither by quantity nor by constituent principles, nor by parts of reality and definition. There belong to it negative attributes such as: its transcendence from multiplicity in every respect, so it is called for that One, Real, Unique, Eternal; and such as: its being free from matter and stripped from the nature of possibility and non-existence, so it is called for that intellect and necessary. And it has relational attributes such as: its being Maker, Originator, Wise, Powerful, Bountiful, Generous. And attributes composed of negation and relation such as: its being Willing; meaning: it is, with its intellectuality and necessity in itself, the principle of the entire order of good, without aversion to what proceeds from it; and Generous; meaning: it is with this attribute, plus a negation; meaning: it does not incline to aversion for itself; and First; meaning: it is negated of origination, with the addition of the existence of everything to it. And their attributes are either purely negative, or purely relational, or composed of negation and relation. And negations and relations do not necessitate multiplicity in the essence.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the reality of divinity (haqiqa al-ilahiyya) and then presents the philosophers' (falasifa) view on divine attributes. The definition is that divinity is an eternal essence described with attributes. Some early scholars (salaf) like al-Kullabi enumerate fifteen attributes without distinguishing between essential and active attributes. The philosophers, however, argue that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) must be absolutely one, so it cannot have distinct attributes. Instead, they classify divine descriptions into three categories: purely negative (e.g., being one, eternal), purely relational (e.g., being creator, wise), and composite of negation and relation (e.g., being willing, which adds a relational aspect and a negation of aversion). They emphasize that negations and relations do not imply multiplicity in the essence, preserving absolute unity.

صفات الذات
صفات الأفعال

?

Philosophers' Classification of Divine Attributes
Presents the philosophers' argument for divine unity and their classification of attributes into negative, relational, and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' view that the Necessary Being must be absolutely one and their threefold classification of attributes into negative, relational, and composite.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is one from every aspect; attributes are only negative or relational to preserve unity.

In this lesson, we examine the philosophers' (falāsifa) rigorous monotheism. They argue that the Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd) cannot have distinct attributes because that would imply multiplicity. Instead, they reinterpret divine descriptions as negative or relational, or a combination of both, which do not compromise absolute unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage from 'قالت الفلاسفة' onwards. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the reasoning for absolute unity.
This is a dense philosophical text; read slowly.
2
Identify the three types of attributes
The passage categorizes attributes into purely negative, purely relational, and composite of negation and relation. Find the examples for each.
3
Understand the examples
For negative attributes: One, Unique, Eternal; for relational: Creator, Wise; for composite: Willing, First. Reflect on how each preserves unity.
The composite attributes combine a negation and a relation.
4
Grasp the key conclusion
The philosophers conclude that negations and relations do not necessitate multiplicity in the essence. This is the core of their position.
This distinguishes them from those who affirm real attributes.

The Sifatiyya's Four-Fold Analogy for Divine Attributes
Presents the Sifatiyya's four-fold analogy and their proof for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sifatiyya school uses the analogy of the witnessed to the unseen to prove that God possesses attributes like knowledge, will, and speech.

Core Idea
The Sifatiyya prove divine attributes by showing cause and effect are inseparable.

The Sifatiyya, a theological school that affirms divine attributes, employed a powerful analogy to argue that God must have attributes like knowledge. They reasoned from the witnessed world of creation to the unseen divine realm using four universal principles. This lesson explores their argument, focusing on the cause-attribute relationship that they deemed necessary and reciprocal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sifatiyya's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how they begin by stating the four universals and then focus on the cause.
2
Understand the four universals
Identify the four terms: cause (ʿilla), condition (sharṭ), proof (dalīl), and limit (ḥadd). Consider how each might be used in analogical reasoning.
3
Examine the cause-attribute reciprocity
The passage emphasizes that cause and effect are necessary concomitants. Trace the logic: if a knower is known by knowledge, then knowledge necessitates a knower. Apply this to God.
4
Note the support from will and speech
The argument is reinforced by similar reasoning for will and speech. See how the same principle applies.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the relationship is uniform across causation, even if attributes differ in pre-eternity. Ask why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : ونحن نعتبر الغائب بالشاهد بجوامع أربعة ؛ وهي العلة والشرط والدليل والحد ، أما العلة فنقول : قد ثبت كون العالم عالما شاهدًا معلل بالعلم والعلة العقلية مع معلولها يتلازمان ولا يجوز تقدير واحد منهما دون الآخر ، فلو جاز تقدير العالم عالما دون العلم، لجاز تقدير العلم من غير أن يتصف محله بكونه عالما، فاقتضى الوصف الصفة كاقتضاء الصفة الوصف، فمن ثبت له هذه الصفات وجب وصفه بها كذلك، إذا وجب وصفه بها وجب إثبات الصفة له ، ثم عضدوا كلامهم بالإرادة والكلام، فإنه لما ثبت له الإرادة والكلام كان مريدا متكلما ، فلما ثبت كونه مريداً متكلما وجب له الإرادة والكلام، فإن العلم رسمان لا يختلفان في العلية والمعلولية وإن كانا يختلفان عندهم في القدم والحدوث.
The Attibutists said: And we consider the unseen by the witnessed through four universals: the cause, the condition, the proof, and the limit. As for the cause, we say: It has been established that the existing world's being knowledgeable in the witnessed is caused by knowledge, and the intellectual cause and its effect are necessary concomitants; it is not permissible to posit one without the other. For if it were permissible to posit the world knowledgeable without knowledge, it would be permissible to posit knowledge without its subject being described as knowledgable. Thus the description necessitates the attribute just as the attribute necessitates the description. So whoever has these attributes established, it is necessary to describe him with them accordingly. When it is necessary to describe him with them, it is necessary to affirm the attribute for him. Then they reinforced their speech with will and speech: for when it was established that He has will and speech, He was willing and speaking. Thus when it was established that He is willing and speaking, it became necessary for Him to have will and speech. For knowledge is of two kinds, not differing in causality and effecthood, even though they differ for them in pre-eternity and origination.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the Sifatiyya's (the school that affirms attributes) argument for establishing divine attributes using the method of qiyās al-ghāʾib ʿalā al-shāhid (analogizing the unseen to the witnessed). They employ four universal principles (jawāmiʿ arbaʿa): cause (ʿilla), condition (sharṭ), proof (dalīl), and limit (ḥadd). They focus on the cause: just as in the witnessed world, a knower (ʿālim) is so because of knowledge (ʿilm), and the cause and effect are inseparable, so in the unseen, God, who is described as knowing, must have the attribute of knowledge. The relationship is reciprocal: the description entails the attribute and vice versa. They further support this with the examples of will (irāda) and speech (kalām): if God is described as willing and speaking, then He must have the attributes of will and speech. They conclude that the relationship between attribute and object is the same in both worlds, even though the attributes themselves differ in pre-eternity and temporality.

Key Terms
الغائب بالشاهد
العلة
الشرط
الدليل
الحد
الصفاتية
العلم
الإرادة
الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy is a simple comparison between God and creation without logical grounding.
Correction: The Sifatiyya argument relies on necessary concomitance (talāzum) between cause and effect, which is a rational principle, not a mere likeness.

Checkpoint
What are the four universals (jawāmiʿ) used by the Sifatiyya in their analogy?
A) Essence, attribute, action, and name
B) Cause, condition, proof, and limit
C) Matter, form, agent, and end
D) Substance, accident, body, and soul

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya used the necessary connection between cause and effect in the witnessed world to argue that God's descriptions (knowing, willing, speaking) require the corresponding attributes (knowledge, will, speech).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sifatiyya argued that if God is described as knowing, He must have knowledge. Do you truly believe that God knows you personally and in every detail? If so, how does that certainty change how you live today? Will you act as if you are fully known by Him?
?
If God's knowledge is necessary and perfect, what does it mean for you that He knows every sin you commit and every good deed you hide? Does that awareness affect your actions today? Will you commit to living with the constant awareness of being known by God?

The Mu'tazila Objection to Causal Analogy for Divine Attributes
Mu'tazila argument that causal reasoning applies only to contingent judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila argue that causal reasoning cannot apply to necessary divine attributes.

Core Idea
Causal reasoning only works for contingent things — and God is necessary.

The Mu'tazila raise a sharp objection: if causal reasoning implies a need for a cause, then it can only apply to contingent (jāʾiz) matters, not to necessary (wājib) ones. Since Allah's attributes are necessary, they cannot be caused. Let's examine their argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from the Mu'tazila once, then read the literal translation. Understand the core claim.
Notice the phrase 'تعليل الأحكام بالعلل نوع احتياج إلى العلل'.
2
Identify the two categories
The Mu'tazila distinguish between contingent (jāʾiz) and necessary (wājib) judgments. Only contingent ones need causes.
Contingent: can exist or not exist. Necessary: must be.
3
Examine the examples from the witnessed world
They give examples of necessary judgments that are uncaused: substance occupying space, accidents needing substrates. These are analogous to divine attributes.
These examples show that necessity precludes causal explanation.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since God's being 'knowing' is necessary, it cannot be caused. The Sifatiyya's analogy fails.
The Mu'tazila are not denying the attribute itself, only the causal proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : تعليل الأحكام بالعلل نوع احتياج إلى العلل، وذلك لا يتحقق إلا إذا كان الحكم جائز الوجود وجائز العدم، فيعلل الحكم بجوازه في الشاهد وكون الباري تعالى عالما واجب وهو مقدس عن الاحتياج إلى التعليل، فلا يعلل الحكم لوجوبه في الغائب، أليس كل حكم واجب في الشاهد غير معلل أصلا، مثل تحيز الجوهر وقبوله للعرض، ومثل قيام العرض بالجواهر واحتياجه إلى المحل إلى غير ذلك.
The Mu'tazila said: The causing of judgments by causes is a kind of need for causes, and that is only realized if the judgment is possible in existence and possible in non-existence. So the judgment is caused by its contingency in the witnessed. And the Exalted Creator's being knowing is necessary, and He is sanctified from the need for causation. Thus the judgment is not caused due to its necessity in the unseen. Is not every necessary judgment in the witnessed uncaused fundamentally, such as the occupation of space by substance and its receptivity to accidents, and such as the subsistence of accidents in substances and their need for a substrate, and so on?
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's objection to the Sifatiyya's causal argument. They argue that causal attribution (taʿlīl) is only applicable to contingent (jāʾiz) matters—things that can exist or not exist—because it implies a need (iḥtiyāj) for the cause. In the witnessed world, only contingent judgments are caused. However, God's attribute of being knowing (ʿālim) is necessary (wājib), not contingent, so it cannot be subject to causation. They illustrate with examples of necessary judgments in the witnessed world that have no cause: e.g., a substance's occupation of space (taḥayyuz) and its receptivity to accidents (qubūl li-l-ʿaraḍ), and accidents' subsistence in substances (qiyām al-ʿaraḍ bi-l-jawāhir). These are necessary and uncaused. Similarly, God's necessary attributes need no cause. Thus the Sifatiyya's analogy from the witnessed to the unseen is invalid for necessary attributes.

Key Terms
تعليل
احتياج
جائز الوجود
واجب
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila deny all divine attributes.
Correction: Here they only reject the causal proof for attributes, not the attributes themselves.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila reject causal reasoning (taʿlīl) for divine attributes?
A) Because God cannot have attributes
B) Because causal reasoning applies only to contingent, not necessary, judgments
C) Because the Sifatiyya's analogy is invalid
D) Because attributes are identical to essence

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that only contingent judgments need causes; necessary judgments like God's attributes are uncaused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge of every detail of your life, do you ever subconsciously assume that His knowledge needs a reason or cause—like He knows because you did something? How does this lesson's insistence that God's attributes are necessary and uncaused challenge that assumption? Will you consciously affirm this truth in your next moment of doubt?
?
You often try to 'figure out' God's actions using human logic, as if He operates under the same causal constraints as created things. But this passage shows that applying causal reasoning to God's necessary attributes is a mistake. Where in your life right now are you trying to force God into your cause-and-effect framework instead of submitting to His uncaused, necessary nature? What will you do to let go of that control?
?
If you truly believe that God's being knowing is necessary and not dependent on anything—not on your actions, not on the world—does that change how you approach Him in prayer? Do you pray to inform God or to align yourself with His uncaused wisdom? Starting today, how can your prayers reflect the reality that God's knowledge is self-sufficient and eternal?

The Sifatiyya's Argument from Analogical Categories
Sifatiyya argue for divine attributes via analogy using four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sifatiyya use the analogy of the unseen to the witnessed to argue that divine attributes are necessary, particularly through the category of cause.

Core Idea
Description and attribute necessitate each other — neither can exist without the other.

The Sifatiyya (those who affirm divine attributes) develop a sophisticated argument from analogy to show that God must possess attributes like knowledge, will, and speech. They identify four categories that bridge the gap between the witnessed world and the unseen divine reality. This lesson focuses on their primary category — cause — and its logical implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage aloud
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm of the argument. Then read the translation to grasp the logical flow.
Notice the key terms: 'illa, sharṭ, dalīl, ḥadd.
2
Identify the four categories
The passage begins by listing the four comprehensive categories used in the analogy. Pause to note them: cause, condition, evidence, and definition.
Only the cause is discussed in detail here.
3
Trace the cause argument
Follow the argument step by step: from the witnessed case of a being knowing due to knowledge, to the necessary concomitance, to the impossibility of separation, to the conclusion that description and attribute are mutually necessary.
The key phrase is 'faqtadā al-waṣf al-ṣifa ka-qtidā' al-ṣifa al-waṣf'.
4
Consider the supporting examples
The Sifatiyya reinforce their argument with will and speech: since God is described as willing and speaking, He must have will and speech.
This mirrors the logic of the cause argument.
5
Reflect on the concluding point
The passage ends by noting that knowledge in God and in creation share the same causal relationship, differing only in pre-eternity and temporality. Think about what this implies for the doctrine of attributes.
This avoids anthropomorphism while preserving analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : ونحن نعتبر الغائب بالشاهد بجوامع أربعة ؛ وهي العلة والشرط والدليل والحد ، أما العلة فنقول : قد ثبت كون العالم عالما شاهدًا معلل بالعلم والعلة العقلية مع معلولها يتلازمان ولا يجوز تقدير واحد منهما دون الآخر ، فلو جاز تقدير العالم عالما دون العلم، لجاز تقدير العلم من غير أن يتصف محله بكونه عالما، فاقتضى الوصف الصفة كاقتضاء الصفة الوصف، فمن ثبت له هذه الصفات وجب وصفه بها كذلك، إذا وجب وصفه بها وجب إثبات الصفة له ، ثم عضدوا كلامهم بالإرادة والكلام، فإنه لما ثبت له الإرادة والكلام كان مريدا متكلما ، فلما ثبت كونه مريداً متكلما وجب له الإرادة والكلام، فإن العلم رسمان لا يختلفان في العلية والمعلولية وإن كانا يختلفان عندهم في القدم والحدوث.
The Sifatiyya said: We consider the unseen by analogy with the witnessed through four comprehensive categories: the cause ('illa), the condition (sharṭ), the evidence (dalīl), and the definition (ḥadd). As for the cause, we say: It has been established that the world is 'knowing' (existing [?]) in the witnessed, caused by knowledge, and the intellectual cause and its effect are mutually concomitant, and it is not permissible to assume one without the other. So if it were permissible to assume the world to be knowing without knowledge, then it would be permissible to assume knowledge without its locus having the attribute of knowing. Thus the description necessitates the attribute just as the attribute necessitates the description. So whoever has these attributes must be described by them; and when he must be described by them, it is necessary to affirm the attribute for him. Then they supported their statement by will and speech: for when will and speech are affirmed for Him, He is willing and speaking; and when it is established that He is willing and speaking, will and speech are necessary for Him. For knowledge [in Him and in us] are two descriptions that do not differ in causality and effect, although they differ, in their view, in pre-eternity and temporality.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the Sifatiyya (those who affirm attributes) using the analogy of the unseen (ghā'ib) to the witnessed (shāhid) through four comprehensive categories (jawāmi'): cause ('illa), condition (sharṭ), evidence (dalīl), and definition (ḥadd). The author focuses on the first category: cause. The argument proceeds as follows: In the witnessed world, a being (like a human) is 'knowing' due to knowledge, and the intellectual cause and its effect are necessarily concomitant—neither can exist without the other. If it were possible for the world (a being) to be knowing without knowledge, then it would also be possible for knowledge to exist without its locus being described as knowing. Therefore, the description (being knowing) necessarily implies the attribute (knowledge), just as the attribute implies the description. So for any being that has these attributes (like God), it is necessary to describe Him with them, and consequently to affirm the attributes. The Sifatiyya then support this by the examples of will and speech: since God is described as willing and speaking, He must have will and speech. They conclude that knowledge in the created and in God are two instances that are identical in the relationship of causality and effect, though they differ in pre-eternity (qidam) and temporality (ḥudūth).

Key Terms
الغائب بالشاهد
العلة
الشرط
الدليل
الحد
التلازم
الصفة
الوصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sifatiyya are claiming that God's attributes are created like human attributes, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: The Sifatiyya explicitly distinguish between created and divine attributes in terms of pre-eternity and temporality. They only argue that the logical relationship (causality) between description and attribute is the same, not the nature of the attributes themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya, what is the relationship between the description (being knowing) and the attribute (knowledge)?
A) The description is independent of the attribute
B) The description necessitates the attribute and vice versa
C) The attribute is merely a name without reality
D) The description is prior to the attribute

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya's argument from cause establishes that any being described by an attribute must possess that attribute, and this logic applies to God without conflating created and uncreated attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that describing God without affirming His attributes is logically impossible. Where in your own heart have you been content with a vague or distant concept of God, avoiding the weight of His specific attributes? What would it look like for you to take even one of His names and truly live as though it is real?
?
The Sifatiyya used the witness of creation to reason about the unseen. Are you actively looking at the world around you—your own desires, fears, and experiences—as signs that point to God's attributes? Or have you been treating creation as merely backdrop? What would change if you saw every moment as a proof?
?
If God's attributes are necessarily connected to His actions and descriptions, then every time you call upon Him as 'The All-Knowing' or 'The All-Wise', you are affirming a real quality in Him. Does your prayer reflect that belief? Will you commit to pausing before each of His names and letting it reshape your worship today?

The Mu'tazila Objection to Causation in God and the Sifatiyya Rejoinder
Mu'tazila objection and Sifatiyya reply on causation of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God cannot be caused and the Sifatiyya's distinction between efficient causation and logical entailment.

Core Idea
Divine causation is logical entailment, not temporal production.

This passage presents a classic Kalam debate on causation. The Mu'tazila object that if God's attributes are caused, they would imply need, contradicting His necessity. The Sifatiyya respond by redefining causation as intellectual entailment, thus preserving divine simplicity while maintaining real relations among divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Identify the core argument: causation implies need, so necessary beings cannot be caused. Note their examples from the witnessed world.
They make an exception for God's will, which they consider contingent.
2
Read the Sifatiyya rejoinder
Notice how they redefine 'ta'līl' as intellectual entailment rather than efficient causation. They argue that necessity does not prevent one necessary from entailing another.
3
Understand the further verification
The Sifatiyya add a point about 'aḥwāl' (states): judgments are not existent things that can be called possible or necessary, so the Mu'tazila's objection is misplaced.
This section ends mid-sentence; the additional argument may continue in later lessons.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider the difference between efficient causation (ījād) and logical entailment (iqtiḍā' 'aqlī). Why does this distinction allow the Sifatiyya to defend their position?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : تعليل الأحكام بالعلل نوع احتياج إلى العلل، وذلك لا يتحقق إلا إذا كان الحكم جائز الوجود وجائز العدم، فيعلل الحكم بجوازه في الشاهد وكون الباري تعالى عالما واجب وهو مقدس عن الاحتياج إلى التعليل، فلا يعلل الحكم لوجوبه في الغائب، أليس كل حكم واجب في الشاهد غير معلل أصلا، مثل تحيز الجوهر وقبوله للعرض، ومثل قيام العرض بالجواهر واحتياجه إلى المحل إلى غير ذلك، وخرجوا عن هذه القاعدة كونه مريدًا ، فإنه لما لم يكن واجب كان معللا، وهذا كله لأن الواجب يستقل بوجوبه عن الافتقار إلى العلة، وإنما الجائز لما لم يستقل بنفسه ؛ أعني أحد طرفي جوازه احتاج إلى علة، إما اختيار مختار وإما إيجاب علة، فوجود العلم في العالم شاهدًا لما كان جائزا احتاج إلى اختيار مختار يوجده، وثبوت حكم العلة في الشاهد لما كان جائزا احتاج إلى علة توجبه وهي العلم، أليس وجود القديم لما كان واجبًا لم يعلل ووجود الحادث لما كان جائزا علل ؟ قالت الصفاتية : يم تنكرون على من يعلل الأحكام الجائزة بالعلل الجائزة، والأحكام الواجبة بالعلل الواجبة؟ فلا الاحتياج والافتقار غير موجب للتعليل، ولا الاستقلال، ولا الاستغناء مانع من التعليل ؛ لأنا لسنا نعني بالتعليل الإيجاد والإبداع حتى يستدعيه الجواز والاحتياج ويمنعه الوجوب والاستغناء، لكنا نعني بالتعليل الاقتضاء العقلي والتلازم الحقيقي، بشرط أن يكون أحدهما ملتزما والآخر ملتزماً، والوجوب والجواز لا أثر لهما في منع الاقتضاء والتلازم، فلا عقلا يمتنع تعليل الواجب بالواجب، وتعليل الجائز بالجائز، وزادوا على ذلك تحقيقا. فقالوا : نحن لا نحكم على الأحكام من حيث إنها أحكام بأنها جائزة، فإنها على مذهب مثبتي الأحوال صفات لا توصف بالوجود والعدم، ولا ذات لها على جائزة أو واجبة وعلى مذهب النفاة عبارات عن الانفراد حتى يقال : اختصاصات المعاني بالمحال، بحيث يعبر عنها بالأسامي، وهي على المذهبيين لا
The Mu'tazila said: Causing judgments by causes is a kind of need for causes, and that is only realized when the judgment is possible of existence and possible of non-existence. So the judgment is caused by its possibility in the witnessed, while the existence of the Exalted Creator as knowing is necessary, and He is sanctified above need for causation; therefore the judgment is not caused due to its necessity in the unseen. Is it not that every necessary judgment in the witnessed is uncaused originally, such as the occupying of space by a substance and its receptivity to accidents, and such as the subsistence of accidents in substances and their need for a locus, etc.? And they excepted from this rule His being willing: for since it was not necessary, it was caused. All this is because the necessary is independent in its necessity from needing a cause, while the possible, because it is not independent in itself—i.e., one of its two possible states—needs a cause, either the choice of a chooser or the necessitation of a cause. So the existence of knowledge in the world, in the witnessed, since it was possible, needed the choice of a chooser to bring it into existence; and the establishment of the effect of the cause in the witnessed, since it was possible, needed a cause to necessitate it, which is knowledge. Is it not that the existence of the pre-eternal, since it was necessary, was not caused, and the existence of the temporal, since it was possible, was caused? The Sifatiyya said: Why do you deny one who causes possible judgments by possible causes, and necessary judgments by necessary causes? For need and dependency do not necessitate causation, nor does independence and self-sufficiency prevent causation; because we do not mean by causation (ta'līl) bringing into existence (ījād) and origination (ibdā') such that possibility and need demand it and necessity and self-sufficiency prevent it; rather, we mean by causation intellectual entailment (iqtiḍā' 'aqlī) and true concomitance (talāzum ḥaqīqī), on condition that one is the concomitant and the other is the concomitant of it. Necessity and possibility have no effect in preventing entailment and concomitance. So intellectually it is not impossible to cause the necessary by the necessary, and to cause the possible by the possible. And they added a further verification: they said: We do not judge judgments insofar as they are judgments as being possible (jā'iza), for, according to the school of the affirmers of states (aḥwāl), they are attributes that are not described by existence or non-existence, and they have no essence such that they are possible or necessary; and according to the school of the deniers [of states], they are expressions of individuation—so that it is said: particularizations (ikhtiṣāṣāt) of meanings to loci, by which they are expressed by names—and according to both schools, they are not...
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage contains the Mu'tazila's objection to the Sifatiyya's use of causation ('illa) for God, and the Sifatiyya's detailed rebuttal. The Mu'tazila argue that causation implies need, and need cannot apply to God, who is necessary in existence. In the witnessed world, only contingent things (possible) need causes; necessary things do not. They give examples: the spatiality of a substance and its acceptance of accidents are necessary concomitants without cause. They make an exception for God's will, which they consider contingent (since it pertains to creation). Their principle: the necessary is independent, the possible needs a cause. They apply this to knowledge: created knowledge is possible, so it needs a cause; but divine knowledge, being necessary, cannot be caused. The Sifatiyya respond by clarifying what they mean by 'ta'līl'. They do not mean efficient causation (ījād) which implies need, but rather logical entailment ('iqtiḍā' 'aqlī) and real concomitance (talāzum ḥaqīqī). In this sense, necessity does not prevent one necessary from entailing another necessary; similarly, possibility does not prevent one possible from entailing another possible. They then add a deeper point: the judgments (aḥkām) themselves, according to the theory of 'states' (aḥwāl) held by some Ash'arites, are attributes that are not described as existent or non-existent; they are not independent entities to be classified as possible or necessary. Even for the deniers of states, judgments are merely expressions of particularizations. So the Mu'tazila's objection based on necessity vs. possibility does not apply. The passage ends mid-sentence on page 181.

Key Terms
التعليل
العلة
الجائز
الواجب
الاقتضاء العقلي
التلازم الحقيقي
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sifatiyya believe God's attributes are created or temporally produced.
Correction: The Sifatiyya explicitly reject efficient causation for divine attributes; they mean logical entailment, where an attribute necessarily implies another without temporal production.

Checkpoint
What do the Sifatiyya mean by 'ta'līl' (causation) in this context?
A) Bringing into existence in time
B) Logical entailment and real concomitance
C) Creating out of nothing
D) Making something necessary

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya defend the causation of divine attributes by redefining 'ta'līl' as logical entailment, not efficient production, thereby sidestepping the Mu'tazila's objection based on need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is Knowing,' do you secretly think of His knowledge as something that could have been otherwise, like a quality that was 'added' to Him? How does the Sifatiyya's redefinition of causation as logical entailment challenge your underlying assumptions about God's attributes? What would change in your worship if you truly believed His knowledge is not a created addition but an intrinsic entailment of His essence?
?
The Mu‘tazila argued that needing a cause implies imperfection. Are there areas in your life where you resist acknowledging your own dependence on causes—like avoiding asking for help because it feels like weakness? How does the Sifatiyya's distinction between efficient causation and logical entailment help you see that dependency does not always equal deficiency? Will you today admit one need you have been hiding out of false pride?
?
This lesson shows that the Sifatiyya use logical reasoning to defend a deeper theological truth. Is your faith protected by such intellectual conviction, or is it fragile because you never thought through these distinctions? If your heart were to be questioned about the nature of God's attributes, would it answer with the precision of this argument or with vague emotion? The Sifatiyya on Judgments as Necessary Relative to Causes
The Sifatiyya argue judgments are necessary relative to their causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sifatiyya's argument that judgments (aḥkām) are not contingent as such but are necessary relative to their causes, and how this applies to the possession of attributes.

Core Idea
Judgments are necessary relative to their causes; contingency belongs to the subject.

The Sifatiyya deepen their analysis of divine attributes by examining the nature of judgments themselves. They argue that once a cause (like knowledge) is present, the resulting judgment (like being knowing) is necessary, not contingent. This passage explains how both affirmers and deniers of states agree that judgments are not inherently contingent, and clarifies where contingency actually lies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the Sifatiyya state their position, then contrast two views, then give an example.
The passage contains technical terms like al-aḥkām, al-ḥāl, al-ʿilal.
2
Identify the two doctrines
Note how the Sifatiyya reference the affirmers of states (muthbiti al-aḥwāl) and the deniers (al-nufāh). They show that both groups agree that judgments themselves are not described as contingent or necessary.
The affirmers see judgments as attributes without essence; deniers see them as expressions of particularizations.
3
Understand the example
Focus on the example: the world's being knowing (kawn al-ʿālim ʿāliman). Why is it not contingent? Because it is the necessary consequence of knowledge existing in the subject.
The judgment itself has no essence; only the subject is contingent.
4
Apply the insight
Reflect on how this argument distinguishes the attribute from the subject. The Sifatiyya are clarifying that necessity attaches to the judgment relative to its cause, not to the subject.
This avoids the problem of making attributes contingent like created things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزادوا على ذلك تحقيقا. فقالوا: نحن لا نحكم على الأحكام من حيث إنها أحكام بأنها جائزة، فإنها على مذهب مثبتي الأحوال صفات لا توصف بالوجود والعدم، ولا ذات لها على جائزة أو واجبة، وعلى مذهب النفاة عبارات عن الانفراد [؟] اختصاصات المعاني بالمحال، بحيث يعبر عنها بالأسامي، وهي على المذهبيين لا يطلق عليها الجواز والوجوب من حيث هي أحكام، بل الوجوب إليها أقرب فإنها بالنسبة إلى عللها واجبة سواء قدرت قديمة أو حادثة، فكون العالم عالما بعد قيام العلم بالمحل ليس بجائز، بل واجب؛ لأنه من حيث هو حكم العلم لا ذات له فلا يتطرق إليه الجواز والإمكان، ومن حيث هو ذو العلم يتطرق إلى ذي العلم الجواز
And they added an investigation to that. They said: We do not judge the rulings insofar as they are rulings as being contingent. For, according to the doctrine of the affirmers of states, they are attributes that are not described by existence or non-existence, and they have no essence as being contingent or necessary. And according to the doctrine of the deniers, they are expressions of singularity [?] particularizations of meanings to loci, such that they are expressed by names. And according to both doctrines, contingency and necessity are not applied to them insofar as they are rulings; rather, necessity is closer to them, for relative to their causes they are necessary, whether they are considered eternal or temporal. So the fact that the world is knowing after the establishment of knowledge in the locus is not contingent, but necessary. Because, insofar as it is the ruling of knowledge, it has no essence, so contingency and possibility do not apply to it; and insofar as it is the possessor of knowledge, contingency applies to the possessor of knowledge.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
In this passage, the Sifatiyya deepen their analysis by examining the nature of attributes or 'rulings' (al-aḥkām) themselves. They argue that judgments are not contingent or necessary as such, but rather are necessary relative to their causes. They note that according to both the affirmers of states (muthbiti al-aḥwāl) and the deniers (al-nufāh), the judgments themselves are not described as contingent. The affirmers consider them attributes without essence; the deniers see them as expressions of particularizations. In any case, once the cause is present, the judgment is necessary. For example, the world's being knowing (kawn al-ʿālim ʿāliman) is not contingent because it is the necessary consequence of knowledge existing in the subject. The judgment itself has no essence and thus no contingency; only the subject (dhū al-ʿilm) is described as contingent. Thus, the Sifatiyya establish that it is not the judgment but the subject that requires a cause (in the sense of specification), while the judgment is necessitated by its cause.

Key Terms
الأحكام (al-aḥkām)
الحال (al-ḥāl)
العلل (al-ʿilal)
الجواز (al-jawāz)
الوجوب (al-wujūb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sifatiyya deny any contingency in attributes, making them necessary like God.
Correction: They actually affirm contingency for the subject (e.g., the world) but say the judgment/attribute itself is necessary relative to its cause.

Checkpoint
According to the Sifatiyya, why is the world's being knowing (al-ʿālim) not contingent?
A) Because the world is eternal
B) Because the ruling of knowledge has no essence and is necessary relative to its cause
C) Because knowledge is contingent
D) Because the world is necessary

Key Takeaway
Judgments are necessary relative to their causes; contingency belongs only to the subject, not to the attribute itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah's attribute of knowledge, do you subconsciously think of it as something separate from His essence, like a created quality? How does the Sifatiyya's insistence that judgments are necessary relative to their causes—not independent—force you to reconsider your understanding of Allah's attributes? Commit to studying the relational nature of attributes so that you never fall into polytheism by mentally separating them from His essence.
?
You are the contingent subject (dhū al-ḥukm) in this analysis. Every attribute you have is merely an accident, necessitated by causes that are not under your ultimate control. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge your contingent dependency on Allah? Let this lesson strip away any illusion of autonomy and drive you to perform every action with humble reliance on Him.
?
The Sifatiyya shows that even the attribute of 'knowing' in the world is necessary when knowledge is present. How often do you treat your own knowledge as a personal possession rather than a temporary state granted by Allah? In your next study session, consciously remind yourself that your understanding is a judgment necessitated by the cause of Allah's guidance—may that realization increase your gratitude and fear of losing it.

Does Knowledge Necessitate an Additional Judgment?
Poses a dilemma about whether knowledge necessitates an additional thing, and if so, what it is

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars argue that knowledge does not produce a separate real attribute or judgment.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not a cause that creates an extra thing.

This passage tackles a subtle problem: if Allah knows something, does that knowing produce a separate 'judgment' or attribute? The author denies this, using a crisp logical dilemma. Before we explore the dilemma, note that the author is building on a prior discussion about the concept of 'state' (ḥāl), which he references at the end.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice that the author begins with a strong denial: knowledge does not necessitate an extra judgment.
2
Identify the central claim
The author's main claim is that knowledge does not necessitate a judgment additional to itself. This means that 'being knowing' is not a separate entity caused by knowledge.
3
Follow the dilemma
The author poses a conditional question: if knowledge did necessitate something, would it necessitate itself or something else? Trace each branch and its consequence.
Branch one leads to sophism; branch two leads to further options.
4
Understand the options for 'something else'
If knowledge necessitates something else, that thing could be an existent, a state (ḥāl), or a mode that is neither existent nor non-existent. The author says the discussion is clear and refers to his earlier treatment.
This shows the author's view that knowledge's effect is merely a state, not a real entity.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author uses the term 'sophism' for self-necessitation. Think about how this argument defends the simplicity of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس العلم يوجب حكماً زائداً على نفسه، فيقال أي شيء فاعله أهي مقتضية شيئًا أم غير مقتضية؟ وإن اقتضت أهي تقتضي نفسها أم غيرها؟ فإن اقتضت نفسها فذلك سفسطة، وإن اقتضت غيرها أفهو موجود أو حال أو جهة لا توصف بالوجود والعدم؟ والكلام على الأمرين ظاهر فقد تكلمنا على ذلك في مسألة الحال بما فيه مقنع
And knowledge does not necessitate a judgment additional to itself. So it is said: what is its agent? Does it necessitate something or not? And if it necessitates, does it necessitate itself or something else? For if it necessitates itself, that is sophism. And if it necessitates something else, is that thing existent or a state (ḥāl) or a mode that is not described by existence and non-existence? The discussion of the two matters is clear, and we have spoken about that in the question of the state (al-ḥāl) sufficiently.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage examines whether knowledge (العلم) itself produces an additional judgment beyond itself. The author begins by stating that knowledge does not necessitate an extra judgment (حكم زائد) – it is not like a cause that produces a distinct effect. Then he frames a dilemma: if we ask what the 'agent' (فاعل) of knowledge is, or more precisely, whether knowledge (as a power) actually necessitates something, we face two cases. If it necessitates itself, that is absurd (sophism). If it necessitates something else, then that something must be either an existent entity, or a state (حال), or a mode that is neither existent nor non-existent. The author says the discussion of these two alternatives is clear (i.e., already settled) and refers to his earlier treatment in the question of the state (مسألة الحال) for a sufficient answer. This passage is a brief argument that knowledge does not produce a new real attribute; rather, the 'being knowing' is a state that is not an additional existent.

Key Terms
العلم
الحكم الزائد
المقتضية
السفسطة
الحال
الموجود
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if knowledge causes the world to be knowing, it must produce a separate entity or attribute.
Correction: The author argues that knowledge's effect is merely a state (ḥāl), not an additional real entity. If it produced itself, that would be sophism.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens if we say knowledge necessitates itself?
A) That is sophism.
B) That is the correct view.
C) That would require an infinite regress.
D) That is the same as saying it necessitates a state.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that knowledge does not produce a real additional attribute; if it did, it would lead to absurdity, and the only coherent outcome is that it is a state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's knowledge, do you unconsciously imagine it as a separate 'thing' from His essence? How might that distance you from truly knowing Him?
?
The author calls the view that knowledge necessitates itself 'sophism'. Have you ever worshipped a mental concept of God that you constructed instead of the real Allah? What would it take for you to surrender that construct?
?
If knowledge does not produce an extra attribute, then your shortcomings do not 'add' anything to Allah. How does that reshape your understanding of His immutability and your need for His mercy?

Refuting the Analogy Between Divine and Human Attributes
Objection and answer: the correlation of cause and effect in the witnessed does not apply to the unseen

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the correlation between cause and effect in human attributes cannot be applied to God.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are only similar in name, not in reality.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection from those who claim that the same causal relationship between knowledge and being-knowing in humans must hold for God. By showing that the two cases are fundamentally different, he defends the uniqueness of divine attributes. Let's see how he dismantles the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand the objection and the response.
Notice the technical terms: الطرد والعكس, الشاهد والغائب, العالمية.
2
Identify the opponent's demand
The opponent insists that if knowledge is the cause of being-knowing in the witnessed, then by correlation (طرد وعكس) the same must hold in the unseen.
The opponent tries to force an analogy between human and divine attributes.
3
Examine the author's answer
The author responds that correlation applies only when the two judgments are similar in every respect. Here, the opponent does not admit such similarity.
The key phrase: 'بل لا تماثل بينهما إلا في اسم مجرد' – only similar in a mere name.
4
Understand the reasoning
The author gives two reasons: (1) two instances of knowledge are similar only if both relate to the same object, and (2) God's knowledge is infinite (relates to unlimited objects) while human knowledge is finite.
Thus the attributes are different in reality, not just in degree.
5
Conclude the lesson
The need for a cause in the witnessed does not imply the same need in the unseen. Analogical reasoning (qiyas) from creation to Creator in matters of causation is invalid.
This safeguards divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقول: ها هنا إن سلم مسلم كون العلم علة العالمية في الشاهد، وألزم الطرد والعكس حتى إذا ثبت العلم وجب كون المحل عالما، وإذا ثبت كون المحل عالما لزم وجود العلم. أجاب : بأن الطرد والعكس شاهدًا وغائبًا إنما يلزم بعد تماثل الحكمين من كل وجه لا من وجه دون وجه والخصم ليس يسلم تماثل الحكمين - أعني : عالمية الباري تعالى وعالمية العبد بل لا تماثل بينهما إلا في اسم مجرد، وذلك أن العلمين إنما يتماثلان إذا تعلقا بمعلوم واحد والعالميتان كذلك ، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أن عالمية الغائب وعالمية الشاهد لا يتماثلان من كل وجه، بل هما مختلفان من كل وجه، فكيف يلزم الطرد والعكس والإلحاق والجمع، أليس لو ألزم طرد حكم للعالمية في الغائب من تعلقها بمعلومات لا تتناهي، وحكم القادرية في الغائب من صلاحية الإيجاد والتعلق بالمقدورات التي لا تتناهى إلى غاية حتى يحكم على ما في الشاهد بذلك لم يلزم، فلذلك احتياج العالمية في الشاهد إلى علة لا يستدعي طرده في الغائب، فإذا لا تعويل على الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب بطريق العلة
And we say: Here, if someone concedes that knowledge is the cause of being-knowing in the witnessed world, and then demands correlation and reciprocity (al-ṭard wa al-ʿaks) so that if knowledge is established, it is necessary that the subject be knowing, and if the subject is established to be knowing, the existence of knowledge is necessary. He answers: That correlation and reciprocity in the witnessed and the unseen only follow after the two judgments are similar in every respect, not in some respect without another. And the opponent does not concede the similarity of the two judgments – I mean: the being-knowing of the Exalted Creator and the being-knowing of the servant. Rather, there is no similarity between them except in a mere name. And that is because two knowledges are only similar if they are related to one piece of knowledge, and likewise the two being-knowings. And it is known without doubt that the being-knowing of the unseen and the being-knowing of the witnessed are not similar in every respect; rather they are different in every respect. So how can correlation, reciprocity, analogy (ilḥāq), and conjunction (jamʿ) be required? Is it not that if one were to require a judgment of being-knowing in the unseen based on its relation to infinite objects of knowledge, and a judgment of being-able (al-qādariyya) in the unseen based on its fitness for origination and relation to infinite measures, so that one could judge that which is in the witnessed by that, it would not be required? Therefore, the need of being-knowing in the witnessed for a cause does not necessitate its application in the unseen. So there is no reliance on the conjunction between the witnessed and the unseen by way of causation.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection (posed by an opponent) runs as follows: If one admits that in the witnessed world (the human realm), knowledge is the cause of being-knowing (العالمية), then by the principle of 'correlation and reciprocity' (الطرد والعكس), the same causal relationship must hold in the unseen (the divine realm). That is, if knowledge is present in God, then He must be knowing; if He is knowing, knowledge must be present. The opponent tries to force an analogy between the human and divine cases. The author's answer is that such correlation and analogical inference (طرد وعكس) only apply when the two judgments (here, the being-knowing of God and the being-knowing of a human) are identical in every respect. However, the opponent does not admit that they are identical; indeed, they are only similar in name, not in reality. The author gives two reasons: first, two instances of knowledge are only similar if they relate to the same object of knowledge; second, God's knowledge encompasses infinite objects, while human knowledge is limited. Therefore, the attributes are essentially different. Hence, the need for a cause for human being-knowing does not imply the same need in God. The argument concludes that analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen in matters of causation is invalid.

Key Terms
الطرد والعكس
الشاهد والغائب
العالمية
القادرية
العلة
تماثل الحكمين
الاسم المجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine attributes like knowledge and power are exactly like human attributes because they share the same names.
Correction: The author clarifies that they are only similar in name (اسم مجرد), not in reality. God's knowledge is infinite and related to all things, while human knowledge is limited. Therefore, analogical reasoning from human to divine attributes is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the correlation (طرد وعكس) between knowledge and being-knowing in the witnessed does not apply to the unseen?
A) Because the two 'being-knowings' are similar only in a mere name, not in every respect.
B) Because knowledge in the unseen has no effect.
C) Because the unseen is not known by reason.
D) Because the opponent rejects the principle of causation entirely.

Key Takeaway
The correlation between cause and effect in human attributes does not apply to God because divine attributes are fundamentally different, sharing only a name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah knows' or 'Allah is powerful,' do you ever imagine His knowledge and power as just bigger versions of yours? How does this lesson challenge that tendency, and what will you do to train your heart to think of His attributes as utterly different?
?
This passage shows that even a shared name can fool us into false comparisons. Think of a time when you described God using a human-like quality (e.g., 'God is angry' as we are angry). Are you willing to examine your daily language about God and root out such subtle anthropomorphism? What practical step will you take today?
?
The author insists that God's being-knowing is not caused by anything, while yours is. Does your soul truly rest in the uncaused, self-sufficient nature of God's attributes, or do you still look for reasons why He should act a certain way? How can you deepen your trust in a God whose attributes are beyond human measure?

Refutation of the Condition Analogy Between Witnessed and Absent
Shahrastani refutes the argument from condition (shart) by showing that specific conditions in the witnessed are not transferable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from condition (shart) that applies in the witnessed world does not necessarily transfer to God (the absent).

Core Idea
Conditions in the witnessed world depend on additional factors not present in God.

The Ṣifātiyya argue that if being living is a condition for being knowing in humans, then it must also be a condition for God's knowledge. But this analogy overlooks the specific conditions required for knowledge in creatures, such as bodily structure and absence of opposites. Shahrastani refutes this by showing that these additional conditions are not applicable to God, thereby blocking the transfer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the dialectical structure: first the objection, then the response.
2
Identify the Ṣifātiyya's argument
Find the claim that the condition (shart) must be co-extensive in the witnessed and absent. Notice how they use the example of life as a condition for knowledge.
3
Analyze the author's response
Shahrastani responds by listing additional conditions required for knowledge in the witnessed: bodily structure and absence of opposites. Understand why these do not apply to God.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Realize that the condition argument fails because the specific conditions in the witnessed are not present in the absent. The flexibility of conditions (one meaning for many, many for one) further undermines the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب الشرط والمشروط قالت الصفاتية: ألستم وافقتمونا على أن الشرط وجب طرده شاهدا وغائبا، فإن كون العالم عالما كان مشروطًا بكونه حيا في الشاهد وجب طرده في الغائب، حتى إذا ثبت كونه حيا بهذا الطريق كذلك في العلم، وأنتم ما فرقتم في الشرط بين الجائز والواجب لذلك يلزمكم في العلم أن لا تفرقوا في العلة بين الجائز والواجب، وهذا لازم على المعتزلة غير أن لهم ولغيرهم طريقاً آخر في إثبات كونه تعالى حيا بدون الشرط، فإن الحياة بمجردها لم تكن شرطا في الشاهد ما لم ينضم إليها شرط آخر، فإن البنية على أصلهم شرط في الشاهد، ثم لم يجب طرده في الغائب، وانتفاء الأضداد شرط حتى يتحقق العلم. ويجوز أن يكون المعنى الواحد شرطًا لمعان كثيرة، ويجوز أن تكون شروط كثيرة لمعنى واحد وبهذا يتحقق التمايز بين الشرط والعلة فلا يلزم الشرط على القوم
And from the combination of the witnessed and the absent regarding condition (al-sharṭ) and the conditioned (al-mashrūṭ). The Ṣifātiyya said: “Did you not agree with us that the condition must be co-extensive (ṭard) in the witnessed and the absent? For the being of the world as knowing was conditioned by its being living in the witnessed; its co-extensiveness must apply in the absent, such that when His being living is established by this path, similarly in knowledge. And you did not differentiate in the condition between the contingent (al-jā'iz) and the necessary (al-wājib). Therefore, it is necessary for you in knowledge not to differentiate in the cause (al-ʿilla) between the contingent and the necessary. This is necessary for the Muʿtazila, except that they and others have another path for proving that He, exalted, is living without the condition. For life by itself was not a condition in the witnessed unless another condition was joined to it. For the bodily structure (al-bunya) in their view is a condition in the witnessed, yet its co-extensiveness was not required in the absent. And the absence of opposites (intifā' al-aḍdād) is a condition until knowledge is verified. And it is possible that one meaning is a condition for many meanings, and it is possible that many conditions are for one meaning, and by this the distinction between condition and cause is verified. So the condition does not apply necessarily against the people.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Ṣifātiyya (those who affirm divine attributes) and the author's response. The Ṣifātiyya argue that if we accept that a condition (sharṭ) must be co-extensive, then, since being living is a condition for being knowing in the witnessed, it must also be a condition for God's knowledge in the absent. They claim that the Muʿtazila, who differentiate between contingent and necessary in conditions, are inconsistent if they do not also differentiate in causes. The author responds by pointing out that life alone is not a sufficient condition in the witnessed; it requires additional conditions such as bodily structure (al-bunya) and absence of opposites (intifā' al-aḍdād). These conditions do not apply to God, so the condition is not automatically transferable. Furthermore, the author notes that a single condition can apply to multiple things, and multiple conditions can apply to a single thing; this shows the flexibility of conditions. Thus, the condition argument does not force the opponent to accept the transfer of conditions from the witnessed to the absent.

Key Terms
شرط
مشروط
صفاتية
بنية
انتفاء الأضداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conditions that apply in the witnessed world must apply equally to God.
Correction: The author shows that the condition of life for knowledge requires additional conditions (bodily structure, absence of opposites) that do not apply to God, so the analogy fails.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Ṣifātiyya's argument that the condition of being living for knowledge must apply to God as it does to humans?
A) Because God's knowledge is not conditioned by life.
B) Because the condition in the witnessed requires additional factors like bodily structure and absence of opposites, which do not apply to God.
C) Because the Ṣifātiyya did not prove that life is a condition.
D) Because the author denies that God is living.

Key Takeaway
The condition of life for knowledge is not transferable to God because it depends on extra conditions like bodily structure and absence of opposites, which are not present in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own knowledge—dependent on a living body, a functioning brain, and the absence of contradictions—do you ever subconsciously limit God's knowledge by imagining it must also depend on such conditions? How would this lesson correct that tendency?
?
The Ṣifātiyya insisted that conditions must be co-extensive. In your own life, where have you assumed that what is true for you must also be true for God, especially regarding how He relates to His creation? Will you actively remind yourself of this distinction in your next moment of worship?
?
This passage shows that conditions are not causes and that many conditions can serve one meaning. Are there areas in your faith where you have mistaken a human condition (e.g., needing proof or effort) for a divine requirement? What would change if you truly internalized that God is free from such constraints?
