Refuting the Uniform Condition Argument
The author refutes the Sifatiyya claim that conditions must be uniform in the witnessed and unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sifatiyya's argument for uniform conditions between the witnessed and unseen fails, and how the author defends the Mu'tazila position by showing that additional conditions in the witnessed are not transferable to God.

Core Idea
Conditions in the witnessed world are not simply transferable to God.

The Sifatiyya (attribute-affirmers) argue that conditions must apply uniformly in both the witnessed and the unseen. They claim that since life is a condition for knowledge in the witnessed, it must also be a condition for God's knowledge. The author refutes this by pointing out that life alone is not sufficient; other conditions like bodily structure and absence of opposites are needed, and these do not apply to God. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: the Sifatiyya's claim, then the author's rebuttal.
2
Identify the Sifatiyya's claim
Find where the Sifatiyya state that the condition must be applied uniformly in the witnessed and unseen. What example do they give?
3
Understand the author's counter-argument
The author says life is not sufficient in the witnessed; other conditions are needed. Identify these additional conditions (bodily structure, absence of opposites).
These additional conditions are not transferable to God.
4
Grasp the distinction between condition and cause
The author concludes that one condition can serve many meanings and many conditions can serve one meaning. How does this distinction undermine the Sifatiyya's argument?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الجمع بين الشاهد والغائب الشرط والمشروط قالت الصفاتية : ألستم وافقتمونا على أن الشرط وجب طرده شاهدا وغائبا، فإن كون العالم عالما كان مشروطًا بكونه حيا في الشاهد وجب طرده في الغائب، حتى إذا ثبت كونه حيا بهذا الطريق كذلك في العلم، وأنتم ما فرقتم في الشرط بين الجائز والواجب لذلك يلزمكم في العلم أن لا تفرقوا في العلة بين الجائز والواجب، وهذا لازم على المعتزلة غير أن لهم ولغيرهم طريقاً آخر في إثبات كونه تعالى حيا بدون الشرط، فإن الحياة بمجردها لم تكن شرطا في الشاهد ما لم ينضم إليها شرط آخر، فإن البنية على أصلهم شرط في الشاهد، ثم لم يجب طرده في الغائب، وانتفاء الأضداد شرط حتى يتحقق العلم. ويجوز أن يكون المعنى الواحد شرطا لمعان كثيرة، ويجوز أن تكون شروط كثيرة لمعنى واحد وبهذا يتحقق التمايز بين الشرط والعلة فلا يلزم الشرط على القوم
And from the comparison between the witnessed and the unseen: the condition and the conditioned. The Sifatiyya said: 'Do you not agree with us that the condition must be applied uniformly in the witnessed and the unseen? For the thing being knowledgeable is conditioned by its being alive in the witnessed, so it must be applied uniformly in the unseen, so that if His being alive is established by this method, likewise in knowledge. And you did not distinguish in the condition between the possible and the necessary; therefore it is incumbent upon you in knowledge not to distinguish in the cause between the possible and the necessary.' This is binding upon the Mu'tazila, except that they and others have another way of establishing that He, exalted, is alive without the condition. For life alone was not a condition in the witnessed unless another condition is joined to it: bodily structure, according to their principle, is a condition in the witnessed, yet it is not necessary to apply it uniformly in the unseen; and the absence of opposites is a condition until knowledge is realized. And it is permissible that a single meaning be a condition for many meanings, and it is permissible that many conditions be for a single meaning, and by this the distinction between the condition and the cause is realized, so the condition does not apply to the [opposing] group.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument by the Sifatiyya (those who affirm attributes) concerning the uniformity of conditions (shart) between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). The Sifatiyya argue: 'You (the Mu'tazila) agree that a condition must be applied uniformly in both realms. For example, being alive is a condition for being knowledgeable in the witnessed world, so it must also be a condition in the unseen. Therefore, to prove that God is knowledgeable, you must first prove He is alive, and you cannot distinguish between the possible and the necessary in conditions.' The author then points out a flaw: In the witnessed world, life alone is not a sufficient condition for knowledge; other conditions are needed, such as bodily structure (al-binya) and the absence of opposites (intifa' al-addad). These additional conditions are not uniform—bodily structure does not apply to God, and the absence of opposites is different. Thus, the condition of life is not simply transferable. Furthermore, one condition can serve multiple things, and multiple conditions can serve one thing, so the Sifatiyya's demand for strict uniformity fails. The author thereby shows that the condition argument does not bind the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
شرط (shart)
مشروط (mashrut)
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)
صفاتية (Sifatiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the condition for knowledge in the witnessed (life) must apply identically to God without considering additional necessary conditions.
Correction: The passage shows that life alone is not sufficient; additional conditions like bodily structure and absence of opposites are also required in the witnessed, and these are not transferable to God.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the Sifatiyya's requirement for uniform conditions fails?
A) Because the Sifatiyya themselves do not apply conditions uniformly.
B) Because life is not a condition for knowledge in the witnessed world at all.
C) Because life alone is insufficient; other conditions like bodily structure and absence of opposites are also required, and these are not transferable.
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not believe in conditions.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya's demand for uniform conditions fails because conditions in the witnessed are composite and not all transferable to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your understanding of God have you unconsciously assumed that what applies to you (e.g., need for a cause, need for bodily processes) must apply to Him? Pause right now and write down one such assumption you have held. What does this lesson require you to correct in your heart?
?
The Sifatiyya insisted on uniformity out of a desire to protect God's attributes, yet they fell into error. Are there areas in your own worship or beliefs where your zeal to protect God's honor has led you to make assumptions about Him that the Qur'an or reason do not support? Ask yourself honestly and commit to re-examining that area.
?
This lesson shows that conditions in the witnessed are not simply transferable. How does this shape how you think about the relationship between God's attributes and His actions? Is there a specific attribute of God you have been reluctant to affirm because you thought it required a condition that only applies to creation? Commit to studying that issue today.

The Priority of Essence Over Attributes
States the priority of essence over attributes and seeks divine help.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine essence is considered logically prior to the attributes in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The essence is prior because it subsists by itself; attributes depend on it.

This passage from Shahrastani's Nihāyat al-Aqdām tackles a subtle point in divine attributes: the relationship between God's essence and His attributes. The author argues for the priority of essence, then notes a dilemma, and finally seeks divine guidance. Let's unpack this dense theological statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the compressed style and the key terms: الذات, الصفات, أولى.
The passage contains a logical argument followed by a supplication.
2
Understand the priority of essence
The author asserts that the essence is 'primarily prior' because the essence is self-subsistent (قائمة بذاتها) while the attributes subsist through the essence (قائمة بها). This makes the essence the foundation.
The phrase 'من حيث إن' introduces the reason.
3
Reflect on the dilemma and the turn to God
The author then notes that from another perspective (that of affirmation and entailment), the attribute could be considered prior to the described, but he calls this 'the uglier of the two matters'. Finally, he seeks guidance from Allah, acknowledging His supreme choice.
This shows the author's humility and reliance on divine wisdom.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالذات أولا أولى منه بالصفات من حيث إن الذات قائمة بذاتها والصفات قائمة بها، حتى يكون الموصوف من حيث الذات أسبق على الصفة، والصفة من حيث الإيجاب والاقتضاء أسبق على الموصوف، وهذا أشنع الأمرين، والاستخارة إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى، وهو خير مخير.
The essence is primarily prior to the attributes in that the essence subsists by itself and the attributes subsist by it, such that the described in terms of essence is prior to the attribute, and the attribute in terms of affirmation and entailment is prior to the described, and this is the uglier of the two matters, and seeking guidance (istikhāra) to God the Exalted, and He is the best of choosers.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage states that the essence (dhāt) is primarily prior to the attributes (ṣifāt) because the essence subsists by itself while attributes subsist by it. Consequently, the described (mawṣūf) in terms of essence is prior to the attribute, but the attribute in terms of positive attribution and entailment is prior to the described. The author declares this latter priority to be the uglier of the two possibilities. He then turns to seek aid from God, affirming that He is the best of helpers.

Key Takeaway
The essence is ontologically prior to the attributes because it is self‑subsistent, while attributes depend on the essence.

Objection Against Defining God’s Attributes from the Seen and Its Refutation
Shahrastani presents an objection about defining God's attributes from the seen and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection to applying definitions from the seen to God's attributes and Shahrastani's multi-pronged response, including the ambiguity of 'possessor' and the alternative derivation from relation to the known.

Core Idea
Defining God by seen categories risks confusing possession with action.

Shahrastani presents a crucial methodological objection: can we define God's attributes using definitions derived from the witnessed world? This passage not only raises the objection but provides a sophisticated refutation, distinguishing between different senses of 'possessor' and offering an alternative derivation of divine attributes from the relation to the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector argues that since 'knower' in the seen is defined as 'possessor of knowledge', the same must apply to God. Read this argument to understand its logical force.
Notice how the objector assumes that definitions are univocal across seen and unseen.
2
Examine the first refutation: defining by the less clear
The response points out that this reasoning defines the more apparent (the knower) by the more hidden (knowledge itself), which is logically flawed. One might know Zayd is moving but doubt what motion is.
This refutation targets the direction of definition.
3
Examine the second refutation: ambiguity of 'possessor'
The response shows that 'possessor of something' can mean attribute, action, or possession. The Quranic example 'Possessor of the Throne' shows possession, not an attribute.
This refutation undermines the assumption that 'possessor of knowledge' must be an attribute.
4
Follow the alternative derivation
The response then proposes a different way: one can infer knowledge from God's being knowing and from the relation to the known, without positing a distinct attribute.
This derivation is more sound because it starts from a clear property (being knowing) rather than an obscure one (knowledge).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال من حاول الجمع بين الغائب والشاهد بالحد والحقيقة: حد العالم في الشاهد أنه ذو العلم والقادر ذو القدرة، والمريد ذو الإرادة، فيجب طرد ذلك في الغائب، والحقيقة لا تختلف شاهدًا أو غائبًا، قيل له: هذا تعريف الشيء بما هو دونه في الخفا والجلا، فإن الإنسان ربما يعرف كون زيد متحركا ويشك في الحركة حتى يبرهن على ذلك، وكذلك في جميع الأوصاف، فكيف اقتبست معرفة الأجلى من الإخفا، وأيضا فإن ذا الشيء قد يكون على سبيل الوصف والصفة، وقد يكون على سبيل الفعل والمفعول وقد يكون على سبيل الملك والمملوك، أليس روي في التنزيل: (رَفِيعُ الدَّرَجَاتِ ذُو الْعَرْشِ) [غافر: 15] ثم العرش خلقه وملكه وليس صفة قائمة بذاته، فما أنكرتم أن يكون معنى كونه ذا العلم والقدرة. قال: قد يقتبس إثبات العلم والقدرة من كونه علما وقادرًا، وقد يقتبس من كون الشيء معلوما ومقدوراً. فيقول: المتعلق بالمعلوم علم والمتعلق بالمقدور قدرة، فإذا قام الدليل على كونه عالما بالمعلوم فيجب أن يكون عالما بالعلم.
He who attempted to combine the unseen and the seen by definition and reality said: The definition of 'knower' in the seen is that he is 'possessor of knowledge', and 'powerful' is 'possessor of power', and 'willing' is 'possessor of will'. So it must be coextensive in the unseen, and reality does not differ whether seen or unseen. It was said to him: This is defining a thing by what is lesser than it in obscurity and clarity. For a person may know that Zayd is moving and doubt about motion until he proves it, and similarly for all attributes. So how did you derive knowledge of the more apparent from the more hidden? Moreover, 'possessor of something' may be in the way of description and attribute, or in the way of action and object, or in the way of possession and possessed. Has it not been narrated in the Revelation: 'Exalted in degrees, Possessor of the Throne' (Ghāfir 40:15)? Then the Throne is His creation and possession, not an attribute subsisting in His essence. So why do you deny that the meaning of His being 'possessor of knowledge and power' [could be similar]? He said: One may derive the affirmation of knowledge and power from His being knowing and powerful, and one may derive from the thing being known and powerful. So he says: that which is related to the known is knowledge, and that which is related to the powerful is power. So if the proof is established that He is knowing of the known, then it is necessary that He be knowing by knowledge.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer format. The objector (the one attempting to combine unseen and seen by definition) argues that in the seen, a knower (عالم) is defined as 'possessor of knowledge' (ذو العلم), a powerful one as 'possessor of power', and a willing one as 'possessor of will'. They claim this definition must apply to the unseen (غائب) as well, since reality (حقيقة) does not differ. The response refutes this on several grounds. First, it is a case of defining the more apparent (الأجلى) by the more hidden (الإخفا), since one might know Zayd moves but doubt motion itself. Second, the expression 'possessor of' (ذو) can indicate different relationships: attribute (وصف/صفة), action (فعل) and its object (مفعول), or possession (ملك) and possessed (مملوك). The Quranic verse 'Possessor of the Throne' (ذو العرش) is cited: the Throne is created and possessed, not an attribute. Similarly, 'possessor of knowledge' might not imply a distinct attribute subsisting in God's essence. Then a different derivation is proposed: one can infer knowledge from the fact that God is knowing (عالم) and from the relation to the known (معلوم). The argument concludes that if it is proven that God is knowing of the known, then He must be knowing by knowledge—but this does not necessarily mean knowledge is a distinct entity; it could be an aspect of His essence.

شاهد
حد
حقيقة

ذو القدرة
صفة
فعل
ملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that 'possessor of knowledge' always implies a distinct attribute subsisting in God's essence.
Correction: The passage shows that 'possessor of' can indicate action or possession, as in 'Possessor of the Throne', where the Throne is created and possessed, not an attribute.

Checkpoint

C) 'Possessor of the Throne'
D) 'Possessor of will'

Key Takeaway
The Quranic example 'Possessor of the Throne' demonstrates that 'possessor of' does not necessarily indicate an intrinsic attribute; it can refer to possession or action.

Objection and Refutation on Analogical Definition
Objection against analogical definition and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning between seen and unseen worlds is insufficient for defining divine attributes, due to ambiguity in key terms.

Core Idea
Analogy fails when the key term is ambiguous.

In this passage, the author presents a common objection that seeks to define God's attributes by analogy with the seen world, and then refutes it. The objection tries to equate 'possessor of knowledge' in God and humans, but the response reveals a critical flaw: the word 'possessor' (dhu) can mean different kinds of relationships. This discussion is essential for proper methodology in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the passage from 'قال من حاول' to 'فيدجب طرد ذلك في الغائب' to grasp the opponent's claim that the same definition applies to seen and unseen.
The opponent assumes that 'dhu' has a univocal meaning.
2
Identify the first response
Then read the response starting with 'قيل له' and note the first objection: that defining by 'possessor of' is actually less clear than the original term.
This is a logical critique of circularity or lack of clarity.
3
Identify the second response
Read the rest of the response to see the second objection: 'dhu' can denote attribute, action, or ownership. The example of 'Dhu al-'Arsh' shows it can mean created possession.
This is the stronger argument from linguistic ambiguity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why this refutation is important: it blocks a simplistic use of qiyas in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال من حاول الجمع بين الغائب والشاهد بالحد والحقيقة : حد العالم في الشاهد أنه ذو العلم والقادر ذو القدرة ، والمريد ذو الإرادة، فيجب طرد ذلك في الغائب، والحقيقة لا تختلف شاهدًا أو غائبًا . قيل له : هذا تعريف الشيء بما هو دونه في الخفا والجلا، فإن الإنسان ربما يعرف كون زيد متحركا ويشك في الحركة حتى يبرهن على ذلك، وكذلك في جميع الأوصاف، فكيف اقتبست معرفة الأجلى من الأخفى، وأيضا فإن ذا الشيء قد يكون على سبيل الوصف والصفة، وقد يكون على سبيل الفعل والمفعول وقد يكون على سبيل الملك والمملوك، أليس روي في التنزيل : ( رَفِيعُ الدَّرَجَاتِ ذُو الْعَرْشِ ) ثم العرش خلقه وملكه وليس صفة قائمة بذاته، فما أنكرتم أن يكون معنى كونه ذا العلم والقدرة.
One who attempted to unite the unseen and the seen by definition and essence said: The definition of the knower in the seen is that he is possessor of knowledge, and the powerful is possessor of power, and the willer is possessor of will, so it is necessary to apply that equally to the unseen, and the essence does not differ whether seen or unseen. It was said to him: This is defining a thing by what is less than it in obscurity and clarity, for a person may know that Zayd is moving and doubt the movement until he proves it, and likewise in all attributes, so how did you derive knowledge of the clearer from the more obscure? And also, 'possessor of a thing' may be in the sense of description and attribute, or in the sense of action and object of action, or in the sense of ownership and owned object. Has it not been narrated in the Revelation: 'Exalted in degrees, Possessor of the Throne'? Then the Throne is His creation and possession, not an attribute subsisting in His essence. So why do you deny that the meaning of His being possessor of knowledge and power is something similar?
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It begins with an opponent (likely a literalist or anthropomorphist) arguing that since in the seen world a knower is defined as 'possessor of knowledge', the same definition must apply to God in the unseen, because essence (haqiqa) does not differ. The response refutes this by pointing out two problems: First, the definition proposed is actually more obscure than the thing defined, as it uses a vague term 'possessor of' which can have multiple meanings. Second, the expression 'possessor of' (dhu) can denote different relationships: it can mean an attribute (as in 'possessor of knowledge' meaning knowing), or it can denote ownership or creation (as in 'Possessor of the Throne' where the Throne is not an attribute but a created thing). The response uses the Quranic verse to show that 'dhu al-'arsh' does not mean an attribute inhering in God, so the analogy fails. Thus, the opponent cannot simply transfer the seen definition to the unseen without clarification.

الشاهد
الحد
الحقيقة
ذو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that analogical reasoning (qiyas) between seen and unseen is always valid for defining divine attributes.
Correction: The author shows that the term 'dhu' is ambiguous, and the analogy fails because it ignores the different possible relationships 'dhu' can denote.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning between seen and unseen worlds is invalid when the key term (like 'dhu') has multiple possible meanings that cannot be assumed to be the same.

Knowledge as an Attribute of Encompassment
The author proves knowledge as a distinct attribute of encompassment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's knowledge must be a distinct attribute of encompassment and not identical to His essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a relation of encompassment; the essence cannot be the relation, so an attribute is needed.

This passage presents a key distinction in Islamic theology: God's knowledge is not just His essence knowing, but an attribute that encompasses all known things. The author argues that the very concept of 'encompassing' requires a separate attribute because the essence itself cannot be the agent of relation. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage quietly. Then read the literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the two possible starting points: from God's description or from the objects known.
2
Understand the two derivations
The author mentions two ways to prove the attributes: from God being described as knowing/powerful, or from the objects being known/powerful. He focuses on the second.
3
Follow the key inference
Since God is knowing of the known, there must be a relation (ta'alluq) between Him and the known. That relation is knowledge. But the essence cannot itself be the relation (that would imply change), so an attribute is required.
The term 'encompassing' (ihata) is the nature of knowledge.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion: God has an attribute of encompassment that is distinct from His essence, and that attribute is knowledge.
This counters the view that God knows by His essence alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : قد يقتبس إثبات العلم والقدرة من كونه عالما وقادرا، وقد يقتبس من كون الشيء معلوما ومقدورا. فيقول : المتعلق بالمعلوم علم والمتعلق بالمقدور قدرة، فإذا قام الدليل على كونه عالما بالمعلوم فيجب أن يكون عالما بالعلم. ويحققه أن العلم إحاطة بالمعلوم، ويستحيل أن تكون الذات محيطا أو متعلقا، فيجب أن يكون للذات صفة إحاطة هي المحيطة المتعلقة بالمعلومات.
He said: The affirmation of knowledge and power may be derived from His being knowing and powerful, and it may be derived from a thing being known and powerful. So he says: That which is related to the known is knowledge, and that which is related to the powerful is power. So if proof is established that He is knowing of the known, then He must be knowing by knowledge. And he verifies it: that knowledge is encompassing of the known, and it is impossible for the essence to be encompassing or related, so the essence must have an attribute of encompassing, which is the encompassing [attribute] related to the known things.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of knowledge as a distinct attribute of God. The author presents two ways to derive the attributes: either from God's being described as 'knowing' (which implies knowledge) or from the objects of knowledge (which imply knowledge as a relation). The author chooses the second approach: the relation (ta'alluq) to the known is knowledge, and to the powerful is power. Since God is knowing of the known, He must have knowledge. Then the author clarifies that knowledge is an encompassing (ihata) of the known. However, the divine essence cannot itself be the encompassing entity or the relation (because that would imply change or limitation). Therefore, the essence must have an attribute of encompassing (sifat ihata) that does the encompassing and relating. This establishes knowledge as a distinct attribute inhering in God, not identical to His essence.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الإحاطة
التعلق
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is simply His essence without any additional attribute.
Correction: If knowledge were identical to the essence, then the essence would be the relation of encompassment, which is impossible because the essence is simple and cannot be a relation to multiple objects.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that God must have a distinct attribute of knowledge rather than being knowing by His essence alone?
A) Because knowledge is an accident and cannot subsist in God.
B) Because the essence cannot be the subject of encompassing or relation.
C) Because the Quran mentions 'knowledge' as a separate thing.
D) Because all attributes are distinct from the essence in all schools.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is a distinct attribute of encompassment because the essence cannot directly relate to all known objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's knowledge is a distinct, real attribute that encompasses everything, then nothing escapes it. How will this truth change the way you speak, act, or think in the next hour? Will you let it, or will you ignore it?

Mu'tazila Refutation of the Encompassing Argument
Mu'tazila refute the encompassing argument via synonymy and verbal equivocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that 'knowledge' and 'encompassing' are synonymous and do not imply distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Encompassing is just another word for knowledge, not a separate attribute.

The Mu'tazila respond to the previous argument that God's knowledge is an 'encompassing' attribute. They argue that 'encompassing' (ihata) is merely a verbal variation of knowledge itself, not a distinct attribute. This passage clarifies their rejection of attribute-reification and highlights their reliance on conceptual parsimony.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language: 'تغيير عبارة وتبديل لفظ بلفظ'.
2
Identify the three synonyms
The passage states that 'knowledge', 'encompassing', and 'certainty' are expressions for a single meaning. Find these terms in the text.
Arabic: العلم, الإحاطة, التيقن.
3
Understand the accusation of verbal equivocation
The Mu'tazila claim that their opponents mistakenly think 'encompassing' implies a physical surrounding. They call this 'verbal equivocation' (ishtirak lafzi). Ask yourself: why would this be a weak argument?
The Quranic verse at the end supports the meaning of knowledge, not physicality.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
By denying that 'encompassing' is a separate attribute, the Mu'tazila reject the notion of multiple eternal attributes. Consider how this aligns with their principle of divine unity (tawhid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : معلوم الله بكونه عالما لا بالعلم ولا بالذات، ولا معنى لكون المعلوم معلوما إلا أنه غير مخفي على العالم كما هو عليه، فليس ثم تعلق حسي أو وهمي حتى يحال به على العلم أو على الذات، وقولكم : العلم إحاطة بالمعلوم تغيير عبارة وتبديل لفظ بلفظ، وإلا فالعلم والإحاطة والتيقن عبارات عن معبر واحد، ومعنى كون الذات عالما أنه محيط، وكذلك معنى كونه محيطا أنه عالم، وإنما وقعتم في إلزام لفظ الإحاطة لظنكم أن الإحاطة لو تحققت للذات كانت تلك الإحاطة كإحاطة جسم بجسم، وذلك الاشتراك في اللفظ وإلا فمعنى الإحاطة هو العلم ( وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَىءٍ عَلِيمٌ ) محيط وبكل شيء عليم وعلى كل شيء قدير.
The Mu'tazila said: God's being known is by His being knowing, not by knowledge nor by the essence, and there is no meaning to a thing being known except that it is not hidden from the knower as it is. So there is no sensory or imagined relation such that it would be referred back to knowledge or to the essence. And your statement: 'Knowledge is encompassing of the known' is a change of expression and substitution of one word for another. Otherwise, knowledge, encompassing, and certainty are expressions for a single meaning. And the meaning of the essence being knowing is that it is encompassing, and likewise the meaning of its being encompassing is that it is knowing. You have only fallen into insisting on the word 'encompassing' because you think that if encompassing were realized for the essence, that encompassing would be like the encompassing of a body by a body. That is verbal equivocation; otherwise, the meaning of encompassing is knowledge. 'And He is of all things Knowing' (Quran), encompassing, and of everything Knowing, and over everything Powerful.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is the Mu'tazila refutation of the previous argument. They make several points: (1) God's being known (i.e., His objects being known) is due to His being 'knowing' (alim), not due to a separate attribute of knowledge nor simply by His essence. (2) 'Known' simply means not hidden from the knower; there is no extra relation (ta'alluq) that needs a distinct attribute. (3) The claim that 'knowledge is encompassing' is just verbal substitution; 'knowledge', 'encompassing', and 'certainty' all mean the same thing. So 'encompassing' is not a distinct attribute but another word for knowledge. (4) The objectors (the Sifatiyya) mistakenly think that 'encompassing' implies a physical encompassing like one body surrounds another, but that is a verbal equivocation (ishtirak). Actually, in God's case, encompassing means the same as knowing. The Mu'tazila thus deny the need for a distinct attribute of knowledge inhering in God.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
العلم
الإحاطة
التيقن
الاشتراك اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'encompassing' implies a physical attribute or a distinct entity separate from knowledge.
Correction: The Mu'tazila explain that 'encompassing' is merely another word for knowledge; it does not denote a separate attribute or a physical relation.

Checkpoint

B) They reject it as anthropomorphic.

D) They say it applies only to God's power, not His knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila reject 'encompassing' as a distinct attribute, considering it synonymous with knowledge and accusing opponents of verbal equivocation.

?
The opponent's error came from assuming that a word must always carry its ordinary physical meaning. Are there words or phrases you use about God (e.g., 'hand', 'face', 'coming') that might subtly shape an unworthy mental image? What concrete step will you take today to align your understanding with the Quran's intent?

The Sifatiyya Argument for Distinct Divine Attributes
The Sifatiyya argue that knowledge and power are distinct because their objects differ in scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sifatiyya use the differing scopes of knowledge and power to argue that these attributes are distinct in God.

Core Idea
The objects of divine knowledge exceed those of divine power, proving the attributes are distinct.

In this passage, the Sifatiyya (scholars who affirm multiple eternal attributes) present a rational argument for distinguishing between Allah's knowledge and His power. They observe that what can be known is a broader category than what can be acted upon, and they use this logical difference to argue that the two attributes cannot be identical. This argument is a classic piece of Sunni kalam defending the multiplicity of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal English translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the Sifatiyya claim that reason differentiates between being known and being powerful.
The passage is cut off at the end but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the key contrast
Notice the difference in scope: 'being known' covers eternal, originated, necessary, possible, and impossible, while 'being powerful' only covers possible/contingent. This is the core evidence.
This shows that knowledge and power have different objects.
3
Understand the dilemma
The Sifatiyya then note that both attributes relate to the same divine essence. If the relation is identical (as opponents claim), then the scopes should be identical, but they are not. Therefore, the attributes must be distinct.
This forces the opponent to accept distinct attributes or reject the obvious difference in scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية: العقل الصريح يفرق بين كون الشيء معلوما وبين كونه مقدورا، أوكيف لا وكونه معلوما أعم من كونه مقدورا، فإن المعلوم قد يكون قديما وقد يكون حادثا وواجبا وجائزا ومستحيلا، وكونه مقدورا ينحصر في كونه ممكنا جائزا، ثم نسبة المعلوم إلى الذات من حيث هي ذات واحدة كنسبة المقدور من حيث هي ذات. فنقول : نسبة الذات إليهما على قضية واحدة عندكم، أم تختلف النسبة؟ فإن كان نسبة الذات إليهما على قضية واحدة فيلزم أن لا يكون أحدهما أعم والثاني أخص، ويجب أن يكون كل معلوم مقدورا كما كان كل مقدور معلوما، وإن اختلف
The Sifatiyya said: Pure reason differentiates between a thing being known and being powerful. How could it not, while being known is more general than being powerful? For the known may be eternal or originated, necessary, possible, or impossible, whereas being powerful is restricted to being possible and contingent. Then the relation of the known to the essence, insofar as it is essence, is one relation like the relation of the powerful [to the essence] insofar as it is essence. So we say: Is the relation of the essence to them according to a single principle in your view, or does the relation differ? If the relation of the essence to them is on a single principle, then it would be necessary that one is not more general and the other more specific, and every known would have to be powerful as every powerful is known. But if they differ...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is the Sifatiyya (attribute-affirmers) argument for the distinction between God's knowledge and power. They begin by stating that reason clearly distinguishes between being known (ma'lum) and being powerful (maqdur), because the set of known things is broader than the set of powerful things: the known includes eternal, originated, necessary, possible, and impossible, while the powerful (i.e., what can be acted upon) is only possible/contingent. Then they note that the relation (nisba) of both the known and the powerful to the divine essence is one (i.e., both are related to the same essence). They then pose a dilemma to the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila or those who deny distinct attributes): If the relation of the essence to both is the same (i.e., if knowledge and power are identical to the essence and thus each other), then the sets 'known' and 'powerful' should be coextensive (every known would be powerful and vice versa), which is false. Therefore, the relation must differ, implying distinct attributes. The argument is left incomplete, but the logical force is clear: the difference in extension proves the attributes are distinct.

Key Terms
الصفاتية
المعلوم
المقدور
النسبة
الأعم والأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if knowledge and power are both attributes of God, they must have the same scope, but the Sifatiyya point out that their objects differ, proving they are distinct.
Correction: The objects of divine knowledge are broader than those of divine power; thus the attributes cannot be identical.

Checkpoint
What evidence do the Sifatiyya use to argue that knowledge and power are distinct attributes?
A) The Quran mentions them separately.
B) The objects of knowledge are broader than those of power.
C) Reason cannot grasp them simultaneously.
D) The tradition of the Prophet distinguishes them.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya prove that knowledge and power are distinct attributes because the class of known things is broader than the class of powerful things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because God knows your situation, He will necessarily change it? How does the Sifatiyya's distinction between what God knows and what He has power over affect the way you understand your prayers and reliance on Him? Ask yourself honestly: do you treat God's knowledge as a guarantee of intervention, or do you submit to His wisdom even when He does not act?
?
Reflect on a time you felt something was 'impossible' for you but you still prayed for it. Did you consider that God's power is limited to possibilities, yet He knows even the impossible? How does this lesson push you to align your requests with what is genuinely possible (i.e., within His power) while trusting that He knows all outcomes, even those beyond your understanding?
?
The Sifatiyya argue that God's knowledge extends to impossible things, such as the existence of another god. In your own heart, have you ever entertained thoughts about Allah that are incompatible with His oneness or attributes? How does knowing that Allah is aware of even those false thoughts move you to guard your mind and heart more carefully?

Distinct Properties Require Distinct Realities
Sifatiyya prove distinct properties require distinct realities via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sifatiyya argue that if God's attributes were not distinct realities, it would lead to the absurdity of combining opposites.

Core Idea
Different properties cannot coexist in a single reality without contradiction.

The Sifatiyya (those who affirm distinct divine attributes) use a rational argument to prove that attributes like knowledge, power, and will must be distinct realities. They show that if these were merely aspects of one essence, then absurd consequences follow, such as a single thing being both substance and accident. This passage presents that argument through a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a general principle, a reductio, and a conclusion of impossibility.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the key principle
The passage states that the intellect distinguishes different realities by their properties, effects, and so on. Without such differences, we cannot judge them as multiple.
This is a foundational premise.
3
Understand the reductio
The author then says: if it were permissible for one essence to have the judgments of knowledge, power, will, and life, then it would be permissible for one reality to have the judgment of both accident and substance, and so on. That would combine opposites.
The reductio shows the absurd implication.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that such a combination is among the most absurd things. Therefore, the original assumption (that attributes are not distinct realities) must be false.
The argument forces a choice: distinct realities or absurdity.
5
Apply to divine attributes
Consider how this argument supports the view that God's attributes are distinct entities (ma'ani) subsisting in the divine essence.
This is a key point in Ash'ari kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحقائق إذا اختلفت من هذه الوجوه فقد اختلفت في ذواتها ولا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر، والعقل إنما يميز هذه المعاني بهذه الوجوه وإلا فأعراض متعددة إذا قامت بمحل واحد لم يحكم العقل باختلافها وتعددها إلا باختلاف خواصها وآثارها وفوائدها ودلائلها ومتعلقها ومن المستحيل في العقل اجتماع خواص مختلفة في حقيقة واحدة، فلو جاز إثبات ذات لها حكم العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة لجاز إثبات حقيقة واحدة لها حكم العرض والجوهر، ولجاز إثبات علم له حكم القدرة ولجاز إثبات لون له حكم الكون، ويلزم على ذلك اجتماع المتضادات وذلك من أمحل ما يتصور.
And if realities differ in these respects, then they differ in their essences (dhāt), and one does not take the place of the other. The intellect only distinguishes these meanings by these respects; otherwise, multiple accidents (a‘rāḍ) when subsisting in a single locus, the intellect does not judge them to be different and multiple except by the difference of their properties (khaṣā’iṣ), effects (āthār), benefits (fawā’id), proofs (adilla), and objects of relation (muta‘allaqāt). It is rationally impossible for different properties to be combined in a single reality. For if it were permissible to posit an essence that has the judgment (ḥukm) of knowledge, power, will, and life, then it would be permissible to posit a single reality that has the judgment of accident and substance. And it would be permissible to posit knowledge that has the judgment of power, and to posit a color that has the judgment of being (kawn). That would necessitate the combination of opposites (ijtimā‘ al-mutaḍāddāt), and that is among the most absurd things conceivable.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage continues the Sifatiyya argument that distinct properties imply distinct realities. The intellect distinguishes multiple accidents by their different properties, effects, benefits, proofs, and objects. It is impossible for different properties to belong to one single reality. The author uses a reductio ad absurdum: if it were allowed to have one essence with the judgments of knowledge, power, will, and life, then it would also be allowed to have one reality with the judgment of both substance and accident—which is impossible. Likewise, one could say that knowledge has the judgment of power, or a color has the judgment of being (which is a different accident). That would require combining opposites, which is utterly absurd. Therefore, the attributes of God cannot all refer to a single essence without distinction.

Key Terms
الحقائق (al-ḥaqā'iq)
الخواص (al-khaṣā'iṣ)
الآثار (al-āthār)
الدلائل (al-adilla)
المتعلقات (al-mutaʿallaqāt)
اجتماع المتضادات (ijtimāʿ al-mutaḍāddāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sifatiyya argument denies that God can have multiple attributes.
Correction: The argument does not deny multiple attributes; it proves they must be distinct realities to avoid absurdity. God has many attributes, but each is a distinct meaning subsisting in His essence.

Checkpoint
What does the author say would follow if it were permissible for a single essence to have the judgment of both knowledge and power?
A) That knowledge and power would be identical.
B) That it would be permissible for a single reality to have the judgment of both accident and substance.
C) That God would have no attributes.
D) That knowledge would not cause power.

Key Takeaway
The Sifatiyya use a reductio to prove that if divine attributes were not distinct realities, it would lead to the absurd combination of opposites, so they must be distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge and power are distinct realities, or do you subconsciously merge them into a vague concept? How does that affect your understanding of His actions in your life?
?
If you believed that God's mercy and justice were the same attribute, would you approach Him differently? This lesson shows they are distinct—are you willing to deepen your relationship with each attribute separately?
?
The reductio argument demands logical consistency. Examine your inner beliefs about God: are there any contradictions you hold? Will you surrender comfortable but inconsistent thoughts to align with this truth?

The Strongest Proof: The Trilemma for Affirming Attributes
Al-Ash'ari presents a trilemma proof for the reality of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the trilemma proof that affirmation and denial of divine attributes must refer to real attributes distinct from the essence and states.

Core Idea
Meaningful statements about God's knowledge and power must refer to real attributes.

This passage presents what the author considers the strongest proof for the reality of God's attributes. The key move is a trilemma: any meaningful affirmation or negation about God's attributes must refer either to the essence, to a state, or to a real attribute. By eliminating the first two, the only logical conclusion is that real attributes exist.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma
The author presents three possibilities for what affirmation/negation of attributes refers to: essence, attributes, or states.
2
Eliminate essence
The author argues that the essence is conceivable without the attribute, and deniers negate attributes while affirming essence, so the reference cannot be to essence.
3
Eliminate states
The author dismisses states as neither affirmable nor negatable by themselves, and claims the doctrine of states has been refuted.
4
Conclusion: attributes
The only remaining possibility is that affirmation/negation refers to real attributes, thus proving them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أقوى ما يتمسك به في إثبات الصفات قولنا : الله عالم قادر وقول المعطل مثلا ليس بعالم ولا قادر إثبات ونفي لا محالة، فلا يخلو الأمر فيه ؛ إما أن يرجع الإثبات والنفي إلى الذات وإما أن يرجع إلى الصفات، وإما أن يرجع إلى الأحوال، ومستحيل رجوعهما إلى الذات فإنها معقولة دون الاتصاف بالعالمية ؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة العلم كونه ذاتا قائما بالنفس مستغنيًا من كل وجه أن يعلم كونه عالما، ولذلك كان الدليل على كونه قائما بالنفس غير الدليل على كونه عالما قادرًا، ولا من ضرورة نفي العالمية نفي الذات، فإن جماعة من المعطلة نفوا كونه عالما وقادرًا مع إقرارهم وعلمهم بثبوت الذات، ويستحيل رجوعهما إلى الحال فإنها لا تنفي على حيالها ولا تثبت، وقد أبطلنا القول بالحال فلم يبق إلا القسم الأخير وهو الرجوع إلى الصفات.
And the strongest thing by which to adhere in affirming the attributes is our statement: 'Allah is knowing, powerful' and the statement of the negator (muʿaṭṭil) for example: 'He is not knowing nor powerful' — affirmation and negation without doubt. The matter regarding it is not free from three possibilities: either the affirmation and negation refer to the essence (al-dhāt), or they refer to the attributes (al-ṣifāt), or they refer to the states (al-aḥwāl). It is impossible for both of them to refer to the essence, for the essence is conceivable without being described as knowing—since it is not a necessary concomitant of one's knowledge that a self-subsistent essence independent in every respect knows that it is knowing. Therefore, the proof for its being self-subsistent is different from the proof for its being knowing and powerful. Nor is it a necessary concomitant of negating 'knowing' to negate the essence, for a group of the negators (muʿaṭṭila) denied that He is knowing and powerful while acknowledging and knowing the subsistence of the essence. And it is impossible for both to refer to the state (ḥāl), for the state is neither negated nor affirmed by itself. And we have already invalidated the doctrine of the state (al-ḥāl). So nothing remains except the last division, which is the reference to the attributes.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage contains what the author presents as the strongest proof for affirming the real attributes of God. The starting point is that both the believer's affirmation 'Allah is knowing, powerful' and the denier's negation 'He is not knowing nor powerful' are meaningful statements that must refer to something. The author poses a trilemma: the affirmation/negation must refer either to the essence (dhāt), the attributes (ṣifāt), or the states (aḥwāl). He then rules out the essence because the essence is conceivable without the attribute of knowledge—we can conceive of a self-subsistent essence without knowing whether it is knowing, and the proof for God's self-subsistence is different from the proof for His knowledge. Moreover, the deniers (like the Jahmiyya or Mu'tazila) negate that God is knowing while still affirming His essence, showing that negation of the attribute does not negate the essence. Next, he rules out the states (aḥwāl) because a state is neither independently existent nor non-existent, and the author claims that the doctrine of states has already been refuted (referring to Abu Hashim's theory). Since the two other possibilities are impossible, the only remaining option is that affirmation and negation refer to real attributes (ṣifāt) distinct from the essence. Thus the attributes are proven.

Key Terms
إثبات الصفات (ithbāt al-ṣifāt)
المعطل (al-muʿaṭṭil)
الذات (al-dhāt)
الصفات (al-ṣifāt)
الحال (al-ḥāl)
العالمية (al-ʿālamiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the existence of attributes from the start.
Correction: The proof does not assume attributes; it infers them from the necessity of meaningful affirmation/negation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that affirming 'God is knowing' cannot refer to the essence?
A) Because the essence is not self-subsistent.
B) Because the essence can be conceived without the attribute of knowledge.
C) Because the deniers deny the essence as well.
D) Because the essence has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The trilemma proof shows that meaningful statements about God's attributes logically require the existence of real attributes distinct from the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is All-Knowing,' do you truly believe that His knowledge is a real attribute distinct from His essence, or have you treated it as just a phrase? Does this lesson compel you to reconsider the weight of your words about God?
?
The trilemma forces a choice: either attributes are real or they are not. In your daily life, do you act as if God's knowledge and power are real attributes that affect your decisions, or do you live as if He is a distant essence? What would change if you truly internalized that His attributes are real?
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that denying attributes leads to contradiction. Have you ever, in your heart, downplayed an attribute of God—perhaps thinking His mercy is just a metaphor or His anger is not real? This lesson demands that you examine and correct such thoughts. Will you commit to affirming every attribute as real?

The Principle of Distinction via Properties
The author states a general principle and deduces absurdities if attributes are not distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational principle that distinct properties imply distinct realities, and how this applies to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Different effects require different causes; a single reality cannot combine contradictory properties.

This lesson opens a key argument for the reality of God’s attributes. The author first establishes a general epistemological rule: reason distinguishes things only by their properties and effects. He then shows that denying the distinction of attributes leads to absurd contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure.
2
Identify the general principle
The author states that when realities differ in their properties, effects, and relations, they differ in their essences. Reason only distinguishes multiple accidents in one substrate by such differences.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Follow the absurd consequences
The author then posits: if a single essence combined attributes like knowledge, power, will, and life, then absurdities would follow, such as a single thing being both substance and accident.
These consequences are meant to be impossible.
4
Reflect on the necessity of distinction
Consider why this principle forces the conclusion that divine attributes must be distinct realities.
This prepares for the stronger argument in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحقائق إذا اختلفت من هذه الوجوه فقد اختلفت في ذواتها ولا يسد أحدهما مسد الآخر، والعقل إنما يميز هذه المعاني بهذه الوجوه وإلا فأعراض متعددة إذا قامت بمحل واحد لم يحكم العقل باختلافها وتعددها إلا باختلاف خواصها وآثارها وفوائدها ودلائلها ومتعلقاتها ومن المستحيل في العقل اجتماع خواص مختلفة في حقيقة واحدة، فلو جاز إثبات ذات لها حكم العلم والقدرة والإرادة والحياة لجاز إثبات حقيقة واحدة لها حكم العرض والجوهر، ولجاز إثبات علم له حكم القدرة ولجاز إثبات لون له حكم الكون، ويلزم على ذلك اجتماع المتضادات وذلك من أمحل ما يتصور. ومما يوضح ذلك وهو أقوى ما يتمسك به في إثبات الصفات قولنا : الله عالم قادر وقول المعطل مثلا ليس بعالم ولا قادر إثبات ونفي لا محالة، فلا يخلو الأمر فيه؛ إما أن يرجع الإثبات والنفي إلى الذات وإما أن يرجع إلى الصفات، وإما أن يرجع إلى الأحوال، ومستحيل رجوعهما إلى الذات فإنها معقولة دون الاتصاف بالعالمية؛ إذ ليس من ضرورة العلم كونه ذاتا قائما بالنفس مستغنيًا من كل وجه أن يعلم كونه عالما، ولذلك كان الدليل على كونه قائما بالنفس غير الدليل على كونه عالما قادرًا، ولا من ضرورة نفي العالمية نفي الذات، فإن جماعة من المعطلة نفوا كونه عالما وقادرًا مع إقرارهم وعلمهم بثبوت الذات، ويستحيل رجوعهما إلى الحال فإنها لا تنفي على حيالها ولا تثبت، وقد أبطلنا القول بالحال فلم يبق إلا القسم الأخير وهو الرجوع إلى الصفات.
And the realities, when they differ in these respects, have differed in their essences, and one does not take the place of the other. Reason only distinguishes these meanings by these respects; otherwise, multiple accidents when they inhere in a single substrate, reason does not judge their difference and multiplicity except by the difference of their properties, effects, benefits, evidences, and relations. And it is impossible in reason for different properties to be combined in a single reality. So if it were permissible to posit an essence having the property of knowledge, power, will, and life, then it would be permissible to posit a single reality having the property of accident and substance, and it would be permissible to posit knowledge having the property of power, and it would be permissible to posit a color having the property of spatiality, and that would entail the conjunction of opposites, and that is among the most absurd things conceivable. What clarifies this—and it is the strongest thing held to in affirming attributes—is our statement: God is knowing, powerful, and the statement of the negator (muʿaṭṭil) for example: He is not knowing nor powerful—affirmation and negation inevitably. So the matter cannot be other than: either the affirmation and negation refer to the essence, or they refer to the attributes, or they refer to the states. It is impossible for them to refer to the essence, for it is conceivable without being described as knowing; because it is not necessary from the knowledge of it being a self-subsistent essence independent in every respect that one know that it is knowing. Therefore the proof for it being self-subsistent is other than the proof for it being knowing, powerful. Nor is it necessary from the negation of knowingness to negate the essence, for a group of the negators denied that He is knowing and powerful while acknowledging and knowing the subsistence of the essence. And it is impossible for them to refer to the state (ḥāl), for it is neither negated nor affirmed on its own. And we have refuted the doctrine of the state. Thus nothing remains except the last division: reference to the attributes.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof that the divine attributes (ṣifāt) are distinct from the divine essence (dhāt). The author begins by establishing a general principle: when realities differ in their properties, effects, and evidences, they are different in their essences; reason recognizes multiplicity only through such distinguishing features. It would be impossible for a single reality to contain mutually exclusive properties, like the simultaneous attribution of substance and accident to one thing. The absurd consequences show that if knowledge, power, will, and life were not distinct, then contradictory combinations would follow. The author then presents what he considers the strongest argument for the reality of attributes: the fact that we affirm and deny statements like 'God is knowing' and 'God is not knowing' regarding the same subject. This affirmation and negation must refer either to the essence, to the attributes, or to states (aḥwāl). They cannot refer to the essence because we can conceive of the essence without conceiving it as knowing; the proof for the essence's existence is different from the proof for its being knowing. Moreover, the Muʿaṭṭila (those who deny attributes) negate that God is knowing while still affirming the essence, proving that negation of attributes does not negate the essence. They cannot refer to states, as the author claims the theory of states (as held by some Muʿtazila) is invalid. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is that affirmation and negation refer to real attributes distinct from the essence.

Key Terms
حقائق
ذات
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does reason judge multiple accidents in one substrate as different?
A) Because they inhere in different substrates.
B) Because they have different properties, effects, and evidences.
C) Because reason knows them intuitively.
D) Because they are contradictory in themselves.

Key Takeaway
The distinction of divine attributes is rationally necessary; otherwise, impossible contradictions would follow from a single essence bearing multiple distinct properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's attributes are just one blurry concept, like 'He is all-powerful and all-knowing' without seeing them as distinct? How might this lazy assumption affect your awe of His specific perfections? Write down one attribute you will consciously reflect on as distinct today.
?
The author says reason distinguishes attributes by their unique properties. When you think about God's power and His mercy, do you treat them as the same 'thing'? What would change in your worship if you recognized each attribute as a distinct source of wonder and gratitude? Commit to praying specifically about one attribute this week.
?
If a single reality cannot have contradictory properties, then God's attributes must be perfectly harmonious. Can you identify any area in your life where you are trying to combine contradictory things—like chasing both worldly success and contentment in Allah? What absurdity are you tolerating, and what step will you take today to remove it?

The Strongest Argument from Predicate Affirmation
The author presents the strongest proof for distinct attributes by analyzing predicate affirmation/negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strongest argument for the distinct reality of attributes: the logical analysis of affirmative and negative statements about God.

Core Idea
When we say 'God is knowing' vs. 'God is not knowing,' the reference must be to real attributes, not essence or states.

Having established the general principle that properties necessitate distinct realities, the author now presents what he considers the most powerful proof. He analyzes the logical structure of predicates like 'knowing' and shows that they must refer to real attributes distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to grasp the logical argument.
Focus on the three options presented.
2
Identify the three possible referents
The author notes that affirmation/negation of predicates about God must refer either to the essence, the attributes, or the states (aḥwāl).
This is a classic division in kalam.
3
Understand why essence and states are ruled out
The essence is conceivable without predicates, and the negation of predicates does not negate the essence. The states are not independently real.
The author refutes the theory of states earlier.
4
Conclude that attributes are the only option
Thus, the predicates must refer to real attributes that exist distinct from the essence.
This is the strongest proof according to the author.

The Mu'tazila Objection on Attribute Multiplicity and the Ash'ari Response
The author presents the Mu'tazila objection and the Ash'ari response focusing on the shāhid/ghā'ib distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila critique of Ash'ari attribute theory and the Ash'ari response based on the distinction between the witnessed and unseen worlds.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim Ash'ari attributes are contradictory; Ash'aris reply that worldly logic doesn't apply to the divine.

This passage presents a sharp challenge from the Mu'tazila to the Ash'ari doctrine of divine attributes. The Mu'tazila argue that the Ash'ari position, which posits multiple distinct realities for a single essence, is inconsistent. Using examples like knowledge, power, and speech, they force the Ash'aris to explain how a single attribute can encompass diverse realities. The Ash'ari response draws a crucial distinction between the witnessed world (shāhid) and the unseen (ghā'ib), arguing that the principle of distinguishing realities by their properties applies only in the witnessed realm. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage first for the objection
Read the Arabic once focusing on the Mu'tazila argument. Notice how they use the example of speech (command, prohibition, etc.) to show that Ash'aris accept multiple realities within one attribute.
The Mu'tazila are not denying attributes, but claiming inconsistency in the Ash'ari position.
2
Identify the key examples
The Mu'tazila list knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and most prominently speech. Understand why each creates a problem for the Ash'aris: if a single attribute covers different realities, then the attribute is effectively multiple.
The strongest example is speech (kalām) because it includes opposites like command and prohibition.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari response
The author responds by distinguishing the witnessed world (shāhid) from the unseen (ghā'ib). The principle that meanings are distinguished by their properties and evidences holds in the witnessed world but not necessarily in the unseen. He points out that even the Mu'tazila accept this in some cases.
The author uses examples like pre-eternity, necessity of essence, etc., which are different realities but not seen as requiring multiple attributes in the unseen.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The passage breaks off mid-sentence. Think about what the full argument might be: the Mu'tazila object that command and prohibition are opposites in the witnessed world, yet Ash'aris say they are one attribute. The author likely meant to complete the rebuttal by showing that this does not force multiplicity in the unseen.
The key is that the rules of the witnessed world are not directly transferable to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : أنتم أول من أثبت حقائق مختلفة وخواص متباينة لذات واحدة؛ حيث قلتم : الرب تعالى عالم بعلم واحد يتعلق بجميع المعلومات، ومن المعلوم أن العلم بالسواد مثلا ليس يماثل العلم بالبياض بل يخالفه؛ لأن المثلين عندكم لا يجتمعان، وقد يجتمع العلمان المختلفان فعلمه سبحانه في حكم علوم مختلفة، وكذلك القدرة الأزلية تتعلق بمقدورات لا تتعلق القدرة الحادثة بها فهي في حكم قدر مختلفة، وكذلك الإرادة والسمع والبصر، وأظهر من ذلك كله الكلام فإنه صفة واحدة أزلية وهي في ذاتها أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار ووعد ووعيد، ومعلوم أن هذه حقائق مختلفة قد ثبتت لكلام واحد، فإن قلتم : هذه الحقائق ترجع إلى الوجوه والاعتبارات، فنحن نقول كذلك في ذات الباري سبحانه، وإن رددتم ذلك إلى الأحوال فمن مذهب أبي هاشم أنها أحوال، وعلى كل وجه قدرتموه يلزمكم مثله في الذات والصفات. وقولكم : إن المعاني إنما تتعرف بعددها من خواصها ومتعلقها وآثارها وأدلتها صحيح في الشاهد غير مسلم في الغائب، بل بإجماع منا في مسائل وانفراد منكم في مسائل خالفنا هذه القاعدة فإن القدم والوجوب بالذات والوحدة والقيام بالذات والبقاء والديمومية، وإنه أول وآخر وظاهر وباطن منزه عن المكان مقدس عن الزمان حقائق مختلفة لو اعتبرناها شاهدًا لكان لكل واحدة منها دليل خاص يدل تعددا في الصفات بالاتفاق، وعندكم الأمر والنهي في الشاهد يتباينان ويتضادان، وما يدل على الأمر غير ما يدل على النهي، وكذلك الخبر والاستخبار يتمايزان من حيث الحقيقة والدليل والمتعلق عليه، ومع ذلك لا يوجب فإن
The Mu'tazila said: You are the first to posit different realities and distinct properties for a single essence, for you say: The Lord—exalted is He—is knowing with a single knowledge that relates to all known things. Yet it is known that knowledge of black, for example, is not similar to knowledge of white, but rather differs from it; because the similar (two like things) according to you do not coexist, yet the two knowledges differ, so His knowledge—glorified is He—is in the category of different knowledges. Similarly, eternal power relates to things that temporal power does not relate to, so it is in the category of different powers. Likewise will, hearing, and sight. And most evident of all is speech, for it is a single eternal attribute, yet in its essence it is command, prohibition, report, question, promise, and threat. And it is known that these are different realities that have been established for a single speech. If you say: these realities return to aspects (wujuh) and considerations, then we say the same concerning the essence of the Creator—exalted is He. And if you refer that to states (ahwal), then from the doctrine of Abu Hashim they are states. And in any way you can conceive, the same would apply to you regarding the essence and attributes. And your statement: meanings are only known in their number by their properties, relations, effects, and evidences is correct in the witnessed world but not admitted in the unseen. Rather, by consensus of us in some matters and by your unique position in others, we have violated this rule. For pre-eternity, necessity of essence, oneness, self-subsistence, permanence, and everlastingness, and that He is First and Last, Manifest and Hidden, transcendent beyond place, holy beyond time—these are different realities; if we considered them in the witnessed world, each would have a specific proof indicating multiplicity of attributes by agreement. And according to you, command and prohibition in the witnessed world are mutually exclusive and contradictory, and what indicates command is other than what indicates prohibition, and likewise report and question are distinguished in terms of reality, proof, and relation, yet that does not necessitate... [then the text breaks off]
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila objection against the Ash'ari position on divine attributes and the author's response. The Mu'tazila argue that the Ash'aris themselves posit multiple distinct realities (ḥaqā'iq) and properties (khaṣā'iṣ) for one essence. For example, they say God knows with one knowledge that applies to all known things, yet knowledge of black and white are different (since like things cannot coexist, according to Ash'aris). Thus God's single knowledge is effectively multiple knowledges. Similarly, eternal power relates to things that temporal power does not, so it is like multiple powers. Will, hearing, sight follow the same pattern. The clearest example is speech (kalām): a single eternal attribute, yet in its essence it comprises command, prohibition, report, question, promise, threat—different realities. The Mu'tazila then say: if you (Ash'aris) claim these realities return to aspects (wujūh) and considerations, then we say the same about the divine essence itself (so attributes are not distinct). If you refer them to states (aḥwāl), that is Abu Hashim's doctrine. Either way, your position is inconsistent. The author responds: Your principle that meanings are distinguished by their properties, relations, and evidences is correct in the witnessed world (shāhid) but not admitted in the unseen (ghā'ib). In fact, we (Ash'aris) by consensus and you (Mu'tazila) in some cases violate this rule. For instance, attributes like pre-eternity, necessity of essence, oneness, self-subsistence, etc., are considered different realities, but in the witnessed world each would require a distinct proof, yet you do not posit multiple attributes corresponding to each (comp. the Mu'tazila view that God is knowing, powerful, etc., but they reduce attributes to the essence). Also, command and prohibition in the witnessed world are opposites and have different evidences, yet in God's speech they are one attribute. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the intended point is clear: consistency requires that the same reasoning that leads to multiplicity of properties in the witnessed world does not necessitate multiplicity in the unseen.

قدرة
كلام
شاهد
غائب
وجوه
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Mu'tazila are simply denying that God has attributes, but here they are not denying attributes per se; they are arguing that the Ash'ari position is internally inconsistent because it posits multiple distinct realities within a single attribute.
Correction: The Mu'tazila in this passage are critiquing the Ash'ari framework from within, not rejecting attributes outright. They use the Ash'aris' own principles (like the incompatibility of likes) to show that a single attribute cannot encompass multiple realities without implying multiplicity in the essence.

Checkpoint
What example do the Mu'tazila use to show the Ash'ari inconsistency regarding a single attribute with multiple realities?
A) The attribute of will.
B) The attribute of speech (kalām) as command, prohibition, etc.
C) The attribute of power.
D) The attribute of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection forces Ash'aris to clarify that divine attributes are not multiple in reality but distinct in aspect, and that the logic of the witnessed world cannot be naively applied to the divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your limited understanding of how things work in this world applies to God? When has that led you to doubt or misunderstand His attributes? Today, commit to letting God be transcendent beyond your logic.
?
The Mu'tazila thought that because knowledge of black and white differ for us, God's knowledge must also be multiple. Where in your life have you imposed human limitations on God, and how can you actively trust that His single knowledge perfectly knows things that seem contradictory to you?
?
If God's speech is one and includes command, prohibition, and promise, does your response to His commands and promises match the reverence that His unified speech demands? Repent for treating some parts of His word as less real than others.

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Divine Attributes Are Distinct Realities
Argument for real diversity in divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the diversity of divine attributes (like knowledge, power, speech) does not contradict their unity in God's essence, and how the author refutes reductionist views.

Core Idea
God's attributes have real diversity without compromising His unity.

In this passage, the author defends the classical Ash'ari position that divine attributes are distinct realities inhering in God's essence. He uses examples from created knowledge and power, and especially the multifaceted nature of divine speech, to show that diversity in attributes is real. He then counters objections that reduce this diversity to mere aspects or states, arguing that such reductionism leads to problems when applied to God's essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the examples given and the structure of the argument.
Notice that the author starts with a concrete example from created things.
2
Identify the three key attributes discussed
The author discusses knowledge, power, and speech. For each, note how he shows that the attribute encompasses multiple distinct realities.
Speech is highlighted as the most evident case.
3
Understand the refutation of reductionism
The author considers the objection that these realities are only aspects or states. He counters by applying the same logic to God's essence, showing inconsistency.
This refutes both the 'aspects' and 'states' (ahwal) positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعلوم أن العلم بالسواد مثلا ليس يماثل العلم بالبياض بل يخالفه ; لأن المثلين عندكم لا يجتمعان، وقد يجتمع العلمان المختلفان فعلمه سبحانه في حكم علوم مختلفة، وكذلك القدرة الأزلية تتعلق بمقدورات لا تتعلق القدرة الحادثة بها فهي في حكم قدر مختلفة، وكذلك الإرادة والسمع والبصر، وأظهر من ذلك كله الكلام فإنه صفة واحدة أزلية وهي في ذاتها أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار ووعد ووعيد، ومعلوم أن هذه حقائق مختلفة قد ثبتت لكلام واحد، فإن قلتم : هذه الحقائق ترجع إلى الوجوه والاعتبارات، فنحن نقول كذلك في ذات الباري سبحانه، وإن رددتم ذلك إلى الأحوال فمن مذهب أبي هاشم أنها أحوال، وعلى كل وجه قدرتموه يلزمكم مثله في الذات والصفات.
It is known that knowledge of blackness, for example, is not similar to knowledge of whiteness but rather differs from it, because two similars according to you do not coexist, and two different knowledges may coexist. So His knowledge, exalted be He, is in the ruling of diverse knowledges. Likewise, eternal power is related to things that temporal power is not related to, so it is in the ruling of diverse powers. Likewise, will, hearing, and sight. And more evident than all of that is speech, for it is a single eternal attribute, and in its essence it is command, prohibition, news, inquiry, promise, and threat. And it is known that these are different realities established for one speech. If you say: these realities reduce to aspects and considerations, we say likewise concerning the essence of the Creator, exalted be He. And if you refer that to states (ahwal), it is the doctrine of Abu Hashim that they are states, and in whatever way you conceive it, the like of it applies to you concerning the essence and attributes.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the reality of the diversity of divine attributes despite their unity in the essence. The author begins by pointing out that knowledges in the created world are not all alike; knowledge of black differs from knowledge of white, and two different knowledges can coexist in a single knower. Similarly, God's knowledge is like diverse knowledges in terms of the multiplicity of its objects. The same is argued for eternal power: it relates to things that temporal power does not, so it is as if it were a different power. Then the author turns to speech (kalam), a single eternal attribute, which in its essence comprises command, prohibition, news, inquiry, promise, and threat—all different realities. The objection that these are merely aspects or considerations is countered by applying the same reasoning to the essence of God: if aspects suffice to explain the diversity in attributes, they would also suffice to explain diversity in the essence itself. The author also mentions the doctrine of Abu Hashim (the Mu'tazili) that these aspects are 'states' (ahwal), and argues that any such approach would equally apply to the essence and attributes, leading to inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الإرادة
الكلام
الوجوه والاعتبارات
الأحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage claims that God's attributes are many separate entities.
Correction: It argues that the realities of the attributes are distinct even though they inhere in one essence, not that they are separate entities.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show that a single attribute can contain multiple distinct realities?
A) Power and will
B) Knowledge and speech
C) Speech as command and prohibition
D) Existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes have real distinct realities, and attempts to reduce them to mere aspects or states are inconsistent with the nature of God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's speech contains command and prohibition, do you obey His commands as real demands or just as suggestions? What step will you take today to align your action with one of His commands?
?
The passage argues that reducing God's attributes to mere aspects is inconsistent. How often do you reduce your understanding of Allah to only His mercy or only His justice? Commit to learning one more attribute this week and reflecting on it daily.
?
If the diversity of God's attributes is real, then your relationship with Him must engage with all of them—not just the ones you find comfortable. Which attribute of Allah do you most avoid, and what would change if you embraced it today?
