The Witness and the Unseen: Defending the Method for Divine Attributes
Response to objection about knowing attributes by properties, with command/prohibition example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the method of knowing attributes by their properties in the witnessed world does not necessarily apply to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes in the unseen are not multiplied by differences in their 'witness' properties.

The author addresses a key objection: if we know attributes by their specific properties and effects, why don't we conclude multiple divine attributes from distinct properties? He argues that the analogy fails, as seen in the example of divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first concedes the method in the witness but rejects it for the unseen.
2
Identify the objection and response
The opponent claimed that attributes are known by their properties. The author agrees for the witness but not for the unseen. Find the list of divine attributes and the argument that each would need a separate evidence.
The author mentions pre-eternity, necessity, oneness, etc.
3
Analyze the command/prohibition example
The author shows that command and prohibition differ in objects and evidences in the witness, yet in God they are one attribute. This proves that difference in properties does not force multiplicity.
Ask yourself: why does the author use this example?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولكم : إن المعاني إنما تتعرف بعددها من خواصها ومتعلقها وآثارها وأدلتها صحيح في الشاهد غير مسلم في الغائب، بل بإجماع منا في مسائل وانفراد منكم في مسائل خالفنا هذه القاعدة فإن القدم والوجوب بالذات والوحدة والقيام بالذات والبقاء والديمومة وإنه أول وآخر وظاهر وباطن منزه عن المكان مقدس عن الزمان حقائق مختلفة لو اعتبرناها شاهداً لكان لكل واحدة منها دليل خاص يدل على تعددا في الصفات بالاتفاق، وعندكم الأمر والنهي في الشاهد يتباينان ويتضادان، وما يدل على الأمر غير ما يدل على النهي، وكذلك الخبر والاستخبار يتمايزان من حيث الحقيقة والدليل والمتعلق عليه، ومع ذلك لا يوجب ذلك تعددا في صفة واحدة. فإن متعلق الأمر غير متعلق الخبر، والخبر يتعلق بالقديم، والأمر لا يتعلق به واختلافهما من حيث التعلق أوجب اختلافهما في الشاهد، ثم لم نستدل باختلافهما في الشاهد على اختلافهما في الغائب، بل كلامه أمر ونهي مع أن الأمر يتعلق بالمأمورات دون المنهيات والنهي يتعلق بالمنهيات دون المأمورات، والخبر يتعلق بكل ما يتعلق به العلم معدوما وموجودا وواجبا وجائزا ومستحيلا. وأما استدلالكم بطريق النفي والإثبات على أصل نفاة الأحوال غير مستقيم، فإن عندهم النفي والإثبات في كل شيء يرجعان إلى أمر مخصوص معين، فلا يتصور إثبات ذات على الإطلاق إلا في مجرد اللفظ، أو يرجع إلى وجه واعتبار، فقولنا : عالم يرجع إلى العلم بذات على وجه واعتبار وعند مثبتي الأحوال قولنا : عالم يرجع إلى إثبات ذات على حال كونه عالما، هذا كمن يعرف بكونه موجوداً ولا يعرف بكونه واحدا وغنيا وقديما أليس النفي والإثبات في ذلك لا يرجع إلى الصفات الزائدة على الذات، وإنما يرجع إلى الاعتبارات والوجوه؟ كذلك نقول الصفات فبطل استدلالكم بالنفي والإثبات
And your statement: meanings are only known in their number from their properties, relations, effects, and evidences - is correct in the witness, but not conceded in the unseen. Rather, by consensus from us in some matters and by your own admission in others, we have opposed this rule: for pre-eternity, necessity by essence, oneness, self-subsistence, everlastingness, perpetuity, and that He is the First and the Last, the Apparent and the Hidden, transcendent above place, holy above time - are different realities. If we considered them in the witness, each would have a specific evidence indicating multiplicity in attributes by agreement. And according to you, command and prohibition in the witness are distinct and opposed, and what indicates command is other than what indicates prohibition, likewise news and inquiry are distinguished in terms of reality, evidence, and what they relate to. Yet that does not necessitate multiplicity in a single attribute. For the object of command is not the object of news; news relates to the Eternal, command does not relate to Him; and their difference in terms of relation necessitated their difference in the witness. Yet we did not infer from their difference in the witness to their difference in the unseen. Rather, His speech is command and prohibition, while command relates to things commanded, not to things prohibited, and prohibition relates to things prohibited, not to things commanded; and news relates to everything knowledge relates to - non-existent and existent, necessary, possible, and impossible. As for your argumentation by way of negation and affirmation against the doctrine of the negators of states, it is not sound. For according to them, negation and affirmation in everything reduce to a specific determinate matter; so affirmation of essence in an absolute sense is only conceivable in mere utterance, or it reduces to an aspect and consideration. So our saying 'knowing' reduces to knowledge of essence in an aspect and consideration. And according to the affirmers of states, our saying 'knowing' reduces to affirmation of essence in the state of its being knowing. This is like one who is known to be existent but not known to be one, self-sufficient, and eternal. Is not negation and affirmation in that not reducing to attributes superadded to the essence, but only to considerations and aspects? Likewise we say concerning attributes; so your argument by negation and affirmation is nullified.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection about the method of knowing divine attributes. The author first restates the opponent's claim: that meanings (attributes) are known by their specific properties and evidences - a valid method in the witnessed world but not conceded for the unseen. However, the author argues that even in the unseen, we find that attributes like pre-eternity, necessity, oneness, etc. are different realities, and if we applied the witness method, each would require a separate evidence, leading to multiplicity in attributes by agreement (of both parties). Then the author addresses the specific case of command and prohibition in the witness: they are different and even opposed, yet they both belong to one speech attribute in God. The author points out that although command and prohibition have different objects (command relates to commanded things, prohibition to prohibited things), we do not conclude that God has two distinct speech attributes. Likewise, news and inquiry differ but not as attributes. Then the author critiques the opponent's appeal to 'negation and affirmation' (nafy wa ithbat) as a proof against the doctrine of states (ahwal). He explains that negation and affirmation always relate to specific matters; absolute affirmation of essence is only verbal or reduces to aspects. The affirmers of states (muthbit al-ahwal) interpret 'knowing' as affirming essence in a state. The author draws an analogy: one may be known as existent but not as one or eternal; negation and affirmation there do not imply superadded attributes. So the same applies to divine attributes, and the opponent's argument fails.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الغائب
الأمر والنهي
الخبر والاستخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage denies that divine attributes have distinctive features or properties.
Correction: The passage does not deny that divine attributes have distinctive features; it argues that multiplicity in the witness does not entail multiplicity in the divine, as shown by the command/prohibition example.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say we cannot infer from the difference between command and prohibition in the witness to their difference in the unseen?
A) Because command and prohibition are not different in the unseen
B) Because they are both part of one speech attribute in God, and their objects differ without causing multiplicity
C) Because the opponent rejects analogy entirely
D) Because command and prohibition are identical in God

Key Takeaway
The method of knowing attributes by their properties in the witness does not automatically apply to the unseen, as shown by the example of command and prohibition being one attribute in God despite their differences in the witness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider attributes of God beyond your experience (like His being First and Last), do you tend to imagine them in human terms? How does this passage call you to submit to revelation rather than your own reasoning? What specific attribute will you strive to accept as He describes it, without analogy to creation?
?
The passage shows that command and prohibition are one attribute in God. Does this affect how you view His commands in your life? Are you tempted to think that different commands come from different 'parts' of God? How can you cultivate a unified reverence for His speech, obeying even when you don't understand the relation?
?
Reflect on an area where you have judged the unseen by the witness - perhaps expecting God to act like a human authority. What step will you take today to trust that His ways are not your ways, and to accept His description of His own attributes without forcing them into your framework?

Refuting the Negation and Affirmation Argument Against States
Critique of the negation/affirmation argument against the affirmers of states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the opponent's use of negation and affirmation to argue against the doctrine of states.

Core Idea
Negation and affirmation always reduce to specific aspects, not to superadded attributes.

The author now turns to a more technical argument. The opponent claimed that their method of negation and affirmation disproves the doctrine of states. The author shows that this argument is unsound because negation and affirmation always relate to particular matters and aspects, not to separate attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the shift to the negation/affirmation argument.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent used the principle that negation and affirmation always reduce to specific matters to argue against the affirmers of states (who posit states as real attributes). Understand what the opponent claims.
The opponent thinks that if we say 'knowing', it must reduce to affirmation of essence with a specific consideration, not a real attribute.
3
Follow the author's critique and analogy
The author argues that negation and affirmation always relate to specific matters; absolute affirmation is only verbal. He uses the analogy of being known as existent but not as one or eternal to show that negation/affirmation do not imply superadded attributes.
The analogy: a person may be known as existent but not as one; that does not mean oneness and existence are separate attributes.

Knowledge Is One Reality: The Ash'ari View
The Ash'aris state that knowledge as such is one reality and differences arise only from objects known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that knowledge is a single reality, with differences arising only from the objects known, and why this matters for divine attributes.

Core Idea
Knowledge is one reality; diversity comes from known objects alone.

This lesson introduces the foundational Ash'ari doctrine regarding the nature of knowledge. According to the Sifatiyya, knowledge in itself is a single undivided reality, not a bundle of properties. The variations we observe in acts of knowledge are not due to differences in the essence of knowledge but solely to the objects toward which knowledge is directed. The passage explains this principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Pay attention to the technical terms like 'العلم', 'الحقيقة الواحدة', 'المتعلق', and 'الخاصية'.
2
Identify the central claim
The Sifatiyya assert that knowledge is one reality (حقيقة واحدة) and not one property (خاصية واحدة). Understand what 'one reality' means here: knowledge has a single essence.
3
Understand the role of objects
Knowledges differ by consideration of their objects (باعتبار متعلقاتها) and are alike when the object is the same. This is the key to the doctrine: diversity comes from the known, not from the essence of knowledge.
4
Explore the deeper reasoning
The passage gives a 'secret' (السر): knowledge follows the known in existence and non-existence. Neither imparts an attribute to the other. Reflect on why this supports the unity of knowledge.
5
Apply to divine attributes
Consider how this applies to God's knowledge. Does God's knowledge become multiple because He knows many things? According to this view, no—it remains one, as differences are only from the objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : العلم من حيث هو علم حقيقة واحدة وليس خاصية واحدة، وإنما تختلف العلوم باعتبار متعلقاتها وتتماثل باتحاد المتعلق، وليس ذلك الاعتبار نفس العلم عن حقيقة العلمية حتى لو قدرنا تقديرا لمحال جواز بقاء العلم الحادث لتعلق العلم الحادث بمعلومين ومعلومات. والسر فيه أن العلم على كل حال يتبع المعلوم عدما ووجودا، فلا يكسب المعلوم صفة ولا يكتسب عنه صفة، فالعلوم تختلف في الشاهد لاستحالة البقاء
The Attribute-ists (al-Sifatiyya) said: Knowledge, insofar as it is knowledge, is a single reality and not a single property. Knowledges only differ by consideration of their objects and are alike by the unity of the object. And that consideration is not the knowledge itself concerning the reality of knowledge-ness, such that even if we supposed an impossibility, the permissibility of the survival of temporal knowledge due to the relation of temporal knowledge to two known things and multiple items of knowledge. And the secret in it is that knowledge in every case follows the known in non-existence and existence, so the known acquires no attribute from it nor does it acquire an attribute from it. Thus knowledges differ in the witness because of the impossibility of survival...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the position of the 'Sifatiyya' (likely the Ash'ari or early Sunni attribute-ists) on the nature of knowledge. They assert that knowledge as such is a single reality, not a single specific property. The differences among various instances of knowledge are due to the objects (mut'allaqat) they relate to; when the object is the same, the knowledges are alike. Crucially, the difference arising from the object is not intrinsic to the knowledge itself. The author then clarifies with a subtle point: even if we suppose an impossible scenario (like the survival of temporal knowledge after its object changes), the principle holds. The underlying reason (sirr) is that knowledge always follows the known in its existence or non-existence. Knowledge does not confer any attribute upon the known, nor does it acquire an attribute from the known. Therefore, in the witnessed world, knowledges differ because of the impossibility of the known object's survival (or persistence of the same knowledge). The passage is cut off, but the argument appears to explain how knowledge can be one in essence yet diverse in application.

Key Terms
العلم
الحقيقة الواحدة
المتعلق
الخاصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if knowledge is one reality, then knowing different things would require multiple knowledges, implying multiplicity in God's knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that knowledge is one in essence; apparent multiplicity comes solely from the diversity of objects known, not from any division in knowledge itself.

Checkpoint

A) A difference in the essence of knowledge itself
B) A difference in the objects of knowledge
C) A difference in the time of knowledge
D) A difference in the knower's capacity

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a single reality; its apparent multiplicity is due only to the diversity of its objects, not to any division in its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If knowledge follows its object, then what does your knowledge of Allah truly reflect? Does it follow Allah as He is, or does it follow your own preconceptions and desires? What change must you make today to align your knowledge with its true Object?
?
The passage says knowledge does not acquire an attribute from the known. This means what you know does not make you inherently greater. When you learn something, do you secretly feel superior? How can you practice humility in light of this truth?

Command and News in God's Speech: Unity Amidst Diversity
Argues that command and news differ in attachment, yet God's speech combines both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine speech can encompass command, prohibition, and news without implying multiplicity in God's attribute of speech.

Core Idea
In God, command and news coexist without conflicting because attachment is diverse, not the essence.

This lesson addresses a subtle question: if command and news refer to different objects, does that mean they are separate attributes in God? The author argues otherwise, using the distinction between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). By the end, you'll see how God's speech remains one while relating to many things.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once to absorb the rhythm and key terms without analyzing deeply.
Notice the concise style of theological argument.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for 'amr' (command), 'nahy' (prohibition), 'khabar' (news), 'shahid' (witnessed), 'gha'ib' (unseen). These are central.
These terms appear multiple times; understanding them is crucial.
3
Follow the argument
The passage first states difference in attachment, then denies inference from shahid to gha'ib, then affirms togetherness in God. Trace the logical flow.
The key move is 'ثم لم نستدل' – we did not infer.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal point
Ask yourself: Why does this defense preserve the unity (tawhid) of attributes? How does it counter anthropomorphism?
This connects to broader 'aqida.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متعلق الأمر غير متعلق الخبر، والخبر يتعلق بالقديم، والأمر لا يتعلق به واختلافهما من حيث التعلق أوجب اختلافهما في الشاهد، ثم لم نستدل باختلافهما في الشاهد على اختلافهما في الغائب، بل كلامه أمر ونهي مع أن الأمر يتعلق بالمأمورات دون المنهيات والنهي يتعلق بالمنهيات دون المأمورات، والخبر يتعلق بكل ما يتعلق به العلم معدوما وموجودًا وواجبا وجائزا ومستحيلا.
The object of command is not the object of news, and news pertains to the eternal, and command does not pertain to Him. Their difference in respect of attachment necessitated their difference in the witnessed world. Then we did not infer from their difference in the witnessed world their difference in the unseen. Rather, His speech is command and prohibition while command pertains to commanded things not prohibited things, and prohibition pertains to prohibited things not commanded things, and news pertains to everything that knowledge pertains to: nonexistent, existent, necessary, possible, and impossible.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage argues about the nature of divine speech (al-kalam). It begins by stating that the object of command (amr) is different from the object of news (khabar). News pertains to the eternal (God), while command does not pertain to Him. Their difference in attachment (ta‘alluq) causes them to differ in the witnessed world (al-shahid). However, the author then asserts that we do not infer from their difference in the witnessed world that they must be different in the unseen world (al-gha’ib). On the contrary, God’s speech is both command and prohibition, even though command attaches to commanded things and not to prohibited things, and prohibition attaches to prohibited things and not to commanded things. News, on the other hand, attaches to everything that knowledge attaches to, whether nonexistent, existent, necessary, possible, or impossible. Thus, the diversity of attachment does not necessitate diversity of the essence of God’s speech. The point is to defend the unity of the divine attribute of speech against those who might argue that differences in the objects imply different realities.

Correction: The author shows that God's speech is both command and prohibition, so the difference in attachment does not force separate attributes. Rather, the unity of divine speech encompasses all modalities.

Refuting the Deniers of States via Negation and Affirmation
Refutes the denial of states via the negation/affirmation argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from negation and affirmation fails to disprove the existence of states (ahwal) and how the correct position views attributes as aspects.

Core Idea
Negation and affirmation refer to aspects, not extra entities.

This passage tackles a key objection from those who deny the reality of states (ahwal). They argue that when we say 'God is knowing,' the negation and affirmation of attributes cannot refer to anything beyond the essence itself — otherwise we would have to affirm extra entities. The author counters by showing that even the deniers themselves rely on aspects and considerations, not separate attributes. Understanding this refutation clarifies the subtle debate over divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then consult the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall argument before analyzing details.
Pay attention to the key terms: نفاة الأحوال, مثبتي الأحوال, الاعتبارات والوجوه.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage contrasts the deniers of states (نفاة الأحوال) with the affirmers of states (مثبتي الأحوال). Notice how each camp interprets the phrase 'God is knowing' (عالم).
The deniers reduce attributes to mere aspects; the affirmers take them as real states.
3
Analyze the analogy
The author gives an example: someone known as 'existent' but not known as 'one' or 'eternal'. In that case, negation and affirmation go back to considerations, not extra attributes. Reflect on how this analogy supports the affirmers' position.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage ends with 'so your argument by negation and affirmation is invalid.' Understand why the analogy shows that the deniers' own logic can be turned against them.
The key point: both sides agree that negation and affirmation involve aspects; the dispute is only about whether these aspects are 'extra' or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استدلالكم بطريق النفي والإثبات على أصل نفاة الأحوال غير مستقيم، فإن عندهم النفي والإثبات في كل شيء يرجعان إلى أمر مخصوص معين، فلا يتصور إثبات ذات على الإطلاق إلا في مجرد اللفظ، أو يرجع إلى وجه واعتبار، فقولنا : عالم يرجع إلى العلم بذات على وجه واعتبار وعند مثبتي الأحوال قولنا : عالم يرجع إلى إثبات ذات على حال كونه عالما، هذا كمن يعرف بكونه موجوداً ولا يعرف بكونه واحدا وغنيا، وقديما أليس النفي والإثبات في ذلك لا يرجع إلى الصفات الزائدة على الذات، وإنما يرجع إلى الاعتبارات والوجوه؟ كذلك نقول الصفات فبطل استدلالكم بالنفي والإثبات
As for your argument by way of negation and affirmation against the principle of the deniers of states, it is not sound. For according to them, negation and affirmation in everything go back to a specific determined matter. So affirmation of an essence absolutely is not conceivable except in mere words, or it goes back to an aspect and consideration. So our saying 'knowing' goes back to knowledge of an essence in a certain aspect and consideration. And according to the affirmers of states, our saying 'knowing' goes back to affirmation of an essence in a state of being knowing. This is like one who is known to be existent but not known to be one and self-sufficient and eternal. Is it not that negation and affirmation in that do not go back to attributes superadded to the essence, but rather go back to considerations and aspects? Similarly we say concerning the attributes. So your argument by negation and affirmation is invalid.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage refutes an argument used by the deniers of states (nufat al-ahwal) concerning negation and affirmation. The author begins by stating that the opponents' argument is unsound. According to the deniers, negation and affirmation in every case refer to a specific determined matter. Therefore, affirming an essence absolutely is possible only in mere words, or it refers to an aspect and consideration. For example, when we say 'knowing' (عالم), it refers to knowledge of an essence in a particular aspect. However, for the affirmers of states (muthbiti al-ahwal), saying 'knowing' means affirming an essence that is in a state of being knowing. The author then draws an analogy: consider someone who is known to be existent but not known to be one, self-sufficient, and eternal. In that case, negation and affirmation do not go back to attributes superadded to the essence, but rather to considerations and aspects. The same applies to divine attributes: they are not extra entities but aspects. Therefore, the opponents' argument is invalid.

Correction: The passage shows that negation and affirmation can refer to mere considerations and aspects, not necessarily extra entities.

Unity of Knowledge and Diversity of Its Objects
Explains that knowledge is one reality but differs by its objects, applied to divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attributists argue that knowledge is one reality but differs by its objects, and how this applies to divine speech without implying multiple attributes in God.

Core Idea
Knowledge is one reality; its diversity comes from objects, not from the attribute itself.

This passage from the attributists tackles a key question: if God knows everything, is His knowledge multiple? The author explains that knowledge itself is a single reality; differences arise from what is known. The same principle applies to divine speech—command, prohibition, news, inquiry—all are one reality of speech, differing only by their objects. This sets the stage for understanding how God's attributes remain singular while relating to many things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the flow from knowledge to speech.
The passage uses a technical style; focus on the main claim and supporting reasoning.
2
Grasp the key distinction
Identify the difference between 'one reality' (haqiqa wahida) and 'one property' (khassa wahida). Why does the author stress that knowledge is not a single property?
This distinction is crucial for avoiding the misconception that God's knowledge would be multiple.
3
Follow the application to speech
See how command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are all united in the reality of speech. The diversity comes from their objects or considerations, not from speech itself.
This reinforces the same pattern: one reality, many objects.
4
Contrast with the impossible case
The passage ends by contrasting the unity of knowledge/speech with the impossibility of one essence having two distinct properties like knowledge and power simultaneously.
This shows the limits of the analogy: attributes themselves are distinct realities, not merely considerations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الصفاتية : العلم من حيث هو علم حقيقة واحدة وليس خاصية واحدة، وإنما تختلف العلوم باعتبار متعلقاتها وتتماثل باتحاد المتعلق، وليس ذلك الاعتبار نفس العلم عن حقيقة العلمية حتى لو قدرنا تقديرا لمحال جواز بقاء العلم الحادث لتعلق العلم الحادث بمعلومين ومعلومات. والسر فيه أن العلم على كل حال يتبع المعلوم عدما ووجودًا، فلا يكسب المعلوم صفة ولا يكتسب عنه صفة، فالعلوم تختلف في الشاهد لاستحالة البقاء وتقدير اختلاف المتعلقات والعلم القديم في حكم علوم مختلفة لا في حكم خواص متباينة، وكذلك نقول في الكلام فإن الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار شملتهما حقيقة الكلام فاجتمعت في حقيقة واحدة واختلفت اعتبارات تلك الحقيقة بالنسبة إلى المتعلقات، وهذا غير مستبعد، وإنما المستحيل كل الاستحالة إثبات ذات واحدة لها خاصية العلم وخاصية القدرة والإرادة، إلى غير ذلك من سائر الصفات، حتى يلزم أن يقال : يعلم من حيث يقدر ويقدر من حيث يعلم، ويقدر بعالمية ويعلم بقادرية، وتجتمع صفتان وخاصيتان لذات واحدة وذلك غير معقول.
The attributists said: Knowledge insofar as it is knowledge is one reality and not one property. Rather, knowledges differ by consideration of their objects and become similar by the unity of the object. And that consideration is not the same as knowledge from the reality of knowledge-ness. Even if we were to suppose an impossible estimation of the permissibility of the permanence of temporal knowledge because of the attachment of temporal knowledge to two known things and information. And the secret in that is that knowledge in every case follows the known in nonexistence and existence. So it does not give the known a quality nor does it acquire a quality from it. So knowledges differ in the witnessed world due to the impossibility of permanence and the estimation of the difference of objects, and the eternal knowledge is in the judgment of different knowledges, not in the judgment of different properties. Similarly we say concerning speech: for command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are encompassed by the reality of speech, so they are united in one reality and differ in considerations of that reality relative to the objects. And this is not improbable. Rather, the utterly impossible is the affirmation of one essence possessing the property of knowledge and the property of power and will, up to the rest of the attributes, such that it would be necessary to say: He knows insofar as He wills and wills insofar as He knows, and He wills by His knowledge and knows by His power, and two attributes and two properties would combine in one essence, and that is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the attributists (al-sifatiyya) on knowledge and speech. It defines knowledge (al-‘ilm) as one reality (haqiqa wahida) when considered as knowledge itself, but it is not a single property (khassa). Knowledges differ by their objects (muta‘allaqat) and become similar when the object is one. The consideration (i‘tibar) of the object is not identical to the reality of knowledge. Even an impossible hypothesis illustrates this: if temporal knowledge could persist while attaching to multiple objects, it would still be one knowledge. The secret is that knowledge follows the known in existence and non-existence, not conferring or acquiring quality. So in the witnessed world, knowledges differ due to the impossibility of permanence and the variation of objects. Eternal divine knowledge is in the judgment of different knowledges (since it knows multiple objects) but not in the judgment of different properties. The same applies to speech: command, prohibition, news, and inquiry are all encompassed by the single reality of speech, differing only in considerations relative to objects. This is not improbable. What is utterly impossible is to affirm that a single essence has both the property of knowledge and the property of power and will, which would require saying that He knows insofar as He wills and wills by His knowledge, combining two distinct properties in one essence—that is inconceivable. Thus, the unity of knowledge and speech is defended, while the diversity of essential attributes is denied.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the multiplicity is in the objects, not in the attribute itself. Eternal knowledge is one reality even though it knows many things.

A) Because knowledge has multiple realities.
B) Because the objects of knowledge differ and permanence is impossible for temporal knowledge.

D) Because God's knowledge is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is one reality; multiplicity comes from objects, not from the attribute itself; the same holds for speech, but distinct attributes like knowledge and power cannot be combined in one essence.

Negative Attributes vs Positive Attributes of Essence
Distinguishes negative attributes of essence from positive ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between negative attributes of essence (which are reduced to negations) and positive attributes like knowledge and power, which are distinct, real meanings.

Core Idea
Some divine attributes deny imperfections; others affirm real, distinct meanings.

The passage explains that not all attributes of God are positive. Some are purely negative, describing what God is not, while others like knowledge and power are positive and distinct. This distinction is crucial for a proper understanding of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author contrasts two types of attributes.
The passage is divided into a list of negative attributes followed by a discussion of positive ones.
2
Identify the five negative attributes
Find the five attributes listed: qadim, wahid, ghani, wajib bi-dhatih, qa'im bi-nafsih. Note how each is defined as a negation.
3
Contrast with positive attributes
Read the section on 'alim and qadir. Notice that they are explained as having real meanings (knowledge and power) with distinct effects.
The passage emphasizes that these two attributes are not identical.
4
Understand the Mu'tazila reference
The author appeals to the Mu'tazila's own admission that 'knowledge-ness' and 'power-ness' are distinct concepts.
This strengthens the case for positive attributes being distinct realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما صفات الذات التي ليست وراء الذات فإنها راجعة إلى النفي لا إلى الإثبات، فمعنى كونه قديما أنه لا أول له ولا آخر ومعنى كونه واحدا : أنه لا شريك له ولا قسيم ومعنى كونه غنيا : أنه لا حاجة له ولا فقر، ومعنى كونه واجبا بذاته : أن وجوده غير مستفاد من غيره، ومعنى كونه قائما بنفسه : أنه غير محتاج إلى مكان وزمان بل هو مستغن على الإطلاق عن الموجد والمكان والمحل، بخلاف قولنا : إنه عالم قادر ، فإن العالم ذو العلم حقيقة ، والقادر ذو القدرة حقيقة، والإحكام والإتقان دليل العلم وأثره، والوجود والحصول دليل القدرة وأثره ، فهما معنيان معقولان بحقيقتهما فلا يتحدان في ذات كما لا يتحدان بذاتهما حتى يكون حقيقة أحدهما حقيقة الثاني، وكذلك قلتم معاشر المعتزلة : إن العالمية ليس بعينها معنى القادرية الجواز العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل بالثاني.
As for the attributes of essence that are not beyond the essence, they revert to negation, not affirmation. So the meaning of His being eternal is that He has no beginning and no end. The meaning of His being one is that He has no partner and no counterpart. The meaning of His being self-sufficient is that He has no need and no poverty. The meaning of His being necessary in Himself is that His existence is not acquired from another. The meaning of His being self-subsistent is that He is not in need of place and time, but rather He is absolutely independent of the originator, place, and locus. Contrary to our saying: He is knowing, able. For the knowing is one who possesses knowledge truly, and the able is one who possesses power truly. Precision and perfection are the proof of knowledge and its effect, and existence and occurrence are the proof of power and its effect. So they are two meanings intelligible in their reality, so they do not unite in an essence just as they do not unite in themselves such that the reality of one becomes the reality of the other. And likewise you, O group of Mu'tazila, say: That 'knowledge-ness' is not identical to the meaning of 'power-ness', because it is possible to know one while being ignorant of the other.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of divine attributes: those that are not beyond the essence (la tasta‘iq al-dhat) — i.e., attributes of essence that are purely negative — and those that are positive and distinct. The negative attributes include being eternal (qadim), one (wahid), self-sufficient (ghani), necessary in Himself (wajib bi-dhatih), and self-subsistent (qa’im bi-nafsih). Each is explained as a negation: eternal means no beginning or end; one means no partner or counterpart; self-sufficient means no need or poverty; necessary means existence not from another; self-subsistent means no need of place or time. In contrast, saying He is knowing (alim) and able (qadir) involves positive realities: the knowing possesses knowledge truly, and the able possesses power truly. The effects of knowledge (precision and perfection) and power (existence and occurrence) are distinct. Therefore, these two meanings are intelligible distinct realities and do not unite in one essence such that the reality of one becomes the other. The author then appeals to the Mu‘tazila, noting that they themselves admit that 'knowledge-ness' (al-‘alimiyya) is not identical to 'power-ness' (al-qadiriyya), because one can know something while being ignorant of another, showing they are distinct concepts. Thus, the passage affirms that positive attributes are real and distinct, while negative ones are reduced to negations.

الإثبات

غني
واجب بذاته
قائم بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes of God are positive and additional entities.
Correction: The passage clarifies that some attributes of essence are purely negative, indicating what God is not, such as eternal meaning 'no beginning'.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT an example of a negative attribute of essence according to the passage?
A) Being eternal (qadim).
B) Being one (wahid).
C) Being knowing (alim).
D) Being necessary in Himself (wajib bi-dhatih).

Key Takeaway
Negative attributes of essence are not additional entities but negations of imperfection; positive attributes like knowledge and power are real distinct meanings.

The passage distinguishes attributes that negate from those that affirm. Which of your own descriptions of Allah are actually negations of imperfection, and which are affirmations of His perfection? How does this distinction affect the way you call upon Him?

Abu Hashim's States vs. Attributes
States that Abu Hashim's theory of states makes no real difference from the theory of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Abu Hashim's theory of states (ahwal) is functionally equivalent to the Ash'ari theory of attributes, making the disagreement merely terminological.

Core Idea
States vs. attributes: a distinction without a difference.

The Mu'tazili theologian Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i proposed a theory of 'states' (ahwal) as an alternative to divine attributes. In this brief passage, he argues that his own theory ultimately collapses into the same position as the Ash'ari attribute theory, revealing a hidden agreement between the two schools.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the short Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: العالمية, القادرية, حال, مفيد, أصحاب الأحوال, أصحاب الصفات.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's claim
Note that he calls 'knowledge-ness' and 'power-ness' states (حال), and says their producer is also a state that necessitates all states.
This is his formulation of the divine attributes in terms of states.
3
Understand the conclusion
Abu Hashim concludes that there is no real difference between his school (adherents of states) and the Ash'aris (adherents of attributes). Ask yourself why he says that.
4
Reflect on the implications
This passage shows that the disagreement is merely terminological: both positions affirm extra-mental realities distinct from the essence. Reflect on why this matters for Islamic theology.
It highlights the necessity of affirming distinct attributes in the divine essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم: العالمية حال والقادرية حال، ومفيدهما حال يوجب الأحوال كلها، فلا فرق في الحقيقة بين أصحاب الأحوال وبين أصحاب الصفات.
Abu Hashim said: Knowledge-ness is a state and power-ness is a state, and their producer is a state that necessitates all states. So in reality there is no difference between the adherents of states and the adherents of attributes.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This short passage presents the view of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a Mu'tazili theologian, on the theory of states (ahwal). According to him, 'knowledge-ness' (al-‘alimiyya) and 'power-ness' (al-qadiriyya) are states (hal, pl. ahwal) that inhere in the essence. The producer or originator (mufid) of these states is itself a state that entails all other states. The conclusion is that, in reality, there is no difference between the proponents of states (ashab al-ahwal, i.e., the Bahshamiyya school of Mu'tazila) and the proponents of attributes (ashab al-sifat, i.e., the Ash'aris). Both ultimately affirm some kind of extra-mental realities (whether states or attributes) that are distinct from the essence. Thus, the theory of states collapses into the theory of attributes, making the disagreement merely terminological.

Key Terms
أبو هاشم
العالمية
القادرية
حال
مفيد
أصحاب الأحوال
أصحاب الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Abu Hashim's theory of states is completely different from the Ash'ari theory of attributes, representing a genuine alternative.
Correction: The passage shows that Abu Hashim himself admits the two positions are equivalent; both affirm distinct realities in the divine essence, so the difference is only terminological.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the adherents of states (ahwal) and the adherents of attributes (sifat)?
A) They have a fundamental disagreement.
B) There is no real difference between them.
C) The adherents of states reject attributes.
D) Abu Hashim alone held the theory of states.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's theory of states is functionally identical to the theory of attributes, revealing that both schools affirm extra-mental distinctions in the divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage suggests that two seemingly opposing views are actually the same. Is there a disagreement you have with another Muslim that, upon honest examination, is merely verbal? Would you be willing to reconcile?

The Impossibility of Identifying Knowledge and Power
An argument against identifying knowledge and power, with a speech analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari tradition argues that God's knowledge and power are distinct attributes with different meanings and cannot be identified as one.

Core Idea
Knowledge and power are distinct realities with different effects; identifying them leads to absurdity.

The author uses the example of divine speech to show that multiple modalities can coexist in one reality. However, he then argues that knowledge and power are special: they are distinct properties that cannot be reduced to one another without contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice how the speech analogy is introduced.
2
Examine the speech analogy
The author compares knowledge to speech: command, prohibition, etc. share one reality but differ in relation to objects. Understand this point.
This is not the main argument, just a comparison.
3
Identify the argument against identity
The author says it is impossible for one essence to have both property of knowledge and property of power in such a way that they are interchangeable. That would lead to contradictions.
This is the core of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقدير اختلاف المتعلقات والعلم القديم في حكم علوم مختلفة لا في حكم خواص متباينة، وكذلك نقول في الكلام فإن الأمر والنهي والخبر والاستخبار شملتهما حقيقة الكلام فاجتمعت في حقيقة واحدة واختلفت اعتبارات تلك الحقيقة بالنسبة إلى المتعلقات، وهذا غير مستبعد، وإنما المستحيل كل الاستحالة إثبات ذات واحدة لها خاصية العلم وخاصية القدرة والإرادة، إلى غير ذلك من سائر الصفات، حتى يلزم أن يقال : يعلم من حيث يقدر ويقدر من حيث يعلم، ويقدر بعالمية ويعلم بقادرية، وتجتمع صفتان وخاصيتان لذات واحدة وذلك غير معقول. وأما صفات الذات التي ليست وراء الذات فإنها راجعة إلى النفي لا إلى الإثبات، فمعنى كونه قديما أنه لا أول له ولا آخر ومعنى كونه واحدا : أنه لا شريك له ولا قسيم ومعنى كونه غنيا : أنه لا حاجة له ولا فقر، ومعنى كونه واجبا بذاته : أن وجوده غير مستفاد من غيره، ومعنى كونه قائما بنفسه : أنه غير محتاج إلى مكان وزمان بل هو مستغن على الإطلاق عن الموجد والمكان والمحل، بخلاف قولنا : إنه عالم قادر ، فإن العالم ذو العلم حقيقة ، والقادر ذو القدرة حقيقة، والإحكام والإتقان دليل العلم وأثره، والوجود والحصول دليل القدرة وأثرها ، فهما معنيان معقولان بحقيقتهما فلا يتحدان في ذات كما لا يتحدان بذاتهما حتى يكون حقيقة أحدهما حقيقة الثاني، وكذلك قلتم معاشر المعتزلة : إن العالمية ليس بعينها معنى القادرية الجواز العلم بأحدهما مع الجهل بالثاني.
And the estimation of the difference of the related objects: the eternal knowledge in the judgment of different sciences, not in the judgment of distinct properties. Likewise we say concerning speech: commanding, forbidding, informing, and inquiring are encompassed by the reality of speech, so they are united in one reality and the considerations of that reality differ with respect to the related objects. This is not improbable. The utterly impossible is the affirmation of a single essence having the property of knowledge and the property of power and will, and so on from the rest of attributes, such that it follows that one should say: He knows inasmuch as He is powerful and He is powerful inasmuch as He knows, and He is powerful by virtue of being knowing and He knows by virtue of being powerful, and two attributes and two properties would be combined in one essence, and that is not intelligible. As for the attributes of essence that are nothing beyond the essence, they revert to negation, not affirmation. So the meaning of His being pre-eternal is that He has no beginning nor end; the meaning of His being one is that He has no partner nor divider; the meaning of His being self-sufficient is that He has no need nor poverty; the meaning of His being necessary by Himself is that His existence is not derived from another; the meaning of His being self-subsisting is that He is not in need of place or time, but is absolutely independent of the giver of existence, place, and substrate. Unlike our saying that He is knowing and powerful: for the knowing one really possesses knowledge, and the powerful one really possesses power. Precision and perfection are the evidence of knowledge and its effect; existence and occurrence are the evidence of power and its effect. So they are two intelligible meanings in their reality; they do not unite in an essence just as they do not unite in their own selves such that the reality of one becomes the reality of the other. Similarly, O company of the Mu'tazila, you said that being-knowing is not the very meaning of being-powerful; it is permissible to know one while being ignorant of the other.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the nature of divine attributes. The author begins by using the example of divine speech: command, prohibition, information, and inquiry all share the single reality of speech, differing only in their relation to objects. This shows that a single essence can have multiple modalities without contradiction. However, the author immediately distinguishes this from the case of knowledge and power, where it is impossible for one essence to possess the property of knowledge and the property of power in such a way that they are identical or interchangeable—otherwise one would have to say that God knows because He is powerful and is powerful because He knows, which is absurd. Then the author introduces a key classification: some divine attributes, which he calls 'attributes of essence that are nothing beyond the essence,' are purely negative. These include being eternal (no beginning/end), one (no partner), self-sufficient (no need), necessary by Himself (existence not derived), and self-subsisting (independent of place and time). These attributes merely deny imperfections. In contrast, the positive attributes like 'knowing' and 'powerful' connote real qualities: the knowing one really possesses knowledge, and the powerful one really possesses power. The effects of knowledge (precision, perfection) and power (existence, occurrence) are distinct. Thus, the two meanings are distinct in essence and cannot be reduced to a single reality, as the Mu'tazila themselves admit that one can know one meaning while ignoring the other.

Key Terms
العلم
القدرة
الكلام
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since God is simple, all attributes are identical in meaning.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge and power have distinct effects and cannot be identical; otherwise absurdities follow.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it impossible for God's knowledge and power to be identical in meaning?
A) Because they are purely negative attributes.
B) Because they would then require each other, leading to contradiction.
C) Because the effects of knowledge and power are different, and combining them would lead to absurd statements.
D) Because the Mu'tazila deny both.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and power are distinct attributes with separate meanings; their identification leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge and power, have you ever slipped into thinking they are the same thing? What in your daily worship or supplication reveals that you treat them as distinct? Will you commit today to reflect on God's attributes as distinct realities, and consider how each attribute uniquely demands something from you?
?
The passage says that identifying knowledge and power leads to saying 'He knows insofar as He is powerful'—do you ever attribute to God a vague, mixed quality that robs each attribute of its specific meaning? What does this lesson demand of you regarding precision in your belief about God?
?
How seriously do you take the logical coherence of your faith? Are you willing to examine your own assumptions about God, even if it means abandoning a comfortable but flawed understanding? What step will you take today to ensure your theology is sound?

Negative vs. Positive Divine Attributes
Classification of attributes into negative and positive, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari classification of attributes into negative (removing imperfections) and positive (affirming real qualities).

Core Idea
Negative attributes deny limitations; positive attributes affirm real distinct meanings like knowledge and power.

After arguing that positive attributes like knowledge and power are distinct, the author turns to another category: attributes that are 'nothing beyond the essence.' These are negative attributes, which simply remove imperfections. This lesson explains each of these negative attributes and contrasts them with the positive ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on negative attributes
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Focus on the list of negative attributes and their explanations.
2
Identify the five negative attributes
The author names: eternal, one, self-sufficient, necessary, self-subsisting. Understand what each denies.
Each is defined by a negation.
3
Contrast with positive attributes
Positive attributes like 'knowing' and 'powerful' imply real qualities with distinct effects.
Precision is effect of knowledge; existence is effect of power.
4
Note the Mu'tazila admission
The author cites Mu'tazila agreement that being-knowing and being-powerful are distinct.
This strengthens the argument.

Proof That 'Alim' and 'Qadir' Are Distinct Attributes
The author uses linguistic and intuitive differences to prove that 'alim' and 'qadir' are not synonyms but two distinct real attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and intuitive proof that the divine attributes of knowledge and power are two distinct real attributes, not synonyms.

Core Idea
Our innate intellect distinguishes between knowing and powerful — they cannot be synonyms.

This lesson dives into a key argument in Islamic theology: proving that Allah's attributes are not mere names but distinct realities. The author, using the method of negation and affirmation, demonstrates that 'alim' (knowing) and 'qadir' (powerful) must be two separate attributes. The passage challenges the assumption of synonymy and eliminates alternative interpretations, ultimately affirming that they are distinct entities subsisting in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice how the author first states the evidence, then sets up a disjunction.
2
Identify the central disjunction
The author presents a key question: are 'alim' and 'qadir' synonyms or not? Trace how he explores this.
3
Understand the implications of synonymy
List the three consequences that would follow if they were synonyms. See how each is shown to be false by intuition.
Focus on the logic: if synonyms, then understanding one implies understanding the other, etc.
4
Refute alternative explanations
The author rejects two other possibilities: one being positive/negative and one being relational/essential. Understand why each fails.
This leads to the conclusion that they must be two distinct real attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستدلال بالنفي والإثبات ،فصحيح، واعتراضكم فاسد، فنقول: قد قام الدليل على كون الصانع عالماً قادراً، فلا يخلو الحال إما أن يكون الاسمان عبارتين عن معبر واحد من غير تفاوت أصلا، فيلزمكم أن تفهم العالمية بفهم القادرية، ويلزمكم أن تنفى العالمية من نفي القادرية، ويلزمكم أن يوجد العالم من حيث كونه عالما فحسب ولا يحتاج إلى إثبات كونه قادرًا، فيلزم أن يدل على العالمية كما يدل على القادرية، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أنه ليس إطلاق لفظ العالم القادر كإطلاق لفظ الموجد الخالق، فإنا ندرك ببدائة عقولنا فروقا ضرورية بين كونه عالما قادرًا وبين كونه موجدًا خالقا ، فلو كان الاسمان مترادفين ترادف هذين الاسمين، لكنا لا ندرك بعقولنا هذه التفرقة ثم ليس يجوز أن يقال: أحد الوصفين وصف إثبات، والثاني وصف نفي، فإنهما في محل تصور الفهم متساويان ، وليس يجوز أن يقال : أحدهما وصف إضافة والثاني وصف حال وصفة ؛ إذ الإضافة من لوازمها لذاتها، بل هما حقيقتان تعرض لهما الإضافة إلى المتعلقات والإضافة معنى لا تعرض له الإضافة، وإذا بطلت هذه الوجوه تعين أنهما صفتان قائمتان بالذات عبر الشرع عن إحداهما بالعلم وعن الثانية بالقدرة.
As for the inference by negation and affirmation, it is sound, and your objection is invalid. We say: The evidence has been established that the Creator is knowing and powerful. So the situation is not free from the possibility that the two names are expressions of one meaning without any difference at all. That would necessitate that you understand being-knowing by understanding being-powerful, and that you negate being-knowing from the negation of being-powerful, and that the knowing one would exist merely by His being knowing without needing the affirmation of His being powerful, and that the expression of being-knowing would indicate the same as the expression of being-powerful. But it is known without doubt that the usage of the term 'knowing, powerful' is not like the usage of the term 'bringer-into-existence, creator'. For we perceive by our innate intellects necessary differences between His being knowing and powerful and His being bringer-into-existence and creator. If the two names were synonymous in the sense of these two names being synonymous, we would not perceive this distinction by our intellects. Then it is not permissible to say: One of the two descriptions is a description of affirmation and the other a description of negation, because they are equal in the place of conceptual understanding. And it is not permissible to say: One of them is a description of relation (idāfa) and the other a description of state and attribute, because relation is from the necessary concomitants of the essence. Rather, they are two realities that may be conjoined with relation to objects, and relation is a meaning that does not itself admit relation. When these considerations are refuted, it becomes established that they are two attributes subsisting in the essence, and the Law has expressed one of them as knowledge and the second as power.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof, using the method of 'negation and affirmation' (al-nafy wa'l-ithbāt), to demonstrate that the divine attributes of knowledge and power are two distinct real attributes. The author first states that it is proven that the Creator is both knowing and powerful. Then he sets up a disjunction: either the terms 'alīm' and 'qadīr' are synonymous (express one meaning) or they are not. If they were synonyms, it would follow that understanding one implies understanding the other, negating one negates the other, and the existence of a knowing being would be enough for being powerful, which is contrary to intuition. The author invokes our innate intellect: we instinctively perceive a difference between saying 'knowing and powerful' and saying 'bringer-into-existence and creator', so the names cannot be synonyms. Next, he refutes two alternative explanations: (1) that one is a positive attribute and the other a negative attribute, because both terms are equal in positive conceptual content; (2) that one is a relational attribute and the other a state/attribute, because relation (idāfa) arises from the essence's own necessities and is not a distinct attribute. After invalidating all these possibilities, the author concludes that the two names must denote two real attributes subsisting in the essence, which Islamic revelation calls 'knowledge' and 'power'.

Key Terms
استدلال
نفي
إثبات
عالم
قادر
صفات
إضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'alim' and 'qadir' are simply different ways of referring to the same attribute.
Correction: The author demonstrates they are genuinely distinct through intuitive and logical reasoning, as proven by our innate perception of a necessary difference between them.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility the author considers and refutes in this proof?
A) That 'alim' and 'qadir' are two different relational attributes.
B) That 'alim' and 'qadir' are synonyms with identical meaning.
C) That one is a positive attribute and the other negative.
D) That the two attributes are actually one attribute with two names.

Key Takeaway
The two names 'alim' and 'qadir' denote two distinct, real attributes subsisting in the divine essence, as proven by logical analysis and intuitive perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe Allah as Knowing and Powerful, do you truly internalize that these are two distinct, real aspects of His perfection, or do you blur them into a vague sense of 'all-good'? How will you consciously separate them in your understanding and worship starting today?
?
This lesson shows that our innate intellect distinguishes between knowledge and power. Have you ever reflected on how your own limited knowledge and power are distinct? How does recognizing this distinction in Allah affect your humility before Him?
?
The author refutes lazy attempts to reduce these attributes to mere relations or negations. Are there areas in your own belief where you settle for vague or imprecise theology because it's comfortable? What will you do to seek more precise knowledge of Allah's attributes?

The Contradiction of Ḥāl with Divine Attributes
Author argues that the Mu'tazilite concept of ḥāl contradicts the real existence of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite concept of ḥāl is inherently contradictory to the real existence of divine attributes, and how the Ash'ari position avoids that contradiction.

Core Idea
A state (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent cannot account for real attributes.

This passage presents a key Ash'ari critique of the Mu'tazilite theory of states (aḥwāl). The author argues that if attributes are mere states, they cannot be real, and that theory leads to an absurdity similar to the Christian Trinity. However, he assures us that the Ash'ari view of attributes as real and distinct does not fall into that trap. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the concise style of the argument.
The passage is a single logical unit.
2
Identify the contradiction
The author states that ḥāl is not described by existence or non-existence, while attributes are existent. Why is this contradictory?
Think about what it means for something to be real: it must be either existent or non-existent.
3
Understand the consequence
The author says that the Mu'tazilite position forces them into a doctrine like the Christian Trinity: one essence but three hypostases. This is an undesirable implication.
This shows that the concept of ḥāl leads to theological problems.
4
Note the exemption
The author clarifies that this contradiction does not apply to the Attributists (Ash'aris), because they hold attributes as real and distinct.
This is an important distinction to remember.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن الحال متناقض للصفات؛ إذ الحال لا يوصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم، والصفات موجودة ثابتة قائمة بالذات، ويلزمهم مذهب النصارى في قولهم: واحد بالجوهرية ثلاثة بالأقنومية، ولا يلزم ذلك التناقض مذهب الصفاتية.
However, the state (ḥāl) is contradictory to the attributes; because the state is not described by existence nor by non-existence, while the attributes are existent, established, subsisting in the Essence. And it would necessitate for them the doctrine of the Christians in their saying: one in essence (jawhariyya), three in hypostases. But that contradiction does not necessitate the doctrine of the Attributists (al-ṣifātiyya).
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage identifies a contradiction in the Mu'tazilite concept of ḥāl (state) as applied to divine attributes. The author begins by stating that the notion of ḥāl is inherently contradictory when used for attributes. The reason: a ḥāl is defined as something that is neither existent nor non-existent—it is a mere state. But the attributes of God (like knowledge and power) are considered by the Ash'aris to be real existents (mawjūdāt) that subsist in the Essence. If the Mu'tazilites adopt the concept of ḥāl to explain attributes, they would be forced into a position analogous to the Christian Trinity: one essence but three hypostases. However, the author notes that this contradiction does not force the Attributists (i.e., the Ash'aris) into a similar problem, because they hold attributes as real and distinct, not mere states.

الصفات (al-ṣifāt)
الصفاتية (al-ṣifātiyya)
النصارى (al-Naṣārā)
الجوهرية (al-jawhariyya)
الأقنومية (al-aqnūmiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Ash'ari attributes are akin to the Christian hypostases (three persons).
Correction: The author clarifies that the Mu'tazilite theory of ḥāl leads to that analogy, not the Ash'ari position. Ash'aris affirm real attributes distinct from the essence, but not multiple gods.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the concept of ḥāl is contradictory to the attributes?
A) Because ḥāl is nonexistent while attributes are existent.
B) Because ḥāl is described by existence and non-existence.
C) Because attributes are mere states.
D) Because the Christians also use the term ḥāl.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite concept of ḥāl cannot account for real divine attributes, and leads to an implication similar to the Christian Trinity, but the Ash'ari doctrine of real attributes is not vulnerable to that objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you reflect on the reality of Allah's attributes in your daily life? If you treated 'His knowledge' or 'His power' as mere states rather than real, how would that affect your trust in Him? Are you willing to examine your own understanding of these attributes and correct it if needed?
?
The Mu'tazilites' concept led them to a contradiction similar to the Christian Trinity. Will you commit to studying the attributes as taught by the Salaf?
?

Does this lesson challenge you to defend the orthodox position with clarity, or have you been avoiding such theological discussions? Ask Allah to give you certainty in His attributes as they are described in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

The Consequences of Synonymy
Reductio argument against synonymy of divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reductio argument that if the names 'knowing' and 'powerful' were synonyms, then absurd consequences would follow, and how our intuitive perception of difference disproves synonymy.

Core Idea
Synonymy would collapse knowing and powerful into one meaning, but we intuitively know they differ.

The author begins by affirming the soundness of the argument from negation and affirmation. He then considers the possibility that the names 'knowing' and 'powerful' might be synonyms. If they were, he argues, absurd consequences would necessarily follow, such as the collapse of distinctions we clearly perceive. This lesson examines that reductio and the appeal to intuitive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's logical structure: hypothesis then consequences.
2
Identify the three consequences
Find the three 'فيلزمكم' phrases. Each states a necessary outcome if the names were synonyms. Understand each one.
The consequences are: 1) understanding knowingness by understanding powerfulness, 2) negating knowingness from negating powerfulness, 3) the knower exists by knowingness alone.
3
Understand the intuitive distinction
The author points to a clear intuitive difference between the pair 'knowing, powerful' and 'creator, sustainer'. Reflect on why this refutes synonymy.
If they were synonyms, we would not perceive this difference, but we do.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستدلال بالنفي والإثبات، فصحيح، واعتراضكم فاسد، فنقول: قد قام الدليل على كون الصانع عالماً قادراً، فلا يخلو الحال إما أن يكون الاسمان عبارتين عن معنى واحد من غير تفاوت أصلاً، فيلزمكم أن تفهم العالمية بفهم القادرية، ويلزمكم أن تنفى العالمية من نفي القادرية، ويلزمكم أن يوجد العالم من حيث كونه عالماً فحسب ولا يحتاج إلى إثبات كونه قادراً، فيلزم أن يدل على العالمية كما يدل على القادرية، ومن المعلوم الذي لا مرية فيه أنه ليس إطلاق لفظ العالم القادر كإطلاق لفظ الموجد الخالق، فإنا ندرك ببدائة عقولنا فروقاً ضرورية بين كونه عالماً قادراً وبين كونه موجداً خالقاً، فلو كان الاسمان مترادفين ترادف هذين الاسمين، لكنا لا ندرك بعقولنا هذه التفرقة ثم ليس يجوز أن يقال: أحد الوصفين وصف إثبات، والثاني وصف نفي، فإنهما في محل تصور الفهم متساويان، وليس يجوز أن يقال: أحدهما وصف إضافة والثاني وصف حال وصفة؛ إذ الإضافة من لوازمها لذاتها، بل هما حقيقتان تعرض لهما الإضافة إلى المتعلقات والإضافة معنى لا تعرض له الإضافة، وإذا بطلت هذه الوجوه تعين أنهما صفتان قائمتان بالذات عبر الشرع عن إحداهما بالعلم وعن الثانية بالقدرة.
As for the argument by negation and affirmation, it is correct, and your objection is invalid. We say: The proof has been established that the Maker is knowing and powerful. So the case is either that the two names are expressions for a single meaning with no difference whatsoever, then it would be necessary for you to understand 'knowingness' by understanding 'powerfulness', and it would be necessary for you to negate knowingness from the negation of powerfulness, and it would be necessary for you that the knower exists merely by being knowing without needing to establish that He is powerful, and that the indication of knowingness would be as the indication of powerfulness. But it is known without any doubt that the usage of the term 'knowing, powerful' is not like the usage of the term 'creator, sustainer'; for we perceive by the primacy of our intellects necessary differences between His being knowing, powerful and His being creator, sustainer. So if the two names were synonyms like the synonymy of these two names, we would not perceive this distinction by our intellects. Moreover, it is not permissible to say that one of the two descriptions is a description of affirmation and the other is a description of negation, for they are equal in the place of conceptual understanding. And it is not permissible to say that one of them is a description of relation and the other is a description of state and attribute, because relation is among their necessary concomitants for themselves; rather they are two realities that are subject to relation to their objects, and relation is a meaning that is not subject to relation. And when these possibilities are invalidated, it becomes certain that they are two attributes subsisting in the Essence, and the Law expressed one of them as knowledge and the other as power.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlāl) that the divine attributes of knowledge and power are distinct and real, not mere synonyms or mental considerations. The author begins by stating the argument from negation and affirmation (al-nafy wa al-ithbāt) is correct, and he addresses an unnamed objector. The proof proceeds: It is established that the Maker is knowing and powerful. Now consider the two names: either they are synonymous (expressing exactly one meaning) or they are not. If synonymous, absurd consequences follow: understanding 'knowingness' would be the same as understanding 'powerfulness'; negating one would negate the other; the knowingness of a knower would be sufficient without needing powerfulness. But we intuitively perceive a necessary difference between these descriptions and another pair (creator/sustainer). If they were mere synonyms, we would not perceive this difference. The author then rejects alternative explanations: that one is affirmative and the other negative (since both are equally positive in conception), or that one is relational and the other a state (since relation is a concomitant of both). Therefore, they must be two distinct realities that subsist in the Essence, and revelation calls them knowledge and power.

Key Terms
الاستدلال (al-istidlāl)
النفي والإثبات (al-nafy wa al-ithbāt)
العالمية (al-‘ālimiyya)
القادرية (al-qādiriyya)
الترادف (al-tarāduf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'knowing' and 'powerful' are synonymous because both are perfections.
Correction: The author shows that if they were synonymous, absurd consequences would follow, and our intellect clearly distinguishes them.

Checkpoint
What is one absurd consequence that would follow if 'knowing' and 'powerful' were synonyms?
A) Understanding 'knowingness' would not require understanding 'powerfulness'.
B) Negating 'knowingness' would negate 'powerfulness'.
C) The knower would need both knowledge and power to exist.
D) The intellect would perceive a clear difference between them.

Key Takeaway
The reductio argument shows that synonymy of 'knowing' and 'powerful' leads to absurdities, and our intuitive perception of difference confirms they are not synonyms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as 'al-'Alim, al-Qadir', do you pause to sense the distinct meanings, or do you let them blend into one vague feeling of greatness? How will you train your heart to feel His knowledge and His power as separate, overwhelming realities today?
?
The author argues that we intuitively perceive the difference between knowing and being powerful. In your own life, do you treat God's knowledge of your sin and His power to forgive as the same thing, or do you approach them differently? What would change if you truly distinguished His all-encompassing awareness from His absolute ability?
?
If Allah's names were synonyms, they would lose their distinct implications for your life. Which name of Allah have you been treating as redundant? Starting now, pick one attribute to meditate on separately—how will you let that single attribute transform a specific action or decision you face this week?

Rejecting Alternative Explanations
Rejection of alternative interpretations and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the names 'knowing' and 'powerful' cannot be explained away as merely affirmative/negative or relational, and how this confirms they are distinct real attributes.

Core Idea
Neither 'knowing' nor 'powerful' can be reduced to a logical or relational description; both are real and distinct.

After establishing that synonymy leads to absurdity, the author addresses alternative interpretations that might try to explain the difference without admitting two distinct attributes. He rejects the idea that one is a description of affirmation and the other of negation, and also the idea that one is a relation while the other is a state. This lesson unpacks those rejections and their conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation of the argument
Read the Arabic passage for this lesson. It begins with 'ثم ليس يجوز أن يقال'. Understand that the author is rejecting alternative explanations.
2
Identify the two rejected views
The first view claims one description is affirmative and the other negative. The second view claims one is relational and the other a state/attribute. Note why each is invalid.
The author says both are equal in conceptual understanding, so cannot be one affirmation and one negation. Also, relation is a concomitant of both, so cannot differentiate them.
3
Grasp the conclusion
After rejecting all alternatives, the author concludes that they are two distinct attributes subsisting in the Essence, called knowledge and power by revelation.
This is the positive proof for distinct attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the argument by negation and affirmation, it is correct, and your objection is invalid. We say: The proof has been established that the Maker is knowing and powerful. So the case is either that the two names are expressions for a single meaning with no difference whatsoever, then it would be necessary for you to understand 'knowingness' by understanding 'powerfulness', and it would be necessary for you to negate knowingness from the negation of powerfulness, and it would be necessary for you that the knower exists merely by being knowing without needing to establish that He is powerful, and that the indication of knowingness would be as the indication of powerfulness. But it is known without any doubt that the usage of the term 'knowing, powerful' is not like the usage of the term 'creator, sustainer'; for we perceive by the primacy of our intellects necessary differences between His being knowing, powerful and His being creator, sustainer. So if the two names were synonyms like the synonymy of these two names, we would not perceive this distinction by our intellects. Moreover, it is not permissible to say that one of the two descriptions is a description of affirmation and the other is a description of negation, for they are equal in the place of conceptual understanding. And it is not permissible to say that one of them is a description of relation and the other is a description of state and attribute, because relation is among their necessary concomitants for themselves; rather they are two realities that are subject to relation to their objects, and relation is a meaning that is not subject to relation. And when these possibilities are invalidated, it becomes certain that they are two attributes subsisting in the Essence, and the Law expressed one of them as knowledge and the other as power.

This passage is a proof (istidlāl) that the divine attributes of knowledge and power are distinct and real, not mere synonyms or mental considerations. Therefore, they must be two distinct realities that subsist in the Essence, and revelation calls them knowledge and power.
