Mu'tazilite Objection: Attributes Are Mental Aspects Only
Mu'tazilites state they deny eternal existent attributes but affirm mental aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument against eternal attributes as distinct entities and their alternative view of attributes as mental considerations.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites accept mental aspects but deny eternal distinct attributes, arguing they would lead to multiple gods.

This passage presents the Mu'tazilite objection directly. They clarify that they do not deny all distinctions of the essence, but only the existence of separate eternal attributes. They then argue that if attributes were distinct and eternal, they would be other gods, a classic argument against the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the Mu'tazilite argument come through clearly.
Notice that the Mu'tazilites start by affirming mental aspects.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position on mental aspects
Observe that the Mu'tazilites do not deny 'aspects and intellectual considerations' of the essence. They affirm attributes only in terms of these aspects, not as real entities.
This is often misunderstood – they accept mental distinctions.
3
Follow the dichotomy argument
The passage presents a dichotomy: if attributes are real entities beyond the essence, they are either identical with the essence or distinct. If identical, that is the Mu'tazilite position; if distinct, they must be either originated or eternal.
Trace each branch and see how it eliminates the Ash'ari view.
4
Understand the conclusion
The Mu'tazilites conclude that if the attributes are eternal and distinct, they share eternity and necessary existence with the essence, making them other gods. This is the core objection.
This argument aims to show that the Ash'ari position leads to polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة: نحن لا ننكر الوجوه والاعتبارات العقلية لذات واحدة، ولا نثبت الصفات إلا من حيث تلك الوجوه؛ إما إثبات صفات هي ذوات موجودات أزلية قديمة قائمة بذاته هو المنكر عندنا، فإنها إذا كانت موجودات وذوات وراء الذات فإما أن تكون عين الذات وإما أن تكون غير الذات، فإن كانت عين الذات فهو مذهبنا، وبطل قولكم: هي وراء الذات، وإن كانت غير الذات فهي حادثة أو قديمة، وليس من مذهبكم أنها حادثة وإن كانت قديمة فقد شاركت الذات في القدم والوجوب بالذات ونفى الأولية فهي آلهة أخرى.
The Mu'tazilites said: We do not deny the aspects and intellectual considerations of a single essence, and we only affirm attributes in terms of those aspects. But the affirmation of attributes that are existent entities, eternal, ancient, subsisting in His Essence is what we deny. For if they are existents and entities beyond the essence, then either they are identical with the essence or they are other than the essence. If they are identical with the essence, that is our doctrine, and your statement that they are beyond the essence is invalid. And if they are other than the essence, then they are originated (ḥāditha) or eternal (qadīma). It is not your doctrine that they are originated. And if they are eternal, then they have shared with the Essence in eternity and necessary existence, and the attribute of priority is negated, so they would be other gods.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite objection to the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal attributes. The Mu'tazilites begin by stating that they do not deny mental aspects (wujūh) and intellectual considerations (i‘tibārāt) of a single essence. They affirm attributes only as those aspects—i.e., the essence is knower by virtue of a mental consideration, not an additional entity. But they deny that there are attributes that are real existents (mawjūdāt), eternal (azaliyya) and ancient (qadīma), that subsist in the Essence. They argue by dichotomy: If such attributes are real entities beyond the Essence (warā’ al-dhāt), then either they are identical to the Essence or distinct from it. If identical, then that is the Mu'tazilite position (attributes are the essence itself), and the Ash'ari claim of 'beyond the essence' is false. If distinct, then they must be either originated (ḥāditha) or eternal (qadīma). The Ash'aris do not say they are originated. So if they are eternal, they share eternity and necessary existence with the Essence, which would make them other gods, because they would be multiple eternal entities. This is a classic Mu'tazilite argument against the existence of distinct, eternal attributes.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (al-Mu‘tazila)
الوجوه العقلية (al-wujūh al-‘aqliyya)
الاعتبارات (al-i‘tibārāt)
الذات (al-dhāt)
القديم (al-qadīm)
الوجوب بالذات (al-wujūb bi al-dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Mu'tazilites deny all distinctions in God's essence.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites explicitly state they do not deny mental aspects and intellectual considerations; they only deny real, eternal entities beyond the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite argument, if the attributes are distinct from the essence and eternal, what follows?
A) They are identical with the essence.
B) They are originated (ḥāditha).
C) They are other gods.
D) They are mere mental aspects.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection denies eternal distinct attributes as real entities, arguing that such attributes would be other gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's knowledge merely as a concept in your mind, or as a real attribute that truly belongs to Him? How does that choice affect the certainty you have in His knowledge of your every action?
?
If you were to defend the Ash'ari position against the Mu'tazilite logical argument, could you do so without downplaying God's oneness? Examine your own heart: do you fear that affirming real attributes might compromise tawheed?
?
When you say 'God is knowing,' do you feel the weight of that attribute as a real, eternal reality that demands your accountability? Or is it simply a phrase you repeat? What would change in your life if you truly believed His knowledge is an eternal entity subsisting in His essence?

Objection: Subsisting Attributes Entail Dependence and Accidents
Objection that attributes subsisting in the essence would be dependent like accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection that if attributes subsist in God's essence, they would be dependent like accidents, and why this is deemed impossible.

Core Idea
Subsisting in something implies need, making attributes like accidents—impossible for God.

This lesson presents a key objection (ilzām) raised against the Ashʿarī view that attributes subsist in the divine essence. The objector argues that 'subsisting in' necessarily implies dependence on the substrate, which is the defining characteristic of accidents. If accepted, this would force the Ashʿarīs to admit that God's attributes are like created accidents, and also imply logical priority and posteriority within God—both of which are impossible. Let's examine the objection closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an initial claim, two consequences, and a final verdict.
The objector uses 'ilzāmāt'—impositions of unacceptable conclusions.
2
Identify the core premise
The objector states that 'that which subsists in something is in need of that thing'. Understand why this premise leads to the consequences.
The premise equates subsistence with dependence.
3
Trace the two consequences
First consequence: attributes become like accidents. Second consequence: priority and posteriority arise between essence and attributes. Both are considered impossible for God.
The impossibility (muḥāl) is asserted but not argued here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الإلزامات على قولكم: إنها قائمة بذاته، أن القائم بالشيء محتاج إلى ذلك الشيء حتى لولاه لما تحقق له وجود به، فيلزمكم إثبات خصائص الأعراض في الصفات، فإن العرض هو المحتاج إلى محل يقوم به، ويلزمكم إثبات التقدم والتأخر الذاتي العقلي في الذات والصفات، وهذا كله محال.
And from the impositions (ilzāmāt) on your statement that they subsist in His Essence: that which subsists in something is in need of that thing, such that without it its existence through it would not be realized. So it would be necessary for you to affirm the characteristics of accidents (a‘rāḍ) for the attributes, for the accident is what is in need of a substrate in which it subsists. And it would be necessary for you to affirm essential intellectual priority and posteriority in the Essence and the attributes. And all this is impossible (muḥāl).
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an additional objection (ilzām) levied against the Ash'ari view that attributes subsist in the divine essence. The objection is framed as an 'imposition'—a logical consequence that is unacceptable. The argument: If a thing subsists (qā'im) in another, it is dependent on that other for its existence; without the substrate, it would not have existence through it. This is precisely the definition of an accident ('araḍ). Therefore, the Ash'aris would have to admit that attributes are like accidents because accidents are things that depend on a substrate. Additionally, this would imply a relation of priority and posteriority in the essence and its attributes (the substrate is prior, the attribute posterior in a logical sense). Both consequences are deemed impossible (muḥāl) for God. The objection is aimed at showing that the Ash'ari conception of attributes implies corporeality or contingency in God.

Key Terms
الإلزامات (al-ilzāmāt)
القائم بالشيء (al-qā'im bi al-shay')
العرض (al-‘araḍ)
التقدم والتأخر (al-taqaddum wa al-ta'akhkhur)
محال (muḥāl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'subsisting in' means merely 'being a property' without dependence.
Correction: The objector interprets 'subsisting in' as physical dependence like an accident, which would entail need and contingency.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of saying attributes subsist in the essence, according to this objection?
A) Attributes would be eternal and independent.
B) Attributes would have the characteristics of accidents.
C) Attributes would be identical to the essence.
D) The essence would be dependent on the attributes.

Key Takeaway
This objection challenges the Ashʿarī view by arguing that subsistence in the essence entails dependence, making attributes like accidents—an impossibility for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes—like His power, mercy, or knowledge—do you ever imagine them as separate 'things' that He 'has'? This objection warns that such a view makes them dependent on His essence like accidents. How does this challenge you to rethink the oneness of God in your heart?
?
The objector says that if attributes subsist in the essence, they need the essence to exist. Have you ever felt that God's attributes need something outside of Him to be actual? This lesson forces you to confront whether you truly believe God is completely self-sufficient. Ask yourself: Do I rely on God's attributes as if they are separate resources, or do I see them as identical to His perfect essence?

Refuting the Identity-or-Otherness Dichotomy for Divine Attributes
Refutation of identity-or-otherness dichotomy for attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relationship between divine attributes and essence cannot be reduced to either identity or complete otherness, and how the analogy of composite realities refutes this false dichotomy.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are neither identical to nor other than the essence—they are constituent parts in a unique relationship.

Theologians have debated whether God's attributes are identical to His essence or distinct from it. This passage from a classical kalam work presents a sophisticated refutation of that very dichotomy, showing that the relationship is more nuanced. Using the example of humanity composed of animality and rationality, the author argues that attributes are like parts of a whole—neither identical nor entirely other.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need of attributes for a subject
The passage begins by stating that attributes require a subject (dhāt) to subsist in, and that the subject attains perfection through attributes.
Notice the asymmetry: knowledge subsists in God, not vice versa.
2
Examine the Sifatiyya's parity argument
The proponents of eternal attributes (al-ṣifātiyya) object using a parity argument: just as you deny eternal attributes, we deny a subject without attributes.
This sets up the refutation.
3
Analyze the refutation of the dichotomy
The author shows that calling attributes 'identical' or 'other' is ambiguous. 'Other' can mean separate existence or merely distinct. The first meaning is rejected for attributes.
The division is only valid if it revolves around negation and affirmation.
4
Learn from the analogy of composite realities
The example of humanity composed of animality and rationality shows that parts are neither identical to nor other than the whole. Similarly, attributes are neither identical to nor other than the essence.
This refutes the false dichotomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) والموصوف جميعا، أما الصفة فاحتاجت في وجودها إلى ذات تقوم بها ؛ إذ يجب أن تقول : قام العلم بالباري، واستحال أن تقول : قام الذات بالعلم، وأما الموصوف فإنما يتم كما له في الإلهية إذا قامت به هذه الصفات حتى لولاها لما كان إلها مستغنيا على الإطلاق، فإذا المستغنى على الإطلاق لا يكون إلا واحدًا من كل وجه ولا كثرة فيه من وجه. (4) قالت الصفاتية : كما أنكرتم إثبات صفات (٣) أزلية أنكرنا عليكم إثبات موصوف بلا صفة إنكارًا بإنكار، واستبعادا باستبعاد، وتقسيمكم أنها عين الذات أم غير الذات إنما يصح إذا (۳) كان التقسيم دائرًا بين النقي والإثبات، فإن من قال : هذا عينه وهذا غيره قد يعني به أنهما شيئان فهذا شيء وذاك شيء آخر، وقد يعني به أنهما شيئان، ويجوز () وجود أحدهما مع عدم الثاني، أو مع تقدير عدم الثاني، ونحن لا نسلم أن الصفات أغيار ولا نقول: إنها عين الذات؛ لأن حد الغيرين عندنا هو المعنى الثاني لا المعنى الأول، ثم إن جاز لمثبتي الأحوال القول بأن الحال لا موجودة ولا معدومة جاز لمثبتي الصفات [القول بأن الصفات (1) لا عين ولا غير الذات، على أن كل حقيقة التأمت من أمرين محققين في الشاهد مثل الإنسانية، فإنها التأمت من حيوانية وناطقية عند القوم، فإذا قيل : الناطقية عين الإنسانية أو غيرها لم يصح ) بأنها لا عينها ولا غيرها، وكذلك جزء كل شيء لا عينه ولا غيره.
(1) And the described (al-mawsūf) together. As for the attribute (al-ṣifa), it needed in its existence a self (dhāt) to subsist in; for it is necessary to say: knowledge subsists in the Creator, and it is impossible to say: the self subsists in knowledge. As for the described, it only attains its perfection in divinity when these attributes subsist in it, such that without them it would not be a self-sufficient deity absolutely. So the absolutely self-sufficient is only one in every respect, and there is no multiplicity in it in any respect. (4) The affirmationists of attributes (al-ṣifātiyya) said: Just as you denied the affirmation of eternal attributes, we deny your affirmation of a described without an attribute—denial in response to denial, and rejection in response to rejection. And your division that they are identical to the self (ʿayn al-dhāt) or other than the self (ghayr al-dhāt) is only valid if the division revolves between negation and affirmation. For he who says 'this is identical to that' or 'this is other than that' may mean by it that they are two things—this is a thing and that is another thing—and may mean by it that they are two things, and it is permissible for one to exist without the other, or with the supposition of the non-existence of the other. And we do not accept that the attributes are others (aghyār), nor do we say that they are identical to the self; because the definition of 'two others' (al-ghayrayn) for us is the second meaning, not the first. Then, if it is permissible for the proponents of states (aḥwāl) to say that the state is neither existent nor non-existent, it is permissible for the proponents of attributes to say that the attributes are neither identical to nor other than the self. Moreover, every reality (ḥaqīqa) composed of two realized matters in the witnessed world, like humanity (al-insāniyya), for it is composed of animality (al-ḥayawāniyya) and rationality (al-nāṭiqiyya) according to them. So if it is said: 'Is rationality identical to humanity or other than it?'—it is not correct that it is either identical to it or other than it. Likewise, every part of a thing is neither identical to it nor other than it.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-refutation passage. The author begins by establishing the basic relationship between an attribute and its subject (al-mawṣūf). An attribute requires a self (dhāt) to subsist in, as we say knowledge subsists in God, not vice versa. The described only attains perfection in divinity through subsisting attributes; without them, it would not be self-sufficient. The absolutely self-sufficient is one in every respect, without multiplicity. Then the author presents an objection from the proponents of attributes (al-ṣifātiyya), who argue using parity: if you deny eternal attributes, we deny a subject without attributes. They attack the common method of dividing the relation into identity (ʿayn) or otherness (ghayr). They explain that the phrase 'this is identical' or 'other' can have two meanings: either it denotes two separate entities, or it denotes two things where one can exist without the other. They reject the first meaning for attributes. Then they draw an analogy with the proponents of states (aḥwāl), who allow a state that is neither existent nor non-existent. Similarly, attributes are neither identical to nor other than the essence. They illustrate with the example of humanity (insāniyya), composed of animality and rationality. If asked whether rationality is identical to humanity, the correct answer is that it is neither identical nor other, because it is a constituent part. So the false dichotomy is refuted.

Key Terms
الصفة
الموصوف
الذات
العين
الغير
الحال
الإنسانية
الحيوانية
الناطقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If attributes are not identical to the essence, they must be entirely separate entities.
Correction: The passage shows that attributes are like parts of a composite whole; they are neither identical nor entirely other. The meaning of 'other' as separate existence does not apply to attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the division of attributes into identical or other than the essence flawed?
A) Because attributes are completely other entities
B) Because the division depends on a false analogy with material composites
C) Because the meaning of 'other' can be ambiguous, and attributes are like parts of a composite that are neither identical nor other
D) Because the division applies only to accidents

Key Takeaway
Attributes are neither identical to nor other than the essence; they are in a unique relationship of inherence without complete separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Merciful,' do you unconsciously imagine mercy as something added to God, or do you reduce mercy to a mere name? How can you train your heart to see God's attributes as truly Him without dividing His oneness?
?
The lesson uses the example of humanity composed of animality and rationality. Examine your own character: Is your kindness identical to you or other than you? How does this analogy challenge you to rethink the relationship between God and His attributes?
?
If you truly believed that God's attributes are neither separate from Him nor merely identical, how would that change the way you call upon His names in your duʿā'? Will you recite His names today with the awareness that each name is a real aspect of His being?

Refuting the Objection of Eternal Attributes as Separate Gods
The author refutes the claim that every eternal attribute would be a separate god by analyzing the meaning of divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that every eternal attribute would be a separate god by analyzing the meaning of divinity and the logical relationship between general and specific attributes.

Core Idea
Eternity alone does not make a god — divinity requires a self with perfect attributes.

This lesson presents a key objection against the doctrine of eternal attributes: if attributes are eternal, each would be a separate god. The author, however, shows this objection is based on a misunderstanding. By carefully defining divinity and analyzing the relationship between general and specific attributes, he turns the objection on its head.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Note the objection and the author's immediate response: he calls it a bare claim and the very point of dispute.
The objection claims that every eternal attribute would be a separate god.
2
Understand the definition of divinity
Focus on the definition: 'divinity is a self qualified with attributes of perfection.' Ask: Why does this definition undermine the assumption that an eternal attribute alone constitutes a god?
Divinity requires an essence (self) and multiple attributes, not just eternity.
3
Trace the logic of general and specific
Walk through the reasoning: if eternity is the most specific attribute, what is the most general? The author suggests existence. Then divinity is composed of general (existence) and specific (eternity). This leads to a dilemma: is the specific identical to the general or not? This same dilemma was raised by the objector about attributes, so the objection is self-defeating.
This shows the objector's own logical tools backfire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هب تكون إلها دعوى مجردة، بل هو محل النزاع، وقولكم : القدم هو أخص وصف الإله دعوى لا برهان عليها، فإن معنى الإلهية هو ذات موصوفة بصفات الكمال، فكيف تسلم أن كل صفة أزلية إله، ثم من قال : القدم أخص الأوصاف فيجب أن يبين أعم الأوصاف، فإن الأخص إنما يتصور بعد الأعم فما الأعم، فإن كان الوجود هو الأعم والقدم هو الأخص فقد التأمت حقيقة الإلهية من أعم وأخص فالأخص عين الأعم أو غيره، ويلزم على مساق ذلك ما ألزمتموه على الصفات.
Then, even if [you say] it becomes a god, that is a bare claim—rather it is the point of dispute. And your statement: 'Eternity (al-qidam) is the most specific attribute of deity' is a claim without proof. For the meaning of divinity (al-ilāhiyya) is a self qualified with attributes of perfection. So how can you accept that every eternal attribute is a god? Then, he who says that eternity is the most specific attribute must state the most general attribute, for the specific is only conceived after the general. So what is the general? If existence (al-wujūd) is the general and eternity is the specific, then the reality of divinity is composed of a general and a specific. Then the specific is either identical to the general or other than it. And that line of reasoning leads to the same [difficulty] you imposed on the attributes.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The author responds to the claim that if attributes are eternal, each would be a separate god. He calls this a bare assertion, not a proof. He then defines divinity as a self qualified with attributes of perfection. Therefore, it does not follow that every eternal attribute is a god. Next, he analyzes the claim that 'eternity is the most specific attribute of deity.' If eternity is the most specific, then there must be a more general attribute that combines with it to form divinity. The general could be existence. Then divinity is a composite of a general (existence) and a specific (eternity). This leads to the same problem of whether the specific is identical to the general or other—the very dilemma the objector raised about attributes. Thus the objection is self-defeating.

Key Terms
القدم
الإلهية
أخص الأوصاف
أعم الأوصاف
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal attribute must be a separate god because eternity is exclusive to God alone.
Correction: Divinity is not merely an eternal attribute; it requires a self (essence) qualified with multiple perfections. Eternity alone is insufficient to constitute a god.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that every eternal attribute would be a separate god?
A) By asserting that attributes are not eternal
B) By showing that divinity means a self with perfect attributes, and eternity alone does not suffice
C) By arguing that eternity is not a real attribute
D) By denying that attributes exist at all

Key Takeaway
The objection that eternal attributes are separate gods fails because divinity means a self with perfect attributes, not just eternity, and the logic of general and specific attributes turns the objection against itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The objector’s logic turned against itself. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold beliefs that, if examined, might undermine themselves? Will you take time today to test your deepest convictions against clear reasoning?

The Subsistence of Attributes Does Not Imply Need
Objection-and-answer: refuting the claim that subsistence implies need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subsistence of attributes in God does not entail that God needs them, and how need only applies to temporal contingent beings.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes are not needy—need only applies to things that come into being.

A common objection against affirming real attributes for God is that if attributes subsist in Him, He would need them, just as accidents need a substrate. In this passage, the author refutes that analogy by clarifying what 'subsistence' means and showing that need is only relevant to contingent, temporal entities. Let's examine his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the opening objection: 'If the attributes subsisted in His self, He would need them...' Understand the opponent's reasoning.
2
Analyze the author's clarification
Notice how the author redefines 'subsistence'—it simply means being described by the attribute, without any additional condition. The description itself does not imply need.
3
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between eternal descriptions and temporal existents. Need only applies to things that were not then came to be, like substances and accidents.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If God is eternal, can He be said to need His attributes? The answer is no, because need presupposes a lack that is filled over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم : إن الصفات لو قامت بذاته تعالى لاحتاجت إليها وكان حكمها حكم الأعراض، وكان وجود الموصوف أسبق وأقدم بالذات على وجود الصفة، فهذا مما يستحق أن يجاب عنه. فنقول : معنى قولنا : الصفات قامت به أنه سبحانه يوصف بها فقط من غير شرط آخر، والوصف من حيث هو وصف لا يستدعي الاحتياج والاستغناء ولا التقدم ولا التأخر، فإن الوصف بكونه قديما واجبًا بذاته من حيث هو وصف لا يستدعي كون القدم والوجوب محتاجًا إلى الموصوف، ولا كون الموصوف سابقا بالقدم والوجوب، بل الاحتياج إنما يتصور في الجواهر والأعراض حيث لم تكن فكانت فاحتاجت إلى موجد الجوازها، وتطلق على الأعراض خاصة حيث لم تعقل إلا في فحال واحتاجت إلى محل، وبالجملة الاحتياج إنما يتحقق فيما يتوقع حصوله فيترقب وجوده، ولن يتصور الاحتياج في القدم.
As for your statement: 'If the attributes subsisted in His self (dhāt), He would need them, and their judgment would be the judgment of accidents (aʿrāḍ), and the existence of the described would be prior and earlier in essence to the existence of the attribute,' this deserves a response. We say: The meaning of our statement 'the attributes subsist in Him' is that He, exalted, is described by them only, without any other condition. The description (al-waṣf) as such does not entail need (iḥtiyāj) or self-sufficiency (istighnāʾ), nor priority or posteriority. For the description of being eternal and necessary in essence, as a description, does not entail that eternity and necessity are in need of the described, nor that the described is prior in terms of eternity and necessity. Rather, need is only conceivable in the case of substances (jawāhir) and accidents (aʿrāḍ) which were not then came to be, so they needed a maker due to their contingency. And it is applied specifically to accidents because they are only understood as in a state (fī ḥāl) and need a substrate. In sum, need is only realized in what is expected to occur, so its existence is awaited. And need is not conceivable in the case of eternity (al-qidam).
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if attributes subsist in God, then God would need them, making them like accidents, and the subject would be prior to the attribute. The author answers by clarifying the meaning of 'subsistence' (qiyām). He explains that 'the attributes subsist in Him' means He is described by them without any additional condition. The description itself does not imply need, self-sufficiency, priority, or posteriority. For example, being described as eternal does not mean that eternity needs the subject or that the subject is prior in eternity. Need is only applicable to things that come into existence after non-existence—contingent substances and accidents that require a cause. Accidents especially need a substrate because they cannot exist independently. But eternal attributes are not contingent; they have no beginning, so the concept of need does not apply. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If attributes subsist in God, then God needs them, just as an accident needs a substrate.
Correction: Subsistence (qiyām) does not imply need. Need only applies to temporal, contingent beings that come into existence. Eternal attributes have no beginning, so they cannot be said to be needed by God.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the subsistence of attributes in God not imply that God needs them?
A) Because attributes are identical to God's essence
B) Because need only applies to things that come into existence, and the attributes are eternal
C) Because attributes are not real
D) Because God is beyond description

Key Takeaway
The subsistence of eternal attributes in God does not imply need, because need only applies to temporal contingent beings, not to what is eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Make a concrete commitment to study one divine attribute this week with the correct understanding.
?
The author says need only applies to what comes into being. Are there moments in your life when you act as though Allah needs your worship or obedience—as if He is dependent on your actions? Repent of this subtle form of entitlement and resolve to worship Him with pure humility, knowing He is self-sufficient.
?

Pre-Eternity is Not the Most Specific Attribute of God
Refutation of claim that pre-eternity is most specific attribute of God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that pre-eternity is the most specific attribute of God is a mere assertion without proof, and how the opponent must first establish the most general attribute.

Core Idea
Divinity is not a single attribute but a composite of general and specific.

In this passage, the author counters an opponent who argues that pre-eternity is the most specific attribute of God and therefore could be considered a deity. The author points out that this is a claim without proof. He then explains that the meaning of divinity includes an essence qualified with attributes of perfection, and that to claim pre-eternity as the most specific, one must first identify the most general attribute. The argument leads to compositional problems that mirror those the opponent raised against divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim is introduced, then the refutation begins with 'قولكم: القدم هو أخص وصف الإله دعوى لا برهان عليها'.
The author immediately labels the claim as unproven.
2
Identify the logical requirement
The author demands that the opponent specify the most general attribute (الأعم) before claiming the most specific (الأخص). Why is this necessary?
The specific is only conceptualized after the general.
3
Analyze the compositional argument
If existence is the general and pre-eternity is the specific, then divinity is composed of both. The specific must be either identical to the general or distinct, leading to problems.
This mirrors the opponent's own objections to the attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هب تكون إلها دعوى مجردة، بل هو محل النزاع، وقولكم : القدم هو أخص وصف الإله دعوى لا برهان عليها، فإن معنى الإلهية هو ذات موصوفة بصفات الكمال، فكيف تسلم أن كل صفة أزلية إله، ثم من قال : القدم أخص الأوصاف فيجب أن يبين أعم الأوصاف، فإن الأخص إنما يتصور بعد الأعم فما الأعم، فإن كان الوجود هو الأعم والقدم هو الأخص فقد التأمت حقيقة الإلهية من أعم وأخص فالأخص عين الأعم أو غيره، ويلزم على مساق ذلك ما ألزمتموه على الصفات.
Then suppose it becomes a deity? a mere claim, rather it is the point of contention. And your saying: pre-eternity (al-qidam) is the most specific description (a khass wasf) of the deity (al-ilah) is a claim without proof. For the meaning of divinity (al-ilahiyya) is an essence (dhat) qualified with attributes of perfection, so how can you accept that every attribute that is eternal (azali) is a deity? Then who said: pre-eternity is the most specific of attributes must show the most general of attributes, for the most specific is only conceived after the most general. So what is the most general? If existence (al-wujud) is the most general and pre-eternity is the most specific, then the reality of divinity has been composed of a general and a specific, and the specific is either identical to the general or other. And there follows on that course what you have imposed on the attributes.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's claim that pre-eternity (al-qidam) is the most specific attribute of God and therefore a candidate for divinity. The author argues that this is a mere claim without proof. The meaning of divinity is an essence qualified with attributes of perfection; not every eternal attribute is a deity. The opponent must first establish the most general attribute, since the specific is only understood after the general. If existence is general and pre-eternity specific, then divinity is composed of both. The specific is either identical to the general or distinct, leading to problems similar to those the opponent raised against the attributes. The author thus exposes the inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any eternal attribute, like pre-eternity, could be considered a god or divine being.
Correction: The author shows that divinity is not a single attribute but an essence qualified by a composite of general and specific attributes. Pre-eternity alone cannot constitute divinity.

Checkpoint

A) That pre-eternity is indeed the most specific attribute
B) That pre-eternity is not an attribute at all
C) That the opponent must first establish the most general attribute, and divinity is composed of general and specific
D) That all eternal attributes are gods

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternity alone cannot define divinity; divinity is a composite of general and specific attributes, and claiming pre-eternity as the most specific requires establishing the most general first.

Answer to the Objection that Attributes Imply Need
Response to objection that attributes subsisting in God's essence entail need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing God with eternal attributes does not imply need or dependency.

Core Idea
Description does not entail need; need only applies to contingent beings.

In this passage, the author responds to a common objection: if attributes subsist in God's essence, then God would need them like accidents need a substratum. The author clarifies that the act of describing does not imply need, priority, or posteriority. The concept of need only applies to things that come into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objection is framed and how the author begins his response.
The objection is presented in the first part of the passage.
2
Understand the key distinction
The author argues that 'description as such' (al-wasf min haythu huwa wasf) does not imply need. Reflect on examples: describing something as eternal does not make the attribute 'need' the described.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Reflect on why need only applies to contingent beings
Need arises only for substances and accidents that come into existence after non-existence. The eternal (qadim) does not come to be, so it cannot be subject to need.
This connects the argument to broader kalam principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولكم : إن الصفات لو قامت بذاته تعالى لاحتاجت إليها وكان حكمها حكم الأعراض، وكان وجود الموصوف أسبق وأقدم بالذات على وجود الصفة، فهذا مما يستحق أن يجاب عنه. فنقول : معنى قولنا : الصفات قامت به أنه سبحانه يوصف بها فقط من غير شرط آخر، والوصف من حيث هو وصف لا يستدعي الاحتياج والاستغناء ولا التقدم ولا التأخر، فإن الوصف بكونه قديما واجبًا بذاته من حيث هو وصف لا يستدعي كون القدم والوجوب محتاجًا إلى الموصوف، ولا كون الموصوف سابقا بالقدم والوجوب، بل الاحتياج إنما يتصور في الجواهر والأعراض حيث لم تكن فكانت فاحتاجت إلى موجد لجوازها، وتطلق على الأعراض خاصة حيث لا تعقل إلا في حال واحتاجت إلى محل، وبالجملة الاحتياج إنما يتحقق فيما يتوقع حصوله فيترقب وجوده، ولن يتصور الاحتياج في القدم.
And as for your saying: If the attributes subsisted in His essence (dhat), He would need them and their ruling would be the ruling of accidents (a'rad), and the existence of the described would be prior and earlier in essence to the existence of the attribute, this deserves to be answered. So we say: The meaning of our saying 'the attributes subsist in Him' is that He, exalted, is described by them only without another condition, and the description (al-wasf) as such does not imply need or self-sufficiency nor priority nor posteriority. For the description as being eternal (qadim) and necessary (wajib) in essence as a description does not imply that pre-eternity (al-qidam) and necessity (al-wujub) are needful of the described, nor that the described is prior in pre-eternity and necessity. Rather need is only conceived in substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad) where they were not (nonexistent) and then came to be, so they needed a creator due to their possibility. And it is applied specifically to accidents, where they are not conceived except in a state and need a substratum. And in summary, need is only realized in what is expected to come to be, so its existence is anticipated. And need is not conceivable in the eternal (al-qadim).
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector claims that if attributes subsist in God's essence, then God would need them, and they would be like accidents, requiring the essence to be prior. The author responds by clarifying what it means for attributes to subsist: God is simply described by them without additional conditions. The act of describing does not entail need, priority, or posteriority. Even describing something as eternal and necessary does not make the attribute needful of the described. Need only applies to contingent beings (substances and accidents) that come into existence and require a cause. The eternal, by definition, does not come to be and thus cannot be subject to need.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any relation of attribute to essence implies need; attributes are like accidents that require a substratum.
Correction: Description (wasf) as such does not entail need. Need only applies to contingent beings that come into existence, not to the eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the attributes of God not imply need?
A) Because God is independent of all attributes
B) Because description as such does not imply need; need only applies to contingent beings
C) Because the attributes are not real
D) Because the essence is prior to the attributes

Key Takeaway
Describing God as eternal and necessary does not make the attributes needful of Him, nor Him needful of them; need is only for contingent beings.

God's Independence and His Attributes
Response to philosophers on absolute independence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians refute the philosophers' claim that God's absolute independence by His attributes implies that He needs those attributes.

Core Idea
God's independence by His attributes does not entail need, just as His self-necessity does not.

In this passage, the author responds to a philosophical objection: if God is independent by His attributes, doesn't He need those attributes? The answer distinguishes between two dimensions of independence—essence and attributes—and shows that need only arises if attributes were like tools, which eternal attributes cannot be. Let's read the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Identify the two types of independence
Notice how the author defines independence in terms of essence (no need for place) and attributes (no need for partners or assistants).
3
Understand the analogy
The author draws an analogy: God's independence by His necessity does not mean He needed that necessity. Similarly, independence by attributes does not imply need.
This is the key refutation.
4
Reflect on the tool analogy
The author explains that need would only arise if attributes were tools, but eternal attributes cannot be instruments. Hence the objection fails.
Consider why eternal attributes cannot be tools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن قول الفلاسفة : إنه تعالى مستغن على الإطلاق، قيل: الاستغناء من حيث الذات أنه لا يحتاج إلى مكان ومحل، والاستغناء من حيث الصفات أنه لا يحتاج إلى شريك في القدرة والفعل ومعين في العلم والإرادة، ووزير في التصرف والتدبير فهو تعالى مستغن على الإطلاق، وإنما يستغني بكمال صفاته وصفات جلاله ، فكيف يقال : إنه احتاج إلى ما به استغنى، أليس القول بأنه استغنى بوجوبه في وجوده عن موجد ، ولا يقال : إنه لما استغنى عند ذلك كان محتاجاً إليه ، كذلك نقول : إنه استغنى بذاته وصفاته فلا الموصوف محتاج إلى الصفة، ولا الصفة احتاجت إلى الموصوف وإنما يتحقق الاحتياج أن لو كانت الصفة على مثال الآلة والأداة ، والصفات الأزلية يستحيل أن تكون آلة ؛ فبطل الإلزام وزال الإيهام.
And as for the answer to the philosophers' statement: He, exalted, is absolutely independent (mustaghni 'ala al-itlaq), it is said: Independence in terms of essence (dhat) is that He does not need a place or substratum, and independence in terms of attributes is that He does not need a partner in power and action nor an assistant in knowledge and will nor a vizier in disposal and administration. So He, exalted, is absolutely independent. And He is independent by the perfection of His attributes and the attributes of His majesty. So how can it be said that He needed that by which He is independent? Is it not said that He is independent by His necessity in His existence from a creator, and it is not said that because He is independent thereby He was needful of it? Similarly we say: He is independent by His essence and attributes, so neither the described is needy of the attribute nor is the attribute needy of the described. Need only occurs if the attribute were like a tool (ala) and instrument (ada). And eternal attributes (al-sifat al-azaliyya) are impossible to be tools. So the objection is refuted and the misconception removed.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the philosophers' claim that God is absolutely independent, which might imply that if He is independent by His attributes, He needs those attributes. The author defines two aspects of independence: from essence (no need for place) and from attributes (no need for partners or assistants). God is independent by the perfection of His attributes. The author uses an analogy: God is independent by His necessity from a creator, but this does not mean He needed that necessity. Similarly, He is independent by His essence and attributes, so neither is needful of the other. Need would only exist if attributes were like instruments, but eternal attributes cannot be instruments. Hence the objection fails.

الصفات
الآلة

الأزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is independent by His attributes, then He needs those attributes.
Correction: The author shows that independence by attributes does not entail need, as demonstrated by the analogy of independence by necessity.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the idea that God's independence by His attributes implies need?
A) By saying God has no attributes
B) By explaining that independence by attributes does not entail need, as shown by the analogy of independence by necessity
C) By asserting that attributes are not real
D) By saying that need only applies to contingent beings

Key Takeaway
God's independence by His attributes does not imply need; the attributes are not tools but essential perfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Self-Sufficiency and Divine Attributes
Refutation of the claim that self-sufficiency negates attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine self-sufficiency does not negate attributes but is perfected by them.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not tools but perfections of His self-sufficiency.

The philosophers argued that absolute divine self-sufficiency implies God has no need for attributes, making them redundant. The theologian responds by distinguishing two types of self-sufficiency and showing that attributes are intrinsic perfections, not external tools. This passage clarifies a key point in divine attribute theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two aspects of self-sufficiency
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the distinction between essential self-sufficiency (not needing place) and attributive self-sufficiency (not needing partners).
The theologian uses this distinction to answer the philosophers.
2
Understand the key analogy with existence
The theologian draws an analogy: just as God's existence is not a tool by which He becomes self-sufficient, so His attributes are not tools. See how this counters the objection.
This analogy is crucial for the refutation.
3
Why eternal attributes cannot be tools
The passage states that need would only arise if attributes were like instruments. Since attributes are eternal, they cannot be instruments. Consider why eternality rules out instrumentality.
4
Conclude how the objection is invalidated
The theologian concludes that the objection (ilzaam) is invalid and the confusion (al-eehaam) is removed. Review how each part of the argument leads to this conclusion.
This step solidifies the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجواب عن قول الفلاسفة : إنه تعالى مستغن على الإطلاق، قيل: الاستغناء من حيث الذات أنه لا يحتاج إلى مكان ومحل، والاستغناء من حيث الصفات أنه لا يحتاج إلى شريك في القدرة والفعل ومعين في العلم والإرادة، ووزير في التصرف والتدبير فهو تعالى مستغن على الإطلاق، وإنما يستغني بكمال صفاته وصفات جلاله ، فكيف يقال : إنه احتاج إلى ما به استغنى، أليس القول بأنه استغنى بوجوده في وجوده عن موجد، ولا يقال : إنه لما استغنى عند ذلك كان محتاجاً إليه، كذلك نقول : إنه استغنى بذاته وصفاته فلا الموصوف محتاج إلى الصفة، ولا الصفة احتاجت إلى الموصوف وإنما يتحقق الاحتياج أن لو كانت الصفة على مثال الآلة والأداة، والصفات الأزلية يستحيل أن تكون آلة؛ فبطل الإلزام وزال الإيهام.
As for the answer to the philosophers' statement that He, exalted, is absolutely self-sufficient, it is said: self-sufficiency from the aspect of the essence is that He does not need a place or location, and self-sufficiency from the aspect of the attributes is that He does not need a partner in power and action, nor a helper in knowledge and will, nor a vizier in disposal and management. So He, exalted, is absolutely self-sufficient, and He is self-sufficient by virtue of the perfection of His attributes and the attributes of His majesty. So how can it be said that He needed that by which He is self-sufficient? Is it not similar to saying that He is self-sufficient by His existence in His existence from a creator, and it is not said that when He became self-sufficient thereby, He was in need of it? Similarly we say: He is self-sufficient by His essence and attributes, so neither is the described in need of the attribute, nor is the attribute in need of the described. Need would only be realized if the attribute were like an instrument and tool, and the eternal attributes cannot be instruments. So the objection is invalidated and the confusion is removed.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (jawaab) of the philosophers' claim that absolute self-sufficiency (al-istighnaa') precludes attributes. The theologian first distinguishes two aspects of self-sufficiency: essential self-sufficiency (not needing place or location) and attributive self-sufficiency (not needing partners, helpers, or viziers). God is absolutely self-sufficient, yet His self-sufficiency is realized through the perfection of His attributes. The objection that attributes would imply need is countered by analogy: just as God's existence is not a tool by which He becomes self-sufficient (since He is self-sufficient by His very essence), similarly, His attributes are not tools but are intrinsic perfections. The attributes are eternal (azaliyya), and the concept of a tool implies dependence, which is impossible for the eternal attributes. Thus, the objection that attributes entail need is baseless; the confusion is removed.

Key Terms
استغناء
ذات
صفات
آلة
أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is absolutely self-sufficient, He cannot have any attributes because attributes would imply a need for them.
Correction: Self-sufficiency from the aspect of attributes means not needing partners or helpers, not lacking attributes. Attributes are the perfection of self-sufficiency, not a need.

Checkpoint
According to the theologian, how is the self-sufficiency of God from the aspect of attributes understood?
A) He does not need a place or location.
B) He does not need a partner in power and action, nor a helper in knowledge and will, nor a vizier in disposal.
C) He does not need any attributes at all.
D) He is self-sufficient solely by essence, not by attributes.

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency is not diminished by attributes; rather, attributes are the very perfection of His self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever think of God's attributes as separate 'things' that He 'uses' rather than as perfections of His very essence? How does this lesson challenge that subtle anthropomorphism in your heart?
?
If you truly believe God is self-sufficient by His attributes, what does that mean for how you ask Him for help? Are you treating His attributes as tools to get what you want?
?
The philosophers' objection that attributes imply need is a logical trick. What spiritual 'objections' do you harbor in your heart that make you uneasy about God's perfection? Will you surrender them today?

Division of Existence Refutes Composition
Philosophers object that attributes would make God composite; Ash'ari responds with division of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari refutes the philosophers' objection that attributes would make God composite by using the division of existence into necessary by itself and necessary by another.

Core Idea
The division of existence shows that necessity and existence can combine without implying true composition.

The philosophers raised a powerful objection: if God has attributes, He would be composed of multiple things, contradicting His oneness. In this passage, the Ash'ari turns the tables by showing that the philosophers' own framework of existence leads to composition if taken to its logical conclusion. We'll see how the division of existence refutes their claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' objection
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers argue that attributes must either be necessary by themselves (leading to multiple gods) or subsist through the essence (leading to composition).
Notice the dilemma: both options seem to lead to an impossible conclusion.
2
Follow the Ash'ari response
The Ash'ari introduces the division of existence into necessary by itself and necessary by another as a decisive proof. Existence is general, necessity is specific; they share the general and differ in the specific.
Focus on the logical structure: existence (wujud) vs. necessity (wujub).
3
Grasp the counter-argument
The Ash'ari shows that if existence is a common genus, then composition is unavoidable in the philosophers' own system. Thus their objection backfires.
This is a classic dialectical move: turning the opponent's own premises against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بذاته بل بالمقوم، والواجب بذاته لن يتصور إلا واحدا، والمعاني التي أثبتموها؛ إما أن تكون واجبة بذاتها فيلزم أن يكون اثنان واجبي الوجود وذلك محال كما قدمناه، وإما أن لا تكون واجبة بذاتها بل يكون قوامها بالذات فيلزم ما ذكرناه آنفا. قيل لهم قسمة الوجود إلى واجب بذاته وإلى واجب بغيره دليل قاطع على نقص إلزامكم، فإن الوجود من حيث هو وجود قد عم الواجب والجائز، والوجوب من حيث هو وجوب قد خص الواجب، فاشتركا في الأعم وافترقا في الأخص، وما به عم غير ما به خص فتركبت الذات من وجود عام ووجوب خاص، فهو كتركيب الذات من واجب الذات، قد شملت الواجبين ويفصل لكل واحدهما بفصل عن الواجب الآخر.
By itself but by the constituent, and the necessary by itself can only be conceived as one. And the attributes which you have affirmed: either they are necessary by themselves, then it follows that there are two necessary of existence, and that is impossible as we have stated; or they are not necessary by themselves but their subsistence is by the essence, then it follows what we mentioned earlier. It was said to them: The division of existence into necessary by itself and necessary by another is a decisive proof of the deficiency of your conclusion. For existence, inasmuch as it is existence, has encompassed the necessary and the contingent, and necessity, inasmuch as it is necessity, has specified the necessary. So they share in the more general and differ in the more specific. And that by which it is general is other than that by which it is specific, so the essence is composed of a general existence and a specific necessity. So it is like the composition of the essence from two necessary essences; it includes the two necessarys and each one is distinguished from the other necessary by a differentia.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage contains an objection from the philosophers and the Ash'ari response. First, the philosophers argue that if the attributes are necessary by themselves, then there would be two necessary beings, which is impossible; if they are not necessary by themselves but subsist through the essence, then the earlier argument about composition applies. The Ash'ari response then presents a key distinction: existence (wujud) is divided into necessary by itself and necessary by another. Existence in general applies to both the necessary and the contingent, while necessity (wujub) specifically applies to the necessary. Thus the essence is composed of a general existence and a specific necessity, analogous to the composition of two necessary essences, each distinguished by a differentia. The point is that the philosophers' own assumption that existence is a common genus leads to composition, so their objection backfires.

Key Terms
واجب بذاته
واجب بغيره
وجوب
وجود
تركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari is admitting composition in God by discussing existence and necessity as separate.
Correction: The Ash'ari is not admitting real composition; he is showing that the philosophers' own framework implies a conceptual composition, so their objection fails.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the philosophers' claim that attributes lead to composition?
A) The attributes are identical with the essence.
B) The division of existence into necessary by itself and necessary by another refutes the claim.
C) The philosophers have misunderstood the nature of existence.
D) The attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari shows that the philosophers' own division of existence actually implies composition, thus their objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even the most refined philosophical reasoning can lead to self-contradiction. When you defend your beliefs about God, how often do you check whether your own premises lead to the very conclusion you deny? Will you commit to examining your arguments with the same rigor the Ash'ari applied here?

Existence and Necessity: A Symmetrical Argument
Philosophers argue existence is not common equally; Ash'ari retorts the same applies to necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari uses symmetry to refute the philosophers' argument that existence is not a genus.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own logic about existence applies equally to necessity.

In this short exchange, the philosophers try to show that existence cannot be a genus because it does not apply equally to necessary and contingent beings. The Ash'ari responds with a clever counter: the same reasoning applies to necessity itself. This lesson unpacks that symmetrical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
First, read the opening sentence where the philosophers argue that existence applies primarily to one division, so it is not a genus.
Notice the terms: 'sawiyya' (equally) and 'bil-awla' (primarily).
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
Then read the response: the Ash'ari says the same applies to necessity, so the philosophers' own logic refutes them.
The response is perfectly symmetrical.
3
Understand the logical move
Reflect on why this argument works: if unequal application disqualifies existence from being a genus, it must also disqualify necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الوجود ليس يعم القسمين بالسوية، بل هو في أحدهما بالأولى والأول، وفي الثاني بلا أولى ولا أول، فلم يكن جزا مقوما، فلم يصلح أن يكون جنسا، فلم يلزم التركيب منه والتقوم به. قيل : هذا بعينه في الوجوب جوابنا، فإن الوجوب ليس يعم القسمين بالسوية، فلم يكن الوجوب جزا مقوما، فلم يصلح أن يكون جنسا، فلم يلزم التركيب والتقوم به.
The philosophers said: Existence does not apply to the two divisions equally, but it is in one of them primarily and first, and in the second without primality and firstness, so it was not a constituent part, so it was not fit to be a genus, so composition from it and subsistence by it does not follow. It was said: This is the very same answer for us concerning necessity. For necessity does not apply to the two divisions equally, so necessity was not a constituent part, so it was not fit to be a genus, so composition and subsistence by it do not follow.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage presents a rejoinder from the philosophers and the Ash'ari counter-response. The philosophers argue that existence (al-wujud) does not apply equally to the necessary and the contingent; it applies primarily to the necessary (God) and only secondarily to contingent beings. Therefore existence is not a genus that would cause composition. The Ash'ari response is symmetrical: the same reasoning applies to necessity (al-wujub). Necessity also does not apply equally to the necessary by itself and the necessary by another, so it too cannot serve as a genus that would cause composition. Thus the philosophers' own argument disproves their objection.

Key Terms
سوية
بالأولى
جزء مقوم
جنس
تركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari is conceding that existence is not a genus.
Correction: He is not conceding; he is using the same logic to show the philosophers' argument would also make necessity not a genus, which they would not accept.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the philosophers' claim that existence is not a genus because it applies primarily?
A) He denies that existence applies primarily.
B) He says the same applies to necessity, so it also cannot be a genus.
C) He argues that necessity is a genus.
D) He introduces a new division of existence.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari uses a symmetrical argument to show that if unequal application disqualifies existence as a genus, the same applies to necessity, thereby refuting the philosophers' objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your beliefs about God, have you ever used an argument that could be turned against you? How does this symmetrical argument challenge you to examine the consistency of your own reasoning?
?

?
Reflecting on this passage: are there areas in your life where you hold others to a standard you yourself do not meet? The Ash'ari uses the philosophers' own logic against them. Is there a 'symmetry' you need to apply to your own heart today?

The Negative Concept Argument Against Genus and Differentia
Philosophers argue necessity is negative so cannot be differentia; Ash'ari retorts it then cannot be genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari theologian uses the philosophers' own definition of necessity as a negative concept to argue that it cannot serve as a genus either, exposing a contradiction in their system.

Core Idea
If necessity is negative, it cannot be a differentia or a genus—the philosophers' logic backfires.

This short but sharp passage presents a classic Kalam rebuttal. The philosophers had argued that necessity, being a negative concept, cannot be a differentia. The Ash'ari theologian retorts by applying the same reasoning to the concept of genus, showing that the philosophers are inconsistent. By examining this exchange, we see how careful definition of terms can unravel an opponent's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Begin by reading the philosophers' assertion that necessity is a negative concept and therefore cannot be a differentia (fasl). Understand the definition they give: 'its existence is not derived from another'.
Notice that they treat necessity as purely privative.
2
Identify the Ash'ari counter-argument
The Ash'ari retorts that if the negative nature disqualifies necessity from being a differentia, then it equally disqualifies it from being a genus (jins). Observe how he turns their own criterion against them.
This is a classic logical move: 'what applies to one applies to the other'.
3
See the logical consequence
The Ash'ari points out that the philosophers themselves treat necessity as a genus when they say both the divine essence and attributes are necessary. If it cannot be a genus, then their own system allows multiple necessary beings, which contradicts their monotheistic tenets.
This step reveals the internal contradiction.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this argument shows the importance of consistent definition and logical consequence in Kalam. The philosophers are forced to either abandon their criterion or accept undesirable conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الوجوب معنى سلبي معناه أن وجوده غير مستفاد من غيره، فلم يصلح أن يكون فصلا للوجود. قيل : وإذا لم يصلح أن يكون فصلا لكونه معنى سلبيا فلا يصلح أن يكون جنسا لكونه معنى سلبيا، وألزمتمونا كونه جنسا إذ قلنا: ذاته تعالى واجبة لذاتها، وصفاته تعالى واجبة لذاتها أيضا بمعنى أن وجودها غير مستفاد من غيرها، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون اثنان كل واحد واجب الوجود لذاته؟ فإن ألزمتمونا بالاشتراك في شيء والافتراق في شيء آخر لزمكم في الوجوب والوجود كذلك.
The philosophers said: Necessity is a negative concept, meaning that its existence is not derived from another, so it was not fit to be a differentia for existence. It was said: And if it was not fit to be a differentia because it is a negative concept, then it is not fit to be a genus because it is a negative concept. And you have required us to regard it as a genus when we say: His essence, exalted, is necessary by itself, and His attributes, exalted, are necessary by themselves as well, in the sense that their existence is not derived from another. So why is it not permissible that there be two, each one necessary of existence by itself? For if you require us to have a commonality in something and a difference in something else, then the same applies to you regarding necessity and existence.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
In this passage, the philosophers argue that necessity (al-wujub) is a negative concept, meaning that the existence of the necessary is not derived from another. Because it is negative, it cannot serve as a differentia (fasl) for existence. The Ash'ari response is that if it cannot be a differentia due to being negative, then it also cannot be a genus (jins) for the same reason. Yet the philosophers themselves treat necessity as a genus when they argue that the divine essence and attributes are both necessary in the sense of not deriving existence from another. The Ash'ari then poses a challenge: if the philosophers reject composition based on shared and differing aspects, they must apply the same logic to their own concept of necessity and existence, thus creating a self-contradiction.

Key Terms
معنى سلبي
فصل
جنس
اشتراك
افتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari is defending the idea that attributes are distinct entities, but he is actually using the philosophers' own principles against them to show inconsistency.
Correction: The Ash'ari does not here affirm any positive ontology of attributes; he merely points out the logical inconsistency in the philosophers' position.

Checkpoint
Why do the philosophers consider necessity unsuitable as a differentia?
A) Because it is a positive attribute.
B) Because it is a negative concept.
C) Because it is shared by many beings.
D) Because it is an accident.

Key Takeaway
Necessity as a negative concept cannot serve as differentia or genus, exposing inconsistency in the philosophers' own scheme.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is necessary,' do you realize that this very concept—if misused—can lead to the problem of multiple necessary beings? How does this passage guard your tawhid? Have you ever considered that using the same term for Allah and His attributes might lead to confusion? What does this teach you about the precision required in speaking of Allah?
?
The philosophers thought their argument was safe, but the Ash'ari showed it backfired. In your own life, are there arguments you rely on that could actually undermine what you believe? Examine one of your common defenses of aqeedah—does it hold up under the same scrutiny the Ash'ari applies here?
?
This passage shows that a negative attribute (such as necessity being 'not dependent') cannot serve as a distinguishing feature. In your understanding of Allah, do you rely on negative descriptions more than positive ones? How does this affect your worship? Are you content to know what Allah is not, or are you seeking to know what He is in His positive attributes? Let this push you beyond mere negation into affirmation of His perfect attributes as He revealed them.

Existence by Analogy and the Problem of Composition
Philosophers argue existence is analogical, yet attributes cause composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers use the concept of analogical predication to avoid genus, but then argue that distinct attributes lead to composition in God.

Core Idea
Analogical existence avoids genus, but distinct attributes still compose God.

The philosophers seek to avoid attributing genus to God by arguing that existence is analogical, not univocal. However, they turn this argument against the Ash'ari: if God has distinct attributes, these act like differentiae, making the divine essence composite. In this passage, they compare divinity to humanity to drive the point home.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two parts: first the claim about tashkik, then the comparison with two necessary existents and attributes.
The argument is compact; take your time.
2
Identify the logical structure
Find where the philosophers state that existence is by analogy (tashkik) and why that avoids genus. Then locate the shift to discussing two necessary existents and attributes leading to composition.
The key phrase is 'بخلاف موجودين واجبين' marking the contrast.
3
Analyze the comparison with humanity
Notice how the philosophers compare divinity (essence + attributes) to humanity (animality + rationality). This analogy drives home the claim that composition is inevitable if attributes are real.
The term 'تأليف معقول' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : الوجود يطلق على الموجودات بالتشكيك لا بالتشريك ولا بالتواطؤ؛ إذ معناه أنه وإن عم إلا أن عمومه ليس بالتسوية، فإنه في الواجب لذاته وبذاته فهو أولى وأول، وفي الجائز لغيره وبغيره فهو لا أولى ولا أول، فلم يصلح أن يكون جنسا، فلم يلزم منه التركيب من جنس وفصل، بخلاف موجودين واجبين كل واحد منهما وجوبه بذاته، وينفصل كل واحد منهما عن الآخر بفصل ذاتي كالعلمية فإنها والذات تشتركان في وجوب الوجود وينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر بفصل ذاتي، وكذلك العلم والقدرة المشتركان في كونهما معنيين متباينين ثابتين أزليين، وينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر بفصل ذاتي، فتكون الإلهية حقيقة ما متركبة من ذات قائمة بنفسها وصفات مختلفة قائمة بالذات، فلا يوجد فرق بين الإنسانية المركبة من الحيوانية والناطقية، وبين الإلهية المركبة من الذات والصفات، وحينئذ لا فرق بين التأليف المحسوس والتأليف المعقول.
The philosophers said: Existence is applied to existents by analogy (tashkik), not by univocity (tashrik) nor by synonymy (tawatu'); its meaning is that although it is general, its generality is not equal. For it is in the necessary by itself and through itself, so it is primary and first; and in the contingent by another and through another, so it is not primary nor first. So it was not fit to be a genus, so composition from genus and differentia does not follow from it. Unlike two necessary existents, each of which has its necessity by itself, and each is distinguished from the other by an essential differentia, like knowledge ('ilmiyya): it and the essence share in the necessity of existence and each is distinguished from the other by an essential differentia. Likewise, knowledge and power share in being two distinct, established, eternal attributes, and each is distinguished from the other by an essential differentia. So divinity (al-ilahiyya) is a reality composed of an essence subsisting by itself and various attributes subsisting by the essence. Thus there is no difference between humanity composed of animality and rationality, and divinity composed of essence and attributes. And in that case, there is no difference between sensible composition and intellectual composition.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' final argument in this exchange. They argue that existence is predicated by analogy (tashkik), meaning it applies primarily to God and secondarily to creatures, so it is not a true genus that would cause composition. However, they then compare two necessary existents (like a possible second god) or the attributes of God: they share the necessity of existence but are distinguished by essential differentiae. For example, knowledge and power are both eternal attributes but differ in their specific natures. Consequently, the divine reality (al-ilahiyya) would be composed of an essence and diverse attributes, just as a human is composed of animality and rationality. The philosophers conclude that there is no difference between sensible composition (like a physical object) and intellectual composition (like the definition of a species). Thus they argue that the Ash'ari affirmation of distinct attributes leads to composition in God, which is impossible.

Key Terms
التشكيك
التشريك
التواطؤ
فصل ذاتي
إلهية
إنسانية
تأليف محسوس
تأليف معقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers are denying that God has attributes entirely, but they are using the logic of genus and species to show that if attributes are real and distinct, God becomes composite like a creature.
Correction: The philosophers do not categorically deny attributes; they argue that affirming distinct essential attributes leads to a composition that is incompatible with divine simplicity.

Checkpoint
What conclusion do the philosophers draw from comparing divinity to humanity?
A) Both are equally composite.
B) Divinity is not composite because God is simple.
C) The Ash'ari view makes God composite like a human.
D) Attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that affirming distinct attributes in God results in a composition analogous to that of a human being, which they consider impossible for the divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that such a view reduces God to a composite being, no different from a creature. Examine your own conception of God: do you truly believe He is absolutely simple, with no composition whatsoever? What steps will you take to purify your understanding of tawhid?

Ask yourself: What mental images or assumptions do I carry about God that might imply composition? Commit to correcting any such notions by deepening your study of divine simplicity.
