Existence by Gradation and Divine Simplicity
Objection and response on existence as genus and divine composition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that existence is applied by gradation (tashkik) to God and creatures, thereby preserving divine simplicity and avoiding composition from genus and differentia.

Core Idea
Existence is not a genus: its gradation prevents divine composition.

In this passage, an objector challenges the philosophers to explain how God's essence and attributes can share existence without implying a genus, which would lead to composition. The philosophers respond by introducing the concept of tashkik (gradation) to show that existence is not univocal, thus the divine essence remains simple.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that an objector (likely an Ash'ari) claims that if existence is a negative meaning and cannot be a differentia, it also cannot be a genus, yet the Ash'ari position that God's essence and attributes are necessary by themselves seems to imply a shared genus of existence.
The objection is: why not two necessary beings if they share existence as a genus?
2
Analyze the philosophers' response: existence by gradation
The philosophers state that existence is applied by tashkik (gradation), not by tashrik (equivocation) or tawatu' (univocity). This means existence is general but not equally: it is more prior in the Necessary and less in the contingent.
Because of gradation, existence cannot serve as a common genus.
3
Understand why gradation prevents genus and composition
A genus requires univocity—equal predication across its species. Since existence is not equal between God and creatures, it cannot be a genus. Therefore, no composition from genus and differentia follows for the divine essence.
Thus divine simplicity is preserved.
4
Compare with the example of multiple necessary beings
The philosophers contrast with a hypothetical case of two necessary beings that would require an essential differentia to separate them, leading to composition. But since God is uniquely necessary by essence, no such differentia exists.
This reinforces that divinity is not composite like humanity (animality + rationality).
5
Reflect on the implication for divine simplicity
The passage concludes that if divinity were composed of essence and attributes (as Ash'aris hold), it would be analogous to humanity's composition, blurring the distinction between sensible and intelligible composition. This challenges the Ash'ari view of multiple eternal attributes.
The philosophers argue for absolute divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل : وإذا لم يصلح أن يكون فصلا لكونه معنى سلبيا فلا يصلح أن يكون جنساً لكونه معنى سلبيًّا، وألزمتمونا كونه جنسًا إذ قلنا : ذاته تعالى واجبة لذاتها، وصفاته تعالى واجبة لذاتها أيضًا بمعنى أن وجودها غير مستفاد من غيرها، فلِمَ لا يجوز أن يكون اثنان كل واحد واجب الوجود لذاته؟ فإن ألزمتمونا بالاشتراك في شيء والافتراق في شيء آخر لزمكم في الوجوب والوجود كذلك. قالت الفلاسفة : الوجود يطلق على الموجودات بالتشكيك لا بالتشريك ولا بالتواطؤ ؛ إذ معناه أنه وإن عم إلا أن عمومه ليس بالتسوية، فإنه في الواجب لذاته وبذاته فهو أولى وأول، وفي الجائز لغيره وبغيره فهو لا أولى ولا أول، فلم يصلح أن يكون جنسا، فلم يلزم منه التركيب من جنس وفصل، بخلاف موجودين واجبين كل واحد منهما وجوبه بذاته، وينفصل كل واحد منهما عن الآخر بفصل ذاتي كالعلمية فإنها والذات تشتركان في وجوب الوجود وينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر بفصل ذاتي، وكذلك العلم والقدرة المشتركان في كونهما معنيين متباينين ثابتين أزليين، وينفصل أحدهما عن الآخر بفصل ذاتي، فتكون الإلهية حقيقة متركبة من ذات قائمة بنفسها وصفات مختلفة قائمة بالذات، فلا يوجد فرق بين الإنسانية المركبة من الحيوانية والناطقية، وبين الإلهية المركبة من الذات والصفات، وحينئذ لا فرق بين التأليف المحسوس والتأليف المعقول.
It was said: Since it is not fit to be a differentia because it is a negative meaning, then it is not fit to be a genus because it is a negative meaning. And you have obliged us that it is a genus when we said: His essence, exalted, is necessary by itself, and His attributes are necessary by themselves also, meaning that their existence is not derived from other than them. So why is it not permissible that there be two, each one necessary of existence by itself? If you oblige us with sharing in something and differing in another, then the same applies to wujub (necessity) and wujud (existence). The philosophers said: Existence is applied to existing things by gradation (tashkik), not by equivocation (tashrik) nor by univocity (tawatu’); because its meaning is that even though it is general, its generality is not by equality. For in the Necessary by itself and in itself, it is more prior and prior, and in the contingent by another and through another, it is neither prior nor prior. So it is not fit to be a genus, so composition (tarkib) from genus and differentia does not follow from it. Unlike two necessary existents, each one its necessity by itself, and each one is separated from the other by an essential differentia, like the attribute of knowledge (al-'ilmiyya), for it and the essence share in the necessity of existence and each is separated from the other by an essential differentia. Likewise, knowledge and power share in that they are two distinct, established, eternal meanings, and each is separated from the other by an essential differentia. Thus divinity would be a reality composed of an essence subsisting by itself and various attributes subsisting by the essence. So there is no difference between humanity composed of animality and rationality and divinity composed of essence and attributes. And then there is no difference between sensible composition and intelligible composition.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection and the philosophers' response. The objection states that if existence cannot be a differentia because it is a negative meaning, then it cannot be a genus either, and the Ash'ari claim that God's essence and attributes are necessary by themselves implies they share in existence as a genus, leading to the possibility of multiple necessary beings. The philosophers then respond by explaining that existence is applied to things by gradation (tashkik), not by univocity or equivocation, meaning its generality is not equal. In the Necessary, existence is more prior, while in the contingent it is not. Therefore, existence cannot serve as a genus, so no composition from genus and differentia follows for the divine essence. The philosophers contrast this with the case of multiple necessary beings, which would require an essential differentia to separate them, leading to composition. They argue that if one posits a God composed of essence and attributes (as the Ash'aris do), then divinity becomes a composite reality analogous to humanity composed of animality and rationality. This blurs the distinction between sensible and intelligible composition, undermining divine simplicity.

Key Terms
معنى سلبي
تشكيك
جنس
فصل
تركيب
واجب الوجود
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think existence is univocal (applied in the same way) between God and creatures.
Correction: The philosophers argue that existence is applied by gradation (tashkik): it is not equal between the Necessary and the contingent, thus avoiding genus and composition.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, why does existence not serve as a genus for the Necessary Being?
A) Because existence is a negative meaning.
B) Because existence is applied by gradation, not equality.
C) Because the Necessary has no attributes.
D) Because existence is only an accident.

Key Takeaway
Existence is applied by gradation, not univocally, so it cannot be a genus for God; thus divine simplicity is preserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the philosophers are right that any comparison between divine and created existence leads to error, then where in your own thinking might you be subtly likening God to creation—perhaps by expecting Him to act like a human or by measuring His power against worldly forces? How will you actively guard against such comparisons?
?
This passage warns that treating God's attributes as distinct and eternal could lead to a view of God as composite. Do you ever feel tension between affirming God's attributes and affirming His oneness? What is one concrete step you can take this week to deepen your understanding of divine simplicity, so that your worship of Him is rooted in awe of His unique, uncompounded reality?

Are Mental Distinctions Real? Responding to the Philosophers
Response to philosophers on mental vs. real distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the reality of the distinction between necessity and possibility, even if it is mentally conceived, and why this suffices for theological description.

Core Idea
Mental distinctions can be real grounds for rational arguments.

The philosophers argue that the distinction between a necessary being and a possible being exists only in the mind, not in external reality. How can we base theological claims on a mere mental distinction? The author responds by showing that many accepted distinctions—like the difference between an accident and a specific color—are also mental, yet we consider them valid. This lesson explores that powerful counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Philosophers' Claim
Read the opening sentence: 'The philosophers said: The distinction between them is a distinction in the mind...' This sets up the objection—they claim the distinction is purely mental.
Note that this is a classic objection against kalam arguments.
2
Follow the Author's Counterexample
The author gives two examples: accident vs. color, and humanity's composition from life and speech. Both distinctions are mental, yet we accept them as real.
The point is that mental does not mean imaginary.
3
Understand the Logical Nuance
The author explains that in logic, some general-specific pairs (genus-differentia) yield definitions, while others do not. But that does not affect the reality of the distinction.
The goal is description, not definition of God.
4
Apply the Lesson
Reflect on how this argument defends the use of rational distinctions in theology. Why is it enough that the distinction is mentally sound?
The author concludes that being described by an attribute is broader than being defined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : التميز بينهما تميز في الذهن بالاعتبار العقلي لا في الوجود. قيل لهم : التميز بين العرضية واللونية تميز في الذهن بالاعتبار العقلي لا في الوجود، وكذلك الإنسانية في كونها مركبة من حياة ونطق فما الفرق بين وجود عام ووجوب خاص، وبين عرض عام ولون خاص؟ والتركيب كالتركيب إلا أن أحد العامين والخاصين من جنس وفصل على اصطلاح المنطق، فيصلح أن يركب منهما حد الشيء، والثاني عام وخاص لا يصلح أن يركب منهما حد الشيء، والفرق من هذا الوجه ليس يقدح في غرضنا من الإلزام، فإنا لم نلزم كون الباري تعالى محدودًا ، بل ألزمنا كونه موصوفا والموصوف بالصفة أعم من المحدود بالحد
The philosophers said: The distinction between them is a distinction in the mind by rational consideration, not in existence. It was said to them: The distinction between accidentalness and coloring is a distinction in the mind by rational consideration, not in existence. Likewise, humanity in its being composed of life and speech [is in the mind]. So what is the difference between a general existence and a specific necessity, and a general accident and a specific color? The composition is like the composition, except that one of the general and specific [pairs] is of genus and differentia according to the convention of logic, so it is valid to combine them to give the definition of a thing, while the second is general and specific and it is not valid to combine them to give the definition of a thing. And the difference from this aspect does not harm our purpose in the necessary implication, for we do not imply that the Exalted Creator is defined, but rather we imply that He is described, and the described by an attribute is more general than the defined by a definition.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer with the philosophers. The philosophers claim that the distinction between necessity and possibility is only mental (fi al-dhihn), not real in existence. The author responds by pointing out that many real distinctions are mental: e.g., the distinction between an accident in general and a specific color is mental, yet we accept it. Similarly, humanity is considered composed of life and speech only in the mind. The author then asks: what is the difference between the case of a general existence and specific necessity, and that of a general accident and a specific color? In both cases, the composition is similar: one is a general concept and the other a specific concept. The only difference is that in logic, some such compositions (genus and differentia) can yield a definition (hadd) of a thing, while others (like existence and necessity) do not yield a definition. However, this only shows a difference in logical function, not a difference in the reality of the distinction. The author clarifies that his purpose is not to define God (which would imply limitation), but to describe Him. Being described by an attribute is more general than being defined by a definition. Thus the mental distinction suffices for his argument.

Key Terms

ذهن (dhihn)
اعتبار عقلي (i'tibar 'aqli)
عرض (arad)
لون (lawn)
حد (hadd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a distinction is 'only in the mind', it is imaginary and has no basis in reality.
Correction: Many valid distinctions (e.g., accident vs. color, humanity as composed of life and speech) are mentally conceived yet grounded in external reality. The mental nature does not negate their validity for rational argument.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that many real distinctions (like accident and color) are also mental, so it is not a problem
C) He says that existence is not a genus
D) He says that the distinction is in the mind but not in existence

Key Takeaway
Mental distinctions can be real and sufficient for rational theological arguments about God's attributes.

The author insists that we describe God, not define Him. Are you comfortable with not being able to fully define or comprehend God? Or does your heart yearn for certainty that goes beyond description? Examine whether you have ever tried to 'define' God in a way that limits Him, and ask yourself: what would it mean to simply describe Him with the attributes He has revealed?
?
This passage argues that mental distinctions can still be real. Where in your own life have you dismissed something as 'just in your head' when it actually had real implications? General-Specific Composition Even Without Genus-Differentia
First argument: general-specific composition yields multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if existence is not a genus, the shared general meaning of existence between necessary and contingent creates a composition of general and specific that implies multiplicity.

Core Idea
Composition follows from any shared meaning, not just from genus-differentia.

Sanusi addresses the philosophers' concession that existence is not a genus. He argues that even so, existence remains a general meaning encompassing both necessary and possible. Since division applies to meaning, not words, the distinction between general existence and specific necessity constitutes a composition of general and specific. This yields the same consequence as composition of genus and differentia: multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify the two types of composition
Sanusi contrasts composition of genus-differentia with composition of general-specific. Understand why general-specific composition still entails multiplicity.
3
Connect to the larger argument
Explain why this argument undermines the philosophers' position. It shows that composition is unavoidable even on their own terms.
This step is crucial for understanding the rest of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن سلم لكم أن الوجود ليس بجنس فلم يخرج عن كونه عاما شاملا للقسمين ؛ إذ لولا شموله وإلا لما صح التقسيم، فإن التقسيم إنما يرد على المعنى لا على مجرد اللفظة، فالمعنى العام للواجب والجائز غير المعنى الخاص بأحد القسمين، فإذا لم يكن التركيب تركبا من جنس وفصل كان تركبا من عام وخاص، فيفيد أحدهما من التصور ما لا يفيده الآخر، فيلزم منه عين ما يلزم من الفصل والجنس.
قالت الفلاسفة : التميز بينهما تميز في الذهن بالاعتبار العقلي لا في الوجود.
قيل لهم : التميز بين العرضية واللونية تميز في الذهن بالاعتبار العقلي لا في الوجود، وكذلك الإنسانية في كونها مركبة من حياة ونطق فما الفرق بين وجود عام ووجوب خاص، وبين عرض عام ولون خاص؟ والتركيب كالتركيب إلا أن أحد العامين والخاصين من جنس وفصل على اصطلاح المنطق، فيصلح أن يركب منهما حد الشيء، والثاني عام وخاص لا يصلح أن يركب منهما حد الشيء، والفرق من هذا الوجه ليس يقدح في غرضنا من الإلزام، فإنا لم نلزم كون الباري تعالى محدودًا، بل ألزمنا كونه موصوفا والموصوف بالصفة أعم من المحدود بالحد.
ثم القول بأن تركبه بالقياس إلى عقولنا وأذهاننا تسليم المسألة وزيادة أمر على الصفاتية، ويلزم عليه أن يقال له مادة وجنس وفصل وخاصية وعرض، إلى غير ذلك من أنواع التركيب بالقياس إلى عقولنا لا بالقياس إلى ذاته، ثم هم معرفة المعلوم على خلاف ما هو له، فإنه في ذاته غير مركب وفي التصور العقلي مركب، ثم الوجوب إن كان معنى إثباتيا في الذهن غير إثباتي في الخارج، فذلك تغيير حقيقة الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى شيئين، والحقائق لا تختلف بالنسبة أصلا وإن كان الوجوب معنى سلبيا في الذهن فقد استغنى بكونه نفيًا عن إلزام التركيب الذهني، وعندهم المعاني السلبية لا توجب التكثير سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج، والمعاني الإيجابية الغير إضافية لا تخلو من التكثر، سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج.
ومن المعلوم أنا إذا قلنا : يعلم ذاته ويعلم غيره فليس علمه بذاته علما بغيره من الوجه الذي كان علما بذاته، بل اعتبار علمه بذاته غير اعتبار علمه بغيره، واعتبار إضافة العقل الأول إليه عندكم غير اعتبار إضافة العقل الثاني إليه، وإذا اختلفت الاعتبارات والوجوه العقلية لزمكم التكثير في الذات، فنحن نسمي كل اعتبار صفة، وما سميتموه بالمعاني السلبية فعندنا القدم والوحدة بمثابتهما، فإن معنى القديم أنه لا أول لوجوده ومعنى الواحد أنه لا انقسام لذاته، وما سميتموه من المعاني بالمعاني الإضافية فعندنا كونه خالقا رزاقا بمثابتهما، فإن معنى الخالقية يتصور من الخلق، والرازقية من الرزق ويقي عندنا أنا أثبتنا معاني من كونه عالما قادرًا حيا، فإن رده إلى السلبية غير ممكن حتى يقال : معنى كونه عالما أنه غير جاهل، فإن غير الجاهل قد يتصور، ولا يكون عالما فهو أعم، وحتى يقال : إن معنى كونه
Then we say: If you concede that existence is not a genus, it still remains general encompassing both divisions; for without its inclusiveness division would not be valid. For division only applies to meaning, not to mere utterance. So the general meaning of the necessary and the possible is different from the specific meaning of one of the two divisions. So if the composition is not a composition of genus and differentia, it is a composition of general and specific, so each informs of something in conception that the other does not, and that entails exactly what is entailed by genus and differentia. The philosophers said: The distinction between them is a distinction in the mind by mental consideration, not in existence. It was said to them: The distinction between accidentalness and colorness is a distinction in the mind by mental consideration, not in existence, and likewise humanity in being composed of life and speech. So what is the difference between general existence and specific necessity, and between general accident and specific color? The composition is like the composition, except that one of the generals and specifics is genus and differentia according to the convention of logic, so it is valid to compose a definition of a thing from them, while the second general and specific is not valid to compose a definition from them. The difference in this respect does not harm our purpose of refutation, for we do not claim that God is defined, but we claim that He is described. And the described by an attribute is more general than the defined by a definition. Then the statement that its composition is relative to our minds and intellects is conceding the issue and adding a matter to the attributists. And it follows that we say of Him: matter, genus, differentia, property, accident, and so on of types of composition relative to our minds, not relative to His essence. Then they described the known contrary to what it is, for in His essence He is not composite, but in mental conception He is composite. Then if necessity is a positive meaning in the mind but not positive in the external, that would be a change in the reality of a single thing relative to two things, but realities do not differ relative to aspect. And if necessity is a negative meaning in the mind, then it suffices by being a negation to avoid mental composition. According to them, negative meanings do not cause multiplicity whether in the mind or in the external, while positive non-relational meanings cannot avoid multiplicity, whether in the mind or in the external. And it is known that when we say: He knows His essence and knows others, His knowledge of His essence is not knowledge of others in the same respect that it is knowledge of His essence. Rather, the consideration of His knowledge of His essence is different from the consideration of His knowledge of others, and the consideration of the addition of the first intellect to Him according to you is different from the consideration of the addition of the second intellect to Him. And if considerations and mental aspects differ, multiplicity in the essence would follow for you. So we call every consideration an attribute. And what you call negative meanings, for us 'qadim' and 'wahid' are analogous: the meaning of qadim is that there is no beginning to its existence, and the meaning of wahid is that there is no partition of its essence. And what you call from meanings relational meanings, for us His being Creator, Provider are analogous: the meaning of Creator is conceived from creation, and Provider from provision. And it remains with us that we have established meanings from His being Omniscient, Omnipotent, Living. For it is not possible to reduce them to negative, such that it is said: the meaning of His being Omniscient is that He is not ignorant. For that which is not ignorant might be conceived and not be omniscient, so it is more general. And such that it is said: the meaning of His being
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the philosophers' position that the distinction between the necessary existent (God) and the contingent is merely mental, with no real composition in the divine essence. The author proceeds step by step. First, he argues that even if existence is not a genus, it still serves as a general meaning encompassing both the necessary and the possible, and that division applies to meaning, not words. If the composition is not genus-differentia, it is general-specific, and this still yields the same consequence of composition. Next, he presents the philosophers' objection: the distinction is only in the mind by mental consideration, not in external existence. The author replies by drawing analogies: the distinction between accidentalness and colorness is also mental, as is the composition of humanity from life and speech. He asks what the difference is between general existence and specific necessity, and general accident and specific color. He concedes that one type of general-specific pair can form a logical definition, while the other cannot, but this does not undermine his goal: he is not claiming that God is defined (limited by a definition), but that He is described by attributes. Being described is more general than being defined. Then he argues that if the philosophers concede that composition is relative to our minds, they have already admitted the point, and on that basis one can apply terms like matter, genus, differentia, etc. to God relative to our minds. This would mean describing the known contrary to its true nature: God in Himself is not composite, but in mental conception He becomes composite. He then examines the nature of necessity (wujub): if it is a positive meaning in the mind but not in reality, that would imply a change in reality relative to two aspects, but realities do not differ. If necessity is a negative meaning, then it suffices to avoid mental composition, because negative meanings do not entail multiplicity. However, positive non-relational meanings necessarily involve multiplicity, both mentally and externally. He then extends the argument to divine knowledge: God's knowledge of His essence is different from His knowledge of others, and the mental considerations differ. If these considerations differ, multiplicity in the essence follows. Thus every consideration is an attribute. He then correlates the philosophers' categories of negative and relational meanings with Ash'ari concepts: 'qadim' and 'wahid' correspond to negative meanings, and 'Creator' and 'Provider' to relational ones. Finally, he insists that attributes like 'Omniscient', 'Omnipotent', and 'Living' cannot be reduced to mere negations (e.g., 'not ignorant') because 'not ignorant' is more general and does not entail actual knowledge. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: the Ash'ari position of real, positive attributes is necessary to avoid reducing God to mere negations.

الوجوب
الإمكان
الجنس
الفصل
العام
الخاص
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is not a genus, then there is no composition in the divine essence.
Correction: The absence of genus-differentia composition does not preclude general-specific composition, which still implies multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does division (تقسيم) apply to meaning and not to mere utterance?
A) Because utterances are ambiguous.
B) Because division is a logical operation based on concepts.
C) Because the philosophers denied division.
D) Because all meanings are general.

Key Takeaway
Even if existence is not a genus, the shared general meaning between necessary and contingent creates a composition of general and specific that entails multiplicity, just as genus-differentia would.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah, do you ever catch yourself mentally separating His essence from His attributes? How does this lesson's argument about unavoidable mental composition challenge you to re-evaluate the way you conceive of your Lord?
?
The author argues that even in our logical analysis, we cannot avoid composition. Have you ever felt content in your theological reasoning as if you have captured the truth about Allah? What does this lesson reveal about the limits of your own intellect?
?
If even a simple concept like existence leads to multiplicity in our minds, how does that humble you before the Oneness of Allah? What practical step can you take today to remind yourself that your understanding is always incomplete?

Refuting the Philosophers’ Mental Distinction Claim
Second part: refuting mental distinction via analogies and reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' claim that the distinction is merely mental fails, as conceding mental composition leads to even greater multiplicity and arbitrary application of terms.

Core Idea
If distinction is mental, then so is composition, but that still forces multiplicity and more terms for God.

The philosophers object that the difference between necessary and possible is only mental, not real. Sanusi replies with analogies: accidentalness and colorness are also mentally distinguished, yet they are real attributes. He then argues that if mental composition is accepted, then one can apply terms like matter, genus, differentia, etc. to God relative to our minds. This would mean describing God contrary to His true nature, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection and Sanusi's reply
The philosophers assert the distinction is only mental. Sanusi counters by drawing analogies to other mental distinctions that correspond to real differences.
2
Analyze the analogy with accidentalness and colorness
How does the analogy show that mental distinction does not preclude real composition? Think about humanity being composed of life and speech.
3
Examine the consequence of conceding mental composition
Sanusi shows that if mental composition is allowed, then many more terms can be applied to God relative to our minds, leading to absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

ثم نقول : إن سلم لكم أن الوجود ليس بجنس فلم يخرج عن كونه عاما شاملا للقسمين ؛ إذ لولا شموله وإلا لما صح التقسيم، فإن التقسيم إنما يرد على المعنى لا على مجرد اللفظة، فالمعنى العام للواجب والجائز غير المعنى الخاص بأحد القسمين، فإذا لم يكن التركيب تركبا من جنس وفصل كان تركبا من عام وخاص، فيفيد أحدهما من التصور ما لا يفيده الآخر، فيلزم منه عين ما يلزم من الفصل والجنس.
قالت الفلاسفة : التميز بينهما تميز في الذهن بالاعتبار العقلي لا في الوجود.
قيل لهم : التميز بين العرضية واللونية تميز في الذهن بالاعتبار العقلي لا في الوجود، وكذلك الإنسانية في كونها مركبة من حياة ونطق فما الفرق بين وجود عام ووجوب خاص، وبين عرض عام ولون خاص؟ والتركيب كالتركيب إلا أن أحد العامين والخاصين من جنس وفصل على اصطلاح المنطق، فيصلح أن يركب منهما حد الشيء، والثاني عام وخاص لا يصلح أن يركب منهما حد الشيء، والفرق من هذا الوجه ليس يقدح في غرضنا من الإلزام، فإنا لم نلزم كون الباري تعالى محدودًا، بل ألزمنا كونه موصوفا والموصوف بالصفة أعم من المحدود بالحد.
ثم القول بأن تركبه بالقياس إلى عقولنا وأذهاننا تسليم المسألة وزيادة أمر على الصفاتية، ويلزم عليه أن يقال له مادة وجنس وفصل وخاصية وعرض، إلى غير ذلك من أنواع التركيب بالقياس إلى عقولنا لا بالقياس إلى ذاته، ثم هم معرفة المعلوم على خلاف ما هو له، فإنه في ذاته غير مركب وفي التصور العقلي مركب، ثم الوجوب إن كان معنى إثباتيا في الذهن غير إثباتي في الخارج، فذلك تغيير حقيقة الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى شيئين، والحقائق لا تختلف بالنسبة أصلا وإن كان الوجوب معنى سلبيا في الذهن فقد استغنى بكونه نفيًا عن إلزام التركيب الذهني، وعندهم المعاني السلبية لا توجب التكثير سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج، والمعاني الإيجابية الغير إضافية لا تخلو من التكثر، سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج.
ومن المعلوم أنا إذا قلنا : يعلم ذاته ويعلم غيره فليس علمه بذاته علما بغيره من الوجه الذي كان علما بذاته، بل اعتبار علمه بذاته غير اعتبار علمه بغيره، واعتبار إضافة العقل الأول إليه عندكم غير اعتبار إضافة العقل الثاني إليه، وإذا اختلفت الاعتبارات والوجوه العقلية لزمكم التكثير في الذات، فنحن نسمي كل اعتبار صفة، وما سميتموه بالمعاني السلبية فعندنا القدم والوحدة بمثابتهما، فإن معنى القديم أنه لا أول لوجوده ومعنى الواحد أنه لا انقسام لذاته، وما سميتموه من المعاني بالمعاني الإضافية فعندنا كونه خالقا رزاقا بمثابتهما، فإن معنى الخالقية يتصور من الخلق، والرازقية من الرزق ويقي عندنا أنا أثبتنا معاني من كونه عالما قادرًا حيا، فإن رده إلى السلبية غير ممكن حتى يقال : معنى كونه عالما أنه غير جاهل، فإن غير الجاهل قد يتصور، ولا يكون عالما فهو أعم، وحتى يقال : إن معنى كونه
Then we say: If you concede that existence is not a genus, it still remains general encompassing both divisions; for without its inclusiveness division would not be valid. For division only applies to meaning, not to mere utterance. So the general meaning of the necessary and the possible is different from the specific meaning of one of the two divisions. So if the composition is not a composition of genus and differentia, it is a composition of general and specific, so each informs of something in conception that the other does not, and that entails exactly what is entailed by genus and differentia. The philosophers said: The distinction between them is a distinction in the mind by mental consideration, not in existence. It was said to them: The distinction between accidentalness and colorness is a distinction in the mind by mental consideration, not in existence, and likewise humanity in being composed of life and speech. So what is the difference between general existence and specific necessity, and between general accident and specific color? The composition is like the composition, except that one of the generals and specifics is genus and differentia according to the convention of logic, so it is valid to compose a definition of a thing from them, while the second general and specific is not valid to compose a definition from them. The difference in this respect does not harm our purpose of refutation, for we do not claim that God is defined, but we claim that He is described. And the described by an attribute is more general than the defined by a definition. Then the statement that its composition is relative to our minds and intellects is conceding the issue and adding a matter to the attributists. And it follows that we say of Him: matter, genus, differentia, property, accident, and so on of types of composition relative to our minds, not relative to His essence. Then they described the known contrary to what it is, for in His essence He is not composite, but in mental conception He is composite. Then if necessity is a positive meaning in the mind but not positive in the external, that would be a change in the reality of a single thing relative to two things, but realities do not differ relative to aspect. And if necessity is a negative meaning in the mind, then it suffices by being a negation to avoid mental composition. According to them, negative meanings do not cause multiplicity whether in the mind or in the external, while positive non-relational meanings cannot avoid multiplicity, whether in the mind or in the external. And it is known that when we say: He knows His essence and knows others, His knowledge of His essence is not knowledge of others in the same respect that it is knowledge of His essence. Rather, the consideration of His knowledge of His essence is different from the consideration of His knowledge of others, and the consideration of the addition of the first intellect to Him according to you is different from the consideration of the addition of the second intellect to Him. And if considerations and mental aspects differ, multiplicity in the essence would follow for you. So we call every consideration an attribute. And what you call negative meanings, for us 'qadim' and 'wahid' are analogous: the meaning of qadim is that there is no beginning to its existence, and the meaning of wahid is that there is no partition of its essence. And what you call from meanings relational meanings, for us His being Creator, Provider are analogous: the meaning of Creator is conceived from creation, and Provider from provision. And it remains with us that we have established meanings from His being Omniscient, Omnipotent, Living. For it is not possible to reduce them to negative, such that it is said: the meaning of His being Omniscient is that He is not ignorant. For that which is not ignorant might be conceived and not be omniscient, so it is more general. And such that it is said: the meaning of His being
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a sustained refutation of the philosophers' position that the distinction between the necessary existent (God) and the contingent is merely mental, with no real composition in the divine essence. The author proceeds step by step. First, he argues that even if existence is not a genus, it still serves as a general meaning encompassing both the necessary and the possible, and that division applies to meaning, not words. If the composition is not genus-differentia, it is general-specific, and this still yields the same consequence of composition. Next, he presents the philosophers' objection: the distinction is only in the mind by mental consideration, not in external existence. The author replies by drawing analogies: the distinction between accidentalness and colorness is also mental, as is the composition of humanity from life and speech. He asks what the difference is between general existence and specific necessity, and general accident and specific color. He concedes that one type of general-specific pair can form a logical definition, while the other cannot, but this does not undermine his goal: he is not claiming that God is defined (limited by a definition), but that He is described by attributes. Being described is more general than being defined. Then he argues that if the philosophers concede that composition is relative to our minds, they have already admitted the point, and on that basis one can apply terms like matter, genus, differentia, etc. to God relative to our minds. This would mean describing the known contrary to its true nature: God in Himself is not composite, but in mental conception He becomes composite. He then examines the nature of necessity (wujub): if it is a positive meaning in the mind but not in reality, that would imply a change in reality relative to two aspects, but realities do not differ. If necessity is a negative meaning, then it suffices to avoid mental composition, because negative meanings do not entail multiplicity. However, positive non-relational meanings necessarily involve multiplicity, both mentally and externally. He then extends the argument to divine knowledge: God's knowledge of His essence is different from His knowledge of others, and the mental considerations differ. If these considerations differ, multiplicity in the essence follows. Thus every consideration is an attribute. He then correlates the philosophers' categories of negative and relational meanings with Ash'ari concepts: 'qadim' and 'wahid' correspond to negative meanings, and 'Creator' and 'Provider' to relational ones. Finally, he insists that attributes like 'Omniscient', 'Omnipotent', and 'Living' cannot be reduced to mere negations (e.g., 'not ignorant') because 'not ignorant' is more general and does not entail actual knowledge. The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: the Ash'ari position of real, positive attributes is necessary to avoid reducing God to mere negations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental distinctions imply no real composition in the object.
Correction: Mental distinctions can reflect real differences, as in the case of accidentalness and colorness.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi mean when he says 'the described by an attribute is more general than the defined by a definition'?
A) Attributes apply to God but definitions do not.
B) Being described does not imply limitation as definition does.
C) Definition is a type of description.
D) He is arguing that God is not describable.

Key Takeaway
Conceding mental composition forces acceptance of many terms for God relative to minds, which describes Him contrary to His true nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally separate Allah's attributes (e.g., His power from His mercy), do you ever treat them as if they are multiple independent deities? How can you train your mind to always unite them in His essence while acknowledging their distinct meanings?
?
Sanusi warns that conceding a small point (mental composition) leads to bigger errors (applying terms like 'matter' to Allah). In your daily life, where have you made small intellectual concessions about Allah's nature that could slowly corrupt your understanding? What will you do to guard against that?
?
The philosophers tried to define God, but Sanusi insisted on describing Him with the attributes He revealed. Are you tempted to 'define' Allah by your own reasoning rather than accepting His descriptions as given in the Quran and hadith? Which specific attribute do you need to stop limiting and start describing as He has taught?

Divine Attributes

Positive vs. Negative Meanings: The Irreducibility of Divine Attributes
Third part: analysis of positive vs. negative meanings and the irreducibility of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between positive and negative meanings and why attributes like omniscience cannot be reduced to mere negations.

Core Idea
Positive attributes are irreducible to negations; otherwise divine perfection is lost.

Sanusi now examines the nature of necessity (wujub) to deepen the argument. He shows that if necessity is a positive meaning only in the mind but not in reality, that would change reality. If it is negative, it avoids composition but does not capture positive attributes. He applies this to divine knowledge: God's knowledge of His essence differs from knowledge of others, leading to multiple considerations. The philosophers' attempt to reduce attributes like omniscience to 'not ignorant' fails because 'not ignorant' is more general and does not entail actual knowledge. Therefore, real positive attributes are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion on necessity (wujub)
Sanusi considers two possibilities: necessity as positive or negative meaning. Understand the implications for composition.
2
Understand the argument from divine knowledge
God's knowledge of Himself and others involves different considerations, leading to multiplicity. This shows that mental multiplicity is unavoidable.
3
Grasp the final point about irreducibility
Attributes like omniscience cannot be reduced to 'not ignorant' because that is too broad. This establishes the need for positive attributes.
This is a key Ash'ari position.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Mental Composition Does Not Imply External Composition
Ash'ari critiques the philosophers' view on mental composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mental composition does not entail real composition in God, and the implications of the philosophers' position.

Core Idea
Mental composition in our intellect does not make God composite in reality.

The Ash'ari theologian begins by critiquing the philosophers who concede composition in relation to our minds but deny it in God's essence. He argues that this still implies real multiplicity because it forces us to ascribe matter, genus, differentia, and other types of composition to God. This passage lays the groundwork for defending divine simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Notice that the philosophers admit composition in relation to our intellects, but not in God's essence. The Ash'ari sees this as a concession that still implies a problem.
2
List the types of composition
The text enumerates matter, genus, differentia, property, and accident as necessary for something composite in intellect. Consider why each is problematic for God.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that God is non-composite in essence but composite in intellectual conception. This shows the distinction between mental and real composition.
This is the key insight: mental composition is merely a human cognitive construct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول بأن تركبه بالقياس إلى عقولنا وأذهاننا تسليم المسألة وزيادة أمر على الصفاتية، ويلزم عليه أن يقال له مادة وجنس وفصل وخاصية وعرض، إلى غير ذلك من أنواع التركيب بالقياس إلى عقولنا لا بالقياس إلى ذاته، ثم هم معرفة المعلوم على خلاف ما هو له، فإنه في ذاته غير مركب وفي التصور العقلي مركب، ثم الوجوب إن كان معنى إثباتيا في الذهن غير إثباتي في الخارج، فذلك تغيير حقيقة الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى شيئين، والحقائق لا تختلف بالنسبة أصلا وإن كان الوجوب معنى سلبيا في الذهن فقد استغنى بكونه نفيًا عن إلزام التركيب الذهني، وعندهم المعاني السلبية لا توجب التكثير سواء كانت في الذهن أو في الخارج، والمعاني الإيجابية الغير إضافية لا تخلو من التكثر، سواء كانت في الذهن أو في خارج
Then the statement that its composition in relation to our intellects and minds is a concession of the issue and an addition of something over the attributes, and it would be required to say of it matter, genus, differentia, property, and accident, and other such types of composition in relation to our intellects, not in relation to its essence. Then they know the known thing contrary to what it is, for it is in itself non-composite but in intellectual conception composite. Then Necessary Existence (al-wujub) if it is a positive meaning (ma‘na ithbati) in the mind, non-positive in external existence, then that is a change of the reality of one thing with respect to two things, but realities do not differ with respect to relation at all. And if Necessary Existence is a negative meaning (ma‘na salbi) in the mind, then it has sufficed, by being a negation, from entailing mental composition. And according to them, negative meanings do not cause multiplicity (al-takthir) whether they are in the mind or in external existence, while positive, non-relational meanings are not free from multiplicity, whether in the mind or in external existence.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the philosophers' position on divine composition. The author (an Ash'ari mutakallim) argues that the philosophers, by claiming composition in relation to our intellects, still imply real multiplicity. He enumerates types of mental composition (matter, genus, differentia, property, accident) that would then apply to God, which is impossible. Then he analyzes the concept of 'wujub' (necessary existence). If wujub is a positive meaning in the mind but not external, this would change the thing's reality—unacceptable. If it is a negative meaning, then it does not entail mental composition. According to the philosophers, negative meanings do not cause multiplicity, while positive non-relational ones do, whether mental or external. This sets up the Ash'ari argument that divine attributes like knowledge and power cannot be reduced to negatives.

Correction: The passage clarifies that mental composition is only in our minds, not in God's essence. God remains absolutely simple.

B) Genus
C) Time

Key Takeaway
Mental composition is a human intellectual construct and does not affect God's absolute simplicity.

?
The philosophers conceded composition in the intellect. Have you ever been satisfied with a mental model of God that actually limits Him? What step will you take today to consciously affirm that God is beyond all mental categories and composition?

Necessary Existence as Positive or Negative Meaning
Ash'ari analyzes the concept of necessary existence (wujub) as positive or negative meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analysis of necessary existence (wujub) as either a positive or negative meaning, and how the Ash'ari uses this to defend divine simplicity.

Core Idea
Necessary existence cannot be a positive meaning in the mind only, nor does it entail composition.

After discussing mental composition, the text turns to the concept of wujub (necessary existence). The theologian considers two possibilities: wujub as a positive meaning in the mind but not in reality, which would change the thing's reality; or as a negative meaning, which avoids composition. This analysis is crucial for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two possibilities for wujub
The passage examines wujub as either a positive meaning (ithbati) or a negative meaning (salbi). Recognize the difference.
2
Analyze the positive meaning scenario
If wujub is positive in the mind but not in external reality, that would change the identity of the thing, which is impossible because realities do not change based on relation.
3
Analyze the negative meaning scenario
If wujub is negative in the mind, then it does not entail mental composition, as negative meanings do not cause multiplicity.
4
Apply the general rule
The passage states that negative meanings never cause multiplicity, while positive non-relational meanings always do, whether in mind or reality.
This rule underpins the Ash'ari position that divine attributes are not positive additions.

The Necessity of Multiplicity from Different Considerations
Ash'ari argues that differing intellectual considerations imply multiplicity, and each consideration is an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari argues that the philosophers' own acceptance of different mental considerations of God necessitates multiplicity in the divine essence, and how the Ash'ari respond by calling each consideration a real attribute.

Core Idea
Different mental considerations of God necessitate multiplicity, and each consideration is a divine attribute.

In this passage, Ash'ari turns the philosophers' own framework against them. They accept that our understanding of God involves distinct intellectual considerations—such as knowledge of self versus knowledge of others, or the relations to different intellects. Ash'ari argues that if these considerations are truly different, then multiplicity in God's essence follows. Then he shows that the Ash'ari embrace this multiplicity by calling each consideration an attribute, and he clarifies how their attributes relate to the philosophers' negative and relational categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' admission
The philosophers admit that there are different mental considerations of God, such as His knowledge of Himself vs. others, and the relation to the first intellect vs. the second intellect.
These are not just differences in our mind but reflect real aspects.
2
See the necessary consequence
If the considerations differ, then multiplicity in the divine essence is necessitated. This is the core conclusion Ash'ari draws against the philosophers.
The philosophers, who profess tawhid (oneness), must account for this multiplicity.
3
Observe the Ash'ari resolution
Ash'ari states that they call each such consideration an attribute (sifa). Then he shows that their own positive attributes (qidam, wahda, khaliq, raziq) correspond to the philosophers' negative and relational categories, affirming real attributes.
This refutes the idea that Ash'ari reduce attributes to mere negations or relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن المعلوم أنا إذا قلنا : يعلم ذاته ويعلم غيره فليس علمه بذاته علما بغيره من الوجه الذي كان علما بذاته، بل اعتبار علمه بذاته غير اعتبار علمه بغيره، واعتبار إضافة العقل الأول إليه عندكم غير اعتبار إضافة العقل الثاني إليه، وإذا اختلفت الاعتبارات والوجوه العقلية لزمكم التكثير في الذات، فنحن نسمي كل اعتبار صفة، وما سميتموه بالمعاني السلبية فعندنا القدم والوحدة بمثابتهما، فإن معنى القديم أنه لا أول لوجوده ومعنى الواحد أنه لا انقسام لذاته، وما سميتموه من المعاني بالمعاني الإضافية فعندنا كونه خالقا رزاقا بمثابتهما، فإن معنى الخالقية يتصور من الخلق، والرازقية من الرزق
And it is known that if we say: He knows His essence and He knows other than His essence, then His knowledge of His essence is not knowledge of other than His essence in the respect in which it was knowledge of His essence. Rather, the consideration (i‘tibar) of His knowledge of His essence is other than the consideration of His knowledge of other than Him, and the consideration of the addition (idafa) of the first intellect to Him according to you is other than the consideration of the addition of the second intellect to Him. And if the considerations and intellectual aspects differ, then multiplicity (al-takthir) in the essence is necessitated for you. So we call every consideration an attribute (sifa). And what you have named negative meanings (al-ma‘ani al-salbiyya)—according to us, pre-eternity (al-qidam) and oneness (al-wahda) are analogous to them. For the meaning of 'pre-eternal' is that there is no beginning to its existence, and the meaning of 'one' is that there is no division to its essence. And what you have named among meanings as relational meanings (al-ma‘ani al-idafiyya)—according to us, His being creator (khaliq) and provider (raziq) are analogous to them. For the meaning of 'creator' is conceived from creation (al-khalq), and 'provider' from provision (al-rizq).
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the philosophers' own acceptance of different intellectual considerations (i‘tibar) implies multiplicity in the divine essence. The author points out that when they say God knows His essence and knows others, the consideration of His knowledge of Himself is different from the consideration of His knowledge of others. Similarly, the addition of the first intellect (as an effect) is different from that of the second intellect. Since these considerations differ, multiplicity is necessitated. The Ash'ari then responds that they call each such consideration an attribute (sifa). The philosophers' negative meanings (like 'not having a beginning' or 'not being divisible') correspond to the Ash'ari attributes of qidam (pre-eternity) and wahda (oneness). Their relational meanings (like 'creator' and 'provider') correspond to the Ash'ari attributes of khaliq and raziq. Thus the Ash'ari do not reduce all attributes to negatives or relations; they affirm real attributes.

Correction: This passage shows the Ash'ari do not reduce attributes; they identify their own positive attributes (qidam, wahda, khaliq, raziq) that correspond to the philosophers' categories, but are real and positive.

Proof That Knowledge and Power Are Real Attributes
Ash'ari demonstrates that 'alim and qadir cannot be reduced to negative or relational meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes 'knowing' and 'powerful' must be real, positive attributes and cannot be reduced to negations or relations.

Core Idea
The divine attributes of knowledge and power are distinct positive realities, not mere negations or relations.

This lesson unpacks a classic Ash'ari proof that God's attributes 'knowing' and 'powerful' are real and positive. The argument systematically eliminates alternative interpretations: first showing they cannot be merely negative (like 'not ignorant'), then showing they cannot be merely relational (like 'creator' implies creation). By process of elimination, they must be real attributes inherent in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the argument. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: two refutations followed by a conclusion.
The passage uses a dense, dialectical style typical of kalam.
2
Understand the negative refutation
Identify why 'knowing' cannot mean simply 'not ignorant'. The author argues that 'not ignorant' is more general—it includes inanimate objects that are not knowing. So the attribute of knowledge adds something positive.
The same logic applies to 'powerful' vs 'not incapable'.
3
Understand the relational refutation
See why knowledge and power cannot be merely relational attributes. Unlike 'creator' which presupposes the act of creation, knowledge and power are prior to their objects—the known follows knowledge, not the other way around.
The author draws on the philosophers' own principle that God's knowledge is active, not passive.
4
Grasp the conclusion
After eliminating negative and relational interpretations, and rejecting a composite of both, the only remaining option is that 'knowing' and 'powerful' are real, positive attributes (sifat) of God.
This conclusion is the foundation for further discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقي عندنا أنا أثبتنا معاني من كونه عالما قادرًا حيا، فإن رده إلى السلبية غير ممكن حتى يقال : معنى كونه عالما أنه غير جاهل، فإن غير الجاهل قد يتصور، ولا يكون عالما فهو أعم، وحتى يقال : إن معنى كونه قادرًا أنه غير عاجز، فإن غير العاجز قد يتصور ولا يكون قادرًا، فنفي الأولية بالضرورة يقتضي القدم، ونفي الانقسام لا يقتضي العلم والقدرة، فقد يخلو الشيء عن الجهل والعلم كالجماد، فإذا معنى العالمية والقادرية ليس معنى سلبيا، وليس هو أيضا معنى إضافيًّا، فإن الاسم الإضافي من المضاف يتلقى بمعنى أنه يحصل الفعل أولا حتى يسمى فاعلا، وليس كذلك كونه عالما قادرًا، فإن وجود المعلوم والمقدور من العلم والقدرة يحصل فهو على خلاف وضع الأسامي الإضافية خصوصا على أصلهم فإنهم قالوا علمه تعالى فعلي لا انفعالي، وعند المتكلمين العلم يتبع المعلوم، وعندهم المعلوم يتبع العلم والمقدور يتبع القدرة، هذا قالوا : إنما يصدر عنه العقل الأول لعلمه بذاته، فإذا لم يكن العلم معنى سلبيًا، ولا معنى إضافيا ولا مركبًا منهما ، فتعين أنه صفة للموصوف.
And it is established with us that we have affirmed meanings (ma‘ani) from His being knowing (‘alim), powerful (qadir), living (hayy). For reducing that to negative (salbiyya) is not possible such that it is said: the meaning of His being knowing is that He is not ignorant—for 'not ignorant' can be conceived without being knowing, so it is more general. And such that it is said: the meaning of His being powerful is that He is not incapable—for 'not incapable' can be conceived without being powerful. Negation of beginning necessarily implies pre-eternity (al-qidam), and negation of division does not imply knowledge and power, for a thing may be free from ignorance and knowledge, like an inanimate object. So the meaning of 'knowingness' (al-‘alimiyya) and 'powerfulness' (al-qadiriyya) is not a negative meaning, nor is it a relational meaning (ma‘na idafi). For the relational noun (al-ism al-idafi) from the relative (al-mudaf) is taken with the meaning that the act occurs first so that it is called an agent (fa‘il), but it is not so with His being knowing and powerful, for the existence of the known and the empowered is from knowledge and power—it occurs contrary to the placement of relational names, especially according to their principle, for they said: His knowledge is active (fi‘li) not passive (infi‘ali). According to the mutakallimun, knowledge follows the known; according to them, the known follows knowledge, and the empowered follows power. They said: The first intellect only emanates from Him due to His knowledge of His essence. So when knowledge is not a negative meaning, nor a relational meaning, nor composed of them, then it is determined that it is an attribute (sifa) of the described.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a proof that attributes like 'knowing' and 'powerful' are real, positive attributes of God, not reducible to negatives or relations. The author argues: (1) 'Not ignorant' is broader than 'knowing'—a stone is not ignorant but not knowing. So 'knowing' cannot be merely the negation of ignorance. (2) Similarly, 'not incapable' is broader than 'powerful'. (3) Negations like 'no beginning' give pre-eternity, not knowledge; 'no division' gives oneness, not knowledge. (4) A relational meaning would require the act to be prior to the name (e.g., 'creator' comes from creation), but knowledge and power are prior to the known and empowered—the known follows knowledge according to the philosophers themselves. Thus, knowledge is not relational. Since it is neither negative nor relational nor a composite, it must be a real attribute of the subject. The argument targets the philosophers' claim that knowledge is purely negative (as abstract from matter) or relational. The conclusion is that it is a positive attribute (sifa).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'knowing' is equivalent to 'not ignorant'.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'not ignorant' is a broader concept—an inanimate object can be not ignorant without being knowing. Thus 'knowing' adds a positive meaning beyond mere negation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that 'knowing' cannot be reduced to 'not ignorant'?
A) Because God is not ignorant, so it is the same.
B) Because 'not ignorant' is more general, as an inanimate object is not ignorant but not knowing.
C) Because negation of ignorance implies knowledge necessarily.
D) Because the philosophers accept it as a positive meaning.

Key Takeaway
The names 'Knowing' and 'Powerful' affirm real, positive attributes that are not reducible to negations or external relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's knowledge of you is active and ever-present, or do you sometimes live as if He only knows after you act? What would change if you fully internalized that His knowledge is a real, positive attribute that precedes and shapes your every moment?
?
When you think of God's power, do you reduce it to the ability to do things, or do you acknowledge it as a real, inherent reality that is always with you? How does your trust in God's power manifest in your daily decisions, especially when you face difficulty?
?
Examine your heart: have you ever felt that God's attributes are abstract concepts, not concrete realities? How does the argument that they are not mere negations or relations challenge you to know God more intimately and to live in constant awareness of His knowledge and power?

Intellection as Pure Negation
Philosophers argue intellection is purely negative, avoiding multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the First Principle's intellection is purely negative and does not imply multiplicity in the divine essence.

Core Idea
Negation of matter is not an attribute; it does not multiply the essence.

This passage presents the philosophers' response to the Ash'ari criticism that affirming knowledge in God implies multiplicity. They argue that the First Principle's intellection is simply the negation of materiality, a purely negative concept. Using the analogy of a human not being a stone, they show that infinite negations do not fragment the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key claims: the First Principle intellects His essence, and this intellection is the cause of the first effect.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أمر سلبي محض' – purely negative.
2
Identify the core claim about negation
The philosophers assert that abstraction from matter is purely negative. Negations, even if infinite, do not multiply the essence. Understand the example of 'human is not a stone, not mud…'
This is central to rebutting the charge of multiplicity.
3
Grasp the logical consequence
Since the intellect is abstracted from matter, and abstraction is negation, the First Principle's intellection is a necessary consequence of His nature. Matter is a veil; its removal yields intellection.
4
Reflect on the apologetic purpose
Consider how this argument defends the philosophers' view that God knows without positive attributes, thereby avoiding both multiplicity and anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : المبدأ الأول يعقل ذاته، ويعقل ما يلزم ذاته، وعقله لذاته من حيث إنه مجرد عن المادة لذاته وهو علة المبدأ الثاني وهو المعلول الأول، فلزم وجوده من حيث إنه يعقل ذاته وسائر الأشياء تكون معلولة على نسق الوجود منه ، وتجرده لذاته عن المادة أمر سلبي محض، فلم تتكثر الذات بسلب شيء منه، كما يقال الإنسان ليس بحجر ولا مدر ولا نجم ولا شجر وكذا إلى غير نهاية فهذا السلب وإن بلغ إلى غير النهاية لا يوجب تكثيرًا في ذاته، وإنما قلنا: إن تعقله لذاته أمر سلبي ؛ لأن العقل هو المجرد عن المادة، وتجريده عن المادة اسلب المادة عنه، فلزم أن يكون عالما لتجرده عن المادة وعلائقها ؛ إذ المادة هي التي كانت حجابًا عن التعقلات، فلما ارتفع الحجاب قصار العقل مدركا عند ارتفاع الحجاب المحسوس أدراك الحس المحسوس، وعند ارتفاع الحجاب المعقول إدراك العقل
The philosophers said: The First Principle (al-mabda’ al-awwal) intellects (ya‘qil) His essence, and intellects what follows from His essence. And His intellection of His essence, inasmuch as He is abstract from matter (mujarrad ‘an al-madda) by His essence, is the cause of the second principle, which is the first effect. So His existence follows from His intellection of His essence, and all other things are effects in the order of existence from Him. And His abstraction from matter is a purely negative (salbi mahd) matter. So the essence is not multiplied by the negation of something from it, just as it is said: a human is not a stone, not mud, not a star, not a tree, and so on ad infinitum. This negation, even if it reaches infinity, does not cause multiplicity in his essence. And we only said that His intellection of His essence is negative because the intellect is that which is abstract from matter, and its abstraction from matter is the negation of matter from it. Thus it follows that He is knowing due to His abstraction from matter and its attachments, since matter is that which was a veil from intellections. So when the veil is removed, the intellect becomes perceptive. With the removal of the sensible veil, sense perceives the sensible; with the removal of the intelligible veil, the intellect perceives.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' position in response to the Ash'ari arguments. They claim that the First Principle intellects His essence and what follows from it. His intellection of Himself, being abstract from matter, is purely negative—it is simply the negation of materiality. This negation does not multiply the essence, just as saying 'a human is not a stone, not mud...' does not create multiplicity despite the infinite negations. The philosophers argue that the intellect is essentially abstract, and abstraction is a negation of matter. Therefore, the First Principle's knowledge of Himself (and of other things) is a necessary consequence of His abstract nature. Matter is a veil to intellection; when it is removed, intellection naturally occurs. Thus, the philosophers maintain that the divine attributes (like knowledge) are not positive additions but rather necessary consequences of pure negativity. This directly counters the Ash'ari claim that knowledge must be a positive attribute.

Key Terms

يعقل ذاته
مجرد عن المادة
أمر سلبي محض
تكثير
حجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers completely deny that God has any knowledge.
Correction: The passage shows they affirm knowledge but as a necessary consequence of abstraction, not a positive attribute—thus knowledge is not denied, but redefined.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers in this passage, why does the First Principle's intellection of Himself not cause multiplicity in His essence?
A) Because His essence is simple and cannot be multiplied.
B) Because His intellection is a purely negative matter—the negation of materiality—and negations do not multiply, just as a human is not a stone etc.
C) Because His intellection is an act and acts do not affect the essence.
D) Because the First Principle is beyond all attributes.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the First Principle's intellection is purely negative—a mere negation of materiality—and thus does not multiply His essence, allowing Him to know without positive attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is not like anything I know,' do you feel that your knowledge of Him is actually increasing, or are you just avoiding saying something wrong? If true knowledge of God is only by negation, would you be satisfied with a God defined only by what He is not? How does this affect your worship?
?
The philosophers argue that removal of the veil (matter) leads to intellection. What material attachments in your life are veiling your heart from a deeper awareness of God today? What specific step can you take this week to remove one of those veils?
?
If infinite negations do not add anything to God's essence, then your saying 'God is not unjust' does not make Him more perfect. But do you truly believe that He is just, or do you only negate injustice from Him? How can you move beyond mere negation to affirm His justice in a way that changes how you treat others?

Divine Attributes
advanced
8 min
Divine Attributes as Positive Realities
Argues that God's knowledge and power are positive attributes, not mere negations or relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of knowledge and power must be understood as positive attributes inhering in God, not merely as negations of their opposites or as relational concepts.

Core Idea
God's attributes are real, not just negations or relations.

Sanusi tackles a foundational issue: what kind of meaning do God's attributes carry? Are they merely negative (e.g., 'knowing' means 'not ignorant') or relational (e.g., 'powerful' means 'able to act')? This passage argues that both options fail, leaving only one conclusion: the attributes are positive, real qualities of the Divine Essence. You'll follow his logical dismantling of the alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the flow: it starts mid-sentence with an example, then makes two arguments, then concludes.
The passage begins mid-sentence; the previous page likely set up the discussion about 'not weak' not implying 'powerful'.
2
Identify the two rejected alternatives
The author rejects two interpretations of 'knowing' and 'powerful': (1) that they are negative meanings (salbī), and (2) that they are relational meanings (iḍāfī). Find the reasoning for each rejection.
The rejection of the negative meaning uses the example of an inanimate object: it is neither ignorant nor knowing.
3
Understand the relational argument
The author argues that relational names, like 'agent', require the action to occur first. But God's knowledge and power exist even if the known or predestined do not exist yet. This shows they are not relational.
The author contrasts the Ash'ari view (knowledge follows the known) with the philosophical view (the known follows knowledge).
4
Grasp the final conclusion
After eliminating negative and relational meanings, the only possibility left is that 'knowing' and 'powerful' are real attributes (ṣifa) of the subject (al-mawṣūf). This is the key takeaway.
The conclusion is stated explicitly: 'fa-taʿayyana annahu ṣifatun li-l-mawṣūf'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قادرًا أنه غير عاجز، فإن غير العاجز قد يتصور ولا يكون قادرًا، فنفي الأولية بالضرورة (۱) يقتضي القدم، ونفي الانقسام لا يقتضي العلم والقدرة، فقد يخلو الشيء عن الجهل والعلم كالجماد، فإذا معنى العالمية والقادرية ليس معنى سلبيا، وليس هو أيضا معنى إضافيًا، فإن الاسم الإضافي من المضاف يتلقى بمعنى أنه يحصل الفعل أولا حتى يسمى فاعلا، وليس كذلك كونه عالما قادرًا، فإن وجود المعلوم والمقدور من العلم والقدرة يحصل فهو على خلاف وضع الأسامي الإضافية خصوصا على أصلهم فإنهم قالوا علمه تعالى فعلي لا انفعالي، وعند المتكلمين العلم يتبع المعلوم، وعندهم المعلوم يتبع العلم والمقدور يتبع القدرة، هذا قالوا: إنما يصدر عنه العقل الأول لعلمه بذاته، فإذا لم يكن العلم معنى سلبيًا، ولا معنى إضافيا ولا مركبًا منهما، فتعين أنه صفة للموصوف.
[He is] powerful, that He is not weak, for 'not weak' can be conceived without being powerful. So the negation of firstness necessarily implies pre-eternity, but the negation of divisibility does not imply knowledge and power, for a thing can be devoid of ignorance and knowledge, like inanimate objects. Thus the meaning of 'knowing' and 'powerful' is not a negative meaning, nor is it a relational meaning. For a relational name from the two relata is understood in the sense that the action occurs first so that he is called an agent. But this is not the case with His being knowing and powerful, for the existence of the known and the predestined from knowledge and power occurs. So it is contrary to the position of relational names, especially according to their principle, for they said that His knowledge is active, not passive. According to the mutakallimun, knowledge follows the known, and according to them, the known follows knowledge, and the predestined follows power. They said: only the First Intellect proceeds from Him by His knowledge of Himself. So if knowledge is not a negative meaning, nor a relational meaning, nor a composite of the two, then it is determined that it is an attribute of the subject.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. The author argues that the divine attributes of knowledge and power are not merely negative attributes (like 'not ignorant') nor relational attributes (like 'being an agent which requires the act to occur first'). He provides reasons: (1) Negation of something does not imply the opposite positive attribute; for example, 'not weak' does not imply 'powerful', and 'not ignorant' does not imply 'knowing', because something may be devoid of both, like an inanimate object. (2) Relational attributes depend on the actual occurrence of the action, whereas God's knowledge and power exist independently of the known and predestined. He cites the Ash'ari principle that knowledge follows the known, but the philosophers say the known follows knowledge. The author concludes that since the meaning of 'knowing' is not negative, not relational, nor composite, it must be a real attribute inhering in the subject.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that God's attributes like 'knowing' are simply negations of their opposites (e.g., 'not ignorant'), or that they are relational (e.g., 'powerful' means 'able to act' only when acting).
Correction: The author shows that negation of ignorance does not imply knowledge (inanimate objects are neither), and relational attributes depend on actual occurrence of the act, which is not the case for God's attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the meaning of 'knowing' not a negative meaning?
A) Because negation of ignorance does not imply knowledge.
B) Because God's knowledge is active, not passive.
C) Because knowledge follows the known.
D) Because relational names require action before the name.

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes of knowledge and power are real, positive qualities of God, not merely negations or relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge is an active, positive reality—not a mere absence of ignorance—how does that change the way you approach Him in your daily life? Are you living as if He truly knows you intimately, or have you reduced His knowledge to a passive fact? Commit to one concrete action today that acknowledges His active knowledge of your heart.
?
The passage says 'not weak' does not equal 'powerful'; you can conceive of something not weak yet not powerful. Have you ever settled for a negative view of God—thanking Him for what He is not rather than praising Him for what He is? Write down one attribute you have understood only negatively and now reframe it positively in prayer.
?
The author argues that God's attributes are not dependent on creation for their reality. Does your heart rest in the fact that God is Knowing and Powerful regardless of your actions, or do you sometimes feel that His attributes are only relevant when you are 'good'? How can you cultivate a deeper trust in His unchanging nature today?
